<description>
**Problem 1 – “Alternating Lexicographic Sort of Binary Strings”**  
You are given **n** binary strings, each of length **m** (1 ≤ n·m ≤ 10⁶).  
Define a custom order: at odd positions (1‑based) ‘0’ < ‘1’, while at even positions ‘1’ < ‘0’.  
Sort the strings according to this order and output their original indices.  
The strings are pairwise distinct.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

import sys

def transform(s):
    # Build a tuple that respects the alternating order.
    # For odd positions keep the character as is.
    # For even positions invert the character: ‘0’ becomes ‘1’, ‘1’ becomes ‘0’.
    # This way a normal ascending tuple comparison yields the required order.
    lst = []
    for i, ch in enumerate(s):
        if (i + 1) % 2 == 1:          # odd index (1‑based)
            lst.append(ch)           # keep original
        else:                         # even index
            lst.append('1' if ch == '0' else '0')   # invert
    return tuple(lst)

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n, m = map(int, data[:2])
    strings = data[2:]

    indexed = [(i + 1, s) for i, s in enumerate(strings)]
    # Sort using the transformed tuple as key
    indexed.sort(key=lambda x: transform(x[1]))
    # Output only the indices
    print(' '.join(str(idx) for idx, _ in indexed))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read input** – first line gives *n* and *m*, followed by *n* strings.  
2. **Create a list of pairs** *(original_index, string)* to keep track of positions after sorting.  
3. **Define a transformation** that converts a string into a tuple reflecting the alternating order:  
   * odd positions → keep the character,  
   * even positions → invert the character (so that normal ascending comparison works).  
4. **Sort** the list of pairs using Python’s built‑in `list.sort` with the transformation as the key.  
5. **Print** the sorted original indices separated by spaces.  
Complexity: building the key costs O(m) per string, total O(n·m). Sorting costs O(n log n). Memory O(n·m) for storing strings and O(n) for the index list.
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 2 – “Custom Dictionary Order for Words”**  
Given **n** distinct lowercase words, each of length at most **L** (∑ |word| ≤ 10⁶).  
A custom alphabet is supplied as a permutation of the 26 letters.  
Sort the words according to this custom alphabet (i.e., the first differing character decides the order based on the permutation) and output the sorted list of words.  
This is the classic “alien dictionary” sorting problem.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

import sys

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    words = data[1:n+1]
    custom_alphabet = data[n+1]          # a 26‑character string

    # Build a rank dictionary: character -> its order (0‑based)
    rank = {ch: i for i, ch in enumerate(custom_alphabet)}

    def key(word):
        # Convert each character to its rank; the tuple can be compared directly.
        return tuple(rank[ch] for ch in word)

    words.sort(key=key)

    for w in words:
        print(w)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Parse input** – first integer *n*, then *n* words, finally the custom alphabet string.  
2. **Create a rank map** that assigns each letter its position in the custom alphabet (0‑based).  
3. **Define a key function** that transforms a word into a tuple of ranks; Python compares tuples lexicographically, which matches the required order.  
4. **Sort** the list of words using the key function.  
5. **Print** the sorted words, one per line.  
Complexity: building the rank map O(26). Transforming each word costs O(|word|), total O(∑|word|). Sorting costs O(n log n) comparisons, each comparison is O(L) in the worst case, but the key pre‑computes the tuple, so overall O(∑|word| + n log n). Memory O(∑|word|) for storing words and O(n) for the key tuples.
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – “Parity‑Based Number Sorting”**  
You are given **n** distinct integers.  
Define a comparator: when comparing two numbers, look at the least significant bit (LSB).  
* If the LSB is 0 (even), the larger number should come first.  
* If the LSB is 1 (odd), the smaller number should come first.  
Sort the array according to this rule and output the sorted sequence.  
The rule can be extended to any fixed bit position; here we use the LSB for simplicity.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

import sys

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    nums = list(map(int, data[1:n+1]))

    def cmp(a, b):
        # Compare based on LSB parity
        a_even = (a & 1) == 0
        b_even = (b & 1) == 0
        if a_even and b_even:
            # both even → larger first
            return b - a
        if not a_even and not b_even:
            # both odd → smaller first
            return a - b
        # different parity: even numbers are considered “greater” in this order
        return -1 if a_even else 1

    # Python's sort does not accept cmp directly in recent versions,
    # so we convert the comparator to a key using functools.cmp_to_key.
    from functools import cmp_to_key
    nums.sort(key=cmp_to_key(cmp))

    print(' '.join(map(str, nums)))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read input** – first integer *n*, followed by *n* integers.  
2. **Implement a comparator** that follows the parity rule:  
   * Both numbers even → descending order.  
   * Both numbers odd → ascending order.  
   * Even vs odd → even is considered “greater”.  
3. **Convert comparator to a key** using `functools.cmp_to_key` because Python’s `list.sort` expects a key function.  
4. **Sort** the list with the generated key.  
5. **Print** the sorted numbers separated by spaces.  
Complexity: each comparison is O(1). Sorting costs O(n log n). Memory O(n) for the list.
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
The original problem asks to sort a collection of equal‑length strings where the comparison rule alternates between ascending and descending at each character position.  
The appropriate technique is **sorting with a custom comparator / key transformation**.

### High‑level Idea
1. **Define the order for each character position**  
   * Odd positions (1‑based): normal alphabetical order (`A` < `B` < …).  
   * Even positions: reversed alphabetical order (`Z` < `Y` < …).  

2. **Transform each string into a comparable key**  
   * For odd indices keep the original character.  
   * For even indices replace the character by its “inverse” in the alphabet, e.g. `c → chr('Z' - (c - 'A'))`.  
   * After this transformation, a **standard lexicographic (ascending) comparison** of the keys yields exactly the required alternating order.

3. **Sort** the list of (index, string) pairs using the transformed key.  
   * In most languages (including Python, C++, Java) the built‑in sort runs in `O(n log n)` time.  
   * The key construction costs `O(m)` per string, so total preprocessing is `O(n·m)`.  
   * Overall complexity: `O(n·m + n log n)`, which satisfies the constraint `n·m ≤ 10⁶`.

### Why a Custom Comparator Works
A comparator decides the relative order of two elements by looking at the first position where they differ.  
The problem’s rule is exactly such a comparator, but it changes direction depending on the parity of the position.  
Instead of writing a comparator that checks parity on the fly (which would be slower because the comparator would be invoked many times), we **pre‑encode the direction into the key**.  
The key is a tuple (or string) where each component already reflects the required ordering, allowing the sort algorithm to use its highly optimized comparison routine.

### Steps to Implement the Solution
1. **Read input** (`n`, `m`, and the `n` strings).  
2. **Store original indices** because the output must be the permutation of indices after sorting.  
3. **Create a function `make_key(s)`**:  
   ```text
   key = []
   for i from 0 to m‑1:
       if (i+1) is odd:   key.append(s[i])                     # keep
       else:               key.append(inverse(s[i]))           # invert
   return tuple(key)   # or a string built from the list
   ```
   The inverse of a character `c` is `chr(ord('Z') - (ord(c) - ord('A')))` for uppercase letters.  

4. **Sort** the list `pairs = [(idx, s), …]` with `pairs.sort(key = lambda p: make_key(p[1]))`.  
5. **Output** the indices in the sorted order.

### Generic Tutorial for “Sorting with Custom Order”

| Phase | What to Do | Reason |
|-------|------------|--------|
| **1. Formalise the order** | Write down how two elements should be compared (e.g., per position, per attribute, parity‑based). | Gives a clear specification for the comparator. |
| **2. Choose representation** | Decide whether to implement a comparator function directly or to transform each element into a *key* that can be compared with the default order. | Key‑based sorting is usually faster because the key is computed once per element. |
| **3. Build the key** | For each element, create a tuple/list/string where each component respects the required direction (normal or reversed). | After transformation, ordinary lexicographic comparison matches the custom rule. |
| **4. Use a stable, efficient sort** | Call the language’s built‑in sort (`O(n log n)`) with the key function. | Guarantees optimal time complexity and leverages highly tuned library code. |
| **5. Retrieve original information** | Keep auxiliary data (original indices, extra fields) alongside each element if the output demands it. | Allows reconstruction of the required answer after sorting. |
| **6. Validate** | Test on edge cases: minimal size, maximal length, all characters equal, reverse‑order strings, etc. | Ensures the transformation correctly captures the custom order. |

### Complexity Summary
* **Key construction:** `O(n·m)` – each of the `n` strings of length `m` is scanned once.  
* **Sorting:** `O(n log n)` comparisons, each comparison is `O(1)` because the keys are pre‑computed.  
* **Memory:** `O(n·m)` for storing the original strings plus `O(n·m)` for the keys (often the key can be a view of the original string, reducing extra memory).

This approach is the standard method for any problem where the ordering rule can be expressed as a per‑position (or per‑attribute) transformation, making it a powerful tool in competitive programming and algorithmic problem solving.

    <description>
      <title>Balanced Parentheses</title>
      Given a string consisting only of characters '(' and ')', determine whether the parentheses are balanced. A string is balanced if every opening parenthesis '(' has a corresponding closing parenthesis ')' and the pairs are properly nested.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def is_balanced(s: str) -> bool:
    """
    Returns True if the parentheses in string s are balanced, otherwise False.
    """
    balance = 0                     # number of unmatched '(' seen so far
    for ch in s:
        if ch == '(':
            balance += 1            # we have one more opening bracket to match
        elif ch == ')':
            balance -= 1            # try to match a previous '('
            if balance < 0:         # more ')' than '(' at this point → invalid
                return False
        else:
            # according to the problem only '(' and ')' appear,
            # but we ignore any other character for safety
            continue
    # after processing all characters, all '(' must be matched
    return balance == 0

# Driver code to read multiple test cases
if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        s = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        out_lines.append("YES" if is_balanced(s) else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – first integer `t` (number of test cases) then `t` strings.  
      2. **For each string** run a linear scan keeping a counter `balance`.  
         * Increment for '(' .  
         * Decrement for ')' .  
         * If `balance` ever becomes negative, a closing bracket appears without a matching opening one → answer is NO.  
      3. After the scan, if `balance` is zero the string is perfectly matched → answer is YES; otherwise there are unmatched '(' → NO.  
      4. Collect answers and output them line‑by‑line.  
      The algorithm runs in **O(n)** time per test case and uses **O(1)** extra memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

    <description>
      <title>String Reduction to Empty</title>
      You are given a string consisting of the letters 'x', 'y', and 'z'. In one operation you may delete any two adjacent characters that are different (e.g., "xy", "yz", "zx", etc.). Determine whether it is possible to reduce the whole string to an empty string using a sequence of such operations.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def can_reduce_to_empty(s: str) -> bool:
    """
    Returns True if the string can be completely erased by repeatedly deleting
    adjacent different characters.
    """
    # The operation removes exactly one of each of two different letters.
    # Therefore the parity (odd/even) of the count of each character never changes.
    # The string can become empty only if all three counts have the same parity.
    cnt = [0, 0, 0]                 # counts for 'x', 'y', 'z'
    for ch in s:
        if ch == 'x':
            cnt[0] += 1
        elif ch == 'y':
            cnt[1] += 1
        elif ch == 'z':
            cnt[2] += 1
    # Check parity
    parity = [c % 2 for c in cnt]
    return parity[0] == parity[1] == parity[2]

if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        s = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        out.append("YES" if can_reduce_to_empty(s) else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Observation** – each operation deletes one of two different letters, so the parity (odd/even) of the count of each letter never changes.  
      2. The only way to reach an empty string (all counts zero, which are even) is that initially all three counts have the same parity.  
      3. **Algorithm**:  
         * Count occurrences of 'x', 'y', and 'z'.  
         * Compute each count modulo 2.  
         * If the three remainders are equal → answer YES, else NO.  
      4. This runs in **O(|s|)** time and **O(1)** memory per test case.  
    </planning>
  </problem>

      <title>Stone Game (Take 1 or 2 Stones)</title>
      Two players take turns removing stones from a pile. On each turn a player may take either 1 or 2 stones. The player who takes the last stone wins. Given the initial number of stones `n`, determine whether the first player has a winning strategy assuming both play optimally.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def first_player_wins(n: int) -> bool:
    """
    Returns True if the first player can force a win with n stones,
    when each move may take 1 or 2 stones.
    """
    # This is a classic impartial combinatorial game.
    # The losing positions are exactly the multiples of 3.
    return n % 3 != 0

if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx])
        idx += 1
        out.append("YES" if first_player_wins(n) else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Game analysis** – compute Grundy numbers (or simply observe small cases):  
         * n = 0 → losing (no move).  
         * n = 1,2 → winning (take all).  
         * n = 3 → any move leaves 1 or 2 (both winning for the opponent) → losing.  
         * The pattern repeats every 3, so positions where `n % 3 == 0` are losing.  
      2. **Decision rule** – first player wins iff `n % 3 != 0`.  
      3. **Implementation** – for each test case read `n`, apply the rule, output "YES" for win, "NO" otherwise.  
      4. Complexity is **O(1)** per test case, total **O(t)** time, **O(1)** memory.  
    </planning>

  <algorithm>
    # Tutorial: Greedy Counting for Pair‑Deletion Problems

    Many problems, like the original *Casimir* task, ask whether a string can be completely erased by repeatedly deleting **pairs** of characters according to a fixed rule set.  
    The key insight is that each allowed operation removes a **fixed multiset of characters**.  
    Consequently, the total number of each character that participates in deletions is constrained, and we can often decide feasibility by simple counting.

    ## Core Idea
    *Identify the invariant* – a quantity that does **not** change (or changes in a predictable way) after each operation.  
    For pair‑deletion problems the invariant is usually the **difference** or **linear combination** of character frequencies.

    ### Example (original problem)
    - Allowed moves: delete **(A,B)** or **(B,C)**.  
    - Each move always removes **exactly one B** and **one non‑B** (either A or C).  
    - Therefore the total number of B's removed equals the total number of non‑B letters removed.  
    - Let `cntA`, `cntB`, `cntC` be the frequencies.  
    - After all deletions the string is empty ⇒ all letters are removed, so  
      `cntB = cntA + cntC`.  
    - Additionally the length must be even (each move deletes two letters).  

    If the condition holds, a sequence of moves always exists (the order can be chosen greedily), otherwise it is impossible.

    ## General Steps for Greedy Counting Solutions
    1. **Parse the input** and count occurrences of each relevant character.  
    2. **Write the invariant equation** derived from the allowed operations.  
       - Often it is a linear equation like `k1*cntX + k2*cntY = k3*cntZ`.  
    3. **Check necessary conditions** (e.g., total length parity).  
    4. **Decide**: if all conditions are satisfied → answer “YES”, else “NO”.  

    ## Why Greedy Works
    When the invariant is satisfied, we can construct a concrete sequence:
    - Repeatedly pick any available pair that respects the rule (e.g., an A with a B, or a B with a C).  
    - Because the counts guarantee that a partner always exists until the string is empty, no backtracking is required.  
    This “any order works” property is what makes the solution greedy.

    ## Common Pitfalls
    - **Ignoring parity**: each operation removes a fixed number of characters, so the total length must be a multiple of that number.  
    - **Overlooking multiple invariants**: some problems have more than one independent constraint (e.g., parity of each character).  
    - **Assuming existence without proof**: after the counting check, briefly argue that a constructive sequence can be built (often by simple iteration).

    ## Complexity
    - Counting characters is linear in the string length, `O(n)`.  
    - Memory usage is `O(1)` because we store only a few integer counters.  

    ## When to Use This Approach
    - The operation set deletes a **fixed-size multiset** of characters.  
    - The problem asks only for the **possibility** of complete erasure, not the actual sequence.  
    - The alphabet size is small (constant), making counting trivial.

    By mastering this invariant‑based greedy counting technique, you can solve a wide range of pair‑deletion and reduction problems efficiently.    
  </algorithm>

2. **Count characters**
   - For each `s`, compute:
     - `cntA` = number of `'A'` in `s`
     - `cntB` = number of `'B'` in `s`
     - `cntC` = number of `'C'` in `s`

3. **Check feasibility**
   - The only invariant of the allowed moves is that each turn removes exactly one `'B'` together with either an `'A'` or a `'C'`.
   - Therefore, to delete the whole string we must have:
     - `cntB == cntA + cntC`
   - (If this holds, the total length is automatically even, so no extra parity check is needed.)

4. **Output result**
   - If the condition in step 3 is true → print `"YES"`.
   - Otherwise → print `"NO"`.

5. **Complexity**
   - Each test case processed in `O(|s|)` time, `O(1)` extra memory.

**Planning – “String Erasure with A‑B and B‑C pairs”**

1. **Understand the operation**
   * One move removes either **(A,B)** or **(B,C)** (positions do not matter).
   * Each move deletes exactly **one B** and **one non‑B** (A or C).

2. **Derive the invariant**
   * Let `cntA`, `cntB`, `cntC` be the frequencies in the current string.
   * After any sequence of moves that empties the string, every character must be removed.
   * Because each move removes one B, the total number of B’s removed equals the total number of non‑B’s removed.
   * Hence, in the original string we must have  

        `cntB = cntA + cntC` .                     (1)

   * Also each move deletes two characters, therefore the initial length `n = cntA+cntB+cntC` must be **even**.  
     (If (1) holds, `n = cntA + (cntA+cntC) + cntC = 2·(cntA+cntC)` is automatically even, so checking (1) alone is sufficient.)

3. **Sufficiency argument**
   * If (1) holds, we can always finish:
        - While there is an A, pair it with any B and delete (A,B).
        - After all A’s are gone, the remaining letters are only B and C, and because `cntB = cntC` now, repeatedly delete (B,C) until empty.
   * No dead‑end can appear because the invariant guarantees a partner for every remaining letter.

4. **Algorithm per test case**
   ```
   read string s
   cntA = number of 'A' in s
   cntB = number of 'B' in s
   cntC = number of 'C' in s
   if cntB == cntA + cntC
        print "YES"
   else
        print "NO"
   ```

5. **Complexity**
   * Counting characters: O(|s|) time, |s| ≤ 50.
   * O(1) extra memory.
   * For t test cases total O( Σ|s| ) ≤ 5·10⁴ operations – easily fits limits.

6. **Implementation notes**
   * Use a simple loop over characters; increment three integer counters.
   * Input: first integer `t`, then `t` lines each containing a string (no spaces).
   * Output: one line per test case, exactly “YES” or “NO” (any case accepted).

<explanation>
The plan correctly captures the necessary and sufficient condition for the string to be completely erased.  
Each operation always removes exactly one ‘B’ together with one non‑‘B’ (either ‘A’ or ‘C’). Therefore, after any sequence of operations that empties the string, the total number of removed ‘B’s must equal the total number of removed ‘A’s plus ‘C’s, i.e.  

 cntB = cntA + cntC.  

If this equality holds, we can always finish the erasure: first pair every ‘A’ with a distinct ‘B’ (possible because cntB ≥ cntA), removing all ‘A’s; the remaining letters are only ‘B’ and ‘C’, and after the first phase we have cntB = cntC, so we can repeatedly delete a ‘B’ with a ‘C’ until the string is empty. No dead‑end can occur, and the length is automatically even when the equality holds.  

Thus the algorithm “count the letters and check whether cntB equals cntA+cntC” is both necessary and sufficient. The implementation runs in O(|s|) per test case, well within the limits.

Consequently, the proposed plan is correct for solving the problem.

**Planning**

1. **Understand the operation invariant**  
   - Each move removes exactly one **B** and one non‑B (**A** or **C**).  
   - Therefore the total number of **B** characters eliminated equals the total number of **A** + **C** characters eliminated.  
   - To delete the whole string we must eliminate all characters, so  
     \[
     \text{cnt}_B = \text{cnt}_A + \text{cnt}_C
     \]
   - Every move deletes 2 letters, thus the initial length must be even. (If the equality holds, the length is automatically even because  
     \(\text{len}= \text{cnt}_A+\text{cnt}_B+\text{cnt}_C = (\text{cnt}_A+\text{cnt}_C)+\text{cnt}_B = 2\text{cnt}_B\).)

2. **Derive decision rule**  
   For a given string `s` let `a`, `b`, `c` be the counts of ‘A’, ‘B’, ‘C’.  
   The string can be completely erased **iff** `b == a + c`.  

3. **Algorithm per test case**  
   - Read string `s`.  
   - Initialise three counters `a = b = c = 0`.  
   - Scan characters of `s` and increment the appropriate counter.  
   - After the scan, if `b == a + c` output `"YES"` else output `"NO"`.

4. **Overall procedure**  
   - Read integer `t`.  
   - Loop `t` times, applying the per‑test‑case algorithm and printing the answer on its own line.

5. **Correctness argument**  
   - *Necessity*: Every move removes one B and one non‑B, so after any sequence the number of removed B’s equals the number of removed A’s plus C’s. If the whole string is removed, all characters are removed, giving `b = a + c`.  
   - *Sufficiency*: If `b = a + c`, we can greedily perform moves: while there is an A, pair it with any B (possible because `b` is at least `a`). Remove those two. After all A’s are gone, the remaining counts satisfy `b = c`; pair each remaining B with a C. All letters are eliminated. Hence a full erasure exists.

6. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Counting characters scans each string once: `O(|s|)` time.  
   - With `t` test cases, total time `O( Σ|s| ) ≤ O( t * 50 )`, well within limits.  
   - Only three integer counters are stored: `O(1)` extra memory.

7. **Edge cases to consider**  
   - Strings of length 1 → automatically `NO` because equality cannot hold.  
   - Strings containing only B’s → `a = c = 0`, condition reduces to `b == 0`; only empty string (not allowed) passes, so answer `NO`.  
   - Strings where `b` is zero but `a` or `c` non‑zero → condition fails, answer `NO`.  
   - All other cases are handled by the equality test.

8. **Implementation notes**  
   - Use `std::string` (C++) / `input()` (Python) to read each test case.  
   - Increment counters with a simple `for ch in s:` switch / if‑elif.  
   - Print results using the exact words `"YES"` or `"NO"` (any case accepted).  
<root>
<explanation>The plan correctly captures the invariant of the operations: each turn removes exactly one ‘B’ together with exactly one non‑‘B’ character. Consequently, after any sequence of moves the total number of removed ‘B’s equals the total number of removed ‘A’s plus ‘C’s. For the whole string to disappear we must have cnt_B = cnt_A + cnt_C, which also guarantees an even length. The plan shows that this condition is not only necessary but also sufficient: if cnt_B = cnt_A + cnt_C we can first pair every ‘A’ with a distinct ‘B’ (possible because cnt_B ≥ cnt_A) and then pair the remaining ‘B’s with ‘C’s (the remaining counts satisfy cnt_B = cnt_C). Thus a complete erasure always exists under this equality. The algorithm simply counts the three letters for each test case and checks the equality, running in O(|s|) time and O(1) memory, well within the limits. Therefore the proposed plan correctly solves the problem.</explanation>

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    out_lines = []
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(t):
        s = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        cntA = s.count('A')
        cntB = s.count('B')
        cntC = s.count('C')
        out_lines.append("YES" if cntB == cntA + cntC else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 1 – Minimum Flips to Obtain a “Good” Sub‑matrix**

You are given an n × m binary matrix (0 = empty, 1 = filled).  
A sub‑matrix of size a × b (a ≥ 2, b ≥ 2) is called *good* if all its border cells are 1 and all inner cells are 0.  
You may flip any cell (0↔1) at a cost of 1.  
Find the minimum total cost to turn **any** sub‑matrix into a good one.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

import sys

def read_input():
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    cases = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it)); m = int(next(it))
        grid = [list(map(int, list(next(it)))) for _ in range(n)]
        cases.append((n,m,grid))
    return cases

# Prefix sums for fast rectangle queries
def build_prefix(grid):
    n, m = len(grid), len(grid[0])
    pref = [[0]*(m+1) for _ in range(n+1)]
    for i in range(n):
        row_sum = 0
        for j in range(m):
            row_sum += grid[i][j]
            pref[i+1][j+1] = pref[i][j+1] + row_sum
    return pref

def rect_sum(pref, x1, y1, x2, y2):
    # inclusive coordinates
    return pref[x2+1][y2+1] - pref[x1][y2+1] - pref[x2+1][y1] + pref[x1][y1]

def solve_one(n,m,grid):
    pref = build_prefix(grid)

    # Pre‑compute for each column the number of 1’s in any vertical segment
    col_one = [[0]*m for _ in range(n)]
    for j in range(m):
        cnt = 0
        for i in range(n):
            cnt += grid[i][j]
            col_one[i][j] = cnt

    INF = 10**9
    ans = INF

    # Enumerate top row (r1) and bottom row (r2) with distance >=3 (a≥4)
    for r1 in range(n):
        for r2 in range(r1+3, n):
            height = r2 - r1 + 1
            # For each column we compute:
            #   left_cost[j] = flips needed to make column j’s interior (rows r1+1 … r2-1) all 0
            #   border_cost[j] = flips needed to make column j’s top and bottom cells 1
            left_cost = [0]*m
            border_cost = [0]*m
            for j in range(m):
                interior_ones = rect_sum(pref, r1+1, j, r2-1, j)
                left_cost[j] = interior_ones                     # need to turn them to 0
                top_bottom = (1 - grid[r1][j]) + (1 - grid[r2][j])
                border_cost[j] = top_bottom
            # Sliding window for width ≥3 (b≥4)
            cur = 0
            best = INF
            for j in range(m):
                cur += left_cost[j] + border_cost[j]   # add new column as right border
                if j >= 3:
                    # remove column j-3 from the middle part (it becomes interior)
                    cur -= left_cost[j-3] + border_cost[j-3]
                if j >= 3:
                    # we have a window of width 4 (columns j-3 … j)
                    # interior columns are those between the two borders
                    interior = cur - (left_cost[j-3] + border_cost[j-3]) - (left_cost[j] + border_cost[j])
                    # need to add flips for the two vertical borders to become 1
                    vertical = (1 - grid[r1][j-3]) + (1 - grid[r2][j-3]) + (1 - grid[r1][j]) + (1 - grid[r2][j])
                    total = interior + vertical
                    ans = min(ans, total)
    return ans

def main():
    cases = read_input()
    out = []
    for n,m,grid in cases:
        out.append(str(solve_one(n,m,grid)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

1. **Read all test cases** – store each matrix as a list of integer lists.  
2. **Build a 2‑D prefix sum** to answer “how many 1’s are inside any rectangle” in O(1).  
3. **Fix the top and bottom rows** of the candidate portal (distance ≥ 3 because a ≥ 4).  
4. For the current pair of rows, compute for every column:  
   * `interior_ones` – number of 1’s between the two rows (these must become 0).  
   * `border_cost` – flips needed to make the two cells on the chosen rows equal to 1.  
5. **Slide a window of width ≥ 4** across columns.  
   * The leftmost and rightmost columns of the window are the vertical borders; all columns in between are interior.  
   * Using the pre‑computed column costs we can update the total cost in O(1) when the window moves.  
6. Keep the global minimum over all row pairs and all window positions.  
7. Output the minimum for each test case.  
Complexity: O(n²·m) ≤ 400³ ≈ 64 M operations, fast enough because the inner work is O(1) per column shift.  
Memory: O(n·m) for the prefix sum and auxiliary arrays.

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 2 – Minimum Cost to Make a “Frame” of 1’s**

Given an n × m binary matrix.  
A *frame* of size a × b (a ≥ 3, b ≥ 3) is defined as the set of cells that lie on the outer border of the rectangle; all those cells must be 1, the inner cells can be anything.  
You may flip any cell at cost 1.  
Determine the minimum number of flips required to obtain at least one frame of 1’s.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step Python 3 solution

import sys

def input_data():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    cases = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it)); m = int(next(it))
        mat = [list(map(int, list(next(it)))) for _ in range(n)]
        cases.append((n,m,mat))
    return cases

def prefix(mat):
    n, m = len(mat), len(mat[0])
    pref = [[0]*(m+1) for _ in range(n+1)]
    for i in range(n):
        row = 0
        for j in range(m):
            row += mat[i][j]
            pref[i+1][j+1] = pref[i][j+1] + row
    return pref

def rect_sum(pref, x1, y1, x2, y2):
    return pref[x2+1][y2+1] - pref[x1][y2+1] - pref[x2+1][y1] + pref[x1][y1]

def solve_one(n,m,mat):
    pref = prefix(mat)
    INF = 10**9
    ans = INF

    # enumerate top and bottom rows (distance >=2)
    for top in range(n):
        for bottom in range(top+2, n):
            # for each column compute:
            #   vertical_border = flips to make cells (top, col) and (bottom, col) = 1
            #   vertical_inside = flips to make cells between top+1 … bottom-1 = 1 (they belong to left/right borders)
            vert_border = [0]*m
            vert_inside = [0]*m
            for c in range(m):
                vert_border[c] = (1 - mat[top][c]) + (1 - mat[bottom][c])
                inside_ones = rect_sum(pref, top+1, c, bottom-1, c)
                # we need these cells to be 1 for the left/right border
                vert_inside[c] = (bottom - top - 1) - inside_ones   # number of 0's to turn into 1
            # sliding window for width >=3
            cur = 0
            for right in range(m):
                cur += vert_border[right] + vert_inside[right]
                if right >= 2:
                    # left column of the window
                    left = right - 2
                    # cost of the two vertical borders (left & right)
                    border_cost = vert_border[left] + vert_border[right]
                    # interior columns (if any) must have only top/bottom cells = 1, already counted in vert_border
                    # but we must also ensure the top and bottom rows between left+1 … right-1 are 1
                    top_bottom_mid = 0
                    for col in range(left+1, right):
                        top_bottom_mid += (1 - mat[top][col]) + (1 - mat[bottom][col])
                    total = border_cost + top_bottom_mid + (vert_inside[left] + vert_inside[right])
                    ans = min(ans, total)
    return ans

def main():
    cases = input_data()
    out = []
    for n,m,mat in cases:
        out.append(str(solve_one(n,m,mat)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read the matrix** for each test case.  
2. **Create a 2‑D prefix sum** to query the number of 1’s in any vertical segment quickly.  
3. **Fix a pair of rows** (top, bottom) that will be the horizontal borders of the frame (need at least one row between them).  
4. For every column compute two values:  
   * `vert_border` – flips needed to make the two cells on the selected rows equal to 1.  
   * `vert_inside` – flips needed to make the cells between the two rows equal to 1 (these belong to the left/right borders).  
5. **Slide a window of width ≥ 3** across columns.  
   * The leftmost and rightmost columns of the window become the vertical borders.  
   * All columns strictly inside the window only need their top and bottom cells to be 1 (already accounted in `vert_border`).  
   * The cost of a window is the sum of:  
        – `vert_border` for the two border columns,  
        – `vert_inside` for the two border columns,  
        – flips for the top/bottom rows of the interior columns.  
6. Keep the smallest cost over all row pairs and all windows.  
7. Output the answer.  
Complexity: O(n²·m) time, O(n·m) memory – acceptable for n,m ≤ 400.
</planning>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – Minimum Flips to Obtain a “Hollow Rectangle”**

You are given an n × m binary grid.  
A *hollow rectangle* of size a × b (a ≥ 5, b ≥ 4) is defined exactly like the portal in the original statement: all cells on the perimeter must be 1, all interior cells must be 0, and the four corner cells are ignored (they may be 0 or 1).  
Find the minimum number of cell flips required to create at least one hollow rectangle.
</description>
<code>
# Detailed Python 3 solution using DP + prefix sums

import sys

def read():
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    T = int(next(it))
    tests = []
    for _ in range(T):
        n = int(next(it)); m = int(next(it))
        g = [list(map(int, list(next(it)))) for _ in range(n)]
        tests.append((n,m,g))
    return tests

def build_prefix(g):
    n, m = len(g), len(g[0])
    pref = [[0]*(m+1) for _ in range(n+1)]
    for i in range(n):
        row = 0
        for j in range(m):
            row += g[i][j]
            pref[i+1][j+1] = pref[i][j+1] + row
    return pref

def col_ones(pref, r1, r2, c):
    # number of 1's in column c between rows r1..r2 inclusive
    return pref[r2+1][c+1] - pref[r1][c+1] - pref[r2+1][c] + pref[r1][c]

def solve_one(n,m,g):
    pref = build_prefix(g)
    INF = 10**9
    ans = INF

    # Pre‑compute for each column the cumulative number of 1's from top to each row
    col_cum = [[0]*m for _ in range(n)]
    for c in range(m):
        cnt = 0
        for r in range(n):
            cnt += g[r][c]
            col_cum[r][c] = cnt

    # Iterate over top row (top) and bottom row (bot) with distance >=4 (a >=5)
    for top in range(n):
        for bot in range(top+4, n):
            height = bot - top + 1

            # For each column compute:
            #   up_down = flips to make cells (top, col) and (bot, col) = 1
            #   middle0 = flips to make cells between top+1 .. bot-1 = 0
            up_down = [0]*m
            middle0 = [0]*m
            for c in range(m):
                up_down[c] = (1 - g[top][c]) + (1 - g[bot][c])
                ones_mid = col_ones(pref, top+1, bot-1, c)
                middle0[c] = ones_mid                     # need to turn them to 0

            # Sliding window for width >=4 (b >=4)
            # We keep a running sum of up_down + middle0 for the current window
            cur = 0
            left = 0
            for right in range(m):
                cur += up_down[right] + middle0[right]

                # Ensure window width at least 4
                if right - left + 1 > 4:
                    cur -= up_down[left] + middle0[left]
                    left += 1

                if right - left + 1 >= 4:
                    # left and right columns are the vertical borders
                    # interior columns are (left+1 .. right-1)
                    interior_cost = cur
                    # subtract the cost of the two border columns because they will be handled separately
                    interior_cost -= (up_down[left] + middle0[left])
                    interior_cost -= (up_down[right] + middle0[right])

                    # vertical border flips: need the cells on the two borders (excluding corners) to be 1
                    # cells (top+1 .. bot-1, left) and (top+1 .. bot-1, right) must be 1
                    vert_border = (bot - top - 1) - (col_ones(pref, top+1, bot-1, left))   # zeros to turn into 1
                    vert_border += (bot - top - 1) - (col_ones(pref, top+1, bot-1, right))

                    # top and bottom rows between left+1 .. right-1 must be 1
                    top_mid_ones = sum(g[top][c] for c in range(left+1, right))
                    bot_mid_ones = sum(g[bot][c] for c in range(left+1, right))
                    top_bottom_mid = (right - left - 1) - top_mid_ones
                    top_bottom_mid += (right - left - 1) - bot_mid_ones

                    total = interior_cost + vert_border + top_bottom_mid
                    ans = min(ans, total)

    return ans

def main():
    tests = read()
    out = []
    for n,m,g in tests:
        out.append(str(solve_one(n,m,g)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Parse input** and store each grid as integers.  
2. **Build a 2‑D prefix sum** to answer “how many 1’s are in any sub‑rectangle” in O(1).  
3. **Fix a pair of rows** (`top`, `bot`) that will be the horizontal sides of the portal.  
   * The distance must be at least 4 because a ≥ 5.  
4. For every column compute two auxiliary arrays:  
   * `up_down[c]` – flips needed to make the two cells on the selected rows equal to 1.  
   * `middle0[c]` – number of 1’s in the vertical segment between the rows (these must become 0).  
5. **Slide a horizontal window** of width ≥ 4 across columns.  
   * The leftmost and rightmost columns of the window are the vertical borders.  
   * The interior columns only need their top/bottom cells to be 1 (already counted in `up_down`).  
   * Using a running sum we can update the total cost when the window moves in O(1).  
6. For each window compute:  
   * Cost to clear interior vertical cells (`middle0` of interior columns).  
   * Cost to set the two vertical borders to 1 (zeros inside the border segment).  
   * Cost to set the top and bottom rows (excluding corners) to 1.  
7. Keep the minimum over all row pairs and all windows – this is the answer.  
8. Output the answer for each test case.  

**Complexity** – The outer two loops over rows give O(n²). Inside we scan the columns once, so total O(n²·m) ≤ 400³, which fits easily. Memory usage is O(n·m) for the prefix sum and auxiliary arrays.  
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
The original problem asks for the minimum number of flips required to obtain a rectangle whose **perimeter (except the four corners) is all obsidian (1) and the interior is completely empty (0)**.  
The most suitable technique is **Dynamic Programming combined with Prefix‑Sum preprocessing and a sliding‑window optimisation**.

### Why this algorithm works
1. **Prefix sums** give O(1) queries for the number of 1’s (or 0’s) inside any sub‑rectangle.  
   - With a 2‑D prefix array `pref`, the sum of a rectangle `(x1,y1)…(x2,y2)` is obtained by a constant‑time formula.  
2. **Fixing two opposite sides** reduces the 2‑D search to a 1‑D problem.  
   - Choose the top row `r1` and bottom row `r2` (distance ≥ 4).  
   - For every column we can now pre‑compute:  
        * `upDown[col]` – flips needed to make the two cells on rows `r1` and `r2` equal to 1.  
        * `midZero[col]` – number of 1’s between `r1+1` and `r2‑1` (these must become 0).  
3. **Sliding window for columns** turns the remaining search into a linear scan.  
   - A window `[L,R]` of width ≥ 4 represents a candidate portal.  
   - The leftmost and rightmost columns are the vertical borders; all columns strictly inside are “interior”.  
   - Using a running sum we can update the total cost when `R` moves one step to the right and when `L` is increased, both in O(1).  
4. **Dynamic programming / incremental update** keeps the best answer seen so far.  
   - For each `(r1,r2)` pair we compute the minimal cost over all column windows, updating a global minimum.  

Thus the overall complexity is **O(n²·m)** (n ≤ 400, m ≤ 400) which is fast enough, and the memory consumption is **O(n·m)** for the prefix array and a few auxiliary 1‑D arrays.

### Generic tutorial for this class of problems

| Step | What to do | Reason |
|------|------------|--------|
| **1. Input & representation** | Read the grid as integers (0/1). Store in a 2‑D list. | Gives direct random access to any cell. |
| **2. Prefix‑sum construction** | Build `pref[i+1][j+1] = pref[i][j+1] + pref[i+1][j] - pref[i][j] + grid[i][j]`. | Allows O(1) queries for the sum of any rectangle, which is the core of “how many cells are already correct”. |
| **3. Enumerate one dimension pair** | Loop over all possible pairs of rows (or columns) that will become opposite sides of the rectangle. Ensure the distance respects the size constraints. | Reduces a 2‑D rectangle search to a 1‑D problem for the remaining dimension. |
| **4. Pre‑compute column (or row) costs** | For the fixed rows, compute for each column: <br>• Cost to make the two border cells = 1. <br>• Cost to make the interior segment = 0 (or 1, depending on the problem). | These values are independent of the horizontal window and can be reused while sliding. |
| **5. Sliding window / two‑pointer** | Move a right pointer across columns, maintaining a running sum of the costs inside the current window. When the window becomes too wide, move the left pointer and subtract the left column’s contribution. Only evaluate windows whose width satisfies the required minimum. | Gives O(m) processing for each row‑pair instead of O(m²). |
| **6. Compute total cost for a window** | Combine: <br>• Interior vertical cost (already in the running sum). <br>• Vertical border cost (zeros that must become 1). <br>• Horizontal border cost (top/bottom rows between the two vertical borders). | This yields the exact number of flips needed for the rectangle defined by the current window. |
| **7. Keep the global minimum** | After evaluating each window, update the answer if a smaller cost is found. | The problem asks for the minimum over *all* possible portals. |
| **8. Output** | Print the final minimum for each test case. | Required format. |

### Key ideas to remember
- **Prefix sums** turn “how many wrong cells” queries into O(1) operations.  
- **Fixing two opposite sides** transforms a 2‑D optimisation into a 1‑D optimisation, dramatically reducing complexity.  
- **Sliding window / two‑pointer** is the classic way to evaluate all contiguous sub‑arrays of a given minimum length in linear time.  
- **Separate handling of corners**: because corners are free, they are simply omitted from the cost calculations.  

By mastering these techniques you can solve a wide range of grid‑based minimisation problems, such as “minimum flips to obtain a border of 1’s”, “minimum changes to get a hollow rectangle”, or “minimum cost sub‑matrix with given border constraints”.

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 1 – Eulerian Numbers (Exact Number of Ascents)**  
Given integers n and k (0 ≤ k < n), count the number of permutations of {1,…,n} that contain exactly k positions i (1 ≤ i < n) with p_i < p_{i+1}.  
Output the answer modulo 10^9+7.  
Constraints: 1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5, 0 ≤ k ≤ n‑1, sum of n over all test cases ≤ 10^5.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution for the Eulerian number problem

import sys

MOD = 10**9 + 7

def precompute_factorials(N):
    fact = [1] * (N + 1)
    inv_fact = [1] * (N + 1)
    for i in range(1, N + 1):
        fact[i] = fact[i-1] * i % MOD
    # Fermat’s little theorem for modular inverse
    inv_fact[N] = pow(fact[N], MOD-2, MOD)
    for i in range(N, 0, -1):
        inv_fact[i-1] = inv_fact[i] * i % MOD
    return fact, inv_fact

def binom(n, r, fact, inv_fact):
    if r < 0 or r > n:
        return 0
    return fact[n] * inv_fact[r] % MOD * inv_fact[n-r] % MOD

def eulerian(n, k, fact, inv_fact):
    # Eulerian number via inclusion‑exclusion:
    # A(n,k) = Σ_{i=0}^{k+1} (-1)^i * C(n+1, i) * (k+1-i)^n
    res = 0
    for i in range(k+2):
        sign = -1 if i & 1 else 1
        term = binom(n+1, i, fact, inv_fact) * pow(k+1-i, n, MOD) % MOD
        res = (res + sign * term) % MOD
    return res % MOD

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data: 
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    max_n = 0
    queries = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); k = int(data[idx+1]); idx += 2
        queries.append((n, k))
        max_n = max(max_n, n)
    fact, inv_fact = precompute_factorials(max_n + 1)
    out = []
    for n, k in queries:
        out.append(str(eulerian(n, k, fact, inv_fact)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read all test cases** and remember the maximum n to know how far factorials must be pre‑computed.  
2. **Pre‑compute factorials and inverse factorials** up to max_n + 1 mod M. They are needed for fast binomial coefficients.  
3. For each query (n, k) use the inclusion‑exclusion formula for Eulerian numbers:  

   \[
   A(n,k)=\sum_{i=0}^{k+1}(-1)^i\binom{n+1}{i}(k+1-i)^n .
   \]

   The sum runs only k+2 terms, so the total complexity over all test cases is  
   \(\mathcal{O}\big(\sum (k+2)\big) \le \mathcal{O}(n\log MOD)\).  
4. Compute each term with the pre‑computed binomial function and fast modular exponentiation (`pow`).  
5. Keep the running total modulo M, adjust negative values, and output the result.  
6. Overall time: \(O(T + \sum (k))\) ≈ \(O(10^5)\); memory: \(O(max_n)\) for factorial tables.
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 2 – Permutations with At Most m Inversions**  
For a given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000) and m (0 ≤ m ≤ n·(n‑1)/2), count the permutations of {1,…,n} that contain at most m inversions (pairs (i,j) with i < j and p_i > p_j).  
Return the answer modulo 10^9+7.  
Multiple test cases, total n ≤ 10^5.
</description>
<code>
# DP solution for counting permutations with at most m inversions

import sys

MOD = 10**9 + 7

def solve_one(n, m):
    # dp[i][j] = number of permutations of size i with exactly j inversions
    dp = [0] * (m + 1)
    dp[0] = 1
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        new = [0] * (m + 1)
        prefix = 0
        # sliding window of size i (the new element can be placed in i positions)
        for j in range(m + 1):
            prefix = (prefix + dp[j]) % MOD
            if j >= i:
                prefix = (prefix - dp[j - i]) % MOD
            new[j] = prefix
        dp = new
    # sum over 0..m
    return sum(dp) % MOD

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    queries = []
    max_n = 0
    max_m = 0
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); m = int(data[idx+1]); idx += 2
        queries.append((n, m))
        max_n = max(max_n, n)
        max_m = max(max_m, m)
    # Process each query independently (n ≤ 1000, so O(n·m) is fine)
    out = []
    for n, m in queries:
        out.append(str(solve_one(n, m)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Interpret the inversion definition** – each new element inserted into a permutation of size i‑1 creates k new inversions where k is the position (0‑based) it is placed at.  
2. **Dynamic programming**:  
   * `dp[i][j]` = number of permutations of length i with exactly j inversions.  
   * Transition: `dp[i][j] = Σ_{k=0}^{min(j,i-1)} dp[i-1][j-k]`.  
   * Implement with a 1‑D array and a sliding‑window prefix sum to achieve O(m) per i.  
3. Loop `i` from 1 to n, update the array, keeping values only up to m (because larger inversion counts are irrelevant).  
4. After processing all i, sum `dp[n][0..m]` to obtain permutations with *at most* m inversions.  
5. Perform all operations modulo M.  
6. Complexity per test case: O(n·m) time, O(m) memory; fits the constraints because n ≤ 1000 and total n·m over all cases ≤ 10^5·1000 ≈ 10^8 (still acceptable in Python with the sliding‑window trick).  
7. Read all test cases, solve each independently, and output results.
</planning>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – Counting Permutations with Exactly k Peaks**  
A *peak* in a permutation p of length n is an index i (2 ≤ i ≤ n‑1) such that p_{i‑1} < p_i > p_{i+1}.  
Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 200) and k (0 ≤ k ≤ n), compute the number of permutations of {1,…,n} that contain exactly k peaks.  
Return the answer modulo 10^9+7.
</description>
<code>
# DP using the “up‑down” (alternating) permutation recurrence

import sys

MOD = 10**9 + 7

def count_peaks(n, k):
    # dp[i][j][s] – number of permutations of size i,
    #               with j peaks, ending with state s
    # s = 0 : last step was a descent (p_{i-1} > p_i)
    # s = 1 : last step was an ascent  (p_{i-1} < p_i)
    dp = [[[0,0] for _ in range(k+2)] for _ in range(n+1)]
    dp[1][0][0] = dp[1][0][1] = 1   # single element, no peak, both states are neutral

    for i in range(2, n+1):
        for j in range(0, min(k, i-1)+1):
            # place the new largest element i at the end:
            # it creates an ascent from previous element
            dp[i][j][1] = (dp[i-1][j][0] + dp[i-1][j][1]) % MOD
            # place i somewhere inside, creating a new peak if we go up then down
            # The classic recurrence for peaks (Eulerian‑type) is:
            # dp[i][j][0] = Σ_{t=0}^{j} dp[i-1][t][1] * C(i-1, j-t)
            # To keep it O(n^2) we use prefix sums:
            # Here we use a simpler O(n^3) version because n ≤ 200.
            total = 0
            for t in range(j+1):
                total = (total + dp[i-1][t][1]) % MOD
            dp[i][j][0] = total
    # total permutations with exactly k peaks = dp[n][k][0] + dp[n][k][1]
    return (dp[n][k][0] + dp[n][k][1]) % MOD

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); k = int(data[idx+1]); idx += 2
        out.append(str(count_peaks(n, k)))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Define a peak** and notice that the property depends only on the relative order of three consecutive elements.  
2. Use a DP that tracks the last “trend” (ascent or descent) because a new peak is created exactly when an ascent is followed by a descent.  
3. State definition: `dp[i][j][s]` – number of permutations of the first i numbers with j peaks and ending with trend s (0 = descent, 1 = ascent).  
4. Transition:  
   * Adding the new maximum i at the end always creates an ascent, so `dp[i][j][1]` receives contributions from both trends of size i‑1.  
   * Inserting i inside the permutation can turn an existing ascent into a descent, thereby possibly creating a new peak. The recurrence can be derived combinatorially; for small n (≤ 200) a cubic implementation is acceptable.  
5. Initialise `dp[1][0][0] = dp[1][0][1] = 1`.  
6. Iterate `i` from 2 to n, fill the table up to k peaks.  
7. Answer for a test case is `dp[n][k][0] + dp[n][k][1] (mod M)`.  
8. Complexity: O(n³) in the naïve version, which is fine for n ≤ 200; memory O(n·k·2).  
9. Implement input handling for multiple test cases, compute each answer, and output modulo 10^9+7.
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
The original problem asks for the number of permutations of length 2n in which the count of indices i with p_i < p_{i+1} (at “ascents”) is at least n.  
This is a classic combinatorial counting task that can be solved using **Eulerian numbers**.

### What are Eulerian Numbers?
For a given N, the Eulerian number A(N, K) counts permutations of `{1,…,N}` that have exactly K ascents (equivalently, exactly K positions where the next element is larger).  
Properties:
* `0 ≤ K < N`.
* They satisfy the recurrence  
  `A(N, K) = (K+1) * A(N‑1, K) + (N‑K) * A(N‑1, K‑1)`,
  with base `A(1,0)=1`.
* A closed‑form inclusion‑exclusion formula is  
  `A(N, K) = Σ_{i=0}^{K+1} (-1)^i * C(N+1, i) * (K+1‑i)^N`.

### Reducing the Original Question
We need permutations of length 2n with **at least** n ascents.  
Let `total = (2n)!` (all permutations).  
The answer is  

```
ans = Σ_{k=n}^{2n‑1} A(2n, k)   (mod M)
```

Because `A(2n, k)` counts permutations with exactly k ascents, summing from n to the maximum 2n‑1 covers all required cases.

### Computing the Sum Efficiently
Directly evaluating the recurrence for all `k` would be O(n²), which is fine for n up to 10⁵ if we pre‑compute factorials and use the closed form:

1. **Pre‑compute factorials and inverse factorials** up to `2·max_n + 1` modulo `M = 1 000 000 007`.  
   They give us fast binomial coefficients `C(N, i)`.

2. **For each needed k** (from n to 2n‑1) evaluate the inclusion‑exclusion sum.  
   The inner sum runs only `k+2 ≤ 2n+1` terms, so the total work per test case is O(n²) in the worst case, but with `n ≤ 10⁵` we can improve:

   * Observe that the sum of Eulerian numbers over a range can be expressed using the same inclusion‑exclusion formula with a prefix of powers.  
   * Compute `prefix_pow[t] = Σ_{k=n}^{2n‑1} (k+1‑t)^ (2n)` for each `t` once, then combine with binomial coefficients.  
   * This reduces the per‑test‑case complexity to O(n) after O(n) pre‑computations.

3. **Take care of modular arithmetic** – every subtraction must be normalized (`(x % M + M) % M`) and exponentiation uses fast modular power (`pow(base, exp, M)`).

### General Steps (Algorithmic Blueprint)

1. **Read all test cases**, store the maximum n.  
2. **Pre‑compute**:
   * `fact[i]` and `inv_fact[i]` for `i = 0 … 2·max_n + 1`.
   * A helper `C(N, r) = fact[N] * inv_fact[r] % M * inv_fact[N‑r] % M`.
3. For each test case with value n:
   * Let `N = 2*n`.
   * Initialise `ans = 0`.
   * For `k` from `n` to `N‑1`:
     * Compute `A(N, k)` using the inclusion‑exclusion formula (or the recurrence if you prefer).
     * Add it to `ans` modulo M.
   * Output `ans`.

### Why This Algorithm Works
* **Correctness** follows from the definition of Eulerian numbers – they partition the set of all permutations of size N by the exact number of ascents. Summing the appropriate part of the partition yields exactly the permutations that satisfy the “at least n ascents” condition.
* **Efficiency** comes from using factorials for binomial coefficients and modular exponentiation, both O(log M) per operation, and from limiting the inner inclusion‑exclusion loop to at most N terms. With pre‑computation the overall complexity per test case is roughly O(N) which meets the constraints (`Σ n ≤ 10⁵`).
* **Modular arithmetic** guarantees that intermediate values never overflow 64‑bit integers and that the final answer respects the required modulus.

### When to Use This Approach
* Problems that ask for the number of permutations with a constraint on the **number of ascents, descents, or “runs”**.  
* Situations where the constraint is “at least/at most K” – sum the appropriate Eulerian numbers.  
* Any combinatorial counting where the objects can be classified by a statistic that follows the Eulerian recurrence.

### Key Take‑aways
* **Eulerian numbers** are the tool for counting permutations by ascents/descents.  
* They have both a **recurrence** (useful for DP) and a **closed‑form** (useful for fast computation with binomials).  
* Pre‑computing factorials and modular inverses is a standard technique for handling binomial coefficients under a prime modulus.  
* Summation over a range of Eulerian numbers can be optimized by re‑using the inclusion‑exclusion terms, turning an O(n²) naïve solution into O(n) for the given constraints.

By following the blueprint above, the original problem can be solved efficiently and correctly.

    <description>
      **Problem 1 – Subset Sum Minimum Difference**  
      You are given an array `arr` of `n` positive integers. Split the array into two groups so that every element belongs to exactly one group.  
      Minimise the absolute difference between the sums of the two groups.  
      Output the minimum possible difference.  
      Constraints: `1 ≤ n ≤ 30`, `1 ≤ arr[i] ≤ 10^9`.  
      This is the classic “partition problem” and can be solved with a meet‑in‑the‑middle approach because `n` is small.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys
from itertools import combinations

def min_difference(arr):
    n = len(arr)
    total = sum(arr)
    # split the array into two halves
    first = arr[:n//2]
    second = arr[n//2:]

    # all possible subset sums of the first half
    sums_first = []
    for r in range(len(first)+1):
        for comb in combinations(first, r):
            sums_first.append(sum(comb))

    # all possible subset sums of the second half
    sums_second = []
    for r in range(len(second)+1):
        for comb in combinations(second, r):
            sums_second.append(sum(comb))

    # sort the second list for binary search
    sums_second.sort()

    best = total
    for s1 in sums_first:
        # we want s1 + s2 as close as possible to total/2
        target = total//2 - s1
        # binary search in sums_second
        lo, hi = 0, len(sums_second)-1
        while lo <= hi:
            mid = (lo+hi)//2
            if sums_second[mid] < target:
                lo = mid+1
            else:
                hi = mid-1
        # check the candidate at lo
        for idx in (hi, lo):
            if 0 <= idx < len(sums_second):
                s2 = sums_second[idx]
                cur = abs(total - 2*(s1+s2))
                if cur < best:
                    best = cur
    return best

if __name__ == "__main__":
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    pos = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[pos]); pos+=1
        arr = list(map(int, data[pos:pos+n])); pos+=n
        out.append(str(min_difference(arr)))
    print("\n".join(out))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the number of test cases.  
      2. For each test case read `n` and the array.  
      3. Split the array into two halves (size ≤ 15 each).  
      4. Enumerate all subset sums of each half (2^(n/2) possibilities).  
      5. Sort the list of sums of the second half.  
      6. For every sum `s1` from the first half, binary‑search the second list to find a sum `s2` that makes `s1+s2` as close as possible to `total/2`.  
      7. Keep the smallest absolute difference `|total‑2*(s1+s2)|`.  
      8. Output the answer for each test case.  
      Complexity: O(2^{n/2}·log 2^{n/2}) ≈ O(2^{n/2}) time, O(2^{n/2}) memory – fast enough for `n ≤ 30`.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      **Problem 2 – Balancing a Scale with Limited Weights**  
      You have an unlimited number of 1‑kg, 2‑kg and 3‑kg weights, but only `a` pieces of 1‑kg, `b` pieces of 2‑kg and `c` pieces of 3‑kg are available.  
      Place some of the weights on the left pan and the rest on the right pan so that the difference between the total weight on the two pans is minimal.  
      Print that minimal possible difference.  
      Constraints: `0 ≤ a, b, c ≤ 10^9`.  
      Because the weight values are tiny, the answer depends only on the total weight modulo 2 and on a few small adjustments.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def minimal_diff(a, b, c):
    total = a*1 + b*2 + c*3
    # If total is even we can always reach difference 0 or 1
    # The only obstacle is when we have no 1‑kg weights to fix oddness.
    if total % 2 == 0:
        return 0
    # total is odd → we need at least one 1‑kg weight to make the two sides differ by 1
    if a > 0:
        return 1
    # No 1‑kg weights. We must remove a 2‑kg or a 3‑kg from one side.
    # The smallest odd weight we can create is by using a single 3‑kg (difference 3) or two 2‑kg (difference 2).
    # The best we can do is 1 if we can replace a 3‑kg by a 2‑kg (difference 1), otherwise 3.
    # With only 2‑kg and 3‑kg, the minimal odd difference is 1 if we have at least one 3‑kg and one 2‑kg.
    if b > 0 and c > 0:
        return 1
    # Otherwise we are forced to have difference 3 (using a single 3‑kg)
    return 3

if __name__ == "__main__":
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    pos = 1
    ans = []
    for _ in range(t):
        a = int(data[pos]); b = int(data[pos+1]); c = int(data[pos+2])
        pos += 3
        ans.append(str(minimal_diff(a, b, c)))
    print("\n".join(ans))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Compute the total weight `S = a + 2b + 3c`.  
      2. If `S` is even, the two pans can be made equal → answer `0`.  
      3. If `S` is odd and at least one 1‑kg weight exists, put one 1‑kg on the lighter side → answer `1`.  
      4. If there is no 1‑kg weight, we must rely on 2‑kg and 3‑kg pieces.  
         * If both a 2‑kg and a 3‑kg are present we can achieve difference `1` (e.g., move a 3‑kg to one side and a 2‑kg to the other).  
         * Otherwise the smallest odd difference we can obtain is `3` (using a single 3‑kg).  
      5. Output the computed minimal difference for each test case.  
      The algorithm runs in O(1) per test case and uses O(1) memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      **Problem 3 – Forming Two Balanced Teams**  
      `n` players are given, each with an integer skill level `s_i`.  
      Split the players into two teams so that every player belongs to exactly one team and the absolute difference between the total skill of the two teams is as small as possible.  
      Print the minimal possible difference.  
      Constraints: `1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5`, `1 ≤ s_i ≤ 10^4`.  
      Because the skill values are relatively small, a greedy approach based on sorting works well when the total sum is not huge, otherwise a DP on the sum up to `10^5` is feasible.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3 (greedy by sorting)
import sys

def min_diff_greedy(skills):
    # Sort descending and always put the current player on the lighter team
    team1 = 0
    team2 = 0
    for x in sorted(skills, reverse=True):
        if team1 <= team2:
            team1 += x
        else:
            team2 += x
    return abs(team1 - team2)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        skills = list(map(int, data[idx:idx+n])); idx += n
        out.append(str(min_diff_greedy(skills)))
    print("\n".join(out))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the number of test cases.  
      2. For each case read `n` and the list of skill levels.  
      3. Sort the list in non‑increasing order.  
      4. Initialise two accumulators `team1` and `team2` to `0`.  
      5. Iterate over the sorted skills: add the current skill to the team whose current total is smaller (or equal).  
      6. After all players are assigned, compute `abs(team1‑team2)` – this is the minimal difference achieved by the greedy heuristic.  
      7. Output the result for each test case.  
      The greedy algorithm runs in `O(n log n)` time (sorting) and `O(1)` extra memory, which easily satisfies the limits.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Tutorial – Greedy & Parity Reasoning for Minimum Difference Partition Problems  

    Many partition‑type problems ask for the smallest possible absolute difference between two groups that together contain all given items.  
    When the item values are **very small** (e.g., only `1`, `2`, `3`) and the counts of each value can be huge, a full‑blown dynamic programming over the total sum is impossible.  
    In such cases the solution hinges on two observations:

    1. **Total Sum Parity**  
       Let `S` be the sum of all item values.  
       - If `S` is even, the ideal target for each side is `S/2`.  
       - If `S` is odd, the best we can ever achieve is a difference of `1` (because the two integer sums must differ by at least one).  

       Therefore the answer is always `0`, `1`, or some small odd number that cannot be avoided because of missing item types.

    2. **Ability to Adjust by 1**  
       To turn an odd total into a difference of `1`, we need at least one item of **odd weight** that can be moved independently.  
       With the set `{1,2,3}` the odd items are `1` and `3`.  
       - A single `1` can always create a difference of `1`.  
       - If we have no `1`s, we may still obtain `1` by using a combination of a `3` and a `2` (replace a `3` on one side with a `2` on the other, the net change is `1`).  

       If neither of these possibilities exists, the smallest odd difference we can force is the smallest odd weight we actually have, which is `3`.

    **Putting the observations together** gives a constant‑time algorithm:

    ```
    S = a*1 + b*2 + c*3
    if S is even:          answer = 0
    else:                  # S is odd
        if a > 0:          answer = 1          # we have a 1‑kg weight
        elif b > 0 and c > 0:
                            answer = 1          # we can trade a 3 for a 2
        else:              answer = 3          # only 2‑kg or only 3‑kg pieces
    ```

    This reasoning is a **greedy/parity** technique: we do not try every distribution; we only check whether the necessary small adjustments are available.  
    The method works for any problem where:

    * The item values are bounded by a tiny constant.  
    * The counts of each value can be huge (up to 10^9).  
    * The objective is to minimise the absolute difference of two sums.

    **General steps to apply this technique**

    1. Compute the total sum `S`.  
    2. Check the parity of `S`.  
    3. If `S` is even → answer `0`.  
    4. If `S` is odd → examine the availability of the smallest odd-valued item (or a combination that yields an odd change of `1`).  
    5. Return the smallest achievable odd difference (`1` if possible, otherwise the smallest odd weight present).

    This approach runs in **O(1)** time per test case and uses **O(1)** memory, making it ideal for the given constraints.  

    The same line of thought can be extended to other small‑value sets (e.g., `{1,4,5}`) by enumerating all ways to obtain a net change of `1` using the available item types.
  </algorithm>
</root>
**Planning**

1. **Read input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - Loop `t` times, each iteration read three integers `a, b, c`.

2. **Compute total duration**
   - `total = a*1 + b*2 + c*3`.

3. **Check parity of total**
   - If `total` is even → minimal possible difference is `0`. Output `0` and continue to next test case.

4. **Total is odd → try to achieve difference `1`**
   - If there is at least one 1‑minute song (`a > 0`) → we can move a single 1 to the other side, achieving difference `1`. Output `1`.
   - Else if there is at least one 2‑minute song **and** at least one 3‑minute song (`b > 0 && c > 0`) → replace a 3 on one side with a 2 on the other side, net change `1`. Output `1`.
   - Otherwise (only 2‑minute songs or only 3‑minute songs) the smallest odd difference we can obtain is `3`. Output `3`.

5. **Complexity**
   - Time: `O(t)` (constant work per test case).
   - Memory: `O(1)`.

6. **Output**
   - Print the answer for each test case on its own line.

Let S = a·1 + b·2 + c·3 be the total duration.  
* If S is even, we can always partition the songs so that both concerts have total duration S/2, giving a minimal difference of 0.  
* If S is odd, the best we can do is to make the two sums differ by 1 minute. Since a > 0, we can always move a single 1‑minute song to the side with the smaller sum, achieving a difference of 1.

The plan implements exactly this logic: it outputs 0 when the total is even, and 1 when the total is odd (the extra checks for the presence of songs are unnecessary because the constraints already guarantee them). Therefore the algorithm is correct, runs in O(t) time and O(1) memory, and will produce the required minimal difference for every test case.</explanation>

**Planning to solve “Balancing a Scale with Limited Weights” (or the equivalent song‑duration partition problem)**  

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   - Loop `t` times, each iteration read three 64‑bit integers `a, b, c`.

2. **Compute total duration (or total weight)**  
   - `S = a * 1LL + b * 2LL + c * 3LL`  
   - Use 64‑bit type (`long long` / `int64`) because each term can be up to `3·10^9` and the sum up to `6·10^9`, which fits in 64‑bit.

3. **Determine minimal possible difference**  
   - If `S` is even (`S % 2 == 0`) → the two concerts (or pans) can be made exactly equal, so answer = `0`.  
   - Otherwise (`S` odd) → the best we can do is a difference of `1`.  
     *Proof sketch*: an odd total forces the two integer sums to differ by at least one.  
     - If at least one 1‑kg (1‑minute) item exists we can place a single such item on the lighter side to achieve difference 1.  
     - If there are no 1‑kg items (possible only when the original constraints allow zeros) we can still obtain difference 1 when both a 2‑kg and a 3‑kg item are present (swap a 3 for a 2).  
     - If neither of the above holds, the smallest odd difference achievable equals the smallest odd weight present (which is `3`).  
   - Since the given constraints guarantee `a, b, c ≥ 1` (or at least one odd‑weight item exists), the answer for odd `S` is always `1`.

4. **Output**  
   - For each test case print the computed answer on its own line.

5. **Complexity**  
   - Time: `O(t)` – constant work per test case.  
   - Memory: `O(1)` – only a few variables.

**Pseudo‑code**

```
read t
repeat t times:
    read a, b, c
    S = a + 2*b + 3*c          // use 64‑bit
    if S % 2 == 0:
        ans = 0
    else:
        ans = 1                // always possible under given constraints
    print ans
```

     * **Case 2:** No 1‑minute songs, but we have both a 3‑minute and a 2‑minute song (`c > 0 && b > 0`).  
       - Replace a 3‑minute song on one side with a 2‑minute song on the other side (or vice‑versa). The net change between sides is `3‑2 = 1`. → answer `1`.
     * **Case 3:** Neither of the above holds.  
       - The only odd‑valued songs present are 3‑minute songs (and possibly only 2‑minute songs).  
       - The smallest odd difference we can obtain is the weight of a single 3‑minute song, i.e. `3`. → answer `3`.

   - Memory: `O(1)` – only a few integer variables.

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        a = int(next(it))
        b = int(next(it))
        c = int(next(it))
        total = a + 2 * b + 3 * c
        if total % 2 == 0:
            out_lines.append("0")
        else:
            if a > 0:
                out_lines.append("1")
            elif b > 0 and c > 0:
                out_lines.append("1")
            else:
                out_lines.append("3")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

      <title>Maximum Size Subset with Even Sum</title>
      <p>Given an array of <code>n</code> distinct positive integers, find a subset with the maximum possible number of elements such that the sum of its elements is even. If several subsets have the same maximum size, any of them can be output.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ n ≤ 1000</code>, <code>1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^5</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def is_even(x):
          return x % 2 == 0
      
      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out_lines = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
              arr = list(map(int, data[idx:idx+n])); idx += n
              
              total = sum(arr)
              # If total is already even, whole array works
              if is_even(total):
                  out_lines.append(str(n))
                  out_lines.append(' '.join(str(i+1) for i in range(n)))
                  continue
              
              # Total is odd → we need to drop one odd element
              # Find first odd element index
              odd_idx = next(i for i, v in enumerate(arr) if v % 2 == 1)
              
              # Subset size becomes n‑1
              out_lines.append(str(n-1))
              subset = [str(i+1) for i in range(n) if i != odd_idx]
              out_lines.append(' '.join(subset))
          
          sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>

      4. If the sum is odd, we must remove exactly one odd element (removing an even element would keep the sum odd).  
         Because the numbers are distinct, at least one odd element exists when the sum is odd.
      5. Output the size <code>n‑1</code> and the indices of all elements except the chosen odd one.
