  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Largest Subset with Sum Divisible by k</title>
      <p>Given an array of <code>n</code> distinct positive integers and an integer <code>k</code> ( <code>2 ≤ k ≤ 100</code> ), find a subset with the maximum possible number of elements whose sum is divisible by <code>k</code>. Any maximal subset may be printed.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ n ≤ 200</code>, <code>1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^4</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Greedy + remainder counting approach
      import sys
      
      def solve():
          it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
          t = int(next(it))
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(next(it)); k = int(next(it))
              a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
              
              # Group indices by remainder modulo k
              buckets = [[] for _ in range(k)]
              for idx, val in enumerate(a):
                  buckets[val % k].append(idx+1)   # store 1‑based index
              
              # Start with all elements
              chosen = set(range(1, n+1))
              total_mod = sum(a) % k
              
              # If total_mod == 0 we are done
              if total_mod != 0:
                  # Try to remove the smallest possible number of elements
                  # 1) Remove one element whose remainder == total_mod
                  if buckets[total_mod]:
                      removed = buckets[total_mod][0]
                      chosen.remove(removed)
                  else:
                      # 2) Remove two elements whose remainders add to total_mod
                      found = False
                      for r in range(1, k):
                          r2 = (k + total_mod - r) % k
                          if r2 == r:
                              if len(buckets[r]) >= 2:
                                  removed = buckets[r][:2]
                                  chosen.difference_update(removed)
                                  found = True
                                  break
                          else:
                              if buckets[r] and buckets[r2]:
                                  removed = [buckets[r][0], buckets[r2][0]]
                                  chosen.difference_update(removed)
                                  found = True
                                  break
                      # Problem guarantees a solution, so one of the above will succeed
              
              out.append(str(len(chosen)))
              out.append(' '.join(map(str, sorted(chosen))))
          
          sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read <code>n</code>, <code>k</code> and the array.
      2. Compute the sum modulo <code>k</code>.
      3. If the modulo is zero, the whole array is a valid maximal subset.
      4. Otherwise try to delete the smallest number of elements to make the remainder zero:
         * First try to delete a single element whose remainder equals the current total remainder.
         * If not possible, delete two elements whose remainders add up to the current remainder (mod <code>k</code>).
      5. Output the remaining indices; their count is maximal because we removed the minimum possible number of elements.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Make Array Sum Composite by Removing Minimum Elements</title>
      <p>Given an array of distinct positive integers, remove the smallest possible number of elements so that the sum of the remaining elements is a composite number. Output the indices of the elements that stay in the array.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>3 ≤ n ≤ 100</code>, <code>1 ≤ a_i ≤ 200</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Simple greedy solution
      import sys
      import math
      
      def is_prime(x):
          if x < 2:
              return False
          if x % 2 == 0:
              return x == 2
          r = int(math.isqrt(x))
          for d in range(3, r+1, 2):
              if x % d == 0:
                  return False
          return True
      
      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          pos = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(data[pos]); pos += 1
              a = list(map(int, data[pos:pos+n])); pos += n
              
              total = sum(a)
              
              # If total already composite, keep everything
              if not is_prime(total):
                  out.append(str(n))
                  out.append(' '.join(str(i+1) for i in range(n)))
                  continue
              
              # Try removing one element
              removed = None
              for i, val in enumerate(a):
                  if not is_prime(total - val):
                      removed = i
                      break
              
              if removed is not None:
                  out.append(str(n-1))
                  out.append(' '.join(str(j+1) for j in range(n) if j != removed))
                  continue
              
              # If still not possible (theoretically never happens for given constraints),
              # remove two elements (brute‑force because n ≤ 100)
              found = False
              for i in range(n):
                  for j in range(i+1, n):
                      if not is_prime(total - a[i] - a[j]):
                          out.append(str(n-2))
                          out.append(' '.join(str(k+1) for k in range(n) if k not in (i, j)))
                          found = True
                          break
                  if found:
                      break
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Compute the total sum of the array.
      2. Check whether the total is composite (i.e., not prime and >1).  
         * If it is composite, the answer is the whole array.
      3. If the total is prime, try to delete a single element:
         * For each element, test whether <code>total - element</code> becomes composite.
         * The first such element gives a subset of size <code>n‑1</code>.
      4. If no single removal works (this situation cannot occur for the given limits, but we handle it for completeness), try removing two elements by brute force (O(n²)).
      5. Output the indices that remain.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original problem
    The task is to obtain the **largest possible subset** of a set of distinct positive integers whose sum is a **composite** number.
    
    ## Observation
    * The sum of all `n` numbers is either **composite** or **prime**.
    * If it is composite, the whole array (size `n`) already satisfies the requirement – this is obviously optimal.
    * If the total sum `S` is **prime**, we must discard at least one element, because removing nothing leaves a prime sum.
    
    ## Key property of parity
    * Any even number greater than `2` is composite (it has divisor `2`).
    * `S` is prime ⇒ `S` is **odd** (the only even prime is `2`, but `S ≥ 3` because `n ≥ 3` and all numbers are positive).
    * Removing an **odd** element from an odd sum yields an **even** sum.
    * The resulting even sum will be at least `4` (since the smallest possible sum after removal is `1+2 = 3` → not possible because numbers are distinct and ≥1, but with `n ≥ 3` the remaining sum is ≥ 4). Hence it is composite.
    
    Therefore, when `S` is prime we only need to delete **any one odd element**. The subset size becomes `n‑1`, which is the maximum possible because we cannot keep all `n` elements (their sum is prime).

    ## Greedy solution
    1. Compute `S = sum(a_i)`.
    2. If `S` is composite → output all indices.
    3. Else (`S` is prime) → find an index `p` such that `a_p` is odd.
       * Such an index always exists because an odd sum must contain at least one odd addend.
    4. Output all indices except `p`. The size is `n‑1`.

    This algorithm runs in `O(n)` per test case (sum computation + a linear scan for an odd element) and uses `O(1)` extra memory.

    ## Generic tutorial for this type of problems
    Problems that ask for the **largest subset with a property on the sum** often admit a simple greedy approach:
    
    1. **Check the whole set** – if the whole set already satisfies the property, it is optimal.
    2. **Identify a minimal modification** that flips the property:
       * For parity‑based properties, removing an element with a specific parity changes the sum’s parity.
       * For divisibility, removing an element whose remainder matches the current sum’s remainder can fix the condition.
    3. **Prove minimality** – show that any solution must discard at least as many elements as your modification, guaranteeing optimality.
    4. **Implement** the modification by scanning the array once to locate a suitable element.
    
    The crucial steps are:
    * **Mathematical insight** about the property (e.g., all even numbers >2 are composite, sum modulo `k`, etc.).
    * **Existence guarantee** – prove that the needed element always exists under the given constraints.
    * **Complexity awareness** – the greedy choice should be verifiable in linear or near‑linear time.
    
    This pattern appears in many Codeforces‑style tasks (e.g., “make sum odd”, “make sum divisible by 3”, “make sum prime/composite”). Recognizing the underlying number‑theoretic fact enables a concise and optimal solution without exhaustive search or DP.
  </algorithm>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Maximum Subarray Sum (Kadane's Algorithm)</title>
      You are given an array of integers (both positive and negative). Find the contiguous subarray with the maximum possible sum and output that sum. If all numbers are negative, the maximum sum is the largest (least negative) number.
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def max_subarray_sum(arr):
          # Initialise current and best sums with the first element
          current = best = arr[0]
          # Iterate from the second element to the end
          for x in arr[1:]:
              # Either extend the previous subarray or start a new one at x
              current = max(x, current + x)
              # Update the best sum seen so far
              best = max(best, current)
          return best
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          n = int(data[0])
          a = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))
          print(max_subarray_sum(a))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the integer n and the n‑element array.
      2. Initialise two variables:
         * `current` – the maximum sum of a subarray that ends at the current position.
         * `best` – the overall maximum sum found so far.
      3. Scan the array from left to right.
         * For each element `x`, update `current = max(x, current + x)`.
         * Update `best = max(best, current)`.
      4. After the loop, `best` holds the answer; output it.
      5. Edge case: when n = 1 the algorithm works automatically.

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Longest Increasing Subsequence (LIS) – O(n log n) method</title>
      Given a sequence of integers, determine the length of the longest strictly increasing subsequence.
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3 using binary search
      import sys
      import bisect
      
      def lis_length(seq):
          # `tails[i]` will hold the smallest possible tail value of an increasing
          # subsequence of length i+1.
          tails = []
          for x in seq:
              # Find the position where x can extend or replace a tail
              idx = bisect.bisect_left(tails, x)
              if idx == len(tails):
                  tails.append(x)          # extend the longest subsequence
              else:
                  tails[idx] = x           # improve a smaller length subsequence
          return len(tails)
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          n = int(data[0])
          a = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))
          print(lis_length(a))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read n and the sequence of n integers.
      2. Create an empty list `tails`.
      3. Iterate over each element `x` in the sequence:
         * Use `bisect_left` to locate the first index in `tails` that is ≥ `x`.
         * If the index equals the length of `tails`, append `x` (a new longer subsequence is formed).
         * Otherwise replace `tails[idx]` with `x` to keep the smallest possible tail for that length.
      4. The size of `tails` after processing all elements equals the length of the LIS.
      5. Output that length.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Counting Islands in a 2D Grid</title>
      You are given an `m × n` grid consisting of ‘1’s (land) and ‘0’s (water). An island is a group of adjacent lands connected horizontally or vertically. Compute the number of distinct islands.
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3 using DFS
      import sys
      sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
      
      def count_islands(grid):
          if not grid:
              return 0
          rows, cols = len(grid), len(grid[0])
          visited = [[False]*cols for _ in range(rows)]
      
          def dfs(r, c):
              # Mark current cell as visited
              visited[r][c] = True
              # Explore four directions
              for dr, dc in [(1,0), (-1,0), (0,1), (0,-1)]:
                  nr, nc = r+dr, c+dc
                  if (0 <= nr < rows and 0 <= nc < cols and
                      not visited[nr][nc] and grid[nr][nc] == '1'):
                      dfs(nr, nc)
      
          islands = 0
          for i in range(rows):
              for j in range(cols):
                  if grid[i][j] == '1' and not visited[i][j]:
                      islands += 1
                      dfs(i, j)
          return islands
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          m = int(data[0])
          n = int(data[1])
          # Next m strings each of length n
          grid = [list(data[2+i]) for i in range(m)]
          print(count_islands(grid))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Parse the dimensions `m` and `n` and read the grid (each row as a string of ‘0’/‘1’).
      2. Initialise a `visited` matrix of the same size, all `False`.
      3. Define a depth‑first search `dfs(r, c)` that:
         * Marks `(r, c)` as visited.
         * Recursively visits all four orthogonal neighbours that are inside the grid, unvisited, and contain ‘1’.
      4. Scan every cell of the grid:
         * When an unvisited land cell (`'1'`) is found, increment the island counter and launch `dfs` from that cell to mark the whole island.
      5. After the full scan, the counter holds the number of islands; output it.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original “Flower Growth” problem
    The problem can be solved with a **greedy linear scan**.  
    At each day we only need to know:
    * Whether the flower was watered today (`a_i = 1`).
    * Whether it was watered the previous day (`a_{i‑1}`) to decide between a growth of **1 cm** or **5 cm**.
    * Whether there have been two consecutive days without water – in that case the flower dies immediately.

    Because the rules depend solely on the current day and the immediate predecessor, a single pass from day 1 to day n is sufficient.  
    This is a classic greedy situation: we make the locally optimal decision (apply the growth rule) and never need to look ahead, as future decisions are independent of past ones except for the “two‑day drought” condition, which can be detected on the spot.

    ## General Greedy Strategy
    1. **Maintain a small amount of state** – here the height of the flower and a flag indicating if yesterday was watered.
    2. **Iterate through the input in order**.
    3. **Update the state** according to the problem’s local rule:
       * If today is watered:
         * Add `5` if yesterday was also watered, otherwise add `1`.
         * Reset the “drought counter”.
       * If today is not watered:
         * Increase the drought counter.
         * If the counter reaches `2`, the process terminates with failure (`-1`).
    4. **Terminate** after processing all days (or earlier if the flower dies) and output the final state.

    ## Why Greedy Works Here
    * **Optimal substructure** – the height after day i depends only on the height after day i‑1 and the watering status of day i and i‑1. There is no hidden future benefit to postponing growth.
    * **No choice** – the problem does not present alternative actions; the rule uniquely determines the outcome for each day. Hence “greedy” simply means “follow the rule directly”.

    ## Typical Steps to Implement a Greedy Linear Scan
    1. Read the number of test cases.
    2. For each test case:
       * Initialise `height = 1` (starting height) and `prev_watered = False`.
       * Initialise a counter `dry_streak = 0`.
       * Loop over the `n` days:
         * If `a_i == 1`:
           * `height += 5` if `prev_watered` else `height += 1`.
           * `prev_watered = True`; `dry_streak = 0`.
         * Else (`a_i == 0`):
           * `dry_streak += 1`.
           * If `dry_streak == 2`: output `-1` for this test case and break.
           * `prev_watered = False`.
       * If the loop finishes without death, output the final `height`.
    3. Ensure each test case’s answer is printed on its own line.

    This greedy linear‑time algorithm runs in **O(n)** per test case and uses **O(1)** additional memory, which easily satisfies the given constraints (`n ≤ 100`, `t ≤ 100`).
  </algorithm>
</root>
**Planning**

1. **Read input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - Loop `t` times:
     - Read integer `n`.
     - Read list `a` of `n` integers (0 or 1).

2. **Initialize per‑test variables**
   - `height = 1` (initial height at day 1).
   - `prev_watered = False` – whether day i‑1 was watered.
   - `dry_streak = 0` – consecutive days without water.

3. **Process days**
   - For each `x` in `a` (iterate from day 1 to day n):
     - If `x == 1` (watered):
       - If `prev_watered` is `True` → `height += 5`.
       - Else → `height += 1`.
       - Set `prev_watered = True`.
       - Reset `dry_streak = 0`.
     - Else (`x == 0`):
       - Increment `dry_streak += 1`.
       - If `dry_streak == 2` → flower dies:
         - Output `-1` for this test case.
         - Skip remaining days of this test case (break loop).
       - Set `prev_watered = False`.

4. **Finalize answer**
   - If loop finished without death, output current `height`.

5. **Complexity**
   - Time: O(n) per test case, total O(∑n) ≤ 10⁴.
   - Memory: O(1) extra.

* If the current day is not watered, it increments the dry‑streak; when the streak reaches 2 the flower dies, the algorithm immediately outputs -1 for that test case and stops processing further days.

All edge cases are handled: the initial day, the first possible death after two leading zeros, and the situation where the flower never dies. The time complexity is O(n) per test case (≤ 10⁴ total operations) and the memory usage is O(1). Therefore the plan correctly solves the problem.

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        height = 1
        prev_watered = False
        dry_streak = 0
        dead = False
        for _ in range(n):
            a = int(next(it))
            if a == 1:
                if prev_watered:
                    height += 5
                else:
                    height += 1
                prev_watered = True
                dry_streak = 0
            else:
                dry_streak += 1
                if dry_streak == 2:
                    dead = True
                    # consume remaining inputs for this test case if any
                    # but we already are at correct position because loop will continue reading
                    # we break out of inner loop and skip remaining days
                    # need to skip remaining days' inputs
                    # calculate how many days left
                    remaining = n - (_ + 1)
                    for __ in range(remaining):
                        next(it)
                    break
                prev_watered = False
        out_lines.append("-1" if dead else str(height))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

      <title>Find a Subarray with Given Sum (All Positive Numbers)</title>
      Given an array <code>A</code> of <code>m</code> positive integers and a target sum <code>S</code>, find the indices <code>(l, r)</code> (1‑based) of a contiguous sub‑array whose elements sum to <code>S</code>. It is guaranteed that such a sub‑array exists.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def solve():
    import sys
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        m = int(next(it))
        S = int(next(it))
        arr = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(m)]

        # Sliding window because all numbers are positive
        left = 0
        cur_sum = 0
        for right in range(m):
            cur_sum += arr[right]
            while cur_sum > S and left <= right:
                cur_sum -= arr[left]
                left += 1
            if cur_sum == S:
                # convert to 1‑based indices
                out.append(f"{left+1} {right+1}")
                break
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the number of test cases.  
      2. For each case read <code>m</code> (size of array) and <code>S</code>.  
      3. Read the array of <code>m</code> positive integers.  
      4. Use the two‑pointer (sliding window) technique: maintain a window <code>[left, right]</code> and its sum.  
      5. Expand <code>right</code> adding <code>A[right]</code> to the current sum.  
      6. While the sum exceeds <code>S</code>, shrink the window from the left.  
      7. When the sum equals <code>S</code>, output the 1‑based indices and stop processing this test case.  
      8. Continue with the next test case.
    </planning>
  </problem>

    <description>
      <title>Represent an Integer as a Sum of Consecutive Positive Integers</title>
      For a given integer <code>n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12)</code>, determine whether it can be expressed as a sum of one or more consecutive positive integers. If possible, output any such sequence; otherwise output <code>-1</code>.  
      Example: <code>15 = 1+2+3+4+5 = 4+5+6 = 7+8</code>.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def solve():
    import sys, math
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    out = []
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        found = False
        # n = k * a + k*(k-1)/2  =>  a = (n - k*(k-1)/2) / k  must be positive integer
        # iterate over possible length k
        max_k = int((2*math.isqrt(2*n)) + 1)  # safe upper bound
        for k in range(1, max_k+1):
            numerator = n - k*(k-1)//2
            if numerator <= 0:
                break
            if numerator % k == 0:
                a = numerator // k
                if a > 0:
                    seq = [str(a + i) for i in range(k)]
                    out.append(" ".join(seq))
                    found = True
                    break
        if not found:
            out.append("-1")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read all test cases.  
      2. For each <code>n</code> try to find a length <code>k</code> of the consecutive block.  
      3. The sum of <code>k</code> consecutive numbers starting from <code>a</code> is  
         <code>S = k·a + k·(k‑1)/2</code>.  
      4. Rearrange to obtain <code>a = (n - k·(k‑1)/2) / k</code>.  
      5. Iterate <code>k</code> from 1 upwards while the numerator stays positive.  
      6. If the numerator is divisible by <code>k</code> and <code>a > 0</code>, we have a valid sequence.  
      7. Output the sequence and stop the search for this test case.  
      8. If no <code>k</code> works, output <code>-1</code>.  
      9. Complexity is roughly <code>O(√n)</code> per test case, which is fast enough for the given limits.
    </planning>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original problem (finding l and r such that the sum from l to r equals n)

    The task can be solved with a **constructive / mathematical** approach that works in **O(1)** time per test case.

    ## Core Idea
    The sum of an arithmetic progression from <code>l</code> to <code>r</code> (inclusive) is  

    \[
    S = \frac{(r-l+1)\,(l+r)}{2}
    \]

    We need <code>S = n</code>.  
    Instead of searching for arbitrary <code>l</code> and <code>r</code>, we can **force** the formula to hold by choosing a convenient pair.

    Consider the interval  

    \[
    l = -n + 1,\qquad r = n
    \]

    Its length is  

    \[
    r - l + 1 = n - (-n+1) + 1 = 2n
    \]

    The average of the first and last term is  

    \[
    \frac{l + r}{2} = \frac{-n+1 + n}{2} = \frac{1}{2}
    \]

    Hence the sum becomes  

    \[
    S = (2n) \times \frac{1}{2} = n
    \]

    So the pair <code>(-n+1, n)</code> always satisfies the requirement, and it respects the bounds  
    <code>-10^{18} ≤ -n+1 < n ≤ 10^{18}</code> because <code>1 ≤ n ≤ 10^{18}</code>.

    ## Steps
    1. Read the number of test cases <code>t</code>.  
    2. For each test case read <code>n</code>.  
    3. Output the pair <code>-n+1  n</code>.  

    This construction is deterministic, constant‑time, and works for every allowed <code>n</code>.

    ## Why This Works (Mathematical Insight)
    * The sum of symmetric numbers around zero cancels out:  
      \[
      (-k) + (k) = 0
      \]
    * By taking all numbers from <code>-n+1</code> up to <code>-1</code>, they cancel with <code>1</code> up to <code>n-1</code>.  
    * The only remaining terms are <code>0</code> and <code>n</code>, giving a total of <code>n</code>.  
    * This symmetry guarantees the sum without any trial‑and‑error.

    ## Complexity Analysis
    * **Time:** O(t) – a constant amount of work per test case.  
    * **Memory:** O(1) – only a few integer variables are needed.

    ## General Tutorial for Constructive Math Problems
    1. **Understand the formula** governing the problem (e.g., sum of arithmetic progression, product, etc.).  
    2. **Look for symmetries** or invariants that simplify the expression.  
    3. **Try to fix one variable** (often the length or one endpoint) and solve for the other analytically.  
    4. **Check boundary conditions** to ensure the constructed values stay inside the allowed range.  
    5. **Prove correctness** by substituting the construction back into the original equation.  
    6. **Implement** the direct formula; no loops or heavy computation are required.

    This style of reasoning is common in competitive programming when a problem guarantees the existence of a solution – the key is to discover a simple, universal construction.
  </algorithm>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 1 – “Two‑Row Grid Escape”**  
You are given a grid with 2 rows and *n* columns. Each cell is either free (`0`) or blocked (`1`).  
You start at cell (1, 1) and want to reach cell (2, n). In one move you may go to any orthogonal neighbour (up, down, left, right) **but you are not allowed to move diagonally**.  
Determine whether a path that avoids blocked cells exists.  
Constraints: 1 ≤ *t* ≤ 100 test cases, 2 ≤ *n* ≤ 10⁵, total *n* over all test cases ≤ 10⁶. Cells (1, 1) and (2, n) are guaranteed to be free.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

import sys
from collections import deque

def can_escape(n, row1, row2):
    # Build adjacency implicitly – we only need to know neighbours of a cell
    # BFS from (0,0) (0‑based indices) to (1,n‑1)
    q = deque()
    q.append((0, 0))
    visited = [[False] * n for _ in range(2)]
    visited[0][0] = True

    while q:
        r, c = q.popleft()
        if r == 1 and c == n - 1:
            return True
        # four orthogonal directions
        for dr, dc in [(0, 1), (0, -1), (1, 0), (-1, 0)]:
            nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
            if 0 <= nr < 2 and 0 <= nc < n:
                if not visited[nr][nc] and (row1 if nr == 0 else row2)[nc] == '0':
                    visited[nr][nc] = True
                    q.append((nr, nc))
    return False

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        r1 = next(it).strip()
        r2 = next(it).strip()
        out.append("YES" if can_escape(n, r1, r2) else "NO")
    print("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Parse input** – read *t*, then for each test case read *n* and the two strings.  
2. **Breadth‑First Search** – treat each free cell as a node, edges exist between orthogonal neighbours.  
3. **Queue** – start from (0, 0). While the queue is not empty, pop a cell, push all unvisited free neighbours.  
4. **Termination** – if we ever dequeue (1, n‑1) we have a path → output “YES”. If BFS finishes without reaching the target → “NO”.  
5. **Complexity** – each cell is visited at most once → O(2·n) time and O(2·n) memory per test case.  
6. **Edge Cases** – the start and end are guaranteed free, but still check bounds when exploring neighbours.
</planning>
</problem>

<description>
**Problem 2 – “Maximum Non‑Overlapping Segments”**  
Given *m* closed intervals on the integer line, each described by its left and right endpoints \([l_i, r_i]\).  
Select the largest possible subset of intervals such that no two chosen intervals intersect (they may touch at endpoints).  
Output the size of this maximum subset.  
Constraints: 1 ≤ *m* ≤ 10⁵, 0 ≤ l_i ≤ r_i ≤ 10⁹.
</description>
<code>
# Greedy algorithm for maximum number of non‑overlapping intervals

import sys

def max_non_overlapping(intervals):
    # Sort by right endpoint (earliest finishing time first)
    intervals.sort(key=lambda x: x[1])
    count = 0
    last_end = -float('inf')
    for l, r in intervals:
        if l >= last_end:          # no overlap (touching is allowed)
            count += 1
            last_end = r
    return count

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        m = int(next(it))
        segs = []
        for _ in range(m):
            l = int(next(it))
            r = int(next(it))
            segs.append((l, r))
        out.append(str(max_non_overlapping(segs)))
    print("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read all test cases** – each case provides *m* intervals.  
2. **Sort intervals** by their right endpoint; this is the classic “earliest‑finish‑time” greedy order.  
3. **Iterate** through the sorted list, keeping `last_end` – the right endpoint of the last chosen interval.  
4. **Select** an interval if its left endpoint is ≥ `last_end`. Increment answer and update `last_end`.  
5. **Return** the final count.  
6. **Complexity** – sorting dominates: O(m log m) time, O(m) memory.
</planning>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – “Longest Increasing Subsequence (LIS) Length”**  
Given an array `a` of *n* integers, find the length of the longest strictly increasing subsequence.  
Constraints: 1 ≤ *n* ≤ 10⁵, |a_i| ≤ 10⁹.  
Multiple test cases are allowed.
</description>
<code>
# Patience‑sorting (binary search) solution for LIS length

import sys
import bisect

def lis_length(arr):
    # `tails[i]` = smallest possible tail of an increasing subsequence of length i+1
    tails = []
    for x in arr:
        pos = bisect.bisect_left(tails, x)   # first element ≥ x
        if pos == len(tails):
            tails.append(x)
        else:
            tails[pos] = x
    return len(tails)

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        arr = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        out.append(str(lis_length(arr)))
    print("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Input handling** – read number of test cases, then for each case read *n* and the array.  
2. **Patience sorting technique** – maintain a list `tails`. For each element `x` find the leftmost position `pos` where `tails[pos] ≥ x` (binary search).  
   * If `pos` equals the current length of `tails`, `x` extends the longest subsequence → append.  
   * Otherwise replace `tails[pos]` with `x` to keep the tails as small as possible.  
3. **Result** – the size of `tails` after processing the whole array equals the LIS length.  
4. **Complexity** – O(n log n) time per test case, O(n) auxiliary space (the `tails` list).  
5. **Edge Cases** – handle empty arrays (answer 0) and duplicate values correctly because we use `bisect_left` for strict increase.
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
Greedy Algorithm – Tutorial  

A **greedy algorithm** builds a solution step by step, always choosing the locally optimal option with the hope that this leads to a globally optimal solution. The key ideas are:

1. **Make a Choice That Looks Best Right Now**  
   - At each decision point, evaluate all feasible actions.  
   - Pick the one that seems most promising according to a simple rule (e.g., earliest finishing time, smallest possible value, shortest distance).

2. **Why Greedy Can Be Correct**  
   - **Optimal‑substructure**: An optimal solution to the whole problem contains optimal solutions to its sub‑problems.  
   - **Greedy‑choice property**: A globally optimal solution can be constructed by making a locally optimal (greedy) choice first.  
   - Proving these properties usually involves a *exchange argument*: show that any optimal solution can be transformed into one that starts with the greedy choice without worsening the result.

3. **Typical Greedy Patterns**  
   - **Activity selection / interval scheduling** – sort by finishing times, pick the earliest finishing activity that does not conflict.  
   - **Fractional knapsack** – sort items by value‑to‑weight ratio, take as much as possible of the best ratio item.  
   - **Huffman coding** – repeatedly merge the two smallest frequency nodes.  
   - **Path building in constrained grids** – move forward, switch rows only when forced, because any unnecessary switch can only make the path longer or block future moves.

4. **Steps to Design a Greedy Solution**  
   a. **Understand the problem structure** – identify what “local” decision influences the rest of the problem.  
   b. **Formulate a simple rule** – e.g., “pick the smallest right endpoint”, “stay in the current row unless blocked”.  
   c. **Prove correctness** – usually by showing that any optimal solution can be rearranged to follow the greedy rule (exchange argument).  
   d. **Implement efficiently** – often requires sorting (O(n log n)) or a single linear scan with constant‑time decisions.  

5. **When Greedy Fails**  
   - If the problem lacks the greedy‑choice property, a locally optimal step may prevent reaching a globally optimal solution.  
   - In such cases, dynamic programming, backtracking, or other exhaustive techniques are needed.

**Applying Greedy to the original “Monocarp” problem**  

- The character moves only forward (column index never decreases) because moving left would never help reach the far right end.  
- At each column we are in either row 1 or row 2.  
- If the cell in the current row is free, we can stay; if it is blocked, we *must* switch rows (provided the other cell is free).  
- Switching rows is only possible when **both** cells of the column are free; otherwise the column is a hard barrier.  
- Therefore the optimal strategy is: scan columns from left to right, keep the current row, switch only when forced, and abort if a column blocks both rows.  
- This linear scan is a classic greedy approach: each decision (stay or switch) is forced by the immediate local configuration, and no future decision can make a previously forced switch unnecessary.

**Complexity** – O(n) time per test case, O(1) extra memory.

By mastering the greedy paradigm and the exchange‑argument proof technique, you can recognise and solve many optimisation problems that appear in contests and real‑world scenarios.  

</algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – “Two‑Row Grid Escape”**

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `t` (number of test cases).  
   - For each test case:  
     * read `n` (number of columns).  
     * read two strings `row1` and `row2` of length `n` (characters ‘0’ = free, ‘1’ = trap).  

2. **Observation**  
   - A move is allowed to any orthogonal or diagonal neighbour, i.e. from `(x, y)` to any cell `(x ± 1, y ± 1)` that stays inside the 2 × n grid.  
   - Because diagonal moves are allowed, the only situation that makes the destination unreachable is a **column where both cells are traps**.  
   - If a column `i` has `row1[i] = '1'` **and** `row2[i] = '1'`, the character cannot occupy any cell of that column, and there is no way to “skip” a column (all moves change the column index by at most 1). Hence any such column blocks the whole path.  
   - If **no column** contains two traps, a path always exists: starting from `(1,1)` we can move right while staying in the current row whenever the cell ahead is free, and whenever the current row cell is blocked we can step diagonally to the other row (the target cell is free because the column is not a double‑trap). Repeating this yields a valid route to `(2,n)`.

3. **Algorithm per test case**  
   ```
   possible = true
   for i = 0 … n‑1:
       if row1[i] == '1' and row2[i] == '1':
           possible = false
           break
   output "YES" if possible else "NO"
   ```

4. **Correctness proof sketch**  
   - *If* a column `c` has both cells blocked, any path from column `c‑1` to column `c+1` would need a move that changes the column index by 2, which is impossible (max change is 1). Therefore reaching any cell to the right of `c` is impossible ⇒ answer must be “NO”.  
   - *If* no column has both cells blocked, construct a path greedily: start at `(1,1)`. For each column `i` from `1` to `n‑1` (1‑based):  
        * If the cell in the current row at column `i+1` is free, move straight right.  
        * Otherwise the current row cell is a trap; because the column is not a double‑trap, the opposite‑row cell at `i+1` is free, so move diagonally to it.  
     This never steps on a trap and reaches column `n` in some row; the destination `(2,n)` is guaranteed free, so we can finish there (if we end in row 1 at column n we simply move down, which is allowed). Hence a path exists ⇒ answer “YES”.  

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Each test case scans the two strings once: **O(n)** time.  
   - Only a few integer/boolean variables are used: **O(1)** extra memory.  
   - Over all test cases the total work is O( Σ n ) ≤ 10⁶, well within limits.

6. **Implementation notes**  
   - Use 0‑based indexing when iterating strings.  
   - The guarantee that `(1,1)` and `(2,n)` are free does not need extra handling; the algorithm works regardless.  
   - Print each answer on its own line, exactly “YES” or “NO” (uppercase).  
<root>
<explanation>The proposed plan correctly solves the problem.  
Because moves are allowed to any orthogonal or diagonal neighbour, the only way the path can be blocked is if there exists a column where both cells are traps. In such a column the character cannot occupy any cell, and since a move can change the column index by at most 1, it is impossible to get past that column. Conversely, if no column contains two traps, a constructive greedy walk (stay in the current row when the next cell is free, otherwise move diagonally to the other row, which is guaranteed to be free) always reaches column n, and the final cell (2,n) is safe by the input guarantee. Hence checking for a “double‑trap” column is both necessary and sufficient. The algorithm runs in O(n) per test case and uses O(1) extra memory, matching the constraints.</explanation>

**Planning – “Monocarp’s 2‑row grid”**

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `t`.  
   - Loop `t` times: read `n`, then two strings `s[0]` and `s[1]` of length `n`.

2. **Observation**  
   - The start cell `(1,1)` → `s[0][0]` and the target cell `(2,n)` → `s[1][n‑1]` are guaranteed to be `'0'`.  
   - From any cell you can move to any of the 8 neighbours, therefore the only thing that can stop you is a *column* where **both** cells are traps (`'1'`). Such a column is an impenetrable wall – you cannot cross it from left to right.  
   - All other columns are “passable”. In a passable column you may stay in the current row or, if you wish, switch to the other row (the diagonal move is allowed because the two cells of the column are both `'0'`).  
   - Consequently the problem reduces to: *Is there any column `c` (1‑based) with `s[0][c‑1] == '1'` **and** `s[1][c‑1] == '1'` that lies between the start and the finish?*  
   - If such a column exists, answer **NO**; otherwise answer **YES**.

3. **Algorithm (per test case)**  
   ```
   possible = true
   for col = 0 .. n-1:
       if s[0][col] == '1' && s[1][col] == '1':
           possible = false
           break
   output "YES" if possible else "NO"
   ```
   - No need to simulate the exact path; the existence of a double‑blocked column is both necessary and sufficient for impossibility.

4. **Correctness Sketch**  
   - *If* a double‑blocked column exists, any path from column `c‑1` to column `c+1` must cross column `c`. Since both cells of column `c` are traps, the character cannot occupy any cell of that column, thus crossing is impossible → answer NO.  
   - *If* no column is double‑blocked, we can construct a path greedily: start at row 1, column 1. Scan columns left‑to‑right; whenever the current row’s cell in the next column is a trap, the other row’s cell must be safe (otherwise we would have a double‑blocked column). Move diagonally to that safe cell. Because we never encounter a wall, we can always advance, finally reaching column n, row 2 (the target is safe). Hence a path exists → answer YES.

5. **Complexity Analysis**  
   - Time: `O(n)` per test case (single pass over the columns).  
   - Memory: `O(1)` besides the input strings.

6. **Edge Cases to Consider**  
   - Minimum `n = 3`.  
   - Double‑blocked column at the very first or last position: start and end cells are safe, so such a column cannot be at column 1 (row 1) or column n (row 2), but could be elsewhere; the loop handles it.  
   - All cells safe → answer YES.  
   - Alternating traps (`0101…` / `1010…`) → no double‑blocked column → answer YES (as in sample 4).  

7. **Implementation Steps**  
   - Use `std::string` (C++) / `string` (Python) to store each row.  
   - Loop through indices `i = 0 … n‑1`.  
   - Check `if (row0[i] == '1' && row1[i] == '1')`.  
   - Break early on first detection.  
   - Print result per test case.

8. **Testing**  
   - Verify with the provided sample.  
   - Additional tests:  
     * `n=5, rows: 00000 / 00000` → YES.  
     * `n=5, rows: 01110 / 01110` → NO (column 3 double‑blocked).  
     * `n=4, rows: 0100 / 0010` → YES (no column with both 1).

**Problem Recap**  
- 2 × n grid, cells are safe (`0`) or traps (`1`).  
- Start: (row 1, col 1) – always safe.  
- Goal: (row 2, col n) – always safe.  
- In one move you may go to any of the 8 neighboring cells (|Δrow| ≤ 1, |Δcol| ≤ 1) staying inside the grid and avoiding traps.  
- Determine if a path exists.

---

### 1. Observation & Key Insight
Because movement can change column by at most 1, the character never needs to move left – doing so cannot help reaching the rightmost column.  
Thus we can scan columns from left to right, keeping track of which rows are reachable at the current column.

A column can be in three states:

| Column `c` | Row 1 safe? | Row 2 safe? |
|------------|-------------|-------------|
| `00`       | both reachable (if at least one was reachable before) |
| `01`       | only row 2 reachable |
| `10`       | only row 1 reachable |
| `11`       | **blocked** – cannot occupy this column at all |

When both cells are safe (`00`), you may stay in the same row or switch rows (diagonal move).  
When only one cell is safe, you can only be in that row; you may also arrive from the opposite row via a diagonal move **only if the opposite cell in the previous column was safe** (which is already encoded in the reachable set).

Therefore the reachable set for column `c` depends only on:
- reachable rows in column `c‑1`
- safety pattern of column `c`

This yields a simple DP/greedy transition.

---

### 2. Algorithm (per test case)

```
read n, strings s[0] (row1) and s[1] (row2)

reachable[0] = true   // row 1 at column 1 is start
reachable[1] = false  // row 2 at column 1 not yet reachable

for col = 1 … n-1 (0‑based index):
    newReach[0] = newReach[1] = false
    for row in {0,1}:
        if reachable[row] == false: continue
        // try to stay in same row
        if s[row][col] == '0':
            newReach[row] = true
        // try to move diagonally to the other row (col+1, otherRow)
        other = 1 - row
        if s[other][col] == '0' && s[other][col-1] == '0':   // actually diagonal uses current column of other row, which is col (next column)
            // moving from (row, col-1) to (other, col) is allowed if both cells are safe
            newReach[other] = true
    reachable = newReach
```

Simplify: because we only move to column `c` (0‑based) from column `c‑1`, the allowed transitions are:

- stay: `(row, c-1) -> (row, c)` if `s[row][c] == '0'`
- diagonal: `(row, c-1) -> (1-row, c)` if `s[1-row][c] == '0'`

No need to check `s[1-row][c-1]` because we already know `(row, c-1)` is reachable, which implies `s[row][c-1] == '0'`. Diagonal move does **not** require the other cell of previous column to be safe.

Thus the transition reduces to:

```
newReach[0] = (reachable[0] && s[0][c]=='0') || (reachable[1] && s[0][c]=='0')
newReach[1] = (reachable[1] && s[1][c]=='0') || (reachable[0] && s[1][c]=='0')
```

But note that diagonal from row 1 to row 2 uses the same target cell safety, so both terms share the same condition. Hence:

```
newReach[0] = s[0][c]=='0' && (reachable[0] || reachable[1])
newReach[1] = s[1][c]=='0' && (reachable[0] || reachable[1])
```

However this would allow moving from row 1 to row 2 even if row 2 in previous column was a trap, which is allowed (diagonal does not pass through that trap). So the simplified formula is correct.

Implementation can be even simpler:

```
bool ok = true;
int curRow = 0; // start row
for col = 1 … n-1:
    if s[curRow][col] == '0':
        continue;               // stay
    else if s[1-curRow][col] == '0':
        curRow = 1 - curRow;    // forced switch
    else:
        ok = false; break;
```

But this greedy works only because when both cells are safe (`00`) you may stay or switch; staying is always safe for future columns, so never harmful to stay. Hence the above linear scan with forced switches suffices.

**Final check:** after processing column `n-1` (0‑based), we must be in row 2 (`curRow == 1`). If `ok` and `curRow == 1` → **YES**, else **NO**.

---

### 3. Correctness Sketch
- **Invariant:** After processing column `c` (0‑based), `curRow` is a row that is reachable at column `c` and the algorithm has never discarded any reachable state that could lead to a solution.
- If the current cell is safe, staying preserves reachability; switching when current cell is blocked is the only possible move (diagonal) because the other row’s cell is safe (otherwise impossible).  
- When both cells are safe, staying does not reduce future possibilities: any path that would switch now can also switch later at the next column where the current row becomes blocked. Hence greedy “stay unless forced” never eliminates a feasible solution.
- By induction over columns, if a solution exists, the algorithm will never get stuck and will end in row 2 at column `n`. Conversely, if the algorithm gets stuck, no move exists from the current reachable state, so no solution exists.

---

### 4. Complexity Analysis
- **Time:** O(n) per test case (single left‑to‑right scan).  
- **Memory:** O(1) extra (only a few booleans / an integer for current row).

---

### 5. Edge Cases & Validation
- `n = 3` minimal size – algorithm works from column 2 onward.
- Columns where both cells are traps (`11`) → immediate failure.
- Starting column (col 0) is guaranteed safe; no special handling needed.
- Ensure final row is 2 (`curRow == 1`); reaching column `n-1` in row 1 is not a success.

---

### 6. Implementation Steps
1. Read integer `t`.  
2. Loop over test cases:
   - Read `n`.
   - Read two strings `row0`, `row1`.
   - Set `curRow = 0` (row 1), `possible = true`.
   - For `c` from `1` to `n-1`:
        * if `row[curRow][c] == '0'` → continue.
        * else if `row[1-curRow][c] == '0'` → `curRow = 1 - curRow`.
        * else → `possible = false; break;`.
   - After loop, output `"YES"` if `possible && curRow == 1` else `"NO"`.

The program follows the required input‑output format and meets all constraints.
<root>
<explanation>
The proposed plan correctly solves the problem.  
The character never needs to move left, so we can process the columns from left to right while keeping track of which row is reachable at the current column.  
If the cell in the current row is safe we can stay; if it is a trap we are forced to move diagonally to the other row (which is allowed as long as the target cell is safe).  
When both cells of a column are safe, staying in the current row cannot make a future solution impossible – any path that would switch rows now could also switch later at the first column where the current row becomes blocked.  
Therefore the greedy rule “stay if possible, otherwise switch if the other row is safe, otherwise fail” never discards a feasible solution and will find one whenever it exists.  
The algorithm runs in O(n) time per test case with O(1) extra memory, satisfying the constraints.

Hence the plan is correct.
</explanation>

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        row1 = next(it).strip()
        row2 = next(it).strip()
        possible = True
        for i in range(n):
            if row1[i] == '1' and row2[i] == '1':
                possible = False
                break
        out_lines.append("YES" if possible else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Sign Assignment to Reach Desired Prefix Sums</title>
      You are given an array of distinct positive integers A[1…N] and a string S of length N consisting of characters ‘+’ and ‘-’.  
      You must assign each number either a ‘+’ sign (add to the current sum) or a ‘-’ sign (subtract from the current sum) **in any order**.  
      After the i‑th assignment the sign of the **current total sum** must match S[i] (‘+’ means the sum is positive, ‘-’ means the sum is negative).  
      Output one possible ordering together with the chosen sign for each number, or report that it is impossible.  
      This problem is closely related to the original balance‑scale problem, where ‘L’ corresponds to a positive total (left heavier) and ‘R’ to a negative total (right heavier).
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data: 
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))
    s = data[1+n].strip()

    # Sort numbers increasingly – we will always use the smallest still unused
    a.sort()
    left, right = 0, n-1          # pointers to smallest / largest unused
    cur = 0                       # current total (positive → left heavier)
    out = []                      # list of (value, sign) to print

    for i,ch in enumerate(s):
        if ch == '+':   # we need total > 0 after this move
            # try to add the smallest number that makes cur positive
            if cur + a[left] > 0:
                cur += a[left]
                out.append((a[left], '+'))
                left += 1
            else:
                # otherwise we must add the largest number
                cur += a[right]
                out.append((a[right], '+'))
                right -= 1
        else:           # we need total < 0 after this move
            if cur - a[left] < 0:
                cur -= a[left]
                out.append((a[left], '-'))
                left += 1
            else:
                cur -= a[right]
                out.append((a[right], '-'))
                right -= 1

        # If after the move the sign does not match, impossible
        if (cur > 0 and ch == '-') or (cur < 0 and ch == '+') or cur == 0:
            print(-1)
            return

    for val,sgn in out:
        print(val, sgn)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – N, the array A and the string S.  
      2. **Sort** A increasingly. The smallest unused element is always at index `left`, the largest at `right`.  
      3. Initialise `cur = 0` (balance is even).  
      4. Iterate over the characters of S:  
         * If the required sign is ‘+’, we need `cur` to become positive after the move.  
           – If adding the smallest element already makes it positive, use it (greedy – keep large numbers for later).  
           – Otherwise we must add the largest remaining element.  
         * If the required sign is ‘-’, symmetric logic with subtraction.  
      5. After each step verify that the sign of `cur` matches the required character and that `cur` is not zero. If it fails, output `-1`.  
      6. Store each chosen `(value, sign)` pair; after processing all characters print them in order.  
      7. Complexity: sorting `O(N log N)`, the loop `O(N)`, memory `O(N)`.  
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Constructing a Sequence with Prefix Majority Constraints</title>
      You are given N distinct integers and a binary string M of length N consisting of ‘L’ and ‘R’.  
      You must arrange the integers into a sequence. After placing the i‑th element, the **majority** of the first i elements must be on the side indicated by M[i] (i.e., more numbers placed on the left side than on the right side if M[i]=‘L’, otherwise the opposite).  
      The task is to output any valid ordering or report impossibility.  
      This problem mirrors the original one, but instead of weight sums we only care about the count of elements on each side.
    </description>
    <code>
# Python 3 solution using a greedy count‑balancing approach
import sys

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))
    m = data[1+n].strip()

    # Sort values – we will always place the smallest remaining value
    a.sort()
    left_cnt = right_cnt = 0
    left_ptr, right_ptr = 0, n-1
    answer = []

    for i,ch in enumerate(m):
        if ch == 'L':          # we need more left than right after this step
            # Prefer to put a small element on the left
            answer.append((a[left_ptr], 'L'))
            left_cnt += 1
            left_ptr += 1
        else:                  # need more right than left
            answer.append((a[right_ptr], 'R'))
            right_cnt += 1
            right_ptr -= 1

        # Validate the majority condition
        if left_cnt == right_cnt:
            print(-1)
            return
        if (left_cnt > right_cnt and ch != 'L') or (right_cnt > left_cnt and ch != 'R'):
            print(-1)
            return

    for val, side in answer:
        print(val, side)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Input** – read N, the list of numbers, and the string M.  
      2. **Sort** the numbers. The smallest unused number will be placed on the side that needs a new element, because it leaves larger numbers for later steps where the majority constraint may be tighter.  
      3. Keep two counters: `left_cnt` and `right_cnt`.  
      4. Scan the string M from left to right:  
         * If the current character is ‘L’, put the smallest remaining number on the left, increment `left_cnt`.  
         * If it is ‘R’, put the largest remaining number on the right, increment `right_cnt`.  
      5. After each insertion verify that the side indicated by M indeed has the strict majority (counts cannot be equal). If the condition ever fails, output `-1`.  
      6. Store each `(value, side)` pair and finally print them.  
      7. Complexity: sorting `O(N log N)`, linear scan `O(N)`.  
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Balancing a Scale with Fixed Order of Moves</title>
      You are given N distinct weights and a string T of length N consisting of ‘L’ and ‘R’.  
      The i‑th move must use the i‑th weight from the original array (i.e., the order of weights is fixed), and you may choose on which side (left or right) to place it.  
      After each move the side indicated by T[i] must be heavier.  
      Determine whether such a placement exists and output the side for each weight, or `-1` if impossible.  
      This is a variant of the original problem where the order of weights is **not** free, highlighting the importance of greedy decisions based on current imbalance.
    </description>
    <code>
# Greedy solution for the fixed‑order version
import sys

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))
    t = data[1+n].strip()

    left_sum = right_sum = 0
    ans = []

    for i in range(n):
        w = a[i]
        need = t[i]

        # Try to place the weight on the side that will satisfy the requirement
        if need == 'L':
            # we need left > right after this move
            if left_sum + w > right_sum:
                left_sum += w
                ans.append('L')
            else:
                # otherwise we must put it on the right and hope the inequality flips
                right_sum += w
                ans.append('R')
                if left_sum <= right_sum:   # still not satisfied
                    print(-1)
                    return
        else:   # need == 'R'
            if right_sum + w > left_sum:
                right_sum += w
                ans.append('R')
            else:
                left_sum += w
                ans.append('L')
                if right_sum <= left_sum:
                    print(-1)
                    return

    for side in ans:
        print(side)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read** N, the weight list A (order is fixed), and the requirement string T.  
      2. Keep two running totals: `left_sum` and `right_sum`.  
      3. Process the weights sequentially:  
         * If the current requirement is ‘L’, we need `left_sum` to become strictly larger than `right_sum` after placing the current weight.  
           – If adding the weight to the left already satisfies the condition, do it.  
           – Otherwise we are forced to put it on the right; after that we check whether the condition is now satisfied; if not, the whole instance is impossible.  
         * The ‘R’ case is symmetric.  
      4. Store the chosen side for each weight.  
      5. If the loop finishes without contradiction, output the stored sides; otherwise output `-1`.  
      6. The algorithm runs in `O(N)` time and `O(1)` extra memory.  
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original problem
    The original task asks for an ordering of distinct weights together with a side (L or R) for each weight such that after the i‑th placement the side indicated by the i‑th character of the string **S** is strictly heavier.  
    The natural way to satisfy a sequence of “which side must be heavier” constraints is a **greedy algorithm** that always keeps the current imbalance as small as possible while respecting the next requirement.

    ## Why a greedy approach works
    * At step *i* we only need to know the current difference  
      `diff = weight_on_left – weight_on_right`.  
      The next character `S[i]` tells us whether `diff` must become **positive** (`L`) or **negative** (`R`).  
    * If we can achieve the required sign by adding the **smallest** still unused weight to the appropriate side, we do so.  
      Using the smallest possible weight leaves larger weights for later steps, where the required sign may be harder to obtain.  
    * If the smallest weight is not enough to flip the sign, we must use the **largest** remaining weight; otherwise the requirement would be impossible later because all remaining weights are even larger and would only increase the same side’s dominance.

    Consequently the algorithm proceeds as:
    1. Sort the array of weights increasingly.  
    2. Keep two pointers: `l` (smallest unused) and `r` (largest unused).  
    3. Initialise `diff = 0`.  
    4. For each character `c` in `S`  
       * If `c == 'L'` (left must be heavier):  
         - If `diff + A[l] > 0` → place `A[l]` on the left, `l++`.  
         - Else → place `A[r]` on the left, `r--`.  
       * If `c == 'R'` (right must be heavier):  
         - If `diff - A[l] < 0` → place `A[l]` on the right, `l++`.  
         - Else → place `A[r]` on the right, `r--`.  
       * Update `diff` accordingly (`+` for left, `-` for right).  
       * If after the update `diff` does **not** have the required sign (or becomes zero) the instance has no solution – output `-1`.  
    5. Record each chosen weight and side; after processing all characters output the recorded sequence.

    ## Complexity analysis
    * Sorting: `O(N log N)`.  
    * One linear scan over the string: `O(N)`.  
    * Memory: the sorted array plus the answer list → `O(N)`.

    ## Generic greedy‑tutorial for “sign‑dominance” problems
    1. **Identify the state** you need to maintain (here the signed difference between two sides).  
    2. **Determine the local decision** that can satisfy the next constraint with the smallest possible “cost”.  
       * “Cost” is usually the magnitude of the element you consume; using the smallest feasible element keeps flexibility for the future.  
    3. **Prove optimality** by exchange argument: if an optimal solution ever uses a larger element while a smaller one could have been placed without breaking the current constraint, swapping them does not worsen any later constraint. Hence a greedy choice never harms optimality.  
    4. **Implement with sorted data** (array, multiset, priority queue) so that the smallest and largest unused elements are available in `O(1)` or `O(log N)`.  
    5. **Validate after each step** – the problem often requires a strict inequality; if the greedy step cannot meet it, the whole instance is impossible.  

    This pattern appears in many competitive‑programming tasks:
    * Constructing a sequence with prescribed prefix signs.  
    * Assigning ‘+’/‘‑’ to numbers to keep the running sum positive/negative.  
    * Scheduling jobs where each step must keep a resource level above/below a threshold.  

    By mastering the “keep the imbalance minimal, use the smallest feasible element” mindset, you can solve a wide class of problems that involve step‑by‑step dominance constraints.
  </algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – Greedy construction of a feasible ordering**

1. **Input**
   - Read `N`.
   - Read array `A[1…N]`.
   - Read string `S` (length `N`, characters ‘L’ or ‘R’).

2. **Pre‑processing**
   - Sort `A` in **non‑decreasing** order.
   - Keep two indices:
     - `l = 0` – points to the smallest unused weight (`A[l]`).
     - `r = N‑1` – points to the largest unused weight (`A[r]`).
   - Initialise current imbalance `diff = 0`.  
     (`diff = sum_left – sum_right`; positive ⇒ left heavier, negative ⇒ right heavier.)
   - Prepare a vector `ans` to store the chosen pair `(weight, side)` for each step.

3. **Greedy iteration over the required signs**
   - For `i = 0 … N‑1` (character `c = S[i]`):
     - **Case `c == 'L'`** (after this move left must be heavier, i.e. `diff > 0`):
       1. *Try the smallest weight*:  
          If `diff + A[l] > 0` then place `A[l]` on the **left** side, `l++`, update `diff += A[l]`.
       2. *Otherwise* the smallest weight is insufficient to flip the sign, so we must use the largest remaining weight:  
          Place `A[r]` on the **left**, `r--`, update `diff += A[r]`.
     - **Case `c == 'R'`** (right must be heavier, i.e. `diff < 0`):
       1. *Try the smallest weight*:  
          If `diff - A[l] < 0` then place `A[l]` on the **right**, `l++`, update `diff -= A[l]`.
       2. *Otherwise* use the largest remaining weight:  
          Place `A[r]` on the **right**, `r--`, update `diff -= A[r]`.
     - **Validation after the update**  
       - If `c == 'L'` and `diff <= 0` → impossible → output `-1` and terminate.  
       - If `c == 'R'` and `diff >= 0` → impossible → output `-1` and terminate.
     - Append the chosen `(weight, side)` to `ans`.

4. **Output**
   - If the loop finishes without contradiction, print the `ans` list in the order they were generated: each line "`weight side`" (`side` is ‘L’ or ‘R’).

5. **Correctness Argument (exchange proof)**
   - At step `i` we only need to satisfy the sign of `diff` after this step.
   - Using the **smallest** weight that already fulfills the sign keeps `|diff|` as small as possible, leaving larger weights for later steps where the required sign may be harder to achieve.
   - If the smallest weight cannot satisfy the sign, any larger weight placed on the same side would also fail (it would not change the sign), therefore we must place the **largest** remaining weight to guarantee the sign change.
   - Swapping a larger weight used now with a smaller weight used later never harms feasibility because the smaller weight would still keep the sign correct at the later step (its magnitude is ≤ the larger one). Hence a greedy choice never blocks a future solution.
   - Consequently, if the greedy algorithm fails, no solution exists.

6. **Complexity Analysis**
   - Sorting: `O(N log N)`.
   - Single pass over `S` with `O(1)` operations per step: `O(N)`.
   - Memory: the sorted array, two pointers, and answer list → `O(N)`.

7. **Edge Cases to Handle**
   - `N = 1`: simply check whether the single weight can give the required sign (always possible because `diff` starts at 0, any non‑zero weight will give the needed sign).
   - All characters identical (`LLLL…` or `RRRR…`): algorithm naturally keeps adding to the same side; if at any point the smallest remaining weight cannot keep the sign, the largest is used.
   - Very large values (`A_i` up to `1e9`): use 64‑bit signed integer (`long long`) for `diff`.
   - Ensure that after each step `diff` is never zero (strict inequality required). The validation step covers this.

8. **Implementation Checklist**
   - Use `vector<long long> a(N);` and sort.
   - Pointers `int l = 0, r = N-1;`.
   - `long long diff = 0;`.
   - For each character `c`:
     - Branch as described, update `diff`, push `{weight, side}` into `ans`.
   - After loop, iterate over `ans` and `cout << weight << ' ' << side << '\n';`.
   - If failure, `cout << -1 << '\n';` and exit.
<root>
<explanation>
The proposed algorithm is a classic greedy solution for this type of “sign‑maintaining balance” problem.  
By sorting the distinct weights and always keeping two pointers to the smallest and the largest unused weight, the algorithm can decide at each step which weight to place on the required side.

* If the current sign requirement is **L** (the left side must become heavier), the algorithm first tries to place the smallest remaining weight on the left.  
  – If this already makes the left side heavier (`diff + smallest > 0`), using the smallest weight is optimal because it keeps the absolute imbalance as low as possible, preserving larger weights for later steps.  
  – If the smallest weight is insufficient, any weight smaller than the largest would also be insufficient, so the algorithm must use the largest remaining weight. If even the largest weight cannot make the left side heavier, the instance is impossible.

* The symmetric reasoning holds for a required **R** step.

The exchange argument shows that replacing a larger weight used now with a smaller one (when the smaller already satisfies the sign) never harms feasibility, while using a larger weight when a smaller one would already work can only reduce the flexibility for future steps. Therefore, if the greedy procedure fails, no valid ordering exists.

The algorithm runs in `O(N log N)` time for sorting and `O(N)` additional work, uses `O(N)` memory, and correctly handles all edge cases (including `N = 1`, all‑same‑letter strings, and large weight values using 64‑bit integers).

Consequently, the plan is correct and will solve the problem as stated.

**Solution Overview**

We have `N` distinct weights `A[ ]` and a string `S` of length `N` (`S[i] ∈ {L,R}`).
We must output an ordering of the weights together with the side (`L` or `R`) on which each weight is placed, such that after the *i‑th* placement the total weight on the side indicated by `S[i]` is **strictly larger** than the weight on the opposite side.

The problem is a classic “prefix‑sign” construction.  
We keep the current signed difference  

```
diff = (total weight on left) – (total weight on right)
```

and at step `i` we need  

```
diff > 0   if S[i] = 'L'
diff < 0   if S[i] = 'R'
```

The greedy rule: **use the smallest possible weight that already satisfies the required sign**; if the smallest weight is insufficient, we must use the largest remaining weight (the only way to flip the sign). This keeps the imbalance as small as possible, preserving flexibility for later steps.

---

### 1. Data structures

* `vector<long long> w` – the weights sorted increasingly.
* Two indices `l = 0` (smallest unused) and `r = N‑1` (largest unused).
* `long long diff = 0`.
* `vector<pair<long long,char>> answer` – stores the chosen `(weight, side)` in order.

All operations are `O(1)`; sorting is `O(N log N)`.

---

### 2. Greedy step (processed from `i = 0 … N‑1`)

For the current character `c = S[i]`:

| Required side | Condition to try smallest weight | Action if condition holds | Action otherwise |
|---------------|-----------------------------------|---------------------------|-------------------|
| `L` (need `diff > 0`) | `diff + w[l] > 0` | place `w[l]` on **left**, `l++`, `diff += w[l]` | place `w[r]` on **left**, `r--`, `diff += w[r]` |
| `R` (need `diff < 0`) | `diff - w[l] < 0` | place `w[l]` on **right**, `l++`, `diff -= w[l]` | place `w[r]` on **right**, `r--`, `diff -= w[r]` |

After the update we **verify** that the sign is correct (`diff > 0` for `L`, `diff < 0` for `R`).  
If it is not (or `diff == 0`), the instance is impossible → output `-1`.

Why this works:

* If the smallest remaining weight already makes the required sign true, using any larger weight would only increase the absolute imbalance unnecessarily and can never help later steps (exchange argument).
* If the smallest weight is insufficient, any weight smaller than the largest will also be insufficient, so we must use the largest to flip the sign; otherwise the requirement cannot be satisfied later because all remaining weights are ≤ that largest and would keep the same sign.

---

### 3. Algorithm

```
read N
read array A
read string S
sort A increasingly -> w
l = 0, r = N-1
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for i = 0 .. N-1:
    c = S[i]
    if c == 'L':
        if diff + w[l] > 0:
            weight = w[l]; l++
            diff += weight
            side = 'L'
        else:
            weight = w[r]; r--
            diff += weight
            side = 'L'
        if diff <= 0:   // must be strictly positive
            print -1 and exit
    else: // c == 'R'
        if diff - w[l] < 0:
            weight = w[l]; l++
            diff -= weight
            side = 'R'
        else:
            weight = w[r]; r--
            diff -= weight
            side = 'R'
        if diff >= 0:   // must be strictly negative
            print -1 and exit
    answer.push_back({weight, side})

print each pair of answer on its own line: weight<space>side
```

---

### 4. Correctness Proof Sketch

**Lemma 1 (Feasibility of smallest choice).**  
If at step `i` the required sign can be achieved by adding the smallest unused weight `w[l]` to the required side, then there exists an optimal solution that also uses `w[l]` at this step.

*Proof.* Suppose an optimal solution uses a larger weight `x > w[l]` on the same side at step `i`. Replace `x` by `w[l]`; the sign after step `i` is still correct (by assumption). All later steps still have at least the same set of unused weights (we have returned `x` to the pool, which is ≥ `w[l]`). Since later steps only need a sign, having a larger weight available cannot make the problem harder. Thus the modified solution is also feasible, contradicting optimality of the original choice. ∎

**Lemma 2 (Necessity of largest choice).**  
If adding the smallest weight `w[l]` to the required side does **not** satisfy the sign, then any feasible solution must use the largest remaining weight `w[r]` on that side at this step.

*Proof.* Adding any weight `y ≤ w[r]` to the required side yields the same sign as adding `w[l]` (still wrong) because `y` is ≤ `w[r]` and the sign condition is monotone in the added magnitude. Only a weight ≥ the smallest that flips the sign can work, and the only such weight among the unused ones is `w[r]`. ∎

**Invariant.** After processing the first `i` characters, `diff` has the correct sign required by `S[i‑1]` and the multiset of unused weights is exactly `{w[l], …, w[r]}`.

*Proof by induction.*  
*Base*: `i = 0`, `diff = 0`, unused set = all weights – invariant holds trivially.  
*Inductive step*: Assume invariant holds for `i`. At step `i+1` we apply the greedy rule. By Lemma 1 we may safely use `w[l]` when it works; by Lemma 2 we must use `w[r]` otherwise. In both cases we update `diff` exactly as the chosen weight contributes, and we remove that weight from the unused set, preserving the invariant. The sign check guarantees the required sign, so the invariant holds for `i+1`. ∎

**Theorem.** If the algorithm finishes without printing `-1`, the produced sequence satisfies all constraints; if it prints `-1`, no valid sequence exists.

*Proof.*  
*Soundness*: By the invariant, after each step the sign of `diff` matches `S[i]`. Hence the output sequence is valid.  
*Completeness*: Suppose a valid sequence exists but the algorithm fails at some step `k`. At that step the algorithm could not achieve the required sign with either `w[l]` or `w[r]`. By Lemma 1 and Lemma 2 any feasible solution must use either `w[l]` (if it would work) or `w[r]` (if `w[l]` fails). Since both choices lead to a wrong sign, no solution can exist – contradiction. ∎

---

### 5. Complexity Analysis

*Sorting*: `O(N log N)`  
*Greedy scan*: `O(N)`  
*Memory*: `O(N)` for the sorted array and answer list.

Both limits satisfy `N ≤ 2·10⁵`.

---

### 6. Edge Cases & Implementation Details

| Situation | Handling |
|-----------|----------|
| `N = 1` | The only weight must make the required side heavier; the algorithm works because `diff` starts at 0 and the sign check is performed. |
| All `L` or all `R` | Greedy automatically picks smallest weights on the required side until the sign flips, then uses largest as needed. |
| `diff` becomes zero after an update | Treated as failure because the requirement is *strict* (`>` or `<`). |
| Large weight values (`≤ 10⁹`) | Use 64‑bit (`long long`) for `diff` and sums. |
| Input reading | Use fast I/O (`ios::sync_with_stdio(false); cin.tie(nullptr);`). |
| Output order | Store pairs in a vector and print after the loop (or print on the fly). |

---

### 7. Pseudocode (ready for translation)

```
function solve():
    N = readInt()
    A = readArray(N)          // long long
    S = readString()          // length N

    sort(A)                   // ascending
    l = 0
    r = N-1
    diff = 0
    answer = empty list

    for i from 0 to N-1:
        if S[i] == 'L':
            if diff + A[l] > 0:
                w = A[l]; l += 1
                diff += w
                side = 'L'
            else:
                w = A[r]; r -= 1
                diff += w
                side = 'L'
            if diff <= 0:
                print -1
                return
        else: // 'R'
            if diff - A[l] < 0:
                w = A[l]; l += 1
                diff -= w
                side = 'R'
            else:
                w = A[r]; r -= 1
                diff -= w
                side = 'R'
            if diff >= 0:
                print -1
                return
        answer.append((w, side))

    for (w, side) in answer:
        print w, side
```

The above plan can be directly implemented in C++, Java, Python, etc., respecting the required I/O format.
<root>
<explanation>The proposed plan assumes that at each step the weight must be placed on the side that the string S requires to be heavier, and it decides between the smallest and the largest remaining weight based only on whether the smallest weight already makes the required sign true.  

In the original problem the weight may be placed on **either** side; the only requirement is that after the placement the side indicated by S is heavier. Consequently, there are situations where the optimal move is to put a weight on the opposite side (e.g., when the current imbalance already favors the required side and a small weight can be added to the other side without changing the sign). By forcing the placement always onto the required side, the algorithm can waste large weights or be unable to satisfy a later requirement even though a valid solution exists that uses the opposite side at some step.

Because of this restriction the greedy rule “use the smallest weight if it already gives the correct sign, otherwise use the largest weight” is not sufficient to guarantee a solution whenever one exists, and the algorithm may incorrectly output -1 or produce an invalid sequence. Therefore the plan does **not** correctly solve the problem.

</explanation>
