**Planning to solve “Balancing a Scale with Fixed Order of Moves”**

1. **Input**  
   - Read `N`.  
   - Read array `A[0…N‑1]` (distinct weights).  
   - Read string `S` of length `N` (characters ‘L’ or ‘R’).

2. **Pre‑processing**  
   - Sort `A` in **non‑decreasing** order.  
   - Keep two indices:  
     * `lo = 0` – points to the smallest unused weight.  
     * `hi = N‑1` – points to the largest unused weight.  

3. **State variable**  
   - `diff = left_sum – right_sum` (initially `0`).  
   - After each move we must have:  
     * `diff > 0` if `S[i] == 'L'` (left heavier).  
     * `diff < 0` if `S[i] == 'R'` (right heavier).

4. **Greedy construction (linear scan over `S`)**  

   For `i = 0 … N‑1`  

   *If `S[i] == 'L'`* (left must become heavier)  

   ```
   if diff + A[lo] > 0               // smallest weight already enough
        w = A[lo];  side = 'L';  lo++;
   else                               // need a larger push
        w = A[hi];  side = 'L';  hi--;
   diff += w;                         // left side gets w
   ```

   *If `S[i] == 'R'`* (right must become heavier)  

   ```
   if diff - A[lo] < 0               // smallest weight already enough
        w = A[lo];  side = 'R';  lo++;
   else                               // need a larger push
        w = A[hi];  side = 'R';  hi--;
   diff -= w;                         // right side gets w
   ```

   *Validation* after updating `diff`  

   ```
   if (S[i] == 'L' && diff <= 0)  → impossible
   if (S[i] == 'R' && diff >= 0)  → impossible
   ```

   - If impossible, stop and output `-1`.  
   - Otherwise store the pair `(w, side)` in an answer vector preserving the order of moves.

5. **Output**  
   - If the whole scan finishes without contradiction, print the stored pairs, one per line: `<weight> <side>`.  
   - If a contradiction was detected, print a single line `-1`.

6. **Correctness sketch**  
   - The only relevant information after step `i` is the signed difference `diff`.  
   - To satisfy the next requirement we need to change the sign of `diff` (or keep it with the required sign) using the **smallest possible** weight that can achieve it.  
   - Using a larger weight when a smaller one already works would only increase the absolute imbalance, reducing flexibility for later steps.  
   - If the smallest unused weight cannot satisfy the sign requirement, any larger weight placed on the *same* side would also fail (it would keep the same sign), so we must place the **largest** remaining weight on that side to flip the sign.  
   - This exchange argument proves that the greedy choice never harms the existence of a solution; therefore if the algorithm fails, no solution exists.

7. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Sorting: `O(N log N)`.  
   - Single pass over `S` with O(1) operations per step: `O(N)`.  
   - Total time: `O(N log N)`.  
   - Extra memory: the sorted array and answer list → `O(N)`.

8. **Edge cases to consider**  
   - `N = 1`: the single weight must make the required side heavier; the algorithm handles it because `diff` starts at `0`.  
   - All `L` or all `R`: the algorithm will keep taking the smallest weight that can keep the required sign, resorting to the largest only when necessary.  
   - Very large weights (`≤ 10^9`): use 64‑bit integers (`long long`) for `diff` to avoid overflow (`N * maxWeight` fits in 64‑bit).

9. **Implementation notes (C++/Java/Python)**  
   - Use `vector<long long>` for weights, sort with built‑in sort.  
   - Store answer as `vector<pair<long long, char>>`.  
   - When printing, follow exactly: `<weight> <side>` with a space between.  
   - Stop early on impossibility to avoid unnecessary work.  
<root>
<explanation>The proposed algorithm correctly solves the problem.  
It keeps only the signed difference `diff = left_sum – right_sum`.  For each character of `S` it tries to satisfy the required sign of `diff` by using the smallest remaining weight that already makes the sign correct; if the smallest weight is insufficient it must flip the sign, and the only way to guarantee a flip is to use the largest remaining weight.  This greedy choice never harms future steps – using a larger weight when a smaller one already works would only increase the absolute imbalance and can only reduce the flexibility for later moves.  An exchange argument shows that if the algorithm ever fails, no other sequence of choices can succeed, so the algorithm is both sound and complete.

The algorithm runs in `O(N log N)` time for sorting and `O(N)` additional work, and uses `O(N)` memory, which meets the constraints (`N ≤ 2·10^5`).  All edge cases (single element, all ‘L’, all ‘R’, large weights) are handled by using 64‑bit integers for the cumulative difference.

Hence the plan is correct and will produce a valid sequence of moves whenever one exists, otherwise it outputs `-1` as required.</explanation>

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()
    a.sort()
    l, r = 0, n - 1
    diff = 0  # left - right
    ans = []
    for c in s:
        if c == 'L':
            # try smallest
            if diff + a[l] > 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
                diff += w
                ans.append((w, 'L'))
            else:
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
                diff += w
                ans.append((w, 'L'))
            if diff <= 0:
                print(-1)
                return
        else:  # 'R'
            if diff - a[l] < 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
                diff -= w
                ans.append((w, 'R'))
            else:
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
                diff -= w
                ans.append((w, 'R'))
            if diff >= 0:
                print(-1)
                return
    out_lines = [f"{w} {side}" for w, side in ans]
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

**Solution Explanation**

For every step we have to know which side of the balance is heavier.
Let  

```
diff = (total weight on the left) – (total weight on the right)
```

* `diff > 0`  → left side is heavier  
* `diff < 0`  → right side is heavier  

The `i`‑th character of the given string `S` tells us which sign `diff`
must have **after** we put the `i`‑th weight.

All weights are different, we may use them in any order and we may put a
chosen weight on any side we like.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Greedy choice

Assume we are at step `i` and the current value of `diff` is known.

*If the required sign is `L`* (after this step `diff` must be `> 0`)

* we would like to keep `|diff|` as small as possible – a small
  imbalance is easier to change later.
* therefore we try to put the **smallest still unused weight** on the
  left side.
* if `diff + smallest > 0` the requirement is already satisfied, we use
  that smallest weight.
* otherwise the smallest weight is not enough to turn the sign.
  Any larger weight placed on the left would also fail, so we have to
  use the **largest remaining weight** – it is the only chance to obtain
  a positive `diff`.

The case `R` (required sign `< 0`) is symmetric:
try the smallest weight on the right, otherwise use the largest one.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Why the greedy choice is optimal  

Consider an optimal solution.
Look at the first step where it uses a weight `w_big` while a smaller
unused weight `w_small` would also satisfy the required sign.
Exchange the two weights:

* put `w_small` at the current step (still satisfies the sign because
  `w_small` is enough);
* keep `w_big` for the later step where `w_small` was used.
  Since `w_big ≥ w_small`, the later sign condition stays satisfied
  (the imbalance after that later step can only become larger in the
  needed direction).

Thus any optimal solution can be transformed into one that follows the
greedy rule without hurting feasibility.
Consequently, if the greedy algorithm ever gets stuck, **no solution
exists**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
sort the array A increasingly
l = 0                     // index of smallest unused weight
r = N-1                   // index of largest  unused weight
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for each character c in S
    if c == 'L'                     // left must be heavier
        if diff + A[l] > 0          // smallest works
            w = A[l]; l += 1
        else                        // need the biggest one
            w = A[r]; r -= 1
        diff += w                    // weight placed on the left
        if diff <= 0:  impossible → print -1
        answer.append( (w,'L') )
    else                            // c == 'R'  right must be heavier
        if diff - A[l] < 0          // smallest works
            w = A[l]; l += 1
        else
            w = A[r]; r -= 1
        diff -= w                    // weight placed on the right
        if diff >= 0:  impossible → print -1
        answer.append( (w,'R') )

print the pairs stored in *answer* (one per line)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence whenever one exists
and prints `-1` otherwise.

---

##### Lemma 1  
When the required sign is `L` (resp. `R`) and the smallest unused weight
makes the sign correct after being added to the left (resp. right) side,
using that smallest weight never harms the existence of a solution for
the remaining steps.

**Proof.**  
Placing the smallest possible weight keeps the absolute value of `diff`
as small as possible while still satisfying the current sign.
Any later step needs to achieve a sign that is at least as hard as the
current one (because the sign may have to be flipped).  
A smaller `|diff|` can only make it easier to reach the needed sign,
never harder. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
When the smallest unused weight is **insufficient** to obtain the
required sign, any solution must use the largest remaining weight on
the same side at this step.

**Proof.**  
Assume we need `L` and `diff + A[l] ≤ 0`.  
All other unused weights are `≥ A[l]`. Adding any of them to the left
gives `diff + w ≤ diff + A[l] ≤ 0`, i.e. the sign would still be wrong.
Therefore the only way to satisfy the sign now is to add the **largest**
weight, which yields the maximal possible increase of `diff`. The same
argument holds for `R`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm declares impossibility (the sign condition fails after
the chosen weight), then **no** feasible ordering exists.

**Proof.**  
Consider the step where the algorithm fails.
*If the required sign is `L`* the algorithm tried the largest remaining
weight (Lemma&nbsp;2) and still obtained `diff ≤ 0`.  
Every other unused weight is **not larger**, therefore would give an
even smaller (or equal) `diff`. Hence no weight can make the left side
heavier at this step. The argument for `R` is symmetric. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm finishes all `N` steps without failure, the produced
sequence satisfies the whole string `S`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over the steps.

*Base*: before the first step `diff = 0`. The algorithm chooses a weight
according to the rule for `S[0]`. By construction the resulting `diff`
has the required sign, otherwise it would have terminated.

*Induction step*: assume after step `i‑1` the invariant holds
(`diff` has the sign prescribed by `S[i‑1]`).  
At step `i` the algorithm follows the rule described above.
If the smallest weight suffices, Lemma&nbsp;1 guarantees that using it
keeps the possibility of a full solution, and the sign is correct by
the condition check.  
If the smallest weight does not suffice, Lemma&nbsp;2 shows that any
solution must use the largest weight; the algorithm does exactly that,
and the sign check guarantees correctness.  
Thus after step `i` the invariant holds.

By induction the invariant holds after the last step, i.e. the whole
string `S` is satisfied. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a valid ordering of the weights and sides
iff such an ordering exists; otherwise it outputs `-1`.

**Proof.**  

*If the algorithm outputs an ordering*: by Lemma&nbsp;4 the ordering
satisfies all constraints, therefore a solution exists.

*If a solution exists*: suppose the algorithm would output `-1`.  
That can only happen when it fails a sign check at some step.
By Lemma&nbsp;3 no solution can exist in that case – contradiction.
Hence the algorithm never rejects a feasible instance. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the `N` distinct weights: `O(N log N)`  
*Greedy scan* over the string: `O(N)`  
Memory consumption: the sorted array and the answer list – `O(N)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()

    a.sort()                     # increasing
    l, r = 0, n - 1               # smallest / largest unused
    diff = 0                      # left - right
    ans = []

    for c in s:
        if c == 'L':              # left must become heavier
            # try smallest weight
            if diff + a[l] > 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
            else:                 # need the biggest one
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
            diff += w
            if diff <= 0:         # still not left‑heavy → impossible
                print(-1)
                return
            ans.append((w, 'L'))
        else:                     # c == 'R'  right must become heavier
            if diff - a[l] < 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
            else:
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
            diff -= w
            if diff >= 0:         # still not right‑heavy → impossible
                print(-1)
                return
            ans.append((w, 'R'))

    out = '\n'.join(f"{w} {side}" for w, side in ans)
    sys.stdout.write(out)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
**Solution Explanation**

For every step we keep the signed difference  

```
diff = (total weight on the left) – (total weight on the right)
```

* `diff > 0` → left side is heavier  
* `diff < 0` → right side is heavier  

The `i`‑th character of the given string `S` tells us which sign `diff`
must have **after** we put the `i`‑th weight.

All weights are distinct, we may use them in any order and we may put a
chosen weight on any side.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Greedy decision

Assume the current value of `diff` is known and the next required sign
is `L` (after this step `diff` must be `> 0`).

*We would like to keep the absolute imbalance as small as possible*,
because a small imbalance is easier to change later.
Therefore we try to put the **smallest still unused weight** on the left
side.

* if `diff + smallest > 0` the requirement is already satisfied – we
  use that smallest weight.
* otherwise the smallest weight is not enough to turn the sign.
  Any other unused weight is **not smaller**, so it would also fail.
  The only chance is to use the **largest remaining weight**.

The case `R` (required sign `< 0`) is symmetric:
try the smallest weight on the right, otherwise use the largest one.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Why the greedy choice never harms optimality  

Consider an optimal solution.
Look at the first step where it uses a weight `w_big` while a smaller
unused weight `w_small` would also satisfy the required sign.
Exchange the two weights:

* put `w_small` at the current step – the sign is still satisfied because
  `w_small` is enough;
* keep `w_big` for the later step where `w_small` was used.
  Since `w_big ≥ w_small`, the later sign condition stays satisfied
  (the imbalance after that later step can only become larger in the
  needed direction).

Thus any optimal solution can be transformed into one that follows the
greedy rule without losing feasibility.  
Consequently, if the greedy algorithm ever gets stuck, **no solution
exists**.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
sort the array A increasingly
l = 0                     // index of smallest unused weight
r = N‑1                   // index of largest  unused weight
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for each character c in S
    if c == 'L'                     # left must become heavier
        if diff + A[l] > 0          # smallest works
            w = A[l]; l += 1
        else                        # need the biggest one
            w = A[r]; r -= 1
        diff += w
        if diff <= 0:   print -1 and exit
        answer.append( (w,'L') )
    else                            # c == 'R'  right must become heavier
        if diff - A[l] < 0          # smallest works
            w = A[l]; l += 1
        else
            w = A[r]; r -= 1
        diff -= w
        if diff >= 0:   print -1 and exit
        answer.append( (w,'R') )

output the pairs stored in *answer* (one per line)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence whenever one
exists and prints `-1` otherwise.

---

##### Lemma 1  
When the required sign is `L` (resp. `R`) and the smallest unused weight
makes the sign correct after being added to the left (resp. right)
side, using that smallest weight never destroys the existence of a
solution for the remaining steps.

**Proof.**  
Placing the smallest possible weight keeps `|diff|` as small as possible
while still satisfying the current sign. A smaller absolute imbalance
can only make it easier to achieve any later required sign. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
When the smallest unused weight is **insufficient** to obtain the
required sign, any feasible solution must use the largest remaining
weight on the same side at this step.

**Proof.**  
Assume we need `L` and `diff + A[l] ≤ 0`.  
All other unused weights are `≥ A[l]`; adding any of them to the left
gives `diff + w ≤ diff + A[l] ≤ 0`, i.e. the sign would still be wrong.
Therefore the only way to satisfy the sign now is to add the **largest**
weight, which yields the maximal possible increase of `diff`. The same
argument holds for `R`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm stops because after the chosen weight the sign
condition is still violated, then **no** feasible ordering exists.

**Proof.**  
Consider the step where the algorithm fails.
*If the required sign is `L`* the algorithm already used the largest
remaining weight (Lemma&nbsp;2) and still obtained `diff ≤ 0`.  
Every other unused weight is not larger, thus would give an even
smaller (or equal) `diff`. Hence no weight can make the left side
heavier at this step. The argument for `R` is symmetric. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm finishes all `N` steps without failure, the produced
sequence satisfies the whole string `S`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over the steps.

*Base*: before the first step `diff = 0`. The algorithm chooses a weight
according to the rule for `S[0]`. By construction the resulting `diff`
has the required sign, otherwise it would have terminated.

*Induction step*: assume after step `i‑1` the invariant holds
(`diff` has the sign prescribed by `S[i‑1]`).  
At step `i` the algorithm follows the rule described above.
If the smallest weight suffices, Lemma&nbsp;1 guarantees that using it
keeps a solution possible, and the sign check guarantees correctness.
If the smallest weight does not suffice, Lemma&nbsp;2 shows that any
solution must use the largest weight; the algorithm does exactly that,
and the sign check guarantees correctness.  
Thus after step `i` the invariant holds.

By induction the invariant holds after the last step, i.e. the whole
string `S` is satisfied. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a valid ordering of the weights and sides
iff such an ordering exists; otherwise it outputs `-1`.

**Proof.**  

*If the algorithm outputs an ordering*: by Lemma&nbsp;4 the ordering
satisfies all constraints, therefore a solution exists.

*If a solution exists*: suppose the algorithm would output `-1`.  
That can happen only when it fails a sign check at some step.
By Lemma&nbsp;3 no solution can exist in that case – contradiction.
Hence the algorithm never rejects a feasible instance. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the `N` distinct weights: `O(N log N)`  
*Greedy scan* over the string: `O(N)`  
Memory consumption: the sorted array and the answer list – `O(N)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()

    a.sort()                     # increasing order
    l, r = 0, n - 1              # smallest / largest unused index
    diff = 0                     # left - right
    ans = []

    for c in s:
        if c == 'L':                     # left must become heavier
            # try the smallest weight
            if diff + a[l] > 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
            else:                        # need the biggest one
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
            diff += w
            if diff <= 0:                # still not left‑heavy → impossible
                print(-1)
                return
            ans.append((w, 'L'))
        else:                            # c == 'R'  right must become heavier
            if diff - a[l] < 0:
                w = a[l]
                l += 1
            else:
                w = a[r]
                r -= 1
            diff -= w
            if diff >= 0:                # still not right‑heavy → impossible
                print(-1)
                return
            ans.append((w, 'R'))

    out_lines = [f"{w} {side}" for w, side in ans]
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
**Solution Explanation**

For a weight `w`

* putting it on the **left** adds `+w` to the current difference  
* putting it on the **right** adds `‑w`

```
diff = (total left) – (total right)
```

After the `i`‑th move the sign of `diff` must be

* `> 0` if `S[i] = 'L'`
* `< 0` if `S[i] = 'R'`

All weights are distinct, we may use them in any order and we may decide
the side for each weight.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Greedy idea  

At a step we know

* the current `diff`
* the sign that has to appear after this step (`need = +1` for **L**, `‑1` for **R**)

We would like to keep `|diff|` as small as possible – a small imbalance
gives us the most freedom later.

*First try to put the **smallest** still unused weight on the **opposite**
side* (the side that would move `diff` towards zero).

```
opposite side weight w  →  new_diff = diff – need * w
condition to stay correct : need * new_diff > 0
⇔  w < need * diff                (1)
```

If the smallest remaining weight satisfies (1) we use it on the opposite
side – this reduces the absolute value of `diff` as much as possible.

If (1) is impossible, the opposite side cannot be used at all.
Then we have to put a weight on the **required** side.
Now we must guarantee the sign after the addition:

```
new_diff = diff + need * w
need * new_diff > 0
⇔  need * diff + w > 0
```

* if `need * diff > 0` (the current `diff` already has the right sign)
  any weight works – we again take the smallest one.
* otherwise `need * diff ≤ 0` (the current sign is wrong or `diff = 0`);
  we need a weight **strictly larger** than `|diff|` :

```
w > -need * diff                         (2)
```

So we have to pick the **smallest unused weight that is larger than the
threshold** `-need*diff`.  
If no such weight exists the whole instance is impossible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Data structure  

All weights are sorted once: `A[0] < A[1] < … < A[N‑1]`.

During the algorithm we repeatedly need

* the smallest unused weight
* the smallest unused weight **greater than** a given threshold

Both operations are “order‑statistics” on a dynamic set.
A Fenwick (Binary Indexed) tree storing `1` for every still unused index
supports

* `kth(k)` – index of the k‑th unused element ( `O(log N)` )
* `prefix_sum(p)` – number of unused elements with index `< p`

With a binary search (`bisect_right`) we obtain the first array index
`pos` whose value is `> threshold`.  
If there is an unused element at or after `pos`, its order is

```
k = prefix_sum(pos) + 1
```

and `kth(k)` returns its exact index.  
All operations are `O(log N)`, overall `O(N log N)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
sort A
build Fenwick tree with 1 on every position
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for each character c in S
        need = +1 if c == 'L' else -1

        # ----- try opposite side (reduce |diff|) -----
        w_small = smallest unused weight               # kth(1)
        if need == +1 and diff > 0 and w_small < diff:
                # put it on the right
                diff -= w_small
                record (w_small, 'R')
                delete w_small from Fenwick
                continue
        if need == -1 and diff < 0 and w_small < -diff:
                # put it on the left
                diff += w_small
                record (w_small, 'L')
                delete w_small from Fenwick
                continue

        # ----- required side -----
        if need * diff > 0:                 # already correct sign
                w = w_small                 # any weight, smallest is best
        else:                               # need a weight > |diff|
                threshold = -need * diff    # positive number
                pos = bisect_right(A, threshold)   # first index with value > threshold
                # find first unused index >= pos
                used_before = fenwick.prefix_sum(pos)   # unused ≤ pos-1
                k = used_before + 1
                if k > fenwick.total_unused():          # none left
                        print(-1); exit
                idx = fenwick.kth(k)                    # 0‑based index
                w = A[idx]

        # place w on the required side
        if need == +1:   diff += w; side = 'L'
        else:            diff -= w; side = 'R'
        if need * diff <= 0:          # sign not strict → impossible
                print(-1); exit
        record (w, side)
        delete w from Fenwick

output the recorded pairs
```

The algorithm never uses a weight twice, respects the strict sign
requirement after every step, and finishes exactly when all `N` moves are
performed.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a valid sequence iff such a sequence
exists.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the smallest unused weight `w_min` satisfies condition (1)  
`w_min < need * diff` (i.e. putting it on the opposite side keeps the
required sign), then using `w_min` on the opposite side is compatible
with some optimal solution.

**Proof.**  
Placing `w_min` on the opposite side reduces `|diff|` as much as any
choice can, because any other usable weight is `≥ w_min`.  
If an optimal solution uses a larger weight on the opposite side, we can
swap that larger weight with `w_min` (which is still unused at that
moment). The sign after the current step stays correct, and later steps are
not harmed because the larger weight is still available later. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If condition (1) is false, the opposite side cannot be used at this step.

**Proof.**  
For the opposite side we need a weight `w` with `w < need * diff`.  
All unused weights are `≥ w_min`. Since `w_min` already violates the
inequality, every other weight does as well. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
When the opposite side is impossible, a weight on the required side
produces the correct sign **iff** its value is larger than the threshold
`T = -need * diff` (inequality (2)).  

*If `need * diff > 0` the threshold is ≤ 0, any positive weight works.*

**Proof.**  
`new_diff = diff + need * w`.  
`need * new_diff > 0 ⇔ need*diff + w > 0`.  
If `need*diff > 0` the left‑hand side is already positive, any `w>0`
satisfies it.  
Otherwise `need*diff ≤ 0` and we need `w > -need*diff = T`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If a solution exists, the algorithm never declares impossibility.

**Proof.**  
Consider the first step where the algorithm would fail.

*Case A – opposite side chosen:*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 this choice is compatible with an optimal solution,
therefore a solution still exists after the step.

*Case B – opposite side impossible, required side needed:*  
The algorithm looks for the smallest unused weight `w` with `w > T`
(Lemma&nbsp;3).  
If such a weight does not exist, then **every** unused weight is `≤ T`,
hence for **any** possible choice the sign after the step would be
wrong. Consequently no solution exists – contradiction.

Thus the algorithm can only stop when no solution exists. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If the algorithm finishes all `N` steps, the produced sequence satisfies
the whole string `S`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over the steps.

*Base*: before the first step `diff = 0`.  
The algorithm selects a weight according to the rules above, which
guarantee `need * diff > 0` after the update (explicit check).

*Induction step*: assume after step `i‑1` the invariant holds.
At step `i` the algorithm distinguishes the two possibilities described
in Lemma&nbsp;1–3 and updates `diff` exactly as the chosen weight and
side dictate. The algorithm checks the strict inequality after the
update, therefore the invariant holds for step `i`. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
If a solution exists, the algorithm outputs a sequence (does not output
`-1`).

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm can only output `-1` when no solution
exists. Hence, when a solution exists, it never aborts and finally prints
the recorded moves. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints `-1` **iff** the instance has no feasible ordering;
otherwise it prints an ordering of the weights and sides that satisfies
the whole string `S`.

**Proof.**  

*If part*:  
When the program prints `-1` it did so because at some step no unused
weight could satisfy the required sign (Lemma&nbsp;4). Hence no
solution exists.

*Only‑if part*:  
If a solution exists, Lemma&nbsp;6 guarantees the program never prints
`-1`. By Lemma&nbsp;5 the printed sequence respects every character of
`S`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the weights: `O(N log N)`  
*Fenwick* operations (`kth`, `prefix_sum`, update) are `O(log N)` and
performed `N` times → `O(N log N)`.

Memory consumption: the sorted array `A` and the Fenwick tree → `O(N)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

# ---------- Fenwick tree with order‑statistics ----------
class Fenwick:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.n = n
        self.bit = [0] * (n + 1)

    def add(self, idx, delta):
        """idx : 0‑based"""
        i = idx + 1
        while i <= self.n:
            self.bit[i] += delta
            i += i & -i

    def sum(self, idx):
        """sum of [0, idx)  (idx may be 0)"""
        i = idx
        s = 0
        while i > 0:
            s += self.bit[i]
            i -= i & -i
        return s

    def total(self):
        return self.sum(self.n)

    def kth(self, k):
        """return smallest index such that prefix sum >= k (1‑based k)"""
        idx = 0
        bit_mask = 1 << (self.n.bit_length())
        while bit_mask:
            nxt = idx + bit_mask
            if nxt <= self.n and self.bit[nxt] < k:
                k -= self.bit[nxt]
                idx = nxt
            bit_mask >>= 1
        return idx          # 0‑based index of the element


# ---------- main solving function ----------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    A = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    S = next(it).strip()

    A.sort()
    ft = Fenwick(n)
    for i in range(n):
        ft.add(i, 1)          # all positions are initially unused

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in S:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        # smallest unused weight
        idx_min = ft.kthOpenRouter model initialized: openai/gpt-oss-120b

<description>
**Problem 1 – Load Balancing on Two Machines**  
You are given `N` distinct jobs, each with a processing time `t_i`.  
You have two identical machines `A` and `B`.  
You may schedule the jobs in any order, and each job must be assigned to exactly one machine.  
After each assignment the absolute difference between the total processing time on the two machines must not exceed a given limit `K`.  
If it is impossible to schedule all jobs, output `-1`.  
Otherwise output any feasible schedule: for each step print the job index and the machine (`A` or `B`).  
Constraints: `1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10^5`, `1 ≤ t_i ≤ 10^9`, `0 ≤ K ≤ 10^12`.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3.

import sys
import bisect

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    N = int(next(it))
    times = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(N)]
    K = int(next(it))

    # keep jobs sorted by processing time (smallest first)
    jobs = sorted([(times[i], i+1) for i in range(N)])   # (time, id)

    # current loads
    loadA = loadB = 0
    answer = []

    # we will always try to put the smallest job that does not break the limit
    # on the machine that keeps the difference small.
    while jobs:
        # try to place on A
        time, idx = jobs[0]
        if abs((loadA + time) - loadB) <= K:
            loadA += time
            answer.append((idx, 'A'))
            jobs.pop(0)
            continue

        # otherwise try to place on B
        if abs(loadA - (loadB + time)) <= K:
            loadB += time
            answer.append((idx, 'B'))
            jobs.pop(0)
            continue

        # if neither side works with the smallest job, we need a larger job
        # that can compensate the current imbalance.
        # Find the smallest job that makes the condition true.
        placed = False
        for j in range(1, len(jobs)):
            time, idx = jobs[j]
            if abs((loadA + time) - loadB) <= K:
                loadA += time
                answer.append((idx, 'A'))
                jobs.pop(j)
                placed = True
                break
            if abs(loadA - (loadB + time)) <= K:
                loadB += time
                answer.append((idx, 'B'))
                jobs.pop(j)
                placed = True
                break
        if not placed:
            print(-1)
            return

    for idx, machine in answer:
        print(idx, machine)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read input** – N, the list of processing times, and the limit K.  
2. **Sort jobs** by their processing time; the smallest job is the easiest to place without breaking the limit.  
3. Keep two variables `loadA` and `loadB` for the current total time on each machine.  
4. While there are still unassigned jobs:  
   * Look at the smallest remaining job.  
   * If putting it on machine A keeps `|loadA+time‑loadB| ≤ K`, assign it to A.  
   * Else if putting it on B keeps the condition, assign it to B.  
   * If neither side works with the smallest job, scan the remaining sorted list to find the first job that can be placed on either side without violating the limit (greedy – we always use the smallest feasible job).  
   * If no job can be placed, the schedule is impossible → output `-1`.  
5. Store each decision `(original_index, machine)` in order and finally print the sequence.  
Complexity: sorting `O(N log N)`, each placement scans at most the remaining list once, overall `O(N log N)` (the inner scan can be implemented with a balanced BST for true `O(N log N)`).  
Edge cases: `K = 0` (loads must stay equal), `N = 1`, very large times – use 64‑bit integers (`int` in Python is arbitrary precision).  
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 2 – Valid Parentheses Sequence with Prefix Constraints**  
You are given a string `P` of length `N` consisting of characters `'('` and `')'`.  
You must construct a new parentheses sequence `Q` of length `N` (also only `'('` and `')'`) such that for every prefix `i` (1 ≤ i ≤ N) the number of opening brackets in `Q[1…i]` is **strictly greater** than the number of closing brackets **iff** `P[i]` is `'('`.  
If several sequences satisfy the condition, output any of them; if none exists, output `-1`.  
Constraints: `1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10^5`.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3.

import sys

def solve():
    s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    if not s:
        return
    n = len(s)

    # The total number of '(' must equal the total number of ')'
    # otherwise a balanced sequence is impossible.
    if n % 2 == 1:
        print(-1)
        return

    # We'll build Q greedily from left to right.
    # Keep current balance = #('(') - #(')') in the prefix built so far.
    balance = 0
    result = []

    # We also need to know how many '(' we are still allowed to place.
    # The final balance must be 0, so we must place exactly n/2 opening brackets.
    opens_left = n // 2
    closes_left = n // 2

    for i, ch in enumerate(s):
        # If we need a strict >0 balance at this position (P[i] == '(')
        # we must make sure after placing the character the balance > 0.
        need_positive = (ch == '(')

        # Try to place '(' if we still have opens left.
        if opens_left > 0:
            # Tentatively place '(' and see if the condition can still be satisfied later.
            new_balance = balance + 1
            if (need_positive and new_balance > 0) or (not need_positive):
                # also we must be able to close the remaining opens later:
                # new_balance must not exceed closes_left (otherwise we cannot bring it back to 0)
                if new_balance <= closes_left:
                    result.append('(')
                    balance = new_balance
                    opens_left -= 1
                    continue

        # Otherwise we have to place ')'
        if closes_left == 0:
            print(-1)
            return
        new_balance = balance - 1
        if (need_positive and new_balance > 0) or (not need_positive):
            # also new_balance cannot become negative (prefix of a parentheses string)
            if new_balance >= 0:
                result.append(')')
                balance = new_balance
                closes_left -= 1
                continue

        # If neither placement works, impossible.
        print(-1)
        return

    # After processing all characters the balance must be zero.
    if balance != 0:
        print(-1)
        return

    print(''.join(result))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read the pattern string `P`.**  
2. If `N` is odd, a balanced parentheses string of length `N` cannot exist → output `-1`.  
3. The final sequence must contain exactly `N/2` opening and `N/2` closing brackets. Keep counters `opens_left` and `closes_left`.  
4. Iterate over the positions `i = 1 … N`.  
   * Let `need_positive = (P[i] == '(')`.  
   * Try to place `'('` if we still have openings left.  
        * Compute the new balance `balance+1`.  
        * The prefix condition is satisfied if `need_positive` implies `new_balance > 0`.  
        * Additionally, we must be able to close all remaining opens later, i.e. `new_balance ≤ closes_left`.  
   * If `'('` cannot be placed, try `')'`.  
        * New balance `balance‑1` must stay non‑negative (a parentheses prefix can never be negative).  
        * The prefix condition must also hold (`need_positive` → `new_balance > 0`).  
   * If neither character can be placed, the construction is impossible → `-1`.  
5. After the loop, check that the final balance is zero; otherwise the sequence is not balanced.  
6. Output the built string.  
The algorithm is greedy: at each step we place the smallest possible character that keeps the future feasible. The whole procedure runs in `O(N)` time and `O(1)` extra memory.  
Edge cases: all `'('` in `P` (requires the balance to stay positive all the time) and all `')'` (balance may be zero after each step).  
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – Alternating Sign Subset Sum**  
You are given an array `B` of `N` distinct positive integers and a string `T` of length `N` consisting of characters `'+'` and `'-'`.  
You must permute the array and assign each element to a position `i` (1‑based).  
After the assignment, define the cumulative sum `C_i = Σ_{j=1..i} (sign_j * B_{p_j})` where `sign_j` is `+1` if `T[j]` is `'+'` and `-1` otherwise, and `p_j` is the index of the element placed at position `j`.  
For every `i` the cumulative sum must be **strictly positive** if `T[i]` is `'+'` and **strictly negative** if `T[i]` is `'-'`.  
Print any feasible ordering of the numbers together with the sign (the sign is already given by `T`). If no ordering exists, output `-1`.  
Constraints: `1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10^5`, `1 ≤ B_i ≤ 10^9`.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3.

import sys
import bisect

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    N = int(next(it))
    B = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(N)]
    T = next(it)

    # Sort numbers to be able to pick the smallest that satisfies a condition.
    B.sort()
    # We'll keep the remaining numbers in a list and use bisect to find needed values.
    remaining = B[:]

    cum = 0          # current cumulative sum
    answer = []      # list of chosen numbers in order

    for i, ch in enumerate(T):
        need_positive = (ch == '+')
        # We need after adding x * sign the cum to have the required sign.
        # sign = +1 for '+', -1 for '-'
        sign = 1 if need_positive else -1

        # If cum already has the required sign, we can add a very small number
        # on the side that does not change the sign.
        if (cum > 0 and need_positive) or (cum < 0 and not need_positive):
            # choose the smallest remaining number and put it on the side that keeps sign
            x = remaining[0]
            remaining.pop(0)
            cum += sign * x
            answer.append(x)
            continue

        # Otherwise we need to flip or strengthen the sign.
        # We need sign * x to make cum go to the required side.
        # That means we need x > |cum| (if sign is opposite to current sign)
        # or x > 0 (if cum == 0).
        target = abs(cum) + 1
        # Find the smallest number >= target
        idx = bisect.bisect_left(remaining, target)
        if idx == len(remaining):
            print(-1)
            return
        x = remaining[idx]
        remaining.pop(idx)
        cum += sign * x
        answer.append(x)

    # After processing all positions, the final cum can be anything.
    # Output the ordering.
    for x in answer:
        print(x, end=' ')
    print()

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
</code>
<planning>
1. **Input reading** – `N`, the array `B`, and the sign string `T`.  
2. **Sort** the array `B`. Keeping it sorted lets us quickly locate the smallest element that is large enough to satisfy a sign requirement (binary search).  
3. Initialise `cum = 0` (current cumulative sum) and an empty list `answer`.  
4. Iterate over each position `i` (character `ch` in `T`):  
   * Determine the required sign: `sign = +1` for `'+'`, `-1` for `'-'`.  
   * If the current `cum` already has the correct sign, we can safely place the smallest remaining number on the same side – it will not change the sign.  
   * Otherwise we must choose a number large enough to overcome the existing magnitude.  
        * The needed magnitude is `|cum| + 1`.  
        * Using `bisect_left` on the sorted list of remaining numbers we find the first element `≥ |cum|+1`.  
        * If such an element does not exist, the task is impossible → output `-1`.  
   * Remove the chosen number from `remaining`, update `cum += sign * chosen`, and append the number to `answer`.  
5. After the loop, any final `cum` is acceptable; print the numbers in the order they were chosen.  
Complexity: sorting `O(N log N)`, each step a binary search and deletion `O(log N)` (with a balanced‑tree structure; using a list gives `O(N)` deletions but still passes for the given limits).  
Edge cases: the first character, `cum = 0`; we need a positive number for `'+'` and a negative contribution for `'-'` (i.e., any number works because `|cum|+1 = 1`).  
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
# Algorithm Overview  

The original problem asks for an ordering of distinct weights together with a side (left/right) such that after each placement the prescribed side of the balance is heavier.  
Formally, let  

* `L` – total mass on the left pan,  
* `R` – total mass on the right pan,  
* `diff = L – R`.  

For step `i` we must choose a still‑unused weight `w` and a side `X ∈ {L,R}` so that  

```
   diff' = diff + w   if X = L
   diff' = diff – w   if X = R
```

and  

```
   diff' > 0   when S[i] = 'L'
   diff' < 0   when S[i] = 'R'
```

The key observation is that the **future feasibility depends only on the current difference `diff` and on the multiset of remaining weights**.  
Therefore a **greedy** choice can be made at each step:

* If the current `diff` already has the required sign, we can place the *smallest* remaining weight on the side that does **not** change the sign (or on any side that keeps the sign).  
* If the sign is wrong (or `diff = 0`), we must increase the magnitude of `diff` in the needed direction.  
  The smallest weight that can achieve this is the **minimal weight `w` such that**  

```
   |diff| < w          (for flipping the sign)   or
   |diff| + w > 0      (for strengthening the sign)
```

  In practice we need `w > |diff|` when the sign must be flipped, otherwise any positive `w` works.  
  Selecting the **minimum feasible weight** leaves larger weights for later steps, which is the essence of the greedy strategy.

To implement the greedy rule efficiently we store the remaining weights in a balanced search structure (e.g., C++ `multiset`, Java `TreeSet`, or in Python a sorted list together with `bisect`).  
At each step we:

1. Compute `need = 'L' ? 1 : -1`.  
2. If `diff * need > 0` (already correct sign) → pick the smallest weight (`pop_front`).  
3. Else → binary‑search the smallest weight `w` with `w > |diff|`.  
   * If such a weight does not exist → no solution.  
   * Place `w` on the side indicated by `need` and update `diff`.  

Because we always use the smallest weight that can satisfy the current requirement, we never waste a larger weight that might be needed later. This greedy choice can be proved optimal by an exchange argument: if an optimal solution uses a larger weight at some step while a smaller feasible weight exists, swapping them does not break any later condition and yields a solution that is at least as good.

**Complexities**

* Sorting the weights once: `O(N log N)`.  
* Each of the `N` steps performs a binary search and a deletion from the ordered container: `O(log N)` per step.  
* Total time: `O(N log N)`.  
* Memory: `O(N)` for the sorted container and the answer list.

**When to use this algorithm**

* The problem involves a sequence of decisions where each decision only depends on a *single numeric state* (here the difference of two sums).  
* The state can be updated monotonically (the absolute value of the difference never needs to be decreased when the sign is wrong).  
* The set of available resources (weights) is static and can be ordered.  
* A greedy “use the smallest resource that satisfies the current constraint” works because larger resources are never more useful for the current step than a smaller one.

**General Greedy Template for Similar Problems**

1. **Sort** the resources (weights, numbers, tasks…) once.  
2. Keep a **current state** that fully describes the effect of previous choices.  
3. For each requirement in the given order:  
   * If the current state already satisfies the requirement, make a *minimal* change (often the smallest remaining resource).  
   * Otherwise, **search** for the smallest resource that can bring the state into the required region.  
   * If none exists → impossible.  
   * Update the state and remove the used resource.  
4. Output the sequence of choices.

This pattern appears in many “balance”, “sign”, or “prefix‑constraint” problems, such as load balancing, constructing valid parentheses with extra constraints, or building alternating‑sign cumulative sums. The tutorial above explains the reasoning, the data structures, and the proof sketch for correctness.  

</algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – Load‑Balancing on Two Machines (equivalent to “balance‑weights” problem)**  

1. **Input reading**  
   - Read `N`.  
   - Read array `A[1…N]`. Keep each element together with its original value (the value itself is the identifier).  
   - Read string `S` of length `N`.  

2. **Pre‑processing**  
   - Sort the weights increasingly and store them in a structure that supports:  
     * `pop_smallest()` – remove and return the smallest remaining weight.  
     * `find_first_greater(x)` – return the smallest weight `> x` (or indicate none).  
   - In C++ use `multiset<long long>`; in Java use `TreeSet<Long>`; in Python keep a sorted list `weights` and use `bisect` together with a “deleted‑flag” BIT/segment‑tree, or simply use `bisect` on a `list` and `pop(idx)` (overall `O(N log N)` because each pop is `O(N)` → replace with `bisect` + `deque` + two‑pointer technique described below).  
   - Simpler for implementation: keep two pointers `l = 0` (smallest) and `r = N‑1` (largest) on the sorted array and a boolean array `used`. When we need the smallest unused weight we move `l` forward until an unused element is found; similarly for “first > x” we binary‑search on the sorted array to get index `pos`, then move `pos` forward until an unused element is found. Because each element becomes used once, total pointer movement is `O(N)`.  

3. **State variables**  
   - `loadA` – total processing time on machine A (left side).  
   - `loadB` – total processing time on machine B (right side).  
   - Both are 64‑bit (`long long`).  

4. **Greedy construction (step i = 0 … N‑1)**  

   For each character `c = S[i]` :

   a. **Desired sign**  
      - If `c == 'L'` we need after this step `loadA > loadB`.  
      - If `c == 'R'` we need `loadB > loadA`.  

   b. **Current difference** `diff = loadA - loadB`.  

   c. **Case 1 – current diff already has required sign** (`(c=='L' && diff>0)` or `(c=='R' && diff<0)`)  
      - We can place the *smallest* remaining weight on the side that does **not** change the sign (i.e. the side that is currently heavier).  
      - Implementation:  
        * If `diff>0` (A heavier) → put smallest weight on **B** (`loadB += w`).  
        * If `diff<0` (B heavier) → put smallest weight on **A** (`loadA += w`).  
        * If `diff==0` (equal) – treat as “sign not satisfied”, go to case 2.  

   d. **Case 2 – sign not satisfied (or diff==0)**  
      - We must increase the magnitude of the difference in the required direction.  
      - Required side = `need = (c=='L') ? A : B`.  
      - We need a weight `w` such that after adding it to `need` the new difference has the correct sign.  
        * If `c=='L'` we need `loadA + w > loadB` → `w > loadB - loadA = -diff`.  
        * If `c=='R'` we need `loadB + w > loadA` → `w > loadA - loadB = diff`.  
      - Hence we need the **smallest remaining weight `w` strictly greater than |diff|**.  

      - Use the data structure to obtain `w = find_first_greater(|diff|)`.  
        * If none exists → impossible → output `-1` and terminate.  

      - Place `w` on the required side (`need`). Update `loadA` or `loadB`.  

   e. **Record the move** – store pair `(w, side)` in answer list in the order of steps.  

5. **Termination**  
   - After processing all `N` characters, we have a feasible schedule.  
   - Output the stored moves, one per line: `<weight> <side>` (`side` is `A`/`B` or `L`/`R` as required by statement).  

6. **Correctness sketch**  
   - The state is fully described by the current difference `diff`.  
   - When the sign is already correct, adding any weight to the heavier side cannot break the sign; using the smallest possible weight preserves larger weights for later steps where a larger threshold may be needed.  
   - When the sign is wrong, any feasible schedule must add a weight larger than `|diff|` to the needed side (otherwise the inequality cannot be satisfied). Choosing the smallest such weight cannot hurt future feasibility because any larger weight could be substituted later for a step that needs an even larger threshold.  
   - By induction over steps, the greedy choice never eliminates a solution that exists, thus if the algorithm fails, no schedule exists.  

7. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Sorting: `O(N log N)`.  
   - Each step performs at most one binary search (`O(log N)`) and a constant‑time removal (pointer movement). Over all steps total pointer movement is `O(N)`.  
   - Overall time: `O(N log N)`.  
   - Memory: `O(N)` for the sorted array, a boolean `used` array, and the answer list.  

8. **Implementation notes**  
   - Use 64‑bit integers for loads and differences (`long long` in C++/Java, Python `int`).  
   - For output side letters, follow the problem statement: use `L` for machine A (left) and `R` for machine B (right).  
   - If at any step the algorithm prints `-1`, stop further processing.  

This plan yields a complete solution that meets the required constraints.
<root>
<explanation>The proposed plan correctly solves the problem. It maintains the current difference between the total masses on the left and right sides and, for each character of S, decides whether the required inequality is already satisfied. 

* If the inequality already holds, it adds the smallest remaining weight to the side that is already heavier, which cannot change the sign of the difference and preserves larger weights for later steps.  
* If the inequality does not hold (or the sides are equal), the algorithm must add a weight that flips the sign. The smallest weight strictly larger than the absolute current difference is the minimal weight that can achieve this, and using the minimal such weight is safe because any larger weight could be saved for a later step that may need an even larger threshold.  

The algorithm uses a sorted list (or multiset) to obtain the smallest remaining weight and the smallest weight greater than a given value in O(log N) time, leading to an overall O(N log N) solution, which meets the constraints (N ≤ 2·10⁵). The correctness argument follows by induction on the steps: the greedy choice never eliminates a feasible solution, so if the algorithm fails, no solution exists. Hence the plan is sound and implementable in Python 3.

</explanation>
<confidence>92</confidence>
</root>
**Goal**  
Choose an ordering of the given distinct weights and decide for each chosen weight whether it is placed on the left (L) or right (R) pan so that after the *i‑th* placement the side required by `S[i]` is strictly heavier.

**Notation**  

* `N` – number of weights.  
* `a[1…N]` – the weights (all different).  
* `S[1…N]` – string of ‘L’/‘R’.  
* `diff = left – right` – current signed difference after some placements.  
  * `diff > 0`  → left is heavier.  
  * `diff < 0`  → right is heavier.  
  * `diff = 0`  → both sides equal (never allowed after a step because the required side must be strictly heavier).

**Observations**

1. Only the current value of `diff` matters for the future; the exact distribution of previously placed weights is irrelevant.
2. When the required side at step `i` is `c = S[i]` we need  

   * `diff' > 0` if `c = 'L'`  
   * `diff' < 0` if `c = 'R'`

   where `diff'` is the value after placing the next weight.
3. To change the sign of `diff` (or to make it non‑zero when it is currently `0`) we must add a weight whose absolute value is **strictly larger** than `|diff|`.  
   If the sign is already correct we may add any weight that does **not** flip the sign (i.e. a weight that keeps the sign positive for ‘L’ or negative for ‘R’). The smallest such weight is always the best choice because larger weights are more valuable for later steps.

These facts lead to a greedy construction.

**Greedy strategy**

Maintain a multiset `M` of all still‑unused weights, sorted increasingly.

For each position `i = 1 … N` :

1. `need = +1` if `S[i] = 'L'`, otherwise `need = -1`.  
   The required sign after the move is `need`.
2. **Case A – current sign already correct** (`diff * need > 0`):  
   *We do not need to flip the sign, we only have to keep it.*  
   Take the **smallest** weight `w` from `M`.  
   * If `need = +1` (left must stay heavier) we must place `w` on the **left** side, because adding it to the right would decrease `diff` and could make it non‑positive.  
   * If `need = -1` we place `w` on the **right** side.  
   Update `diff += need * w` (i.e. `diff += w` for left, `diff -= w` for right) and erase `w` from `M`.
3. **Case B – current sign wrong or zero** (`diff * need ≤ 0`):  
   We must **flip** the sign (or make it non‑zero).  
   We need the smallest weight `w` such that `w > |diff|`.  
   * Find it by binary searching in the sorted container (`lower_bound(|diff|+1)`).  
   * If no such weight exists → impossible → output `-1`.  
   * Place `w` on the side indicated by `need` (left if `need = +1`, right otherwise).  
   * Update `diff += need * w` (now `diff` has the required sign) and erase `w` from `M`.
4. Record the pair `(w, side)` for the answer.

After processing all `N` steps the construction automatically satisfies every prefix condition, because each step enforces the required sign.

**Why the greedy choice is safe**

*If the sign is already correct* we never need to increase the magnitude; using the smallest remaining weight leaves larger weights for later steps where a larger magnitude may be required to flip the sign. Any larger weight placed now could be swapped with the smallest one without breaking any earlier condition, so the greedy choice never harms feasibility.

*If the sign must be flipped* we need a weight larger than `|diff|`. Choosing the smallest such weight is optimal for the same exchange argument: a larger weight would also flip the sign but would waste a bigger resource that could be useful later.

Thus the algorithm either finds a valid sequence or correctly reports impossibility.

**Data structures**

* `vector<long long> a` – store the weights.  
* `multiset<long long>` (or `std::set` with iterator) – ordered container supporting:
  * `begin()` → smallest element.  
  * `lower_bound(x)` → first element `≥ x`.  
  * `erase(iterator)` → O(log N).  

All operations are `O(log N)`.

**Algorithm outline**

```
read N
read array a[1..N]
read string S
M = multiset of all a
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for i = 0 .. N-1:
    need = (S[i] == 'L') ? +1 : -1

    if diff * need > 0:                     // sign already correct
        w = *M.begin()
        side = (need == +1) ? 'L' : 'R'
        diff += need * w
        erase w from M
        push (w, side) to answer
    else:                                   // need to flip / make non‑zero
        it = M.lower_bound( abs(diff) + 1 )
        if it == M.end():  output -1 and exit
        w = *it
        side = (need == +1) ? 'L' : 'R'
        diff += need * w
        erase it from M
        push (w, side) to answer

output each pair from answer on its own line
```

**Complexities**

*Sorting / building the multiset*: `O(N log N)` (insertion of N elements).  
*Loop*: `N` iterations, each does at most one `lower_bound` and one `erase` → `O(N log N)`.  
*Memory*: `O(N)` for the multiset and answer list.

**Edge cases**

* `N = 1` – works: we need a weight > 0, any weight placed on the required side.
* `diff = 0` at some step – treated as “sign wrong”, we must pick a weight `> 0` (always possible if any weight remains).
* All characters in `S` are the same – the algorithm will keep adding the smallest remaining weight to the same side; if at any point we run out of weights (impossible only when we need to flip but no weight larger than current `|diff|` exists) we output `-1`.
* Large values (`A_i` up to 1e9) – use 64‑bit (`long long`) for `diff`.

**Correctness proof sketch**

We prove by induction on the step index `i`.

*Induction hypothesis*: After processing the first `i` steps the algorithm has produced a sequence that satisfies the first `i` constraints and uses exactly the weights removed from `M`. Moreover, among all possible sequences that satisfy the first `i` constraints, the current `diff` has the smallest possible absolute value.

*Base*: `i = 0` – empty sequence, `diff = 0`, trivially minimal.

*Inductive step*: Consider step `i+1` with required sign `need`.

- If `diff * need > 0` (sign already correct), any feasible continuation must keep the sign after the next placement. Placing any weight on the opposite side would either flip the sign or reduce `|diff|` possibly to zero, violating the strictness. The smallest weight `w` placed on the correct side yields the smallest possible new `|diff|`. Any solution using a larger weight can be transformed by swapping that larger weight with `w` (which is still unused) without breaking earlier constraints, contradicting minimality. Hence the greedy choice preserves the induction hypothesis.

- If `diff * need ≤ 0`, any feasible continuation must flip the sign, which requires a weight `w > |diff|`. Choosing the smallest such `w` yields the smallest possible new `|diff|` after the flip. If a solution used a larger weight `w'`, swapping `w` (still unused) with `w'` would still satisfy the flip condition and would not affect earlier steps, again contradicting minimality. Therefore the greedy choice is optimal.

Thus after step `i+1` the algorithm still satisfies all constraints and keeps `|diff|` minimal, establishing the hypothesis for all `i`. At the end (`i = N`) all constraints are satisfied, so the produced sequence is valid. If at any step the required weight does not exist, no solution can satisfy the constraints (otherwise a weight larger than `|diff|` would exist), so output `-1` is correct.

**Implementation notes (C++/Java/Python)**

* Use `long long` (64‑bit) for `diff` and weights.  
* In Python, `bisect` on a sorted list can be used, but deletions are `O(N)`. Prefer `sortedcontainers` module if allowed, otherwise implement a balanced BST via `heapq` + two‑heap trick, but the simplest for CP is to store weights in a list, sort it, and maintain an index pointer for the smallest unused weight; for the “flip” case we need the smallest weight > `|diff|`, which can be found by binary search on the remaining suffix and then marking it used (e.g., using a Fenwick tree or segment tree). However, using `bisect` together with a `list` and `pop(idx)` is acceptable for `N ≤ 2·10⁵` if we use `bisect` on a `sortedlist` from `bisect` module and `del` (amortized O(N) worst‑case). In practice, many contestants use `bisect` + `list` and it passes; otherwise use `bisect` + `array` + `union‑find` to skip used positions.  
* For C++: `multiset<long long> ms;` and `auto it = ms.lower_bound(abs(diff)+1);`.  

**Final output**

Print each chosen weight followed by a space and the side character (`L` or `R`) on its own line, in the order they were placed. If at any moment the algorithm determines impossibility, print a single line containing `-1`.
<root>
<explanation>The proposed greedy algorithm is correct.  
It maintains the current difference `diff = left – right`. For each step it distinguishes two cases:

*If the current sign already matches the required side* (`diff·need > 0`), any feasible continuation must keep the sign. Adding the smallest remaining weight to the required side is optimal because it preserves the sign and leaves larger weights for later steps where a larger magnitude may be needed.

*If the sign is wrong or zero* (`diff·need ≤ 0`), the sign must be flipped. This is only possible with a weight strictly larger than `|diff|`. Choosing the smallest such weight is again optimal – a larger weight would also flip the sign but would waste a larger resource that could be useful later.  

The algorithm therefore either constructs a sequence satisfying all prefix conditions or correctly reports impossibility when no weight larger than `|diff|` exists at a “flip” step. All operations are performed with a multiset (or equivalent balanced BST), giving `O(N log N)` time and `O(N)` memory, which meets the constraints (`N ≤ 2·10⁵`).  

Thus the plan correctly solves the problem.

</explanation>
