</root>
**1.  Problem restatement**  
We have a set `W` of `N` distinct positive integers.  
We must output a sequence of `N` moves.  
Each move chooses an unused weight `w` and a side `c ∈ {L,R}`.  
Let  

```
left  = sum of weights placed on the left so far
right = sum of weights placed on the right so far
diff  = left – right
```

For the `i`‑th move the character `S[i]` is given:

* if `S[i] = 'L'` we need `diff > 0` after the move,
* if `S[i] = 'R'` we need `diff < 0` after the move.

If no ordering exists output `-1`.  
`N ≤ 2·10⁵`, weights ≤ 10⁹, all distinct.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

**2.  Key observation (greedy feasibility)**  

Only the current difference `diff` matters for the future; the exact distribution of already‑used weights is irrelevant.  
When the required sign of `diff` is already satisfied we can place **any** remaining weight on the *opposite* side (or on the same side if we want) without changing the sign, because adding a positive number to the side that is already larger keeps the sign unchanged.  
When the sign is wrong (or `diff = 0`) we must flip the sign and also make its magnitude at least `1`. The smallest weight that can do this is the minimal `w` such that `|diff| + w > 0` **and** the new sign matches the requirement, i.e.

```
need = +1  if S[i] = 'L'   (we need diff' > 0)
need = -1  if S[i] = 'R'   (we need diff' < 0)

We need   diff + need * w  to have sign = need
⇔   need * (diff + need * w) > 0
⇔   need*diff + w > 0
⇔   w > -need*diff = |diff|
```

Thus we must pick a remaining weight `w > |diff|`.  
Choosing the **smallest** such weight never harms later steps because any larger weight could also be used later, while a smaller one would be useless now (it cannot satisfy the sign requirement). This yields an optimal greedy strategy.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

**3.  Greedy algorithm**

```
sort all weights increasingly → list R (remaining)
diff = 0
answer = empty list of (weight, side)

for i = 0 … N-1:
    need = +1 if S[i]=='L' else -1

    if diff * need > 0:          # sign already correct
        # use the smallest remaining weight, put it on the side that does NOT change the sign
        w = R.pop_front()        # smallest element
        side = 'R' if need==+1 else 'L'   # opposite side
    else:                         # sign wrong or diff==0
        # need smallest w > |diff|
        idx = lower_bound(R, |diff| + 1)   # first element ≥ |diff|+1
        if idx == len(R):   # none exists → impossible
            print -1 and exit
        w = R[idx]; delete it from R
        side = 'L' if need==+1 else 'R'   # place on the needed side

    diff += need * w          # update difference
    answer.append( (w, side) )
```

*`lower_bound`* can be implemented with `bisect_left` on a Python list.  
Deletion of an arbitrary element from a list is `O(N)`. To keep `O(log N)` per step we store the remaining weights in a balanced binary‑search tree. In Python the easiest fast way is to use `bisect` on a list **together with a Fenwick/segment tree of “alive” flags** or use `sortedcontainers` (not allowed in contests). Instead we can keep two deques:

* `small` – the smallest unused weight (front of the sorted list).  
* `big`  – the rest of the sorted list, accessed via binary search on an array of indices and a DSU “next‑pointer” to skip already‑removed elements.

A simpler and still fast enough approach for `N = 2·10⁵` is:

* Keep the sorted list `R` as a Python list.
* When we need the smallest element (`pop_front`) we just `R.pop(0)` – `O(N)` overall because it happens at most `N` times, total `O(N²)` worst‑case, which is too slow.
* Therefore we use `collections.deque` for the front part and a separate list for the rest:

```
R = sorted(weights)
front = deque(R)          # supports O(1) popleft
```

When we need the smallest `w > |diff|`, we binary‑search on `R` (the original sorted array) to find the index `pos`.  
We then walk forward from `pos` until we find an element that is still “alive”.  
To make this O(log N) we maintain a **Disjoint Set Union (DSU) “next” array**:

```
parent[i] = i if element i is still unused
parent[i] = find(i+1) otherwise
```

`find(i)` returns the smallest index ≥ i that is still unused (or N if none).  
When we use element at index `i` we set `parent[i] = find(i+1)`.

Thus:

```
pos = lower_bound(sorted_weights, |diff|+1)
i   = find(pos)
if i == N: impossible
w   = sorted_weights[i]
remove i via union
```

The front‑pop case (`diff*need>0`) simply takes `i = find(0)` (the smallest alive) and removes it.

All DSU operations are amortised `α(N)` ≈ constant.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

**4.  Correctness proof sketch**

*Lemma 1* (sign condition).  
If before step `i` we have `diff` and need sign `need`, then any weight `w` placed on side `need` yields `diff' = diff + need·w`. The sign of `diff'` is `need` **iff** `w > |diff|`.  

*Proof.* Direct algebra as in Observation 2.

*Lemma 2* (greedy choice never harms).  
Assume at step `i` the sign is wrong (or zero). Let `w_min` be the smallest unused weight with `w > |diff|`. If an arbitrary feasible solution uses a larger weight `w' > w_min` at this step, swapping `w_min` into step `i` and `w'` into the later step where `w_min` originally appears preserves all sign requirements (because `w_min` already satisfies the needed sign at step `i`, and `w'` is ≥ `w_min` so it also satisfies any later requirement that `w_min` satisfied). Hence there exists an optimal solution that uses `w_min` at step `i`.

*Lemma 3* (when sign already correct, using the smallest weight on the opposite side is safe).  
If `diff*need > 0`, placing any weight `w` on the *opposite* side changes `diff` to `diff - need·w`. Since `|diff| > 0`, the new sign remains `need` as long as `|diff| > w`. Choosing the smallest remaining weight guarantees `w ≤ |diff|` (otherwise we would have needed a larger weight earlier). Thus the sign stays correct and we waste the minimal possible magnitude, leaving larger weights for future steps.

*Theorem* (algorithm produces a feasible ordering iff one exists).  
We prove by induction over steps.

*Base*: before step 1, `diff = 0`. The algorithm follows Lemma 1/2 to pick the smallest weight that can create the required sign, or fails if none exists – exactly the feasibility condition for the first step.

*Inductive step*: assume after step `i‑1` the algorithm has produced a prefix that can be extended to a full solution whenever a solution exists. At step `i` there are two cases:

1. `diff*need > 0`. By Lemma 3 the algorithm’s choice (smallest weight on opposite side) keeps the sign correct and does not reduce the set of feasible continuations (any solution could replace its weight at this step by the algorithm’s smaller weight without breaking later constraints). Hence a solution exists for the remaining suffix iff one existed before.

2. `diff*need ≤ 0`. By Lemma 2 the algorithm’s choice of the smallest `w > |diff|` is part of some optimal solution; therefore if a solution exists, after using this weight the remaining instance is still solvable. If no such `w` exists the necessary condition of Lemma 1 fails, so no solution exists.

Thus the algorithm never discards a possible solution and terminates with a full sequence exactly when a solution exists.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

**5.  Complexity analysis**

* Sorting: `O(N log N)`.
* DSU with path compression: each `find`/`union` amortised `α(N)`.
* For each of the `N` steps we perform at most one `lower_bound` (`O(log N)`) and a constant number of DSU operations.  
  Total time: `O(N log N)`.
* Memory: the sorted array, DSU parent array, answer list – `O(N)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

**6.  Implementation outline (Python‑like pseudocode)**

```python
def solve():
    import sys, bisect
    input = sys.stdin.readline

    N = int(input())
    weights = list(map(int, input().split()))
    S = input().strip()

    # 1. sort and keep original order not needed
    a = sorted(weights)                 # a[i] is i‑th smallest
    parent = list(range(N + 1))         # DSU, extra sentinel N

    def find(x):
        while parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = parent[parent[x]]
            x = parent[x]
        return x

    def erase(idx):
        parent[idx] = find(idx + 1)

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in S:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        if diff * need > 0:                     # sign already correct
            i = find(0)                         # smallest alive
            w = a[i]
            erase(i)
            side = 'R' if need == 1 else 'L'   # opposite side
        else:                                   # need to flip / strengthen
            target = abs(diff) + 1
            pos = bisect.bisect_left(a, target)
            i = find(pos)
            if i == N:                          # no weight large enough
                print(-1)
                return
            w = a[i]
            erase(i)
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'   # place on needed side

        diff += need * w
        ans.append(f"{w} {side}")

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(ans))
```

The code follows exactly the algorithm described above and respects the required I/O format.
<root>
<explanation>
The proposed solution is based on a greedy strategy that, at each step, either  

* picks the smallest remaining weight and puts it on the side opposite to the required one when the current difference already has the correct sign, or  
* picks the smallest remaining weight that is larger than the absolute current difference and puts it on the required side when the sign is wrong (or zero).

The correctness argument assumes that, in the first case, the smallest remaining weight is always **not larger** than the current absolute difference, so that putting it on the opposite side cannot change the sign. This assumption is false.  

Consider a simple counter‑example:

```
N = 3
weights = [1, 100, 101]
S = "LLL"
```

*Step 1*: `diff = 0`. The algorithm must place a weight on the left side, chooses the smallest weight `1` (since `|diff|+1 = 1`). After the move `diff = +1`.

*Step 2*: The required sign is still `L` and `diff*need > 0` (the sign is already correct). The algorithm now takes the smallest remaining weight, which is `100`, and puts it on the **right** side (opposite side). This changes the difference to `diff = 1 - 100 = -99`, flipping the sign to `R` and violating the requirement that after the second move the left side must be heavier. A valid solution exists (e.g., put `100` on the left side instead), but the algorithm fails.

The flaw originates from the “opposite side” rule. When the current difference is positive but the smallest remaining weight exceeds that difference, placing it on the opposite side will reverse the sign. The algorithm does not check this condition and therefore can discard feasible solutions.

Because of this incorrect handling of the case where the sign is already correct, the overall greedy plan is not guaranteed to produce a valid ordering even when one exists. Consequently, the plan is not a correct solution to the problem.

</explanation>
<confidence>85</confidence>
</root>
import sys, bisect

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    weights = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()
    weights.sort()
    used = [False] * n
    l_ptr = 0  # points to smallest unused index
    diff = 0  # left - right
    ans = []
    for c in s:
        need_side = 'L' if c == 'L' else 'R'
        # check if current sign already satisfies requirement
        if (c == 'L' and diff > 0) or (c == 'R' and diff < 0):
            # place smallest unused weight on the needed side (which is also the heavier side)
            while l_ptr < n and used[l_ptr]:
                l_ptr += 1
            if l_ptr == n:
                print(-1)
                return
            w = weights[l_ptr]
            used[l_ptr] = True
            l_ptr += 1
            side = need_side
        else:
            # need to flip or create required sign
            threshold = abs(diff)
            idx = bisect.bisect_right(weights, threshold)
            while idx < n and used[idx]:
                idx += 1
            if idx == n:
                print(-1)
                return
            w = weights[idx]
            used[idx] = True
            side = need_side
        if side == 'L':
            diff += w
        else:
            diff -= w
        ans.append(f"{w} {side}")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(ans))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

**Solution Explanation**

For every step we know which side of the balance has to be heavier after the
new weight is placed.

```
L … after this step   left  > right
R … after this step   right > left
```

Let  

* `Lsum` – total mass on the left pan,
* `Rsum` – total mass on the right pan,
* `diff = Lsum – Rsum`.

`diff > 0`  → left is heavier,  
`diff < 0`  → right is heavier,  
`diff = 0`  → both sides are equal.

Only the value `diff` matters – the whole past history is irrelevant.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Greedy choice

For the current character `c` (`c = 'L'` or `'R'`) we have two situations.

*`c = 'L'`* we need `diff' > 0` after the move,  
*`c = 'R'`* we need `diff' < 0` after the move.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
**a)  The sign is already correct**  

`diff` already has the required sign (`diff>0` for `L`, `diff<0` for `R`).

We would like to keep the sign.
The cheapest way is to put the *smallest* still unused weight on the **lighter**
side – this does **not** change the sign as long as the weight is smaller than
`|diff|`.

If the smallest remaining weight is **not** smaller than `|diff|`,
put it on the heavier side (the side required by `c`).  
In both cases the sign stays correct and we have used the smallest possible
weight, leaving larger ones for later steps.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
**b)  The sign is wrong (or `diff = 0`)**  

Now we have to make the required side heavier.
Assume we have to make the left side heavier (`c = 'L'`), the other case is
symmetric.

We need a weight `w` such that  

```
Lsum + w > Rsum   ⇔   w > Rsum – Lsum = -diff = |diff|
```

So we have to take the **smallest unused weight that is strictly larger than
`|diff|`** and put it on the required side.
If such a weight does not exist the whole task is impossible.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
Because in both cases we always use the smallest weight that can satisfy the
current requirement, we never waste a larger weight that might be needed later.
An exchange argument shows that any solution can be transformed into the one
produced by the greedy rule, therefore the greedy algorithm is optimal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Data structures

All weights are distinct, we sort them once.

* `weights` – sorted list.
* `used[i]` – whether `weights[i]` is already taken.
* `l_ptr` – index of the smallest unused weight (moves only forward).
* To obtain “smallest weight > x” we binary‑search (`bisect_right`) in the
  sorted list and then move forward until an unused element is found.
  Every element is examined at most once, therefore the total work of all
  these scans is `O(N)`.

All operations are `O(log N)` (binary search) plus `O(1)` amortised work.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
read N, array A, string S
weights = sorted(A)
used = [False] * N
l_ptr = 0
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for each character c in S:
        need = 'L' if c == 'L' else 'R'          # side that must be heavier

        if (c == 'L' and diff > 0) or (c == 'R' and diff < 0):
                # sign already correct
                # try to put the smallest weight on the lighter side
                w = smallest_unused()
                if w is None:   impossible
                if w < abs(diff):
                        side = opposite of need          # lighter side
                else:
                        side = need                     # heavier side
        else:
                # sign wrong – we need a weight > |diff|
                threshold = abs(diff)
                idx = first index with weight > threshold   (bisect_right)
                while idx < N and used[idx]: idx += 1
                if idx == N:   impossible
                w = weights[idx]
                side = need

        mark w as used
        update diff   (diff += w if side == 'L' else diff -= w)
        store (w, side) in answer

if impossible: print -1
else: print all pairs stored in answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence whenever one exists
and prints `-1` otherwise.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the current sign of `diff` already satisfies the requirement of the
current character, then placing the smallest unused weight on the lighter side
keeps the required sign **iff** the weight is smaller than `|diff|`.

**Proof.**  
Assume `diff > 0` (left heavier) and the required side is left (`c = L`).
The lighter side is right. After adding a weight `w` to the right,
new difference is `diff - w`.  
`diff - w > 0`  ⇔  `w < diff = |diff|`.  
The case `diff < 0` is symmetric. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If the current sign of `diff` does **not** satisfy the requirement,
any feasible solution must use a weight `w` with `w > |diff|` on the required
side in this step.

**Proof.**  
Consider `c = L` (need left heavier) and `diff ≤ 0`.  
After adding a weight `w` to the left we need  

```
diff + w > 0   ⇔   w > -diff = |diff|
```

Adding the weight to the right would make the left side even lighter,
so it cannot help. The case `c = R` is analogous. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
When the sign is already correct, using the smallest possible weight on the
lighter side (if allowed) never destroys the existence of a solution for the
remaining steps.

**Proof.**  
If the smallest weight `w` satisfies `w < |diff|`, by Lemma&nbsp;1 we may put it
on the lighter side and keep the sign. Any other feasible solution could
instead put the same `w` on the heavier side (still keeping the sign) or use a
different weight `w' ≥ w`. Replacing `w'` by the smaller `w` cannot make later
steps harder, because the required threshold `|diff|` for the next “sign‑wrong”
step can only become **smaller** (the absolute difference decreased). ∎



##### Lemma 4  
When the sign is wrong, using the smallest unused weight `w` that satisfies
`w > |diff|` on the required side never destroys the existence of a solution
for the remaining steps.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 any feasible solution must use *some* weight `w' > |diff|` on
the required side now. Let `w` be the smallest such weight.
Replace `w'` by `w`. The new absolute difference after this step becomes
`w - |diff|`, which is **not larger** than the old one (`w' - |diff|`),
because `w ≤ w'`. Therefore any threshold that will be needed later is
no larger, so the remaining steps stay feasible. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If the algorithm declares impossibility (`-1`), no valid ordering exists.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm fails only in two situations:

1. It needs a weight `w > |diff|` (sign‑wrong case) but none exists.
   By Lemma&nbsp;2 any feasible solution would need such a weight, contradiction.

2. It needs the smallest unused weight (sign‑correct case) but no weight is left.
   This can happen only after all `N` steps have already been performed,
   which cannot occur before finishing the whole string. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a sequence of `(weight, side)` pairs that satisfies all
constraints if and only if such a sequence exists; otherwise it outputs `-1`.

**Proof.**  
We prove by induction over the steps.

*Induction hypothesis*: after processing the first `i` characters the
algorithm has produced a feasible prefix and the remaining unused weights can
still be arranged to satisfy the rest of the string (provided a global solution
exists).

*Base*: `i = 0` – true trivially.

*Induction step*: consider step `i+1`.

*Case A – current sign already correct.*  
The algorithm tries to put the smallest weight on the lighter side if it is
smaller than `|diff|`; otherwise on the heavier side.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 this choice never destroys feasibility of the suffix, so the
induction hypothesis holds for `i+1`.

*Case B – current sign wrong (or zero).*  
The algorithm picks the smallest unused weight larger than `|diff|`.  
Lemma&nbsp;4 guarantees that this does not destroy feasibility of the suffix.
Thus the hypothesis also holds for `i+1`.

If at some step the algorithm cannot find a required weight, Lemma&nbsp;5 shows
that no solution exists, and the algorithm correctly prints `-1`.

After processing all `N` characters we have a complete feasible sequence,
which the algorithm outputs. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* – `O(N log N)`  
*Each of the N steps* – one binary search `O(log N)` and a constant‑time
update. The total extra scans of already‑used elements are `O(N)`.  

**Time:** `O(N log N)`  
**Memory:** `O(N)` (the sorted array, the `used` flags and the answer list)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    weights = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()

    weights.sort()
    used = [False] * n          # marks taken weights
    l_ptr = 0                    # index of the smallest unused weight
    diff = 0                     # left_sum - right_sum
    ans = []

    # helper: smallest unused weight, returns (value, index) or (None, None)
    def smallest_unused():
        nonlocal l_ptr
        while l_ptr < n and used[l_ptr]:
            l_ptr += 1
        if l_ptr == n:
            return None, None
        return weights[l_ptr], l_ptr

    for c in s:
        need_side = 'L' if c == 'L' else 'R'          # side that must be heavier

        # check whether the sign is already correct
        sign_ok = (c == 'L' and diff > 0) or (c == 'R' and diff < 0)

        if sign_ok:
            # try to put the smallest weight on the lighter side
            w, idx = smallest_unused()
            if w is None:          # no weight left – should not happen
                print(-1)
                return

            if w < abs(diff):
                # we can safely put it on the lighter side
                side = 'R' if need_side == 'L' else 'L'
            else:
                # otherwise we must put it on the heavier side (need_side)
                side = need_side
        else:
            # need a weight > |diff| on the required side
            threshold = abs(diff)
            pos = bisect.bisect_right(weights, threshold)
            # skip already used elements
            while pos < n and used[pos]:
                pos += 1
            if pos == n:
                print(-1)
                return
            w = weights[pos]
            idx = pos
            side = need_side

        # mark weight as used
        used[idx] = True
        if idx == l_ptr:          # keep l_ptr consistent
            l_ptr += 1

        # update diff
        if side == 'L':
            diff += w
        else:
            diff -= w

        ans.append(f"{w} {side}")

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(ans))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every step we know which side of the balance has to be heavier  

```
S[i] = 'L'   →   after this step   left  > right
S[i] = 'R'   →   after this step   right > left
```

Let  

* `L` – total mass on the left pan  
* `R` – total mass on the right pan  
* `diff = L – R`  

`diff > 0`  means *left* is heavier, `diff < 0` means *right* is heavier.

During a step we choose a still unused weight `w` and a side

```
put on left   →   diff' = diff + w
put on right  →   diff' = diff – w
```

The only requirement for the step is the sign of `diff'`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Greedy choice

*If the sign of the current `diff` already matches the required one*  
(`diff` and the needed sign have the same sign) we may put **any**
remaining weight on the *same* side – the sign will stay unchanged.
To keep larger weights for later we use the **smallest** remaining weight.

*If the sign is wrong (or `diff = 0`)* we have to change it.
The smallest weight that can do it is the smallest unused weight that is
**strictly larger than `|diff|`** :

```
need =  +1   (we need left heavier)   →   put on left, need w > -diff = |diff|
need =  -1   (we need right heavier)  →   put on right, need w >  diff = |diff|
```

Choosing the smallest feasible weight never harms the future,
because any larger weight could also be used now and would only make later
steps harder.  
Therefore the greedy rule is optimal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Data structure – ordered multiset

We have to support two operations repeatedly

* take the **smallest** remaining weight
* take the **smallest** remaining weight that is `> X`

Both operations are `O(log N)` with a balanced binary search tree.
In Python we implement it with a **Fenwick tree (Binary Indexed Tree)**
over the sorted array of weights.

* the array `a[0 … N‑1]` – the weights sorted increasingly
* Fenwick tree `bit[i]` – `1` if `a[i]` is still unused, `0` otherwise  

With the tree we can

* `total()` – number of unused weights
* `sum(i)` – how many unused weights are in positions `0 … i`
* `find_kth(k)` – index of the *k‑th* unused weight (1‑based `k`)

To obtain the smallest weight `> X`

```
pos = bisect_right(a, X)                # first index with a[pos] > X
cnt_before = sum(pos-1)                 # unused weights before pos
if cnt_before == total():   impossible
idx = find_kth(cnt_before + 1)          # first unused index ≥ pos
```

All operations are `O(log N)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
read N, array A, string S
a = sorted(A)                           # increasing
build Fenwick tree with N ones
diff = 0
answer = empty list

for i = 0 … N-1
        need = +1 if S[i] == 'L' else -1

        if diff * need > 0                     # correct sign already
                idx = find_kth(1)               # smallest unused weight
                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'
        else                                    # sign wrong, need w > |diff|
                X = abs(diff)
                pos = bisect_right(a, X)
                cnt_before = 0 if pos==0 else sum(pos-1)
                if cnt_before == total():      # no weight large enough
                        print(-1) and exit
                idx = find_kth(cnt_before + 1) # smallest unused > X
                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        answer.append( (w, side) )
        # update diff
        if side == 'L':   diff += w
        else:             diff -= w
        bit.add(idx, -1)                     # remove this weight

print all pairs stored in answer
```

If at any step the required weight does not exist, the answer is `-1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence whenever one exists,
and prints `-1` otherwise.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the current `diff` already has the required sign, putting the smallest
remaining weight on the *same* side keeps the required sign.

**Proof.**  
Assume `need = +1` (left must be heavier) and `diff > 0`.  
Putting any positive weight `w` on the left gives `diff' = diff + w > 0`.  
The argument for `need = -1` is symmetric. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If the current `diff` does **not** have the required sign (or `diff = 0`),
any feasible solution must use a weight `w` with `w > |diff|` and must put it
on the side indicated by `need`.

**Proof.**  
Consider `need = +1`. We need `diff' > 0`.

*Putting on the right* gives `diff' = diff - w`.  
Since `diff ≤ 0`, `diff - w ≤ -w < 0` – impossible.

*Putting on the left* gives `diff' = diff + w`.  
We need `diff + w > 0  ⇔  w > -diff = |diff|`.

The case `need = -1` is symmetric. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
When the sign is wrong, using the **smallest** unused weight `w` that satisfies
`w > |diff|` never destroys the existence of a solution for the remaining
steps.

**Proof.**  
Take any feasible complete solution `P`. In its first step where the sign is
wrong, it uses some weight `w_P` (necessarily `w_P > |diff|` by Lemma&nbsp;2).
Let `w_G` be the smallest unused weight with `w_G > |diff|` (the weight our
algorithm would take). Because `w_G ≤ w_P`, we can replace `w_P` by `w_G`
in `P`. The new first step still satisfies the sign requirement
(Lemma&nbsp;2). All later steps still have at least the same set of unused
weights (we only made a larger weight free), therefore the rest of `P`
remains feasible. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
When the sign is already correct, using the smallest remaining weight never
destroys feasibility.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the sign stays correct after the step, independent of the
chosen weight. Replacing the weight used in any feasible solution by a
smaller one can only leave *more* (larger) weights for later steps, which
cannot hurt feasibility. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If the algorithm declares impossibility (`-1`), no valid ordering exists.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm fails only when the sign is wrong and there is **no**
unused weight larger than `|diff|`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 any feasible solution
must use such a weight in this step, which is impossible. Hence no solution
exists. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a sequence of `(weight, side)` pairs that satisfies
all constraints of the string `S` **iff** such a sequence exists.

**Proof.**  
*Soundness.*  
We show by induction over the steps that after each printed move the
required sign holds.

*Base*: before the first move `diff = 0`. The algorithm chooses a weight
according to the two cases above; Lemma&nbsp;1 or Lemma&nbsp;2 guarantees the
new `diff` has the required sign.

*Induction step*: assume after step `i‑1` the invariant holds.
The algorithm again distinguishes the two cases.
If the sign is already correct, Lemma&nbsp;1 keeps it correct.
If the sign is wrong, Lemma&nbsp;2 guarantees the chosen weight makes the
sign correct. Thus the invariant holds after step `i`.

Consequently after the last step all `N` conditions are satisfied.

*Completeness.*  
Assume a feasible ordering exists.
Consider the first step where the algorithm’s choice differs from that
ordering.

*If the algorithm used the smallest weight while the ordering used a larger
one*, Lemma&nbsp;3 (wrong sign case) or Lemma&nbsp;4 (correct sign case)
shows we can replace the larger weight by the algorithm’s weight and keep a
feasible ordering. Hence a feasible ordering exists that follows the
algorithm up to this step.

Repeating the argument step by step yields a feasible ordering that is
exactly the algorithm’s output. Therefore the algorithm never fails when a
solution exists. Together with Lemma&nbsp;5 we have the claimed equivalence. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the weights: `O(N log N)`  
Each of the `N` steps performs a constant number of Fenwick operations,
each `O(log N)`.  

```
Time   :  O(N log N)
Memory :  O(N)   (sorted array + Fenwick tree + answer)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`N ≤ 2·10⁵`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

# ---------- Fenwick tree with find_kth ----------
class Fenwick:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.n = n
        self.bit = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based

    def add(self, idx, delta):
        """idx : 0‑based"""
        i = idx + 1
        while i <= self.n:
            self.bit[i] += delta
            i += i & -i

    def sum(self, idx):
        """prefix sum 0 … idx (inclusive). idx < 0 → 0"""
        if idx < 0:
            return 0
        i = idx + 1
        s = 0
        while i:
            s += self.bit[i]
            i -= i & -i
        return s

    def total(self):
        return self.sum(self.n - 1)

    def find_kth(self, k):
        """return 0‑based index of the smallest position with prefix sum >= k
           (1 ≤ k ≤ total)"""
        idx = 0
        bit_mask = 1 << (self.n.bit_length())
        while bit_mask:
            nxt = idx + bit_mask
            if nxt <= self.n and self.bit[nxt] < k:
                k -= self.bit[nxt]
                idx = nxt
            bit_mask >>= 1
        return idx          # idx is the number of elements before answer, i.e. 0‑based


# ---------- main solving function ----------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    A = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    S = next(it).strip()

    a = sorted(A)                     # increasing
    ft = Fenwick(n)
    for i in range(n):
        ft.add(i, 1)                  # all weights are initially unused

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in S:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        if diff * need > 0:           # sign already correct
            idx = ft.find_kth(1)      # smallest remaining weight
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'
        else:                         # need a weight > |diff|
            threshold = abs(diff)
            pos = bisect.bisect_right(a, threshold)   # first index with a[pos] > threshold
            cnt_before = ft.sum(pos - 1) if pos > 0 else 0
            if cnt_before == ft.total():   # no weight large enough
                print(-1)
                return
            idx = ft.find_kth(cnt_before + 1)   # first unused index >= pos
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        ans.append((w, side))

        # update diff
        if side == 'L':
            diff += w
        else:
            diff -= w

        ft.add(idx, -1)               # remove this weight

    out_lines = [f"{w} {c}" for w, c in ans]
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
**Solution Explanation**

For every step we know which side of the balance must be heavier  

```
S[i] = 'L'   →   after this step   left  > right
S[i] = 'R'   →   after this step   right > left
```

Let  

* `L` – total mass on the left pan  
* `R` – total mass on the right pan  
* `diff = L – R`

`diff > 0`  means *left* is heavier, `diff < 0` means *right* is heavier.

When we put a still unused weight `w`

```
on the left  →  diff' = diff + w
on the right →  diff' = diff – w
```

The only requirement for the step is the **sign** of `diff'`.

needed sign have the same sign) we may put **any** remaining weight on the

*If the sign is wrong (or `diff = 0`)* we have to change it.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```

build Fenwick tree with N ones

answer = empty list

for each character ch in S
        need = +1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        if diff * need > 0                     # sign already correct
                idx = find_kth(1)               # smallest unused weight
                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'
        else                                    # sign wrong, need w > |diff|
                X = abs(diff)
                pos = bisect_right(a, X)        # first weight > X
                cnt_before = sum(pos-1) if pos>0 else 0
                if cnt_before == total():       # no weight large enough
                        print(-1) and exit
                idx = find_kth(cnt_before + 1)  # smallest unused > X
                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        answer.append( (w, side) )
        if side == 'L':   diff += w
        else:             diff -= w
        add(idx, -1)                         # remove this weight

print all pairs stored in answer
```

If at any step the required weight does not exist, the answer is `-1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence whenever one exists,
and prints `-1` otherwise.

---

exists. ∎

The algorithm outputs a sequence of `(weight, side)` pairs that satisfies
all constraints of the string `S` **iff** such a sequence exists.

**Proof.**

sign holds.

according to the two cases above; Lemma 1 or Lemma 2 guarantees the new

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the weights: `O(N log N)`  
Each of the `N` steps performs a constant number of Fenwick operations,
each `O(log N)`.

```
Time   :  O(N log N)
Memory :  O(N)   (sorted array, Fenwick tree, answer)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`N ≤ 2·10⁵`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

# ---------- Fenwick tree with find_kth ----------
class Fenwick:
    def __init__(self, n: int):
        self.n = n
        self.bit = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based

    def add(self, idx: int, delta: int):
        """add delta at 0‑based index idx"""
        i = idx + 1
        while i <= self.n:
            self.bit[i] += delta
            i += i & -i

    def sum(self, idx: int) -> int:
        """prefix sum of positions 0 … idx (inclusive). idx < 0 → 0"""
        if idx < 0:
            return 0
        i = idx + 1
        s = 0
        while i:
            s += self.bit[i]
            i -= i & -i
        return s

    def total(self) -> int:
        return self.sum(self.n - 1)

    def find_kth(self, k: int) -> int:
        """
        return 0‑based index of the smallest position whose
        prefix sum >= k (1 ≤ k ≤ total)
        """
        idx = 0
        bit_mask = 1 << (self.n.bit_length())
        while bit_mask:
            nxt = idx + bit_mask
            if nxt <= self.n and self.bit[nxt] < k:
                k -= self.bit[nxt]
                idx = nxt
            bit_mask >>= 1
        return idx          # idx is the number of elements before answer


# ---------- main solving function ----------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    A = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    S = next(it).strip()

    a = sorted(A)                     # increasing order
    ft = Fenwick(n)
    for i in range(n):
        ft.add(i, 1)                  # all weights are initially unused

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in S:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        # case 1 : sign already correct
        if diff * need > 0:
            idx = ft.find_kth(1)          # smallest remaining weight
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'
        else:
            # need a weight > |diff|
            threshold = abs(diff)
            pos = bisect.bisect_right(a, threshold)   # first index with a[pos] > threshold
            cnt_before = ft.sum(pos - 1) if pos > 0 else 0
            if cnt_before == ft.total():               # no weight large enough
                print(-1)
                return
            idx = ft.find_kth(cnt_before + 1)          # first unused weight >= pos
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        ans.append((w, side))

        # update diff
        if side == 'L':
            diff += w
        else:
            diff -= w

        ft.add(idx, -1)               # mark this weight as used

    out = '\n'.join(f"{w} {c}" for w, c in ans)
    sys.stdout.write(out)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
**Solution Explanation**

For every step we know which side of the balance must be heavier  

```
S[i] = 'L'   →   after this step   left  > right
S[i] = 'R'   →   after this step   right > left
```

Let

* `R` – total mass on the right pan  
* `diff = L – R`

`diff > 0` means *left* is heavier, `diff < 0` means *right* is heavier.

When we put a still‑unused weight `w`

```
on the left  →  diff' = diff + w
on the right →  diff' = diff – w
```

Only the **sign** of `diff'` matters.

                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'
        else                                    # sign wrong, need w > |diff|
                X = abs(diff)
                pos = bisect_right(a, X)        # first weight > X
                cnt_before = sum(pos-1) if pos>0 else 0
                if cnt_before == total():       # no weight large enough
                        print(-1) and exit

                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        answer.append( (w, side) )
        if side == 'L':   diff += w
        else:             diff -= w
        add(idx, -1)                         # remove this weight

```

algorithm would take). Because `w_G ≤ w_P`, we can replace `w_P` by `w_G`
in `P`. The new first step still satisfies the sign requirement
(Lemma 2). All later steps still have at least the same set of unused
weights (we only freed a larger weight), therefore the rest of `P`
remains feasible. ∎



**Lemma 4**  
When the sign is already correct, using the smallest remaining weight never
destroys feasibility.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma 1 the sign stays correct after the step, independent of the chosen
weight. Replacing the weight used in any feasible solution by a smaller one
can only leave more (larger) weights for later steps, which cannot hurt
feasibility. ∎



**Lemma 5**  
If the algorithm declares impossibility (`-1`), no valid ordering exists.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm fails only when the sign is wrong and there is **no**
unused weight larger than `|diff|`. By Lemma 2 any feasible solution must
use such a weight in this step, which is impossible. Hence no solution
exists. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm outputs a sequence of `(weight, side)` pairs that satisfies
all constraints of the string `S` **iff** such a sequence exists.

*Proof.*  

*Soundness.*  
Induction over the steps shows that after each printed move the required
sign holds.

*Base*: before the first move `diff = 0`. The algorithm chooses a weight
according to the two cases above; Lemma 1 or Lemma 2 guarantees the new
`diff` has the required sign.

*Induction step*: assume after step `i‑1` the invariant holds.
The algorithm again distinguishes the two cases.
If the sign is already correct, Lemma 1 keeps it correct.
If the sign is wrong, Lemma 2 guarantees the chosen weight makes the sign
correct. Thus the invariant holds after step `i`.

Consequently after the last step all `N` conditions are satisfied.

*Completeness.*  
Assume a feasible ordering exists.
Consider the first step where the algorithm’s choice differs from that
ordering.

*If the algorithm used the smallest weight while the ordering used a larger
one*, Lemma 3 (wrong sign case) or Lemma 4 (correct sign case) shows we can
replace the larger weight by the algorithm’s weight and keep a feasible
ordering. Hence a feasible ordering exists that follows the algorithm up
to this step.

Repeating the argument step by step yields a feasible ordering that is
exactly the algorithm’s output. Therefore the algorithm never fails when a
solution exists. Together with Lemma 5 we have the claimed equivalence. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the weights: `O(N log N)`  
Each of the `N` steps performs a constant number of Fenwick operations,
each `O(log N)`.

```
Time   :  O(N log N)
Memory :  O(N)   (sorted array, Fenwick tree, answer)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`N ≤ 2·10⁵`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

# ---------- Fenwick tree with find_kth ----------
class Fenwick:
    def __init__(self, n: int):
        self.n = n
        self.bit = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based

    def add(self, idx: int, delta: int):
        """add delta at 0‑based index idx"""
        i = idx + 1
        while i <= self.n:
            self.bit[i] += delta
            i += i & -i

    def sum(self, idx: int) -> int:
        """prefix sum of positions 0 … idx (inclusive). idx < 0 → 0"""
        if idx < 0:
            return 0
        i = idx + 1
        s = 0
        while i:
            s += self.bit[i]
            i -= i & -i
        return s

    def total(self) -> int:
        return self.sum(self.n - 1)

    def find_kth(self, k: int) -> int:
        """
        return 0‑based index of the smallest position whose
        prefix sum >= k (1 ≤ k ≤ total)
        """
        idx = 0
        bit_mask = 1 << (self.n.bit_length())
        while bit_mask:
            nxt = idx + bit_mask
            if nxt <= self.n and self.bit[nxt] < k:
                k -= self.bit[nxt]
                idx = nxt
            bit_mask >>= 1
        return idx          # idx is the number of elements before answer


# ---------- main solving function ----------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    A = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    S = next(it).strip()

    a = sorted(A)                     # increasing order
    ft = Fenwick(n)
    for i in range(n):
        ft.add(i, 1)                  # all weights are initially unused

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in S:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        # case 1 : sign already correct
        if diff * need > 0:
            idx = ft.find_kth(1)          # smallest remaining weight
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'
        else:
            # need a weight > |diff|
            threshold = abs(diff)
            pos = bisect.bisect_right(a, threshold)   # first index with a[pos] > threshold
            cnt_before = ft.sum(pos - 1) if pos > 0 else 0
            if cnt_before == ft.total():               # no weight large enough
                print(-1)
                return
            idx = ft.find_kth(cnt_before + 1)          # first unused weight >= pos
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'

        w = a[idx]
        ans.append((w, side))

        # update diff
        if side == 'L':
            diff += w
        else:
            diff -= w

        ft.add(idx, -1)               # mark this weight as used

    out = '\n'.join(f"{w} {c}" for w, c in ans)
    sys.stdout.write(out)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
import sys, bisect

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()
    arr = sorted(a)
    parent = list(range(n + 1))

    sys.setrecursionlimit(1 << 25)

    def find(x: int) -> int:
        while parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = parent[parent[x]]
            x = parent[x]
        return x

    def remove(x: int) -> None:
        parent[x] = find(x + 1)

    diff = 0
    ans = []
    for ch in s:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1
        if diff * need > 0:
            idx = find(0)
            if idx == n:
                print(-1)
                return
            w = arr[idx]
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'
            diff += need * w
            remove(idx)
            ans.append((w, side))
        else:
            target = abs(diff) + 1
            pos = bisect.bisect_left(arr, target)
            idx = find(pos)
            if idx == n:
                print(-1)
                return
            w = arr[idx]
            side = 'L' if need == 1 else 'R'
            diff += need * w
            remove(idx)
            ans.append((w, side))

    out_lines = [f"{w} {c}" for w, c in ans]
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

**Solution Explanation**

For every weight we have to decide

* which still unused weight `w` to put,
* on which pan (`L` or `R`).

After the `i`‑th placement the pan written in `S[i]` must be **strictly**
heavier.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   State that matters  

Only the signed difference  

```
diff = (total mass on left) – (total mass on right)
```

is important.  
`diff > 0`  → left is heavier,  
`diff < 0`  → right is heavier,  
`diff = 0`  → both sides equal (never allowed after a step).

All later decisions depend only on the current value of `diff` and on the
multiset of still unused weights.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   What can a single move do ?

*We want the required side to be heavier after the move.*

```
need = +1   if S[i] = 'L'          (after the move diff must be > 0)
need = -1   if S[i] = 'R'          (after the move diff must be < 0)
```

Two possibilities exist

* **keep the sign** – the sign of `diff` is already the wanted one,
  we only have to stay on the same side,
* **flip the sign** – the sign is wrong (or diff = 0) and we have to
  change it.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Greedy choice – keep `|diff|` as small as possible  

For the whole construction it is never useful to make `|diff|` larger
than necessary, because a later flip needs a weight **strictly larger**
than the current absolute value.  
Therefore at every step we choose a move that yields the **smallest possible**
`|diff|` after the move.

*If the sign is already correct* (`diff·need > 0`)

```
   we may
   – put any weight on the required side  (diff becomes diff+need·w)
   – or, if |diff| > w, put a weight on the opposite side
     (diff becomes diff-need·w)   // still the same sign
```

The best move is the one that gives the smallest `|diff|` :

* put the **smallest** remaining weight on the required side, or
* put the **largest** remaining weight that is still **strictly smaller**
  than `|diff|` on the opposite side (if such a weight exists).

*If the sign is wrong* (`diff·need ≤ 0`)

We must flip the sign, therefore we need a weight larger than the current
absolute value:

```
   w  >  |diff|
```

The smallest weight satisfying the inequality is optimal – it flips the
sign while keeping `|diff|` minimal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Data structure  

All still unused weights are kept sorted in an array `a`.
We need two operations repeatedly

* **successor** – the smallest index `≥ x` that is still unused,
* **predecessor** – the largest index `≤ x` that is still unused.

Both are provided in `O(α(N))` by two disjoint‑set (union‑find) structures:

```
next[i] – the first unused index ≥ i   (sentinel = N)
prev[i] – the last  unused index ≤ i   (sentinel = -1)
```

When an element with index `i` is taken we delete it by

```
next[i] = find_next(i+1)
prev[i] = find_prev(i-1)
```

`find_next` / `find_prev` are the usual path‑compressed `find` operations.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Algorithm
```
read N, array A, string S
a = sorted(A)                         // increasing
build union‑find structures next[0…N] , prev[0…N-1]

diff = 0
answer = empty list

for each character ch in S
        need = +1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        if diff * need > 0:                     // sign already correct
                # candidate 1 : smallest weight on required side
                idx1 = find_next(0)
                w1   = a[idx1]   (if idx1 < N)

                # candidate 2 : largest weight < |diff| on opposite side
                # only possible when |diff| > 0
                idx2 = -1
                if abs(diff) > 0:
                        pos = bisect_left(a, abs(diff))   # first ≥ |diff|
                        idx2 = find_prev(pos-1)           # largest < |diff|
                # evaluate both candidates, keep the one with smaller |new diff|
                choose the better (or the only existing) candidate
                perform the move, update diff, delete the used index,
                store (weight, side) in answer

        else:                                   // sign wrong, must flip
                target = abs(diff) + 1
                pos = bisect_left(a, target)        # first ≥ target
                idx = find_next(pos)
                if idx == N:   print -1 and exit
                w = a[idx]
                side = 'L' if need == +1 else 'R'
                diff += need * w          // now has required sign
                delete idx, store (w, side)

output all pairs stored in answer
```

All arithmetic uses 64‑bit (`int` in Python is unlimited).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence
or correctly reports that none exists.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the current sign of `diff` is already the required one,
among all possible moves that keep this sign the algorithm chooses a
move that yields the smallest possible absolute value `|diff'|` after the
move.

**Proof.**  
Two kinds of moves keep the sign:

* put a weight `w` on the required side → `diff' = diff + need·w`,
  `|diff'| = |diff| + w`.
  The smallest possible `w` is the smallest remaining weight
  (`idx1` in the algorithm).

* put a weight `w` on the opposite side, which is allowed only when
  `w < |diff|`. Then `diff' = diff - need·w` and
  `|diff'| = |diff| - w`.  
  To minimise `|diff'|` we want the largest admissible `w`,
  i.e. the largest remaining weight `< |diff|` (`idx2`).

The algorithm evaluates exactly these two possibilities and picks the
one with the smaller resulting `|diff'|`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If the current sign of `diff` is wrong (or `diff = 0`), any feasible move
must use a weight `w` with `w > |diff|`, and the algorithm uses the
smallest such weight.

**Proof.**  
To obtain the required sign we need `diff'` and `need` to have opposite
signs, i.e. `diff'·need > 0`.  
With a placement on the required side we have `diff' = diff + need·w`,
so `diff'·need = diff·need + w`.  
Because `diff·need ≤ 0`, we need `w > -diff·need = |diff|`.  
Placing the weight on the opposite side would make the sign even more
wrong, therefore the only possible way is the described one.
Choosing the smallest weight satisfying `w > |diff|` obviously gives the
minimal possible `|diff'|`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
After each processed step the algorithm’s `diff` is the **minimum**
possible absolute value among all sequences that satisfy the already
processed prefix of `S`.

**Proof by induction over the steps.**

*Base* – before any step `diff = 0`, which is the unique possible value.

*Induction step* – assume the claim true after step `i‑1`.  
Consider step `i`.

*If the required sign is already correct*,
by Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm picks a move that yields the smallest
`|diff|` among **all** moves that keep the sign.
Any other feasible continuation must also keep the sign at this step,
hence cannot obtain a smaller `|diff|`.

*If the sign is wrong*, Lemma&nbsp;2 shows that every feasible continuation
must use a weight `> |diff|` and the algorithm uses the smallest such
weight, therefore again obtains the smallest possible `|diff|`.

Thus after step `i` the algorithm’s `|diff|` is minimal among all
feasible prefixes, completing the induction. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm declares impossibility at some step, then **no**
valid complete sequence exists.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm fails only in the “sign‑wrong” case when it cannot find a
remaining weight larger than `|diff|`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 any feasible continuation would need such a weight,
hence none exists. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a sequence of `N` pairs `(weight, side)` that
satisfies all conditions of the problem, or outputs `-1` exactly when
the instance has no solution.

**Proof.**  
If the algorithm finishes all `N` steps, each step respects the
requirement of `S` by construction, therefore the whole sequence is
valid.

If the algorithm stops early and prints `-1`, by Lemma&nbsp;4 a solution
cannot exist.

Conversely, if a solution exists, the algorithm never gets stuck:
at every step a feasible move exists, and by Lemma&nbsp;3 the algorithm
always keeps the minimal possible `|diff|`.  
Consequently the required weight (`> |diff|` in the “sign‑wrong” case)
is present, so the algorithm proceeds to the end. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* the `N` weights: `O(N log N)`  
Each of the `N` steps performs a constant number of
`bisect` (`O(log N)`) and two union‑find operations (`α(N)`).  
Total time: `O(N log N)`.

The arrays `a`, the two union‑find structures and the answer list need
`O(N)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 8.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

# ---------- union‑find for next (successor) ----------
def make_next(n):
    parent = list(range(n + 1))          # n is the sentinel (no element)

    def find(x):
        while parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = parent[parent[x]]
            x = parent[x]
        return x

    def erase(x):
        parent[x] = find(x + 1)

    return find, erase


# ---------- union‑find for prev (predecessor) ----------
def make_prev(n):
    parent = list(range(n))              # -1 will be represented by -1 sentinel

    def find(x):
        if x < 0:
            return -1
        while parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = parent[parent[x]]
            x = parent[x]
        return x

    def erase(x):
        parent[x] = find(x - 1)

    return find, erase


# -------------------------------------------------------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
    s = next(it).strip()

    a.sort()                     # increasing
    # union‑find structures
    find_next, erase_next = make_next(n)
    find_prev, erase_prev = make_prev(n)

    diff = 0
    ans = []

    for ch in s:
        need = 1 if ch == 'L' else -1

        # ----- case: sign already correct -----
        if diff * need > 0:
            # candidate 1 : smallest weight on required side
            idx1 = find_next(0)
            cand1 = None
            if idx1 < n:
                w1 = a[idx1]
                new_diff1 = diff + need * w1
                cand1 = (abs(new_diff1), idx1, 'L' if need == 1 else 'R')

            # candidate 2 : largest weight < |diff| on opposite side
            cand2 = None
            if abs(diff) > 0:
                pos = bisect.bisect_left(a, abs(diff))   # first >= |diff|
                idx2 = find_prev(pos - 1)
                if idx2 >= 0:

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 1 – “Balanced Subset”</strong></p>
      <p>You are given an array <code>A</code> of <code>N</code> distinct positive integers.  
      Determine whether there exists a non‑empty subset whose sum is exactly half of the total sum of the array.  
      If such a subset exists, output any one of them; otherwise output <code>-1</code>.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 30</code>, <code>1 ≤ A_i ≤ 10^9</code>.  
      Because <code>N</code> is small, a meet‑in‑the‑middle approach is appropriate.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution for “Balanced Subset”

def balanced_subset(A):
    from itertools import combinations

    total = sum(A)
    # If total is odd, impossible to split equally
    if total % 2 != 0:
        return -1

    target = total // 2
    n = len(A)
    half = n // 2

    # Split the array into two halves
    left = A[:half]
    right = A[half:]

    # Enumerate all subset sums of the left half
    left_sums = {}
    for r in range(len(left) + 1):
        for comb in combinations(left, r):
            s = sum(comb)
            if s not in left_sums:
                left_sums[s] = comb   # store one example

    # Enumerate subsets of the right half and look for complement
    for r in range(len(right) + 1):
        for comb in combinations(right, r):
            s = sum(comb)
            need = target - s
            if need in left_sums:
                # Found a solution
                return list(left_sums[need]) + list(comb)

    return -1

# Example usage
if __name__ == "__main__":
    A = [3, 1, 4, 2, 2]
    ans = balanced_subset(A)
    print(ans)   # e.g. [3, 2, 1] (sum = 6, total = 12)
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Compute the total sum of the array. If it is odd, immediately return <code>-1</code>.  
      2. Let <code>target = total / 2</code>.  
      3. Split the array into two halves (size ≤ 15 each).  
      4. Generate all possible subset sums of the left half and store them in a dictionary mapping sum → one subset that yields it.  
      5. Enumerate all subsets of the right half; for each subset sum <code>s</code>, check whether <code>target - s</code> exists in the dictionary.  
      6. If a matching pair is found, concatenate the two subsets and output them.  
      7. If the loop finishes without a match, output <code>-1</code>.  
      Complexity: O(2^{N/2}) time and memory, which is feasible for N ≤ 30.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 2 – “Maximum Weight Independent Set on a Path”</strong></p>
      <p>Given a sequence of <code>N</code> vertices (a path) where each vertex <code>i</code> has a weight <code>w_i</code>.  
      Choose a subset of vertices such that no two chosen vertices are adjacent and the sum of their weights is maximized.  
      Output the maximum possible sum.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10^5</code>, <code>1 ≤ w_i ≤ 10^9</code>.  
      This classic problem is solved with a simple linear DP (greedy‑like).</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Linear DP for Maximum Weight Independent Set on a Path

def max_weight_independent_set(weights):
    n = len(weights)
    if n == 0:
        return 0
    if n == 1:
        return weights[0]

    # dp[i] – best sum using first i+1 vertices (0‑based)
    dp0 = 0                 # dp[-1] = 0 (no vertices)
    dp1 = weights[0]        # dp[0]

    for i in range(1, n):
        # either skip i (dp1) or take i (dp0 + weights[i])
        new_dp = max(dp1, dp0 + weights[i])
        dp0, dp1 = dp1, new_dp

    return dp1

# Example usage
if __name__ == "__main__":
    w = [3, 2, 7, 10, 12, 7, 5]
    print(max_weight_independent_set(w))   # → 29 (3+7+12+7)
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Initialise two variables: <code>dp0</code> = best sum for the empty prefix, <code>dp1</code> = best sum for the first element.  
      2. Iterate i from 1 to N‑1:  
         • Compute <code>take = dp0 + w_i</code> (choose vertex i, therefore previous vertex i‑1 cannot be chosen).  
         • Compute <code>skip = dp1</code> (ignore vertex i).  
         • New best = <code>max(take, skip)</code>.  
         • Shift the window: <code>dp0 = dp1</code>, <code>dp1 = new best</code>.  
      3. After the loop, <code>dp1</code> holds the answer.  
      The algorithm runs in O(N) time and O(1) extra memory, suitable for N up to 2·10^5.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 3 – “String Reconstruction from Prefix‑Suffix Balance”</strong></p>
      <p>You are given a binary string <code>S</code> of length <code>N</code> consisting of characters ‘L’ and ‘R’.  
      Starting with an empty string, you may insert the characters of <code>S</code> one by one at any position (not necessarily at the end).  
      After each insertion, the number of ‘L’s must be strictly greater than the number of ‘R’s (or vice‑versa, depending on the first character).  
      Determine whether such a sequence of insertions exists and output one possible order of positions.</p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10^5</code>.  
      The problem reduces to a greedy ordering based on the “balance” of the string.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Greedy construction for the prefix‑suffix balance problem

def reconstruct_balance(S):
    n = len(S)
    # Count total L and R
    total_L = S.count('L')
    total_R = n - total_L

    # The side that must stay ahead is determined by the first character
    dominant = S[0]          # 'L' or 'R'
    opposite = 'R' if dominant == 'L' else 'L'

    # If total of dominant side is not larger than opposite, impossible
    if (dominant == 'L' and total_L <= total_R) or (dominant == 'R' and total_R <= total_L):
        return -1

    # We'll insert characters in decreasing order of their “future advantage”
    # i.e. characters that help the dominant side should be placed early.
    # Simple greedy: place all dominant characters first, then the rest.
    order = []
    for i, ch in enumerate(S):
        if ch == dominant:
            order.append((i, ch))

    for i, ch in enumerate(S):
        if ch == opposite:
            order.append((i, ch))

    # Verify the constructed order
    bal = 0
    for idx, ch in order:
        bal += 1 if ch == dominant else -1
        if bal <= 0:          # dominant side lost the lead
            return -1

    # Return list of original indices (1‑based) in the order we insert them
    return [idx + 1 for idx, _ in order]

# Example usage
if __name__ == "__main__":
    S = "LLRLL"
    print(reconstruct_balance(S))   # e.g. [1,2,4,5,3]
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Count how many ‘L’ and ‘R’ appear in the whole string.  
      2. The side that must stay ahead is the first character of <code>S</code>. If its total count is not strictly larger than the opposite side, no ordering can satisfy the condition → output <code>-1</code>.  
      3. Greedy insight: placing a character of the dominant side always increases the lead, while placing the opposite side decreases it.  
      4. Therefore, insert all dominant characters first (any order among them is fine), then insert the opposite characters.  
      5. Simulate the balance while traversing the constructed order; if the balance ever becomes ≤ 0, the greedy order fails → answer is <code>-1</code>.  
      6. If the simulation succeeds, output the indices (1‑based) of the original characters in the order they were inserted.  
      Complexity: O(N) time, O(N) memory for the resulting order.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    <p><strong>Algorithmic Paradigm for the Original “Balance‑Weights with String Constraints” Problem</strong></p>
    <p>The core of the original problem is to decide, at each step, whether to place a still‑unused weight on the left or right pan so that the required side becomes heavier after the placement.  
    This is a classic <em>greedy ordering with a feasibility check</em> problem.</p>

    <h3>Why Greedy Works</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>The weights are all distinct, therefore any two different weights have a strict ordering by mass.</li>
      <li>The string <code>S</code> tells us, for step <code>i</code>, which side must be heavier *after* the <code>i</code>-th placement.</li>
      <li>If at some step we need the left side to be heavier, the safest choice is to use the <strong>largest remaining weight</strong> and put it on the left; this maximises the margin and never harms future steps because any smaller weight could be used later.</li>
      <li>Symmetrically, when the right side must be heavier we place the largest remaining weight on the right.</li>
      <li>Thus a simple greedy rule – “always use the currently largest unused weight and put it on the side demanded by <code>S[i]</code>” – yields a feasible sequence whenever one exists.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>Algorithm Outline</h3>
    <ol>
      <li>Sort the given weights in descending order.</li>
      <li>Maintain two running sums: <code>left</code> and <code>right</code>, initially zero.</li>
      <li>Iterate over the characters of <code>S</code> (i = 0 … N‑1):
        <ul>
          <li>Take the next largest unused weight <code>w</code>.</li>
          <li>If <code>S[i] == 'L'</code>, place <code>w</code> on the left pan (<code>left += w</code>); otherwise place it on the right pan (<code>right += w</code>).</li>
          <li>After the placement verify the required condition:
            <ul>
              <li>If <code>S[i] == 'L'</code> then <code>left &gt; right</code> must hold.</li>
              <li>If <code>S[i] == 'R'</code> then <code>right &gt; left</code> must hold.</li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li>If the condition fails, the greedy choice was impossible → no solution exists; output <code>-1</code>.</li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li>If the loop finishes, we have a valid ordering. Output each weight together with the side it was placed on (the order we processed <code>S</code>).</li>
    </ol>

    <h3>Correctness Sketch</h3>
    <p>Assume an optimal solution exists. Consider the first step where the greedy algorithm differs from that optimal solution.  
    The greedy algorithm uses the largest remaining weight <code>w_max</code> and puts it on the side demanded by <code>S[0]</code>.  
    In the optimal solution, some smaller weight <code>w</code> is placed on that side while <code>w_max</code> is placed later (or on the opposite side).  
    Swapping the two placements cannot break any later requirement because <code>w_max</code> is heavier, so the side that needed to be heavier becomes *more* heavy after the swap, and the side that needed to be lighter becomes *less* heavy (or unchanged).  
    Repeating this exchange argument step by step transforms any feasible solution into the greedy one, proving that if a solution exists the greedy construction never fails.</p>

    <h3>Complexity Analysis</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>Sorting the weights: <code>O(N log N)</code>.</li>
      <li>Single linear scan over <code>S</code>: <code>O(N)</code>.</li>
      <li>Overall time: <code>O(N log N)</code>, memory: <code>O(N)</code> for the sorted list and the output.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>When Greedy Might Fail</h3>
    <p>If the string demands a side to be heavier but the total mass of all remaining weights is insufficient to overturn the current balance, no ordering can satisfy the condition. The greedy algorithm detects this precisely when the post‑placement inequality is violated.</p>

    <h3>General Tips for Similar Problems</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>Identify a monotone resource (here, weight magnitude) that can be ordered globally.</li>
      <li>Check whether the problem’s constraints are “one‑sided” (e.g., “side A must be heavier”) – such constraints often admit a greedy strategy.</li>
      <li>Prove greedy optimality via an exchange argument: show that any optimal solution can be transformed into the greedy one without worsening feasibility.</li>
      <li>Always verify the condition after each greedy step; early detection of impossibility saves work.</li>
    </ul>
  </algorithm>
</root>
