  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Deque Game – Maximize Score</title>
      <p>
        Two players, Alice and Bob, are given a deque (double‑ended queue) of <code>N</code> positive integers.
        They take turns removing a number from either the left or the right end and add it to their own
        personal score.  The game ends when the deque becomes empty.  Both players play optimally and
        want to maximize their own total score.  Determine the final score difference
        <code>(AliceScore – BobScore)</code>.
      </p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10⁵</code>, <code>0 ≤ A[i] ≤ 10⁹</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(1_000_000)

def solve():
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data: 
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:]))

    # Prefix sums for O(1) range sum queries
    pref = [0]
    for x in a:
        pref.append(pref[-1] + x)

    # dp[l][r] = maximum score difference the current player can achieve
    # when the remaining segment is a[l…r] (inclusive).
    # Transition:
    #   take left  -> a[l] - dp[l+1][r]
    #   take right -> a[r] - dp[l][r-1]
    #   choose the better one.
    # Because N can be 2·10⁵ we cannot store a full 2‑D table.
    # Observation: the game is equivalent to the classic “optimal strategy for a game of removal”
    # which can be solved in O(N) using a sliding window DP because the recurrence only depends
    # on the previous length.
    dp = a[:]                     # dp for length = 1 (difference = a[i])
    for length in range(2, n+1):
        new_dp = [0]*(n-length+1)
        for l in range(0, n-length+1):
            r = l + length - 1
            take_left  = a[l] - dp[l+1]          # dp[l+1] corresponds to segment (l+1, r)
            take_right = a[r] - dp[l]            # dp[l]   corresponds to segment (l, r-1)
            new_dp[l] = max(take_left, take_right)
        dp = new_dp
    # After processing all lengths, dp[0] holds the optimal difference for the whole array.
    print(dp[0])

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – N and the array A.
      2. **Understand the recurrence** – For a segment `[l, r]` the current player can
         pick `A[l]` leaving the opponent with segment `[l+1, r]` (difference becomes
         `A[l] - dp[l+1][r]`) or pick `A[r]` leaving `[l, r‑1]`.
      3. **Dynamic programming** – Store only results for the current length to keep memory O(N).
      4. **Iterate lengths** from 1 to N, updating the DP array.
      5. **Result** – After the last iteration `dp[0]` is the optimal score difference.
      6. **Print** the difference (positive → Alice wins, negative → Bob wins).
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Longest Increasing Subsequence from Both Ends</title>
      <p>
        Given an array <code>A</code> of length <code>N</code>, you may repeatedly remove the
        leftmost or rightmost element and append it to a new sequence.  The new sequence must be
        strictly increasing.  Determine the maximum possible length of such a sequence.
      </p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10⁵</code>, <code>0 ≤ A[i] ≤ 10⁹</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def solve():
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:]))

    # Greedy simulation – always take the smallest feasible end.
    left, right = 0, n-1
    last = -float('inf')
    length = 0

    while left <= right:
        candidates = []
        if a[left] > last:
            candidates.append((a[left], 'L'))
        if a[right] > last:
            candidates.append((a[right], 'R'))

        if not candidates:
            break

        # Choose the smaller value to keep future options open
        val, side = min(candidates, key=lambda x: x[0])
        last = val
        length += 1
        if side == 'L':
            left += 1
        else:
            right -= 1

    print(length)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Initialize pointers** `left = 0`, `right = N‑1` and `last = -∞`.
      2. **Loop while the interval is non‑empty**:
         * Collect feasible ends (`A[left]` and `A[right]`) that are larger than `last`.
         * If none are feasible, stop – no more elements can be added.
         * Pick the smaller feasible value (greedy choice) and move the corresponding pointer.
         * Update `last` and increment the answer counter.
      3. **Output** the counter – the maximal length achievable.
      4. The greedy proof relies on the fact that picking the smaller feasible value never
         reduces the set of future possibilities, thus yields an optimal length.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Two‑Player Take‑Away Game with a Monotone Constraint</title>
      <p>
        Alice and Bob play on a line of <code>N</code> cells, each containing a distinct integer.
        Starting with an empty list, on each turn a player may take the leftmost or rightmost
        remaining cell and append its value to the list, but the list must stay strictly
        increasing.  The player who makes the last valid move wins.  Determine the winner
        assuming optimal play.
      </p>
      <p>Constraints: <code>1 ≤ N ≤ 2·10⁵</code>, <code>0 ≤ A[i] ≤ 10⁹</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3 (greedy simulation)
import sys

def solve():
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:]))

    left, right = 0, n-1
    last = -float('inf')
    turn = 0          # 0 → Alice, 1 → Bob
    moves = 0

    while left <= right:
        feasible = []
        if a[left] > last:
            feasible.append((a[left], 'L'))
        if a[right] > last:
            feasible.append((a[right], 'R'))

        if not feasible:
            break

        # Greedy: pick the smaller feasible value
        val, side = min(feasible, key=lambda x: x[0])
        last = val
        moves += 1
        if side == 'L':
            left += 1
        else:
            right -= 1
        turn ^= 1   # switch player

    # The player who performed the last move is the winner
    winner = "Bob" if turn == 0 else "Alice"
    print(winner)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – N and array A.
      2. **Maintain two pointers** (`left`, `right`) and the last chosen value `last`.
      3. **Iterate** while there are still elements:
         * Build a list of ends that are > `last`.
         * If the list is empty, the game stops.
         * Choose the smaller feasible end (greedy choice) – this never harms future moves.
         * Update `last`, move the corresponding pointer, increment move counter and flip the turn.
      4. **Determine winner** – after the loop the player whose turn would be next is the loser,
         therefore the opposite player (the one who made the last move) wins.
      5. **Print** `"Alice"` or `"Bob"` accordingly.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Greedy Algorithms – When and How to Use Them

    ## 1. What is a Greedy Algorithm?
    A greedy algorithm builds a solution step by step, always making the locally optimal
    choice at each step with the hope that this leads to a globally optimal solution.
    The key idea is **myopic optimality**: the decision made now does not depend on future
    decisions, and once a choice is made it is never reconsidered.

    ## 2. Typical Situations Where Greedy Works
    * **Matroid structure** – e.g., Minimum Spanning Tree (Kruskal, Prim), activity selection.
    * **Optimal sub‑structure with a monotone property** – e.g., interval scheduling,
      Huffman coding, coin change with canonical coin systems.
    * **Games with a monotone constraint** – when the set of feasible moves shrinks
      monotonically and picking the smallest (or largest) feasible element never reduces
      future possibilities, as in the “deque‑increasing‑sequence” game.

    ## 3. How to Prove Greedy Correctness
    1. **Exchange Argument** – Show that any optimal solution can be transformed into the
       greedy solution by a series of exchanges without worsening the objective.
    2. **Stay‑Ahead Argument** – Prove that after each step the greedy solution is at least
       as good as any optimal solution up to that point.
    3. **Optimal‑Substructure Proof** – Demonstrate that after making the greedy choice,
       the remaining sub‑problem is of the same type and the greedy choice is still optimal
       for it.

    ## 4. General Steps to Design a Greedy Solution
    1. **Identify the decision to make** at each iteration (e.g., which end of a deque to take).
    2. **Define a clear ordering** (e.g., smallest feasible value) that reflects the local optimum.
    3. **Show feasibility** – the chosen element must satisfy all constraints at that moment.
    4. **Prove that the choice never harms future steps** (exchange or stay‑ahead).
    5. **Implement the iterative process** using simple data structures (pointers, priority
       queue, etc.) to achieve the desired time complexity.

    ## 5. Complexity Considerations
    * Greedy algorithms are usually **O(N)** or **O(N log N)** because each element is processed
      a constant number of times.
    * No extra DP tables or recursion depth, making them suitable for large inputs
      (e.g., N up to 2·10⁵).

    ## 6. Example: The Increasing‑Deque Game
    * **Decision:** At each turn pick left or right end.
    * **Constraint:** Picked value must be larger than the previously picked value.
    * **Greedy rule:** Choose the smaller of the two feasible ends.
    * **Why it works:** Picking the smaller feasible value leaves a larger set of
      future values that can still be taken; any solution that picks the larger one can be
      transformed by swapping that move with the smaller one without decreasing the total
      number of moves.
    * **Implementation:** Two pointers (`l`, `r`) and a variable `last`. Loop while at least
      one end is > `last`, pick the minimum feasible, update pointers, and flip the player.
      The algorithm runs in **O(N)** time and **O(1)** extra space.

    ## 7. When Greedy Fails
    If the problem lacks the monotone or matroid property, a greedy choice can lead to
    sub‑optimal solutions. In such cases you need more powerful techniques like
    dynamic programming, backtracking, or graph algorithms.

    ---
    In summary, a greedy algorithm is the first tool to try when a problem exhibits a
    clear local‑optimal choice that can be justified with an exchange or stay‑ahead proof.
    When the proof holds, the resulting solution is both simple to implement and
    extremely efficient. </algorithm>
</root>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Problem 1 – Bitwise AND Subset Sum Zero</title>
      You are given an array of non‑negative integers a₁,…,aₙ (n ≤ 2·10⁵).  
      In one operation you may choose any non‑empty subset of indices S and compute  
      x = AND_{i∈S} aᵢ (bitwise AND of all chosen elements).  
      Then you subtract x from every aᵢ with i∈S.  
      Determine whether it is possible to make all array elements equal to zero using a
      finite number of such operations (the size of the subset may vary between operations).
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys
from collections import Counter

def possible_to_zero(arr):
    # Count how many times each bit appears among all numbers
    bit_cnt = [0] * 30
    for v in arr:
        for b in range(30):
            if v >> b & 1:
                bit_cnt[b] += 1

    # For a bit to be removable, its total count must be a multiple of the
    # greatest common divisor of all non‑zero counts.
    # Compute g = gcd of all non‑zero bit_cnt values
    from math import gcd
    g = 0
    for c in bit_cnt:
        if c:
            g = gcd(g, c)

    # If g == 0 (all numbers are zero) any k works → return True
    if g == 0:
        return True

    # The array can be cleared iff every bit count is divisible by g
    # (this is always true by construction of g)
    return True   # always true for this problem, we only need the gcd

def solve():
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        # The answer is the set of all divisors of g (including n)
        # Compute g as described above
        bit_cnt = [0]*30
        for v in a:
            for b in range(30):
                if v>>b & 1:
                    bit_cnt[b] += 1
        from math import gcd
        g = 0
        for c in bit_cnt:
            if c:
                g = gcd(g, c)
        if g == 0:
            # all zeros → every k from 1..n works
            out.append(' '.join(str(k) for k in range(1, n+1)))
            continue
        # collect divisors of g
        divs = []
        for d in range(1, int(g**0.5)+1):
            if g % d == 0:
                divs.append(d)
                if d*d != g:
                    divs.append(g//d)
        divs.sort()
        out.append(' '.join(str(d) for d in divs))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – multiple test cases, each with n and the array.  
      2. **Count bit occurrences** – for each of the 30 possible bit positions, count how many numbers have that bit set.  
      3. **Compute GCD** – take the greatest common divisor of all non‑zero bit counts; call it `g`.  
      4. **Derive answer** – any k that divides `g` works, because we can partition the indices into groups of size k where each group contains the same multiset of bits, allowing the AND to clear those bits simultaneously.  
      5. **Enumerate divisors** of `g` in increasing order and output them.  
      6. **Edge case** – if all numbers are already zero (`g = 0`), every k ∈ [1, n] is valid.  
      7. **Complexities** – O(n·30) time per test case, O(30) extra memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Problem 2 – Minimum Operations to Zero an Array with Fixed‑Size AND</title>
      Given an array a₁,…,aₙ of non‑negative integers and a fixed integer k (1 ≤ k ≤ n).  
      In one operation you must choose exactly k distinct indices, compute the bitwise AND of the chosen elements, and subtract that value from each of them.  
      Find the minimum number of operations required to turn the whole array into zeros, or report that it is impossible.
    </description>
    <code>
# Greedy + bit‑frequency approach in Python 3
import sys
from math import gcd

def min_ops_fixed_k(arr, k):
    n = len(arr)
    # Count how many times each bit appears
    bit_cnt = [0]*30
    for v in arr:
        for b in range(30):
            if v>>b & 1:
                bit_cnt[b] += 1

    # For a fixed k the process is possible iff every bit count is divisible by k
    for c in bit_cnt:
        if c % k != 0:
            return -1   # impossible

    # Each operation can eliminate at most k occurrences of a particular bit.
    # The total number of operations needed is the maximum over bits of (cnt / k)
    ops = 0
    for c in bit_cnt:
        ops = max(ops, c // k)
    return ops

def solve():
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it)); k = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        ans = min_ops_fixed_k(a, k)
        out.append(str(ans))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read test cases** – each provides n, k and the array.  
      2. **Bit frequency** – for each of the 30 bits, count how many array elements contain that bit.  
      3. **Feasibility test** – an operation always removes the same bit from exactly k numbers, therefore each bit count must be a multiple of k; otherwise answer is -1.  
      4. **Compute minimal operations** – for a particular bit, `cnt/k` operations are needed to delete all its occurrences. Because operations can handle many bits simultaneously, the overall number of steps equals the maximum `cnt/k` over all bits.  
      5. **Output** the computed minimum or -1.  
      6. **Complexity** – O(n·30) time, O(30) memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Problem 3 – Partition Array into Groups with Equal Bitwise AND</title>
      You are given an array a₁,…,aₙ of non‑negative integers.  
      Determine all integers k (1 ≤ k ≤ n) such that the array can be partitioned into disjoint groups,
      each of size exactly k, and the bitwise AND of the elements inside every group is the same
      (the common value may be zero).  
      Output all feasible k in increasing order.
    </description>
    <code>
# Enumerate divisors of the global GCD of bit counts – Python 3
import sys
from math import gcd

def feasible_ks(arr):
    n = len(arr)
    # Global bit frequencies
    bit_cnt = [0]*30
    for v in arr:
        for b in range(30):
            if v>>b & 1:
                bit_cnt[b] += 1

    # Overall GCD of non‑zero frequencies
    g = 0
    for c in bit_cnt:
        if c:
            g = gcd(g, c)

    if g == 0:                     # all numbers are zero
        return list(range(1, n+1))

    # Any divisor of g works as a group size
    ks = []
    for d in range(1, int(g**0.5)+1):
        if g % d == 0:
            ks.append(d)
            if d*d != g:
                ks.append(g//d)
    ks.sort()
    return ks

def solve():
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        ks = feasible_ks(a)
        out.append(' '.join(map(str, ks)))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – multiple test cases.  
      2. **Count bit occurrences** across the whole array (30 bits).  
      3. **Compute GCD** of all non‑zero bit counts; denote it `g`.  
      4. **Reasoning** – if we can split the array into groups of size k, each group must contain the same multiset of bits, therefore k must divide the count of every bit, i.e., k | g. Conversely, any divisor of `g` can be realized by repeatedly taking arbitrary k‑sized subsets; the common AND will be the AND of all numbers, which is the same for every group.  
      5. **Enumerate divisors** of `g` (or output 1…n when `g = 0`).  
      6. **Print** the divisors in ascending order.  
      7. **Complexity** – O(n·30) per test case, O(√g) for divisor enumeration, negligible memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Tutorial – Solving the original “Elimination with parameter k” problem

    ## 1.  Core observation
    The operation subtracts the **bitwise AND** of the chosen `k` elements from each of them.
    For a particular bit position `b` (0 ≤ b < 30) consider how many array elements have this bit set.
    Let `cnt[b]` be that number.

    *When we apply an elimination on a set of `k` indices, the AND has bit `b` equal to 1
    **iff** **all** `k` chosen numbers contain that bit.*  
    Consequently, the operation removes the bit `b` from exactly those `k` elements,
    decreasing `cnt[b]` by `k`.  
    If at least one of the chosen numbers does **not** have the bit, the AND’s bit `b`
    becomes 0 and `cnt[b]` stays unchanged.

    Therefore each operation can only **decrease** a bit‑count by a multiple of `k`,
    and it can never increase a count.

    ## 2.  Feasibility condition
    To end with all zeros we must bring every `cnt[b]` down to 0.
    This is possible **iff** each `cnt[b]` is divisible by `k`:

    ```
    for every bit b:   cnt[b] % k == 0
    ```

    If some bit appears a number of times that is not a multiple of `k`,
    after any sequence of operations there will always be a remainder of that bit,
    because we can only subtract it in chunks of size `k`.

    ## 3.  Reducing the condition to a single number
    Let `g` be the greatest common divisor of all non‑zero `cnt[b]` values.
    By definition, `g` divides each `cnt[b]`.  
    Consequently, **every divisor of `g` satisfies the divisibility condition**,
    and any `k` that is **not** a divisor of `g` fails for at least one bit.

    Edge case: if all array elements are already zero, every `cnt[b]` is 0,
    `g` is defined as 0, and **any** `k` (1 … n) works.

    Hence the set of valid `k` is exactly the set of positive divisors of `g`
    (or the whole range `1…n` when `g = 0`).

    ## 4.  Constructive argument (why divisors are sufficient)
    Suppose `k` divides `g`.  
    For each bit `b` we can pair the `cnt[b]` occurrences into groups of size `k`.
    Process the array repeatedly:

    * pick any `k` indices that still share a common set of bits (possible because
      we can always choose indices that contain the current bit),
    * perform the elimination – the AND will contain exactly those bits,
      and they disappear from the chosen elements.

    Repeating this for all bits eventually zeroes every element.
    The constructive proof is not required for the answer, but it guarantees that
    the divisibility condition is not only necessary but also sufficient.

    ## 5.  Algorithm outline
    1. **Read** the array.
    2. **Count** for each of the 30 bits how many numbers have that bit set (`cnt[0…29]`).
    3. **Compute** `g = gcd of all cnt[b] that are > 0`.  
       If all `cnt` are zero, set `g = 0`.
    4. **If** `g == 0` → output `1 2 … n`.  
       Otherwise, **enumerate** all divisors of `g` (iterate up to √g) and output them sorted.
    5. Repeat for every test case.

    ## 6.  Correctness proof sketch
    *Lemma 1*: During any elimination with parameter `k`, the count of each bit
    decreases by either `0` or `k`.  
    *Proof*: The AND has bit `b` = 1 only when all `k` chosen numbers have that bit.
    In that case each of the `k` numbers loses the bit, decreasing the total count by `k`.
    Otherwise the bit stays unchanged. ∎

    *Lemma 2*: If for some bit `b` we have `cnt[b] mod k ≠ 0`, then it is impossible
    to reach all zeros using parameter `k`.  
    *Proof*: By Lemma 1 the count can only change by multiples of `k`. Starting from a
    value not divisible by `k`, we can never reach 0. ∎

    *Lemma 3*: Let `g` be the gcd of all non‑zero `cnt[b]`. Every divisor `k` of `g`
    satisfies the condition of Lemma 2.  
    *Proof*: Since `k | g` and `g | cnt[b]` for each bit, we have `k | cnt[b]`. ∎

    *Lemma 4*: For any divisor `k` of `g` there exists a sequence of eliminations that
    turns the whole array into zeros.  
    *Proof Sketch*: Process bits independently. For a fixed bit `b`, its count `cnt[b]`
    is a multiple of `k`. Group the indices containing this bit into `cnt[b]/k` disjoint
    groups of size `k`. Perform an elimination on each group; the AND will contain the
    bit, removing it from all members. Repeating for all bits eventually clears every
    element. Because groups may overlap across different bits, we can interleave the
    operations; the existence of a schedule follows from the fact that each operation
    never creates new bits. ∎

    *Theorem*: The set of all feasible `k` is exactly the set of positive divisors of `g`
    (or `1…n` when `g = 0`).  
    *Proof*:  
    – Necessity follows from Lemma 2 and the definition of `g`.  
    – Sufficiency follows from Lemma 4. ∎

    ## 7.  Complexity analysis
    *Counting bits*: O(n·30) time, O(30) extra memory.  
    *GCD computation*: O(30) time.  
    *Divisor enumeration*: O(√g) ≤ O(√n·30) ≤ O(√2·10⁵) ≈ 450 operations, negligible.  
    Overall per test case: **O(n·30)** time, **O(1)** additional memory.

    ## 8.  When to use this algorithm
    Any problem where an operation removes a common property from exactly `k` chosen
    elements (e.g., bitwise AND, bitwise OR, minimum, etc.) often reduces to checking
    divisibility of frequency counts. The gcd‑divisor technique is a standard tool for
    such “simultaneous reduction” problems.

  </algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – “Bitwise AND Subset Sum Zero”**

1. **Input handling**  
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   - For each test case:  
     * read `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 2·10⁵).  
     * read the array `a[1…n]`.  

2. **Count set bits**  
   - Initialise an array `cnt[0…29]` with zeros (30 bits are enough because `a_i < 2³⁰`).  
   - For each element `v` in `a`  
        * for `b = 0 … 29`  
            – if `(v >> b) & 1` is 1, increment `cnt[b]`.  

3. **Compute the global GCD**  
   - Initialise `g = 0`.  
   - For each `b = 0 … 29`  
        * if `cnt[b] > 0` → `g = gcd(g, cnt[b])`.  
   - After the loop `g` is the greatest common divisor of all non‑zero bit‑counts.  

4. **Determine feasible `k` values**  
   - **Case A – all numbers already zero** (`g == 0`): every `k` from `1` to `n` works.  
   - **Case B – `g > 0`**: the only feasible `k` are the positive divisors of `g`.  

5. **Enumerate divisors of `g` (if `g > 0`)**  
   - Create an empty vector `ans`.  
   - For `d` from `1` while `d * d ≤ g`  
        * if `g % d == 0` → push `d` into `ans`.  
        * if `d != g / d` → also push `g / d`.  
   - Sort `ans` in increasing order.  

6. **Output**  
   - For the current test case, print the elements of `ans` (or `1 … n` for case A) separated by a single space, followed by a newline.  

7. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Bit counting: `O(n * 30)` per test case.  
   - GCD computation: `O(30)`.  
   - Divisor enumeration: `O(√g) ≤ O(√n)`.  
   - Overall: `O(n * 30)` time, `O(30)` extra memory.  

8. **Correctness justification (high‑level)**  
   - For any fixed bit position `b`, let `cnt[b]` be the number of array elements that currently have this bit set.  
   - An operation on a set of exactly `k` indices can only clear this bit from **all** chosen elements when *every* chosen element has the bit set; otherwise the AND’s bit is 0 and the count does not change.  
   - Hence each operation changes `cnt[b]` by either `0` or `-k`. Consequently `cnt[b]` can reach `0` only if `cnt[b]` is a multiple of `k`.  
   - Therefore a necessary condition for a given `k` is: `cnt[b] % k == 0` for every bit `b`.  
   - Let `g = gcd` of all non‑zero `cnt[b]`. The condition above holds exactly for the divisors of `g`.  
   - Conversely, if `k` divides `g`, we can repeatedly pick groups of `k` indices that all still contain a particular bit, clear that bit from the whole group, and continue for all bits. Because each `cnt[b]` is a multiple of `k`, such grouping is always possible, guaranteeing a sequence of operations that zeroes the whole array.  
   - When all `cnt[b]` are zero (`g = 0`), the array is already all‑zero, so any `k` works.  

9. **Edge cases to watch**  
   - `n = 1` – divisor enumeration works (g equals the number of set bits in the single element).  
   - All elements are zero – handle `g == 0` specially.  
   - Large `n` (up to 2·10⁵) – ensure O(n·30) fits easily.  

10. **Implementation notes**  
    - Use 64‑bit integers for intermediate GCD calculations (though counts ≤ n ≤ 2·10⁵ fit in 32‑bit).  
    - Re‑use the same `cnt` array for each test case, resetting to zero.  
    - Fast I/O (e.g., `ios::sync_with_stdio(false); cin.tie(nullptr);`) to handle up to 10⁴ test cases.  

Following these steps yields a solution that meets the required time and memory limits and correctly outputs all feasible `k` values for each test case.

  Therefore a necessary condition for a given `k` to be feasible is `cnt[b] % k == 0` for every bit `b`.

  If all `cnt[b]` are zero (`g == 0`), the array is already all‑zero and any `k` (1 … n) works.
* The plan then outputs all divisors of `g` (or all `k` when `g == 0`).  
  This matches the known editorial proof that when `k` divides `g` we can repeatedly pick groups of `k` indices that all still contain a particular bit, clear that bit, and continue until the whole array becomes zero.

**Planning to solve “Minimum Operations to Zero an Array with Fixed‑Size AND”**

1. **Input handling**  
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   - For each test case:  
     * Read `n`.  
     * Read the array `a[1…n]`.  

2. **Bit‑frequency counting**  
   - Initialise an array `cnt[0…29]` with zeros (30 bits are enough because `a_i < 2^30`).  
   - For each element `x` in `a`:  
     * For each bit `b` from 0 to 29:  
       - If `(x >> b) & 1` is `1`, increment `cnt[b]`.  

3. **Compute the global GCD**  
   - Initialise `g = 0`.  
   - For each bit `b`:  
     * If `cnt[b] > 0`, set `g = gcd(g, cnt[b])`.  
   - After the loop:  
     * If all `cnt[b]` are zero (i.e. the whole array is already all‑zero), treat `g = 0` specially (any `k` from `1` to `n` is feasible).  

4. **Determine feasible `k` values**  
   - **Case A – `g == 0`** (array already zero):  
     * Output the integers `1, 2, …, n`.  
   - **Case B – `g > 0`**:  
     * All feasible `k` are exactly the positive divisors of `g`.  
     * Enumerate divisors:  
       - Create an empty vector `ans`.  
       - For `d` from `1` to `⌊√g⌋`:  
         * If `g % d == 0`:  
           - Push `d` into `ans`.  
           - If `d != g/d`, push `g/d` as well.  
     * Sort `ans` in increasing order.  

5. **Output**  
   - For the current test case, print the numbers in `ans` separated by a single space, followed by a newline.  

6. **Correctness justification (high‑level)**  
   - *Lemma 1*: In one operation with parameter `k`, for any bit `b` the total number of occurrences of that bit (`cnt[b]`) either stays unchanged or decreases by exactly `k`.  
   - *Lemma 2*: Therefore `cnt[b]` can become zero only if `cnt[b]` is a multiple of `k`.  
   - *Necessity*: If some `cnt[b]` is not divisible by `k`, the array can never be reduced to all zeros → `k` must divide every non‑zero `cnt[b]`. Hence `k` must divide `g = gcd(cnt[b] | cnt[b] > 0)`.  
   - *Sufficiency*: If `k` divides `g`, then for each bit `b` we can partition the indices that contain this bit into groups of size `k`. Performing an elimination on each group removes the bit from all its members. Repeating for all bits (bits are independent because subtraction never creates new 1‑bits) yields all zeros.  
   - Consequently the set of feasible `k` is exactly the set of divisors of `g` (or all `1…n` when `g = 0`).  

7. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Counting bits: `O(n * 30)` per test case.  
   - GCD computation: `O(30)`.  
   - Divisor enumeration: `O(√g) ≤ O(√n)` (worst‑case `g ≤ n`).  
   - Overall per test case: `O(n)` time, `O(1)` extra memory (aside from the 30‑size `cnt` array and answer list).  

8. **Implementation notes**  
   - Use 64‑bit integers for safety when computing `gcd`.  
   - Reuse the `cnt` array (reset to zero) for each test case.  
   - Ensure total `n` over all test cases ≤ 200 000, so the algorithm easily fits time limits.  

Following these steps yields a solution that prints all possible `k` values for each test case.

**Problem Restatement**  
For each test case we are given an array `a[1…n]` of non‑negative integers (`0 ≤ a_i < 2^30`).  
Fix a value `k (1 ≤ k ≤ n)`. An *elimination* with parameter `k` chooses any `k` distinct indices, computes  

```
x = a[i1] & a[i2] & … & a[ik]          (bitwise AND)
```

and replaces each chosen element by `a[ij] – x`.  
All other elements stay unchanged.  

We have to output, in increasing order, all `k` for which it is possible (using a finite sequence of such operations, always with the same `k`) to turn the whole array into all zeros.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 1. Key Observation – behaviour of each bit independently  

Consider a single bit position `b` (0 … 29).  
Let `cnt[b]` be the number of array elements whose `b`‑th bit is `1`.  

During one elimination with parameter `k`:

* The AND `x` has bit `b` equal to `1` **iff** *all* the `k` chosen numbers have bit `b` = `1`.  
* If that happens, each of those `k` numbers loses the bit (subtracting `x` clears it), therefore `cnt[b]` decreases by exactly `k`.  
* If at least one chosen number has bit `b` = `0`, the AND’s bit `b` is `0` and `cnt[b]` does **not** change.

Hence **`cnt[b]` can only change by multiples of `k` and never increase**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 2. Necessary condition  

To finish with all zeros we must bring every `cnt[b]` down to `0`.  
Because we can only subtract `k` at a time, we need  

```
cnt[b] % k == 0   for every bit b
```

If for some bit the remainder is non‑zero, that bit will survive forever.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 3. Reducing to a single number  

Let  

```
g = gcd of all cnt[b] that are > 0
```

*If all `cnt[b]` are zero (the whole array already consists of zeros) we define `g = 0`.*

* Every divisor `k` of `g` satisfies `cnt[b] % k == 0` for all bits → the necessary condition holds.
* If `k` is **not** a divisor of `g`, then `k` fails the condition for at least one bit.

Thus the set of feasible `k` is exactly the set of positive divisors of `g` (or `1…n` when `g = 0`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 4. Sufficiency – why any divisor works  

Assume `k | g`. For each bit `b` we have `cnt[b] = m·k`.  
Group the indices that contain this bit into `m` disjoint groups of size `k`.  
Perform an elimination on each group; the AND of the group contains bit `b`, so the bit disappears from all its members.  

Processing bits one after another never creates new `1`‑bits (subtracting `x` can only clear bits). Consequently after handling all bits every element becomes zero.  
Therefore any divisor of `g` is indeed achievable.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 5. Algorithm per test case  

1. **Read `n` and the array.**  
2. **Count bits**  
   ```
   cnt[0…29] = 0
   for each value v in a:
       for b = 0 … 29:
           if (v >> b) & 1: cnt[b]++
   ```
3. **Compute `g`**  
   ```
   g = 0
   for b = 0 … 29:
       if cnt[b] > 0:
           g = gcd(g, cnt[b])   // gcd(0, x) = x
   ```
4. **Generate answer**  
   *If `g == 0`* → output all integers `1 … n`.  
   *Else* enumerate all divisors of `g`:
   ```
   ans = empty list
   for d = 1 … sqrt(g):
       if g % d == 0:
           ans.push_back(d)
           if d != g/d: ans.push_back(g/d)
   sort(ans)
   output ans
   ```
5. Repeat for all test cases.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 6. Correctness Proof Sketch  

*Lemma 1* (bit count change): During any elimination with parameter `k`, for each bit `b` the total number of `1`s (`cnt[b]`) either stays unchanged or decreases by exactly `k`.  
*Proof*: Follows directly from the definition of AND – the bit is `1` in the AND iff all chosen numbers have it, then subtraction clears it from each of the `k` numbers. ∎  

*Lemma 2* (necessary divisibility): If for some bit `b` we have `cnt[b] mod k ≠ 0`, the array cannot be reduced to all zeros using parameter `k`.  
*Proof*: By Lemma 1 `cnt[b]` can only change by multiples of `k`; a non‑multiple can never become `0`. ∎  

*Lemma 3* (gcd captures all constraints): Let `g` be the gcd of all non‑zero `cnt[b]`. Then a positive integer `k` satisfies the condition of Lemma 2 **iff** `k` divides `g`.  
*Proof*: If `k | g`, then `k` divides every `cnt[b]`. Conversely, if `k` does not divide `g`, there exists a bit with `cnt[b]` not divisible by `k`. ∎  

*Lemma 4* (sufficiency of divisors): For any divisor `k` of `g` there exists a sequence of eliminations that turns the whole array into zeros.  
*Proof*: For each bit `b`, `cnt[b] = m·k`. Partition the indices containing this bit into `m` groups of size `k`. Performing an elimination on each group clears the bit from all its members. Since operations never set a previously cleared bit, after processing all bits every element becomes zero. ∎  

*Theorem*: The set of feasible `k` is exactly the set of positive divisors of `g` (or `1…n` when `g = 0`).  
*Proof*: Necessity follows from Lemma 2 and Lemma 3. Sufficiency follows from Lemma 4. ∎  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 7. Complexity Analysis  

*Counting bits*: `O(n·30)` time, `O(30)` extra memory.  
*GCD computation*: `O(30)` time.  
*Divisor enumeration*: `O(√g)` ≤ `O(√n)` (worst‑case `g = n`).  
Overall per test case: **`O(n·30)`** ≈ `O(n)` time, **`O(1)`** additional memory.  
The sum of `n` over all test cases ≤ 200 000, well within limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### 8. Reference Implementation Outline (C++/Python/Java)

```pseudo
read T
repeat T times:
    read n
    read array a[1..n]
    cnt[0..29] = 0
    for v in a:
        for b = 0 to 29:
            if (v >> b) & 1: cnt[b]++

    g = 0
    for b = 0 to 29:
        if cnt[b] > 0: g = gcd(g, cnt[b])

    if g == 0:
        output numbers 1 .. n
    else:
        ans = []
        for d = 1; d*d <= g; ++d:
            if g % d == 0:
                ans.append(d)
                if d != g/d: ans.append(g/d)
        sort(ans)
        output ans separated by spaces
```

The algorithm follows exactly the plan described above and satisfies the required input‑output format.
<root>
<explanation>
The proposed plan correctly solves the problem.

* It observes that each bit behaves independently: during an elimination with a fixed k the number of 1‑bits at any position can only decrease by exactly k (when all chosen elements have that bit) or stay unchanged.  
* Consequently, for every bit position b the initial count cnt[b] must be divisible by k; otherwise that bit can never be eliminated.  
* Let g be the greatest common divisor of all non‑zero cnt[b]. Then the necessary condition “cnt[b] % k = 0 for all b” is equivalent to “k divides g”.  
* The plan also shows sufficiency: if k divides g, the indices containing each bit can be partitioned into groups of size k, and performing an elimination on each group clears that bit. Since operations never create new 1‑bits, after processing all bits the whole array becomes zero.  
* Therefore the feasible k are exactly the positive divisors of g (or all k = 1…n when the array is already all zeros, i.e., g = 0).

The algorithm implements this reasoning:
1. Count the set bits for each of the 30 positions – O(n·30).  
2. Compute the gcd of the non‑zero counts.  
3. If the gcd is zero, output 1…n; otherwise output all divisors of the gcd in sorted order.

Both the correctness proof and the complexity analysis (O(n) time per test case, O(1) extra memory) are sound, and the implementation details match the required input/output format. Hence the plan is correct and will solve the problem for all allowed test cases.

</explanation>

import sys, math

def solve() -> None:
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.read().split()))
    it = iter(data)
    t = next(it, 0)
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = next(it)
        cnt = [0] * 30
        for _ in range(n):
            x = next(it)
            b = 0
            while x:
                if x & 1:
                    cnt[b] += 1
                x >>= 1
                b += 1
        g = 0
        for c in cnt:
            if c:
                g = math.gcd(g, c)
        if g == 0:
            out_lines.append(' '.join(str(k) for k in range(1, n + 1)))
        else:
            divs = []
            d = 1
            while d * d <= g:
                if g % d == 0:
                    divs.append(d)
                    if d != g // d:
                        divs.append(g // d)
                d += 1
            divs.sort()
            out_lines.append(' '.join(map(str, divs)))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

      <p><strong>Problem 1 – Minimum Cost Partition with Prefix Sums</strong></p>
      <p>Given an array <code>a[1…n]</code> of positive integers and an integer <code>k</code> (1 ≤ k ≤ n), split the array into <code>k</code> contiguous parts.  
      The cost of a part <code>[l, r]</code> is the sum of its elements.  
      Find the minimum possible total cost, i.e. the sum of the costs of the <code>k</code> parts.</p>
      <p>This is a classic “partition DP” problem where the transition uses prefix sums.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      #
      # 1. Read n, k and the array a.
      # 2. Build prefix sums: pref[i] = a[1] + … + a[i].
      # 3. dp[i][j] – minimal cost to split first i elements into j parts.
      #    dp[0][0] = 0, dp[*][0] = INF, dp[0][*] = INF.
      # 4. Transition:
      #        dp[i][j] = min_{p < i} ( dp[p][j‑1] + cost(p+1, i) )
      #    where cost(p+1,i) = pref[i] – pref[p].
      # 5. The naive O(n²·k) is too slow for n = 10⁵, so we use the
      #    Convex Hull Trick (or Divide‑and‑Conquer DP optimisation) because
      #    the cost function is linear in pref[p].
      # 6. Implement a recursive D&C routine that computes dp[·][j] from dp[·][j‑1]
      #    in O(n log n) per layer.
      # 7. After processing k layers, answer = dp[n][k].
      #
      # Pseudocode:
      #
      # def compute(j, l, r, optL, optR):
      #     if l > r: return
      #     mid = (l+r)//2

      #
      # for j in 1..k:
      #     compute(j, 1, n, 0, n-1)
      #     dp_prev = dp_cur
      #
      # print(dp_prev[n])
    </code>

      4. Implement the divide‑and‑conquer optimisation routine `compute`.

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 2 – Counting Pairs with GCD at Least a Threshold</strong></p>
      <p>Given two integers <code>L</code> and <code>R</code> (1 ≤ L ≤ R ≤ 10⁶), count the number of ordered pairs <code>(i, j)</code> such that <code>L ≤ i ≤ j ≤ R</code> and <code>gcd(i, j) ≥ L</code>.</p>
      <p>This problem is directly related to the function <code>c(L,R)</) in the original statement. A typical solution uses the inclusion‑exclusion principle together with the divisor‑sieve.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      #
      # 1. For every d from L to R we will count pairs whose gcd is exactly d.
      # 2. Let cnt[d] = number of multiples of d in [L, R].
      #    cnt[d] = floor(R/d) - floor((L‑1)/d)
      # 3. Number of pairs (i, j) with both multiples of d and i ≤ j is
      #        pairs[d] = cnt[d] * (cnt[d] + 1) // 2
      # 4. Using Möbius inversion we can obtain the number of pairs whose gcd is
      #    exactly d:
      #        exact[d] = pairs[d] - Σ_{m≥2} exact[m·d]
      #    We process d from R down to L.
      # 5. The required answer c(L,R) is Σ_{d ≥ L} exact[d]   (or simply Σ_{d=L}^{R} pairs[d] after the subtraction step).
      # 6. Implementation details:
      #    * Pre‑compute the Möbius function μ up to 10⁶ (linear sieve).
      #    * For each test case, compute cnt[d] for d in [L,R] in O(R‑L+1).
      #    * Accumulate the answer using the formula:
      #          ans = Σ_{d=L}^{R} μ[d] * ( floor(R/d) * (floor(R/d)+1)//2 -
      #                                    floor((L‑1)/d) * (floor((L‑1)/d)+1)//2 )
      #    * This works because Σ_{d|gcd(i,j)} μ[d] = 1 iff gcd(i,j)=1.
      #
      # Pseudocode:
      #
      # def mobius_sieve(N):
      #     mu = [1]*(N+1)
      #     prime = []
      #     is_comp = [False]*(N+1)
      #     for i in range(2, N+1):
      #         if not is_comp[i]:
      #             prime.append(i)
      #             mu[i] = -1
      #         for p in prime:
      #             if i*p > N: break
      #             is_comp[i*p] = True
      #             if i % p == 0:
      #                 mu[i*p] = 0
      #                 break
      #             else:
      #                 mu[i*p] = -mu[i]
      #     return mu
      #
      # mu = mobius_sieve(10**6)
      #
      # def count_pairs(L, R):
      #     ans = 0
      #     for d in range(L, R+1):
      #         a = R // d
      #         b = (L-1) // d
      #         total = a*(a+1)//2 - b*(b+1)//2
      #         ans += mu[d] * total
      #     return ans
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Pre‑compute the Möbius function up to the maximum possible <code>R</code> (10⁶) using a linear sieve.  
      2. For each query <code>(L,R)</code> iterate over <code>d = L … R</code>.  
      3. For each <code>d</code> compute the number of multiples in the interval, transform it into the number of unordered pairs, and multiply by <code>μ[d]</code>.  
      4. Accumulate the result; the final sum equals <code>c(L,R)</code>.  
      5. Complexity per test case: O(R‑L+1). With up to 10⁵ queries and R ≤ 10⁶ this is fast enough after the sieve.  
      6. Output the computed value.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 3 – Optimal Segmentation with Quadratic Cost (DP with Convex Hull Trick)</strong></p>
      <p>Given a sequence <code>a[1…n]</code> and an integer <code>k</code>, split the sequence into <code>k</code> contiguous segments.  
      The cost of a segment <code>[l, r]</code> is <code>(sum_{i=l}^{r} a_i)²</code>.  
      Find the minimum total cost.</p>
      <p>The quadratic nature of the cost makes the DP transition linear in the prefix sum, which allows the use of the Convex Hull Trick (CHT) to achieve O(k·n) time.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      #
      # 1. Compute prefix sums: S[i] = a[1] + … + a[i].
      # 2. DP definition:
      #        dp[i] = minimum cost to partition first i elements into current number of parts.
      #    Transition for a fixed number of parts j:
      #        dp[i] = min_{p < i} ( dp_prev[p] + (S[i] - S[p])² )
      #    Expand the square:
      #        = min_{p < i} ( dp_prev[p] + S[i]² - 2·S[i]·S[p] + S[p]² )
      #        = S[i]² + min_{p < i} ( (dp_prev[p] + S[p]²) - 2·S[p]·S[i] )
      #    This is of the form  (m * x + b) where:
      #        x = S[i],   m = -2·S[p],   b = dp_prev[p] + S[p]²
      # 3. Maintain a lower hull of lines (m, b). Query the hull for each S[i] to obtain the minimum.
      # 4. Use a deque implementation of CHT because slopes (m) are added in decreasing order
      #    (since S[p] is non‑decreasing, -2·S[p] is non‑increasing).
      # 5. For each part j = 1 … k:
      #        - Initialise an empty hull.
      #        - Insert line for p = 0 (S[0]=0, dp_prev[0]=0).
      #        - For i = 1 … n:
      #              val = hull.query(S[i]) + S[i]²
      #              dp_cur[i] = val
      #              hull.add_line( m = -2·S[i], b = dp_prev[i] + S[i]² )
      #        - After the loop, dp_prev = dp_cur.
      # 6. Answer is dp_prev[n] after k iterations.
      #
      # Helper functions for CHT:
      #
      # def intersect_x(l1, l2):
      #     # returns x-coordinate where l1 and l2 intersect
      #     return (l2.b - l1.b) / (l1.m - l2.m)
      #
      # class Line:
      #     def __init__(self, m, b):
      #         self.m = m
      #         self.b = b
      #         self.x_left = -inf   # intersection with previous line
      #
      # class ConvexHull:
      #     def __init__(self):
      #         self.deq = deque()
      #
      #     def add_line(self, m, b):
      #         new = Line(m, b)
      #         while len(self.deq) >= 2 and \
      #               intersect_x(self.deq[-2], self.deq[-1]) >= intersect_x(self.deq[-1], new):
      #             self.deq.pop()
      #         if self.deq:
      #             new.x_left = intersect_x(self.deq[-1], new)
      #         self.deq.append(new)
      #
      #     def query(self, x):
      #         while len(self.deq) >= 2 and self.deq[1].x_left <= x:
      #             self.deq.popleft()
      #         line = self.deq[0]
      #         return line.m * x + line.b
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read <code>n, k</code> and the array <code>a</code>.  
      2. Build prefix sum array <code>S</code>.  
      3. Initialise <code>dp_prev</code> with INF, set <code>dp_prev[0] = 0</code>.  
      4. Loop over part count <code>part = 1 … k</code>:  
         a. Create an empty convex hull.  
         b. Insert the line corresponding to <code>p = 0</code>.  
         c. For each position <code>i = 1 … n</code>:  
            i. Query the hull with <code>x = S[i]</code> to obtain the best value.  
            ii. Compute <code>dp_cur[i] = S[i]² + query_result</code>.  
            iii. Add the line for the current <code>p = i</code> using slope <code>-2·S[i]</code> and intercept <code>dp_prev[i] + S[i]²</code>.  
         d. After processing all <code>i</code>, replace <code>dp_prev</code> with <code>dp_cur</code>.  
      5. After the outer loop, output <code>dp_prev[n]</code>.  
      6. Complexity: O(k·n) time, O(n) memory; suitable for n ≤ 10⁵ and k ≤ 100.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    <p>
      The original problem asks for the minimum possible sum of the function <code>c(l, r)</code> over <code>k</code> disjoint consecutive intervals that together cover <code>[1, n]</code>.  
      This is a classic <strong>dynamic programming over partitions</strong> problem.  
      Let <code>dp[i][j]</code> be the minimal total cost for the prefix <code>[1, i]</code> when it is divided into exactly <code>j</code> intervals.  
      The transition is
      <br/>
      <code>dp[i][j] = min_{p < i} ( dp[p][j‑1] + c(p+1, i) )</code>.
      <br/>
      The difficulty lies in evaluating <code>c(p+1, i)</code> quickly and in speeding up the <code>min</code> operation.
    </p>

    <h3>Key Observations</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Structure of <code>c(l, r)</strong>:</li>
        <ul>
          <li>For a fixed left bound <code>l</code>, the condition <code>gcd(i, j) ≥ l</code> means that the gcd is a multiple of <code>l</code>. </li>
          <li>Using divisor counting and Möbius inversion we can compute <code>c(l, r)</code> in <code>O(1)</code> after an <code>O(N log N)</code> preprocessing that stores, for every <code>d</code>, the number of multiples of <code>d</code> up to any index.</li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li><strong>Monotonicity / Convexity:</strong> When the left border grows, the set of admissible gcd values shrinks, making <code>c(l, r)</code> a non‑increasing function of <code>l</code>. This monotonicity often yields the <em>quadrangle inequality</em> or the <em>Monge property</em>, which permits <strong>Divide‑and‑Conquer DP optimisation</strong> (also known as <em>Aliens trick</em> or <em>Knuth optimisation</em> in special cases).</li>
      <li><strong>Divide‑and‑Conquer DP Optimisation:</strong> If the optimal split point for <code>dp[i][j]</code> is monotone with respect to <code>i</code> (i.e., <code>opt[i] ≤ opt[i+1]</code>), we can compute an entire DP layer in <code>O(n log n)</code> instead of <code>O(n²)</code>. The recurrence above satisfies this property because <code>c(l, r)</code> depends only on the interval length and on a function that is “convex” in the left endpoint.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>Algorithm Outline</h3>
    <ol>
      <li><strong>Pre‑processing:</strong>
        <ul>
          <li>Compute the Möbius function <code>μ</code> up to <code>n</code> using a linear sieve.</li>
          <li>For every <code>d</code> store a prefix array <code>cnt_d[x] = ⌊x/d⌋</code>. This allows us to obtain the number of multiples of <code>d</code> in any interval <code>[l, r]</code> as <code>cnt_d[r] - cnt_d[l‑1]</code>.</li>
          <li>Using the formula from Problem 2, pre‑compute <code>c(l, r)</code> on‑the‑fly in <code>O(1)</code> time:
            <br/>
            <code>c(l, r) = Σ_{d = l}^{r} μ[d] * ( (⌊r/d⌋·(⌊r/d⌋+1)/2) - (⌊(l‑1)/d⌋·(⌊(l‑1)/d⌋+1)/2) )</code>.
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li><strong>Dynamic Programming:</strong>
        <ul>
          <li>Initialize <code>dpPrev[0] = 0</code> and <code>dpPrev[i] = ∞</code> for <code>i > 0</code>.</li>
          <li>For each part count <code>j = 1 … k</code> compute a new array <code>dpCur</code> using the divide‑and‑conquer optimisation:</li>
          <pre>
def compute(l, r, optL, optR):
    if l > r: return
    mid = (l + r) // 2
    best = (INF, -1)
    for p in range(optL, min(mid, optR) + 1):
        val = dpPrev[p] + c(p+1, mid)
        if val < best[0]:
            best = (val, p)
    dpCur[mid] = best[0]
    opt = best[1]
    compute(l, mid-1, optL, opt)
    compute(mid+1, r, opt, optR)
          </pre>
          <li>Call <code>compute(1, n, 0, n‑1)</code> for the current layer, then swap <code>dpPrev</code> and <code>dpCur</code>.</li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li><strong>Result:</strong> After processing <code>k</code> layers, <code>dpPrev[n]</code> equals <code>f(n, k)</code>.</li>
    </ol>

    <h3>Complexity Analysis</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>Pre‑processing Möbius: <code>O(N)</code> time and memory.</li>
      <li>Each DP layer via divide‑and‑conquer: <code>O(n log n)</code> because each level of recursion processes <code>O(n)</code> candidates and the recursion depth is <code>log n</code>.</li>
      <li>Total time: <code>O(k·n·log n)</code>, which fits the constraints (<code>n ≤ 10⁵, k ≤ n</code>).</li>
      <li>Memory: <code>O(n)</code> for two DP arrays plus <code>O(N)</code> for Möbius and auxiliary prefix data.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>Why This Works</h3>
    <p>
      The DP formulation captures exactly the definition of <code>f(n, k)</code>.  
      The monotone‑optimal‑split property holds because <code>c(l, r)</code> is a “convex” cost with respect to the left border – increasing the left border never makes a previously optimal split worse.  
      Consequently, the divide‑and‑conquer optimisation is valid and reduces the naïve <code>O(k·n²)</code> DP to a feasible <code>O(k·n·log n)</code>.  
      The Möbius‑based formula provides <code>c(l, r)</code> in constant time, so the overall algorithm stays within the required limits.
    </p>

    <h3>General Tips for Similar Problems</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>Identify whether the cost function depends only on interval endpoints and whether it exhibits convexity/Monge properties.</li>
      <li>If the cost can be computed in <code>O(1)</code> after preprocessing, focus on speeding up the DP transition.</li>
      <li>Check for monotone optima – if they exist, apply divide‑and‑conquer DP or Knuth optimisation.</li>
      <li>When the cost is quadratic or linear in a prefix sum, consider Convex Hull Trick or Li Chao segment tree.</li>
      <li>For GCD‑related counting, Möbius inversion is a powerful tool to turn “gcd ≥ X” into a sum over multiples.</li>
    </ul>
  </algorithm>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Minimum Insertions to Make an Array Non‑Decreasing</title>
      <p>
        You are given an array <code>a[1…n]</code>. In one operation you may insert any positive integer at any position of the array.  
        Find the minimum number of insertions required so that the final array becomes non‑decreasing (i.e. <code>a_i ≤ a_{i+1}</code> for all <code>i</code>).
      </p>
      <p>
        Example: <code>[5, 1, 3]</code> → insert <code>2</code> before <code>3</code> → <code>[5, 1, 2, 3]</code> (still not non‑decreasing), then insert <code>6</code> at the front → <code>[6,5,1,2,3]</code>. The optimal answer is <code>2</code>.
      </p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def min_insertions_non_decreasing(arr):
          """
          Greedy approach:
          - Scan the array from left to right.
          - Keep the last kept value `last`.
          - If current value >= last, keep it (no insertion needed).
          - Otherwise we must insert numbers to raise `last` up to current.
            The cheapest way is to insert exactly (last - current) numbers:
            1,2,…,last‑current (any positive values work because they will be placed
            before the current element and keep the order non‑decreasing).
          The total number of inserted elements is the sum of all deficits.
          """
          last = arr[0]
          ops = 0
          for x in arr[1:]:
              if x >= last:
                  last = x
              else:
                  # we need (last - x) insertions
                  ops += last - x
                  # after inserting those numbers, the current element becomes the new last
                  # (because we can imagine we inserted values just before x)
                  # so `last` stays the same as before the deficit
          return ops
      
      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
              arr = list(map(int, data[idx:idx+n])); idx += n
              out.append(str(min_insertions_non_decreasing(arr)))
          print("\n".join(out))
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – number of test cases, then for each case read <code>n</code> and the array.
      2. **Greedy scan** – keep the maximum value seen so far (`last`).  
         • If the next element is ≥ `last`, no insertion is needed; update `last`.  
         • If it is smaller, we must insert enough numbers to “bridge” the gap. The gap size is `last - a_i`; add this to the answer.
      3. **Output** the accumulated number of insertions for each test case.
      4. The algorithm runs in **O(n)** time per test case and uses **O(1)** extra memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Make Sequence Satisfy a_i ≤ i by Adding Missing Numbers</title>
      <p>
        Given a sequence <code>a[1…n]</code> of positive integers, you may insert any positive integer at any position any number of times.  
        Determine the minimum number of insertions required so that after all insertions the condition <code>a_i ≤ i</code> holds for every position <code>i</code> of the final sequence.
      </p>
      <p>
        This is exactly the original problem, but we present it as a separate “reference” problem to illustrate the greedy technique that will be used later.
      </p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Greedy solution in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def min_ops_a_le_i(arr):
          """
          The idea:
          - Sort the array because the relative order does not matter for the condition.
          - Walk through the sorted values.
          - Keep a pointer `pos` that represents the current length of the built sequence.
          - For each value `x`:
                * If x ≤ pos+1 we can place it at position pos+1 (no extra insertion).
                * Otherwise we must insert (x - (pos+1)) dummy numbers before we can place x.
          - The sum of all required dummy numbers is the answer.
          """
          arr.sort()
          pos = 0          # length of the sequence built so far
          ops = 0
          for x in arr:
              if x <= pos + 1:
                  pos += 1
              else:
                  # need to insert (x - (pos+1)) numbers
                  ops += x - (pos + 1)
                  pos = x      # after inserting the missing numbers, x occupies position x
          return ops
      
      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
              arr = list(map(int, data[idx:idx+n])); idx += n
              out.append(str(min_ops_a_le_i(arr)))
          print("\n".join(out))
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Sort** the original array – the order of elements does not affect the feasibility of the condition.
      2. Initialise `pos = 0` (the length of the already‑valid prefix) and `ops = 0`.
      3. Iterate over each sorted value `x`:
         - If `x ≤ pos+1`, we can place `x` at the next position, so increment `pos`.
         - Otherwise we must insert `x - (pos+1)` dummy numbers (any positive integers) before `x`. Add this amount to `ops` and set `pos = x`.
      4. After processing all elements, `ops` is the minimal number of insertions.
      5. Output `ops` for each test case.
      6. Complexity: **O(n log n)** for sorting, **O(n)** extra work, **O(1)** additional memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Minimum Insertions to Form a Permutation 1…m</title>
      <p>
        You are given an array <code>a[1…n]</code> of positive integers (duplicates allowed).  
        In one operation you may insert any positive integer at any position.  
        Find the minimum number of insertions required so that the final array becomes a permutation of the first <code>m</code> natural numbers for some <code>m</code> (i.e., it contains each integer from <code>1</code> to <code>m</code> exactly once, in any order).</p>
      <p>
        Example: <code>[2, 5, 2]</code> → insert <code>1,3,4</code> → <code>[1,2,5,2,3,4]</code> (now we have a permutation of <code>1…5</code> after removing the duplicate <code>2</code> by inserting missing numbers). The optimal answer is <code>3</code>.
      </p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Python 3 solution using a greedy set‑based approach
      import sys
      
      def min_insertions_to_permutation(arr):
          """
          The optimal `m` is the smallest integer such that
          count of distinct numbers ≤ m and every number ≤ m.
          Greedy steps:
          1. Put all distinct values into a set.
          2. Starting from 1, check if the number is already present.
             - If yes, continue.
             - If not, we must insert it (increase answer).
          3. Stop when we have processed `max(set) + answer` numbers,
             because after that the array already contains all numbers 1..m.
          """
          present = set(arr)
          m = max(present) if present else 0
          ans = 0
          i = 1
          while i <= m + ans:
              if i not in present:
                  ans += 1          # we need to insert i
              i += 1
          return ans
      
      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
              arr = list(map(int, data[idx:idx+n])); idx += n
              out.append(str(min_insertions_to_permutation(arr)))
          print("\n".join(out))
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Collect distinct elements** of the given array in a set `present`.
      2. Initialise `ans = 0` (number of insertions) and iterate `i` from 1 upwards.
      3. While `i` ≤ `max(present) + ans`:
         - If `i` is missing from `present`, we must insert it → `ans += 1`.
         - Increment `i`.
      4. When the loop finishes, we have inserted exactly the missing numbers from 1 to `m = max(present) + ans`, therefore the final array is a permutation of `1…m`.
      5. Output `ans` for each test case.
      6. The algorithm runs in **O(n + m)** time, where `m` is at most `n + answer`, and uses **O(n)** memory for the set.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    <title>Greedy Algorithms – When and How to Use Them</title>
    <p>
      A greedy algorithm builds an answer step by step, always choosing the locally optimal option
      with the hope that this leads to a globally optimal solution.  Greedy methods are especially
      powerful when the problem exhibits the following two properties:
    </p>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Greedy‑choice property:</strong> A globally optimal solution can be constructed by
        making a locally optimal (greedy) choice at each step.</li>
      <li><strong>Optimal‑substructure:</strong> An optimal solution to the whole problem contains
        optimal solutions to its sub‑problems.</li>
    </ul>
    <p>
      If both properties hold, a simple iterative or recursive procedure that always picks the best
      immediate option will produce an optimal answer.  Many classic problems fit this pattern:
      activity selection, Huffman coding, Kruskal’s minimum spanning tree, Dijkstra’s shortest path,
      and the “insert‑to‑satisfy a_i ≤ i” problem we just examined.
    </p>

    <h3>Typical Steps to Design a Greedy Solution</h3>
    <ol>
      <li><strong>Understand the goal.</strong> Write down precisely what a feasible solution looks like
        and what you need to minimise or maximise.</li>
      <li><strong>Identify a natural ordering.</strong> Often the elements can be sorted by a key
        (value, index, deadline, etc.).  The order in which you process them is crucial.</li>
      <li><strong>Formulate the greedy choice.</strong> Decide, for the current step, which element
        or action gives the best immediate improvement while keeping the possibility of completing
        the solution later.</li>
      <li><strong>Prove correctness.</strong> Show the greedy‑choice property:
        any optimal solution can be transformed to one that makes the same greedy choice without
        worsening the objective.  Then argue optimal‑substructure (the remaining sub‑problem is of
        the same type).</li>
      <li><strong>Implement efficiently.</strong> Usually a single pass after sorting is enough,
        giving <code>O(n log n)</code> time (the sort) and <code>O(1)</code> extra space, or even
        <code>O(n)</code> if the input is already ordered.</li>
    </ol>

    <h3>Why Greedy Works for the “a_i ≤ i” Problem</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Sorting first.</strong> The relative order of the original numbers does not affect
        the feasibility of the condition; only their values matter.  Sorting lets us consider the
        smallest numbers first, which gives us the most “room” to place them.</li>
      <li><strong>Greedy insertion.</strong> While scanning the sorted array we keep the current
        length <code>pos</code> of the already‑valid prefix.  If the next value <code>x</code> is
        ≤ <code>pos+1</code>, we can place it without any extra work – this is the best we can do.
        If <code>x</code> is larger, the only way to make it satisfy <code>a_i ≤ i</code> is to
        create enough positions before it, i.e., insert <code>x‑(pos+1)</code> dummy numbers.
        Inserting fewer would violate the condition, inserting more would be wasteful.
        Hence the locally optimal decision (insert exactly the deficit) is forced and optimal.</li>
      <li><strong>Optimal‑substructure.</strong> After handling the current element and the
        necessary insertions, the remaining problem is exactly the same: we now have a shorter
        suffix that must satisfy the same condition, starting from position <code>pos+1</code>.
        Solving the suffix greedily yields an overall optimal solution.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>When Greedy Fails</h3>
    <p>
      Not every optimization problem admits a greedy solution.  If a locally optimal choice can
      block a better global arrangement, the greedy‑choice property does not hold.  Classic
      counter‑examples include the knapsack problem (without fractional items) and the
      traveling‑salesman problem.  In such cases you need dynamic programming, backtracking,
      or approximation algorithms.
    </p>

    <h3>Summary</h3>
    <p>
      Greedy algorithms are simple, fast, and often the right tool for problems that involve
      constructing a sequence under monotonic constraints, covering intervals, or building
      structures with minimal cost.  The key is to prove that making the best immediate decision
      never harms the ability to finish optimally.  Once that proof is in place, the implementation
      usually reduces to sorting followed by a linear scan, exactly as demonstrated in the three
      example problems above.
    </p>
  </algorithm>
</root>

    <description>
      <title>Maximum Subarray Sum (Kadane's Algorithm)</title>
      You are given an array of integers (both positive and negative). Find the contiguous subarray with the largest sum and output that sum.
      The classic solution runs in linear time using a greedy approach known as Kadane's algorithm.
    </description>
    <code>
# Kadane's algorithm in Python 3
def max_subarray_sum(arr):
    # Initialize current and global maximums with the first element
    current_max = global_max = arr[0]
    for x in arr[1:]:
        # Either extend the previous subarray or start a new one at x
        current_max = max(x, current_max + x)
        # Update the best answer seen so far
        global_max = max(global_max, current_max)
    return global_max

# Example usage
if __name__ == "__main__":
    t = int(input())
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(input())
        a = list(map(int, input().split()))
        print(max_subarray_sum(a))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the number of test cases.
      2. For each test case, read the array.
      3. Initialise two variables:
         • current_max – the maximum sum of a subarray that ends at the current position.
         • global_max – the maximum sum found over all positions.
      4. Iterate through the array from the second element:
         • Update current_max = max(current element, current_max + current element).
         • Update global_max = max(global_max, current_max).
      5. After the loop, global_max holds the answer; output it.
    </planning>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Longest Increasing Subsequence (LIS) – O(n log n) Solution</title>
      Given a sequence of integers, find the length of the longest strictly increasing subsequence.
      An efficient solution uses a greedy strategy combined with binary search to maintain a
      list of the smallest possible tail values for increasing subsequences of each length.
    </description>
    <code>
import bisect

def lis_length(seq):
    # tails[i] will hold the smallest possible tail of an increasing subsequence of length i+1
    tails = []
    for x in seq:
        # Find the position where x can extend or replace a tail
        idx = bisect.bisect_left(tails, x)
        if idx == len(tails):
            tails.append(x)          # x extends the longest subsequence found so far
        else:
            tails[idx] = x           # x improves the tail for length idx+1
    return len(tails)

# Example driver
if __name__ == "__main__":
    t = int(input())
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(input())
        arr = list(map(int, input().split()))
        print(lis_length(arr))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read the number of test cases.
      2. For each test case, read the sequence.
      3. Maintain an array `tails`:
         • It is kept sorted.
         • Its length equals the length of the longest increasing subsequence found so far.
      4. For each element `x` in the sequence:
         • Use binary search (`bisect_left`) to locate the first element in `tails` ≥ `x`.
         • If such position is at the end, append `x` (we have a longer subsequence).
         • Otherwise replace `tails[pos]` with `x` (a better tail for that length).
      5. After processing all elements, `len(tails)` is the LIS length; output it.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Minimum Number of Platforms Required for a Railway Station</title>
      Given arrival and departure times of trains at a station, determine the minimum number of platforms
      needed so that no train has to wait. This classic scheduling problem can be solved greedily by
      sorting the events and scanning them in chronological order.
    </description>
    <code>
def min_platforms(arr, dep):
    # Sort both arrival and departure times
    arr.sort()
    dep.sort()
    n = len(arr)
    i = j = 0
    needed = max_needed = 0
    while i < n and j < n:
        # If next event is an arrival, we need an extra platform
        if arr[i] <= dep[j]:
            needed += 1
            i += 1
            max_needed = max(max_needed, needed)
        else:
            # A train departs, free a platform
            needed -= 1
            j += 1
    return max_needed

# Driver code
if __name__ == "__main__":
    t = int(input())
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(input())
        arr = list(map(int, input().split()))
        dep = list(map(int, input().split()))
        print(min_platforms(arr, dep))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. For each test case read `n`, the list of arrival times and the list of departure times.
      2. Sort both lists independently.
      3. Use two pointers `i` (arrival) and `j` (departure):
         • If the next arrival occurs before or at the same time as the next departure,
           increment the current platform count and move `i`.
         • Otherwise, decrement the count (a train left) and move `j`.
      4. Keep track of the maximum value of the current count – that is the answer.
      5. Output the maximum needed platforms.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Greedy Algorithm – Tutorial

    ## What is a Greedy Algorithm?
    A greedy algorithm builds a solution step‑by‑step, always choosing the locally optimal
    option at each step with the hope that this leads to a globally optimal solution.
    It does **not** backtrack; once a choice is made it is never reconsidered.

    ## When Can Greedy Work?
    Greedy works when the problem exhibits two key properties:

    1. **Greedy‑choice property** – an optimal solution can be constructed by making a
       locally optimal choice first.
    2. **Optimal‑substructure** – after fixing the greedy choice, the remaining sub‑problem
       must be of the same type as the original problem.

    Classic examples include:
    * Activity selection / interval scheduling
    * Huffman coding (building optimal prefix codes)
    * Minimum spanning tree (Kruskal, Prim)
    * Dijkstra’s shortest path (with non‑negative edges)
    * The “balance AB and BA substrings” problem – we only need to adjust at most one
      character because fixing a single position can change several adjacent substrings,
      satisfying the equality condition with the minimum number of steps.

    ## General Steps to Design a Greedy Solution
    1. **Understand the objective** – what are we trying to maximise/minimise?
    2. **Identify the decision points** – where do we have to make a choice?
    3. **Propose a greedy rule** – a simple rule that looks best locally (e.g., pick the
       earliest finishing activity, pick the smallest possible tail, pick the character that
       reduces the imbalance the most).
    4. **Prove correctness** (informally for contests):
       * Show that an optimal solution exists that starts with the greedy choice.
       * Show that after taking the greedy choice, the remaining problem is of the same
         form, so the same rule can be applied recursively.
    5. **Implement efficiently** – often the rule can be applied after sorting, using a
       priority queue, or with simple linear scans.

    ## Typical Greedy Patterns
    * **Sorting‑based** – sort items by a key that reflects the greedy priority (e.g., finish
      times, tail values, event times) and then scan once.
    * **Selection‑based** – repeatedly pick the best candidate from a set (e.g., smallest
      edge weight, nearest unvisited vertex) using a data structure like a heap.
    * **Replacement‑based** – maintain a structure (e.g., `tails` in LIS) and replace an
      element when a better one appears, guaranteeing the structure stays optimal.

    ## Pitfalls
    * Not all problems admit a greedy solution; a counter‑example often shows that the
      greedy choice can lead to a sub‑optimal result.
    * Ensure the greedy rule does not depend on future information that is not yet known.
    * When the problem involves constraints like “exactly k changes”, verify that the
      greedy step respects those constraints.

    ## Applying Greedy to the Original Problem
    The task is to make the number of substrings “ab” equal to the number of substrings “ba”
    by changing as few characters as possible. Observe:

    * The difference `diff = AB(s) - BA(s)` can only be `-n … n`.
    * Changing a single character can affect at most two adjacent substrings,
      therefore it can change `diff` by at most 2.
    * If `diff == 0` we need 0 steps.
    * If `diff != 0` we can always fix the imbalance with **one** change:
      - If `diff` is odd, flipping the first or last character eliminates the single
        extra occurrence.
      - If `diff` is even, flipping any character that lies between an “ab” and a “ba”
        reduces the difference by 2.

    Hence the greedy rule is: **perform at most one modification; if the counts are already
    equal, do nothing, otherwise change any character (commonly the first or last) to obtain
    equality.** This yields the minimum number of steps because any solution needs at least
    one change when `diff ≠ 0`, and one change is sufficient.

    ## Summary
    Greedy algorithms are powerful for problems where a locally optimal decision leads
    to a globally optimal solution. The key is to identify the greedy‑choice property and
    prove (or convincingly argue) that the choice never harms optimality. Once the rule
    is established, implementation is usually straightforward: sort, scan, or maintain a
    simple data structure while applying the rule.
  </algorithm>

**Plan**

1. **Read input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - Loop `t` times, reading string `s` for each case.

2. **Count substrings**
   - Initialise `ab = 0`, `ba = 0`.
   - For `i` from `0` to `len(s)-2`:
     - If `s[i] == 'a'` and `s[i+1] == 'b'` → `ab++`.
     - If `s[i] == 'b'` and `s[i+1] == 'a'` → `ba++`.

3. **Decide minimal modification**
   - If `ab == ba` → answer is the original string `s`.
   - Otherwise → we need exactly one change.
     - Flip the first character:
       - If `s[0] == 'a'` set `s[0] = 'b'`.
       - Else set `s[0] = 'a'`.
     - The resulting string now satisfies `AB = BA` (proof: changing the first character affects only the substring formed with the second character, adjusting the difference by ±1, which is enough because `|ab‑ba|` is always 1 for any string of length ≥2 when they are unequal).

4. **Output**
   - Print the resulting string for each test case on its own line.

5. **Complexity**
   - For each test case we scan the string once → `O(n)` time, `n ≤ 100`.
   - Only constant extra space.

Therefore a single change of the first character is sufficient and also minimal (zero changes when AB = BA).
