**Planning**

1. **Input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - Loop `t` times, reading string `s` (length `n`).

2. **Count substrings**
   - Initialise `ab = 0`, `ba = 0`.
   - For each index `i` from `0` to `n‑2`:
     - If `s[i] == 'a'` and `s[i+1] == 'b'` → `ab++`.
     - If `s[i] == 'b'` and `s[i+1] == 'a'` → `ba++`.

3. **Check balance**
   - Compute `diff = ab - ba`.
   - If `diff == 0` → the string is already balanced; output `s` and continue to next test case.

4. **Perform one optimal change**
   - The string length is at least 2 when `diff ≠ 0` (otherwise `ab = ba = 0`).
   - If `diff > 0` (more `ab` than `ba`):
     - Change the **first** character to `'b'`.
   - Else (`diff < 0`, more `ba` than `ab`):
     - Change the **first** character to `'a'`.
   - This single modification affects only the substring starting at position 0, decreasing `|diff|` by either 1 or 2, which is enough to make `ab == ba`.

5. **Output**
   - Print the modified string.

6. **Complexity**
   - For each test case we scan the string once → `O(n)` time, `O(1)` extra memory.  
   - With `n ≤ 100` and `t ≤ 1000` this easily fits limits.
<root>
<explanation>The plan is correct.  
For any binary string consisting of ‘a’ and ‘b’, the difference Δ = AB(s) − BA(s) can only be –1, 0 or 1, because each adjacent pair contributes +1 for “ab”, –1 for “ba” and 0 otherwise, and the sum telescopes to (first character == ‘a’) − (last character == ‘a’).  
Hence, if Δ = 0 the string is already balanced and no change is needed.  
If Δ = 1 (more “ab”), the string starts with ‘a’ and ends with ‘b’. Changing the first character to ‘b’ makes the first character ‘b’, so the new Δ becomes 0.  
If Δ = –1 (more “ba”), the string starts with ‘b’ and ends with ‘a’. Changing the first character to ‘a’ makes the first character ‘a’, again giving Δ = 0.  

Thus a single modification of the first character is sufficient and also necessary (when Δ ≠ 0), guaranteeing the minimal number of steps. The algorithm scans the string once to compute AB and BA, decides whether a change is needed, possibly flips the first character, and outputs the resulting string. This runs in O(n) time per test case with O(1) extra memory, satisfying the constraints.

Therefore the proposed plan correctly solves the problem.</explanation>

**Plan to solve the problem**

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   - Loop `t` times, reading the string `s` (length `n`, `1 ≤ n ≤ 100`).

2. **Utility: count AB and BA**  
   ```text
   cntAB = 0, cntBA = 0
   for i = 0 … n‑2
       if s[i] == 'a' and s[i+1] == 'b' → cntAB++
       if s[i] == 'b' and s[i+1] == 'a' → cntBA++
   diff = cntAB – cntBA          // can be negative
   ```

3. **If already balanced** (`diff == 0`)  
   - Output `s` unchanged and continue with next test case.

4. **Otherwise we need exactly one modification** (it is provable that one step is always enough).  
   We try a few simple candidates until we obtain a balanced string.

   *Candidate 1 – change the first character*  
   - Create `t = s` with `t[0]` flipped (`'a' → 'b'` or `'b' → 'a'`).  
   - Re‑count `cntAB, cntBA` for `t`.  
   - If `cntAB == cntBA` → output `t` and continue.

   *Candidate 2 – change the last character* (only if candidate 1 failed)  
   - Flip `s[n‑1]` similarly, recompute the counts, and output if balanced.

   *Candidate 3 – change a character at a border between different letters*  
   - Scan the string for an index `i` (`0 ≤ i < n‑1`) with `s[i] != s[i+1]`.  
   - Two possible flips:
        1. Set `t[i] = s[i+1]` (make the two equal).  
        2. Set `t[i+1] = s[i]`.  
   - For each of the two possibilities recompute the counts; the first one that yields equality is printed.  
   - Because `diff` is even in this situation, flipping a border changes the number of “ab” and “ba” substrings by exactly 2, thus it will balance the string.

   *Guarantee* – one of the three candidates always succeeds:
   - If `|diff|` is odd, flipping an endpoint changes the count by 1 → balance.
   - If `|diff|` is even, there exists at least one border (`ab` or `ba`) in the string (otherwise the whole string is uniform and `diff` would be 0). Flipping a border changes the count by 2 → balance.

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Counting substrings: `O(n)`.  
   - Trying the three candidates: each needs at most another `O(n)` count, so overall `O(n)` per test case.  
   - With `n ≤ 100` and `t ≤ 1000` the total work is negligible (`≤ 10⁵` operations).  
   - Memory usage: `O(n)` for the string copy.

6. **Output**  
   - For each test case print the resulting string on its own line.

7. **Implementation notes**  
   - Use 0‑based indexing for simplicity.  
   - Flipping a character: `newChar = (oldChar == 'a' ? 'b' : 'a')`.  
   - Write a small helper function `balanced(string)` that returns `true` iff `cntAB == cntBA`.  
   - Stop as soon as a balanced candidate is found; any valid answer is acceptable.
<root>
<explanation>The proposed plan correctly solves the problem.  
The key observation is that for any binary string consisting of ‘a’ and ‘b’, the difference Δ = AB(s) − BA(s) can only be –1, 0 or 1 (it equals 1 if the string starts with ‘a’ and ends with ‘b’, –1 if it starts with ‘b’ and ends with ‘a’, otherwise 0). Consequently, if the string is already balanced (Δ = 0) no changes are needed, and if it is not balanced a single character change is always sufficient to make Δ = 0.  

The algorithm first counts AB and BA in O(n). If they are equal it outputs the original string. Otherwise it tries a few simple modifications (flipping the first character, the last character, or a character at a border) and recomputes the counts; one of these modifications is guaranteed to balance the string because a single flip can change Δ by exactly ±1 (at an endpoint) or ±2 (at an interior border), and |Δ|≤1.  

The algorithm runs in O(n) per test case, uses O(n) memory, and respects all constraints (n ≤ 100, t ≤ 1000). Therefore the plan is correct and will produce a minimum‑step solution for every test case. 
</explanation>

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    out = []
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(t):
        s = list(data[idx])
        idx += 1
        ab = ba = 0
        for i in range(len(s) - 1):
            if s[i] == 'a' and s[i + 1] == 'b':
                ab += 1
            elif s[i] == 'b' and s[i + 1] == 'a':
                ba += 1
        if ab != ba and len(s) > 0:
            # flip first character
            s[0] = 'b' if s[0] == 'a' else 'a'
        out.append(''.join(s))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Keyboard Distance Sum</title>
      You are given a keyboard layout as a permutation of the 26 lowercase English letters.  
      For a given word `s`, compute the total time required to type it, where moving the hand
      from one key to another costs the absolute difference of their positions on the keyboard.
      The first character costs nothing to reach.  
      Constraints: up to 1000 test cases, each word length ≤ 50.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        layout = data[idx]          # 26‑character string
        word   = data[idx + 1]      # word to type
        idx += 2

        # 1️⃣ Build a dictionary: letter → position (0‑based)
        pos = {ch: i for i, ch in enumerate(layout)}

        # 2️⃣ Accumulate the distance between consecutive letters
        total = 0
        cur = pos[word[0]]          # hand starts on the first letter
        for ch in word[1:]:
            nxt = pos[ch]
            total += abs(nxt - cur)
            cur = nxt

        out.append(str(total))

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – use `sys.stdin.read()` for speed; split into tokens.  
      2. **Iterate over test cases** – each case supplies a 26‑char layout and a word.  
      3. **Create a position map** – a dictionary that stores the index of every letter in the layout.  
      4. **Traverse the word** – start from the first character (cost 0), then for each next character add `abs(position[next] - position[current])` to the answer.  
      5. **Collect results** – store each answer in a list and finally output them separated by newlines.  
      6. **Complexity analysis** – building the map is O(26) ≈ O(1); scanning the word is O(|s|). Overall O(|s|) per test case, well within limits.
    </planning>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Circular Keyboard Typing</title>
      A keyboard is arranged in a circle containing the 26 lowercase letters in a given order.
      Moving the hand from one key to another can be done clockwise or counter‑clockwise;
      the cost is the minimum number of steps along the circle.
      For a word `s`, compute the minimal total time to type it, starting on the first character.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    answers = []
    for _ in range(t):
        layout = next(it)          # 26‑char circular order
        word   = next(it)

        # position of each letter on the circle (0‑based)
        pos = {ch: i for i, ch in enumerate(layout)}
        n = 26                     # circle length

        total = 0
        cur = pos[word[0]]
        for ch in word[1:]:
            nxt = pos[ch]
            diff = abs(nxt - cur)
            total += min(diff, n - diff)   # shortest way around the circle
            cur = nxt
        answers.append(str(total))

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(answers))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Parse input** – same technique as before.  
      2. **Map letters to indices** on the circular layout.  
      3. **For each consecutive pair** compute the direct distance `d = |i‑j|`.  
      4. **Take the shorter way**: `min(d, 26‑d)`. Add it to the running total.  
      5. **Output** the accumulated time for each test case.  
      6. **Complexity** – O(|s|) per case, O(1) extra memory.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Numeric Keypad Distance</title>
      A classic mobile phone keypad has the digits 0‑9 arranged in a 3×4 grid:

      ```
      1 2 3
      4 5 6
      7 8 9
      * 0 #
      ```

      Each digit occupies a cell with Manhattan distance as movement cost.
      Given a string of digits `d` (length ≤ 100), compute the total time to type it,
      starting on the first digit (cost 0).  
      Example: typing “2580” costs `|2‑5|+|5‑8|+|8‑0| = 1+1+2 = 4`.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    # Pre‑compute coordinates for each digit
    coord = {
        '1': (0, 0), '2': (0, 1), '3': (0, 2),
        '4': (1, 0), '5': (1, 1), '6': (1, 2),
        '7': (2, 0), '8': (2, 1), '9': (2, 2),
        '*': (3, 0), '0': (3, 1), '#': (3, 2)
    }

    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        digits = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        total = 0
        cur = coord[digits[0]]
        for d in digits[1:]:
            nxt = coord[d]
            total += abs(nxt[0] - cur[0]) + abs(nxt[1] - cur[1])   # Manhattan distance
            cur = nxt
        out.append(str(total))

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Store coordinates** of each keypad symbol in a dictionary.  
      2. **Read each test case** (a digit string).  
      3. **Initialize** the current position with the first digit’s coordinates.  
      4. **Iterate** over the remaining digits, adding the Manhattan distance  
         `|x1‑x2| + |y1‑y2|` to the answer and updating the current position.  
      5. **Print** the accumulated distance for every test case.  
      6. **Complexity** – O(L) per case where L is the length of the digit string; O(1) extra space.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original “Keyboard Distance Sum” problem
    The task reduces to a **direct greedy computation**: the optimal way to type the word
    is uniquely determined by the given keyboard layout, because the hand must visit the
    keys in the exact order of the word. No alternative ordering or back‑tracking can
    reduce the total distance – each move is forced.

    ## Steps (generic for any similar problem)
    1. **Pre‑processing – mapping**  
       Build a lookup table `pos[letter] = index` that tells where each character lies
       on the keyboard. This is O(26) time and O(26) memory, effectively constant.

    2. **Iterate through the word**  
       - Start with the position of the first character (cost 0).  
       - For each subsequent character `c`, retrieve its position `p = pos[c]`.  
       - Add `abs(p - current_position)` to the answer.  
       - Update `current_position = p`.

    3. **Output** the accumulated sum.

    ## Why this works (Greedy justification)
    The hand’s movement cost is **additive** and **independent** of future moves:
    the cost of moving from key A to key B depends only on their positions, not on where
    we will go afterwards. Therefore, minimizing each individual move is equivalent to
    minimizing the total sum, and the only possible move at each step is the one forced
    by the next character of the word. Hence a simple linear scan yields the optimal
    answer.

    ## Complexity analysis
    *Time*: O(|s|) per test case, where |s| ≤ 50.  
    *Memory*: O(1) besides the fixed 26‑element map.

    ## Typical variations
    * **Circular keyboards** – replace `abs(p‑q)` with `min(abs(p‑q), 26‑abs(p‑q))`.  
    * **2‑D keyboards** – store `(row, col)` coordinates and use Manhattan or Euclidean
      distance instead of 1‑D absolute difference.  
    * **Multiple hands** – a dynamic programming approach may be required, but the
      single‑hand version stays greedy.

    The core idea remains: **translate characters to positions**, then **sum the
    distances** between consecutive positions. This pattern appears in many “typing”,
    “robot movement”, or “string navigation” problems.
  </algorithm>
</root>

     - `layout = tokens[idx]` (26‑char string), `idx += 1`.
     - `word   = tokens[idx]` (1‑50 chars), `idx += 1`.

2. **Build position map**
   - For each character `c` in `layout` at index `pos` (0‑based):
     - `pos_map[c] = pos`.

3. **Compute time for the word**
   - `total = 0`
   - `prev = pos_map[word[0]]`   (first move costs 0)
   - For each subsequent character `ch` in `word[1:]`:
     - `cur = pos_map[ch]`
     - `total += abs(cur - prev)`
     - `prev = cur`

4. **Store result**
   - Append `total` to answer list.

5. **Output**
   - Print each stored answer on its own line.

- Scanning word: `O(|s|)` ≤ `O(50)`.
- Overall per test case: `O(|s|)` time, `O(1)` extra memory.

      <title>Construct a Strictly Increasing Sequence with No Consecutive Divisibility</title>
      Given an integer <code>n</code>, output any sequence <code>a₁, a₂, …, aₙ</code> such that:
      <ul>
        <li>1 ≤ aᵢ ≤ 10⁹ for every i.</li>
        <li>a₁ < a₂ < … < aₙ (strictly increasing).</li>
        <li>For every i ≥ 2, aᵢ is **not** divisible by aᵢ₋₁.</li>

      It is guaranteed that a solution exists for all 1 ≤ n ≤ 1000.
    </description>

# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys

def build_sequence(n):
    # A simple construction: use consecutive odd numbers.
    # Odd numbers are never multiples of each other because the only way
    # x divides y when both are odd is if y = k*x with k odd.
    # By taking consecutive odds we guarantee k = 1 (which would mean equality)
    # or k ≥ 3, which makes y ≥ 3*x > next odd number.
    # Hence a_i will never be a multiple of a_{i-1}.
    seq = [2*i + 1 for i in range(n)]   # 1,3,5,7,...
    return seq

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        seq = build_sequence(n)
        out_lines.append(' '.join(map(str, seq)))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read input** – number of test cases <t> and each <code>n</code>.
      2. **Construct a sequence** – choose a simple arithmetic progression that never creates a divisor relationship.
         * The odd numbers 1, 3, 5, … work because any multiple of an odd number is at least three times larger, so the next odd number can’t be a multiple of the previous one.
      3. **Generate the first n odd numbers** using the formula <code>2*i + 1</code> for <code>i = 0 … n‑1</code>.
      4. **Print** the sequence for each test case on its own line.
      5. **Complexity** – O(n) per test case, O(1) extra memory.
    </planning>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Output the First n Prime Numbers</title>
      For a given integer <code>n</code> (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000) print the first <code>n</code> prime numbers in increasing order.
      Each prime must be ≤ 10⁹, which is easily satisfied because the 1000‑th prime is 7919.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
import sys
import math

def is_prime(x):
    if x < 2:
        return False
    if x % 2 == 0:
        return x == 2
    r = int(math.isqrt(x))
    for d in range(3, r+1, 2):
        if x % d == 0:
            return False
    return True

def first_n_primes(n):
    primes = []
    candidate = 2
    while len(primes) < n:
        if is_prime(candidate):
            primes.append(candidate)
        candidate += 1
    return primes

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        out.append(' '.join(map(str, first_n_primes(n))))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. **Read** the number of test cases and each <code>n</code>.
      2. **Prime test** – implement a deterministic trial division up to √x (fast enough for x ≤ 10⁴).
      3. **Generate primes** sequentially:
         * Start from 2, test each integer, append to list when prime.
         * Stop when we have collected <code>n</code> primes.
      4. **Print** the collected primes separated by spaces for each test case.
      5. **Complexity** – O(n·√p) where p is the n‑th prime (≈ 8000 for n = 1000), well within limits.
    </planning>
  </problem>

    # gcd(k, k+1) = 1 for any integer k.

  <algorithm>
    # Tutorial: Greedy Construction for Sequence‑Generation Problems

    Many competitive‑programming tasks ask you to build a sequence that satisfies a set of local
    constraints (e.g., “the i‑th element must not be divisible by the (i‑1)-th”, “adjacent elements must be coprime”, “the sequence must be strictly increasing”).  
    A powerful technique for such problems is **greedy construction**:

    1. **Understand the Local Constraint**  
       Identify what relationship is required between consecutive elements.  
       Example constraints:  
       * `a_i` must not be a multiple of `a_{i‑1}`.  
       * `gcd(a_i, a_{i‑1}) = 1`.  
       * `a_i` must be larger than `a_{i‑1}` by at least a fixed amount.

    2. **Find a Simple Infinite Family**  
       Look for a mathematical family of numbers that *always* satisfies the constraint when taken
       in order.  
       * Odd numbers → never a multiple of the previous odd (except equality).  
       * Consecutive integers → always coprime because `gcd(k, k+1)=1`.  
       * Powers of two → each is a multiple of the previous, so they are **not** suitable for the “not divisible” condition, but they are useful for other constraints.

    3. **Choose a Starting Point Within Bounds**  
       The problem usually limits each element to ≤ 10⁹. Pick the smallest member of the family that
       respects the lower bound (often 1 or 2). Because the family grows predictably, you can be sure
       that the first `n` members stay inside the upper bound for the given limits (e.g., the 1000‑th odd number is 1999 < 10⁹).

    4. **Generate the First n Elements**  
       Use a direct formula (e.g., `2*i+1` for odds, `i+2` for consecutive integers) or a simple loop.
       This step is O(n) and requires only constant extra memory.

    5. **Proof of Correctness (Optional but Helpful)**  
       *Strictly increasing*: the formula produces a monotonic sequence.  
       *Local constraint*: argue mathematically why any two consecutive members of the chosen family satisfy the required property.  
       *Bounds*: show that the nth element ≤ 10⁹ for the maximum n in the problem.

    6. **Complexity Analysis**  
       Greedy construction typically runs in linear time O(n) and uses O(1) auxiliary space, which easily meets the limits for n up to 10⁴ or 10⁵.

    7. **When Greedy Fails**  
       If no simple infinite family exists, you may need a more elaborate method (e.g., sieve for primes, backtracking, or dynamic programming).  
       Still, start by trying to reduce the problem to a known family; many constraints are satisfied by primes, powers, or arithmetic progressions.

    **Key Takeaway**  
    For sequence‑generation problems with only *adjacent* constraints, look for a deterministic, monotonic number family that inherently respects the constraint. Construct the required prefix of that family greedily, and you obtain a correct, efficient solution without any heavy algorithmic machinery.  
  </algorithm>
</root>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p>**Problem 1 – Compare Two Numbers Given as (x, p) Pairs**</p>
      <p>You are given two pairs (x₁, p₁) and (x₂, p₂).  
      Each pair represents the integer <code>x × 10^p</code> (i.e., the decimal representation of <code>x</code> with <code>p</code> zeros appended).  
      The values of <code>x</code> are at most 10⁶, while <code>p</code> can be as large as 10⁶, so the actual numbers may not fit in standard 64‑bit types.  
      For each test case you must output “&lt;”, “&gt;” or “=”, indicating whether the first number is smaller, larger, or equal to the second one.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys

      def normalize(x, p):
          """
          Remove trailing zeros from x and decrease p accordingly.
          Returns (new_x, new_p) where new_x has no factor 10.
          """
          while x % 10 == 0 and p > 0:
              x //= 10
              p -= 1
          return x, p

      def compare(x1, p1, x2, p2):
          # First, make the total length (digits + zeros) comparable
          len1 = len(str(x1)) + p1
          len2 = len(str(x2)) + p2
          if len1 < len2:
              return '&lt;'
          if len1 > len2:
              return '&gt;'

          # Lengths are equal – we can safely bring the exponents to the same value
          # Reduce both numbers by removing common trailing zeros
          x1, p1 = normalize(x1, p1)
          x2, p2 = normalize(x2, p2)

          # After normalization the remaining p values are small (≤ 6)
          # Bring both to the same exponent by multiplying the smaller x
          if p1 < p2:
              x1 *= 10 ** (p2 - p1)
          elif p2 < p1:
              x2 *= 10 ** (p1 - p2)

          # Now compare the integer parts directly
          if x1 < x2:
              return '&lt;'
          if x1 > x2:
              return '&gt;'
          return '='

      def solve():
          it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
          t = int(next(it))
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              x1 = int(next(it)); p1 = int(next(it))
              x2 = int(next(it)); p2 = int(next(it))
              out.append(compare(x1, p1, x2, p2))
          sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Read all input values efficiently.</li>
        <li>For each test case compute the total number of digits: <code>len(str(x)) + p</code>.</li>
        <li>If the total lengths differ, the longer one is larger – output the result immediately.</li>
        <li>If lengths are equal, remove common trailing zeros from both numbers (function <code>normalize</code>).</li>
        <li>After normalization the remaining exponents are at most 6, so we can safely multiply the smaller <code>x</code> by <code>10^{|p1‑p2|}</code> using Python’s arbitrary‑precision integers.</li>
        <li>Finally compare the two integer values and output “&lt;”, “&gt;” or “=”.</li>
      </ol>
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p>**Problem 2 – Multiply Two Numbers Given as (x, p) Pairs**</p>
      <p>Each number is described by a pair (x, p) meaning <code>x × 10^p</code>.  
      Given two such pairs, compute the product and output it in the same format: the product’s mantissa (an integer without trailing zeros) and the number of zeros appended.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys

      def trim_zeros(num):
          """Return (mantissa, zeros) where mantissa has no trailing zero."""
          zeros = 0
          while num % 10 == 0 and num != 0:
              num //= 10
              zeros += 1
          return num, zeros

      def multiply(x1, p1, x2, p2):
          # Multiply mantissas
          mant = x1 * x2
          # Add exponents
          zeros = p1 + p2
          # Remove trailing zeros from mantissa and add them to exponent
          mant, extra = trim_zeros(mant)
          zeros += extra
          return mant, zeros

      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              x1 = int(data[idx]); p1 = int(data[idx+1]); idx += 2
              x2 = int(data[idx]); p2 = int(data[idx+1]); idx += 2
              m, z = multiply(x1, p1, x2, p2)
              out.append(f"{m} {z}")
          sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Read the number of test cases and the four integers for each case.</li>

        <li>Add the two exponents <code>p₁ + p₂</code> to obtain the total number of zeros.</li>
        <li>Remove any trailing zeros from the product mantissa (function <code>trim_zeros</code>) and transfer those zeros to the exponent.</li>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p>**Problem 3 – Check Whether the Sum of Two (x, p) Numbers Exceeds a Threshold**</p>
      <p>For each test case you are given two numbers described by (x₁, p₁) and (x₂, p₂) and a threshold value <code>T</code> (given as a normal integer).  
      Determine if <code>(x₁·10^{p₁} + x₂·10^{p₂}) > T</code>. Output “YES” if the sum is larger, otherwise “NO”.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys

      def align(x, p, target_len):
          """
          Return x * 10^{p} as an integer whose total length equals target_len.
          If the current length is smaller, pad with zeros; if larger, truncate
          (which never happens in this problem because we only increase length).
          """
          cur_len = len(str(x)) + p
          if cur_len < target_len:
              # Need to add (target_len - cur_len) zeros
              return x * (10 ** (target_len - cur_len))
          return x * (10 ** p)

      def solve():
          data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
          t = int(data[0])
          idx = 1
          out = []
          for _ in range(t):
              x1 = int(data[idx]); p1 = int(data[idx+1])
              x2 = int(data[idx+2]); p2 = int(data[idx+3])
              T  = int(data[idx+4])
              idx += 5

              # Determine the maximum length of the two numbers
              len1 = len(str(x1)) + p1
              len2 = len(str(x2)) + p2
              max_len = max(len1, len2)

              # Bring both numbers to the same length (as integers)
              a = align(x1, p1, max_len)
              b = align(x2, p2, max_len)

              # Now the sum fits safely in Python's big integer
              total = a + b
              if total > T:
                  out.append("YES")
              else:
                  out.append("NO")
          sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

      if __name__ == "__main__":
          solve()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Read all input values.</li>
        <li>Compute the total digit length of each (x, p) number.</li>
        <li>Choose the larger length as a common scale.</li>
        <li>Convert each number to an integer with that common scale using the <code>align</code> helper (multiply by the required power of ten).</li>
        <li>Add the two scaled integers; Python’s arbitrary‑precision integers guarantee correctness.</li>
        <li>Compare the sum with the given threshold <code>T</code> and output “YES” or “NO”.</li>
      </ol>
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    <p><strong>Algorithm for the original problem (comparing two numbers given as (x, p) pairs)</strong></p>
    <p>The core difficulty is that the actual values <code>x·10^p</code> can be astronomically large (up to 10^{1,000,006}), far beyond the range of built‑in numeric types.  
    The comparison can be performed without constructing the full numbers by using two simple observations:</p>
    <ol>
      <li><strong>Length comparison.</strong>  
      The number of decimal digits of <code>x·10^p</code> equals <code>len(str(x)) + p</code>.  
      If the total lengths differ, the longer one is strictly larger, because both numbers are positive.</li>
      <li><strong>Equal length case.</strong>  
      When the lengths are identical, the exponents can be balanced.  
      First, strip common trailing zeros from both mantissas: while <code>x % 10 == 0</code> and <code>p > 0</code>, divide <code>x</code> by 10 and decrement <code>p</code>.  
      After this reduction, the remaining exponents are at most the number of digits of the original mantissa (≤ 6 for the given constraints).  
      Now we can safely equalize the exponents by multiplying the smaller mantissa by <code>10^{|p1‑p2|}</code>.  
      Python’s integers are arbitrary‑precision, so this multiplication never overflows.  
      Finally, compare the two integer mantissas directly.</li>
    </ol>
    <p><strong>Why this works</strong></p>
    <ul>
      <li>Appending zeros only changes the magnitude by a power of ten; the relative order of two numbers is determined first by the total number of digits.</li>
      <li>Removing common trailing zeros does not affect the value but reduces the exponent, making the remaining exponent small enough to handle explicitly.</li>
      <li>Balancing the exponents converts the problem to a plain integer comparison, which is trivial for arbitrary‑precision languages.</li>
    </ul>
    <p><strong>General tutorial for similar “large‑number‑represented‑by‑mantissa‑and‑exponent” problems</strong></p>
    <ul>
      <li><em>Step 1 – Work with the representation, not the value.</em> Keep numbers as <code>(mantissa, exponent)</code> pairs.</li>
      <li><em>Step 2 – Compare lengths.</em> For decimal representation, length = <code>digits(mantissa) + exponent</code>. Different lengths give the answer immediately.</li>
      <li><em>Step 3 – Normalise.</em> Remove factors of the base (10 for decimal) that appear in both mantissa and exponent. This reduces the exponent to a manageable size.</li>
      <li><em>Step 4 – Align exponents.</em> If exponents differ after normalisation, multiply the smaller mantissa by the appropriate power of the base to make exponents equal.</li>
      <li><em>Step 5 – Use arbitrary‑precision arithmetic.</em> Languages like Python, Java (BigInteger), or C++ (Boost.Multiprecision) can store the resulting mantissas safely.</li>
      <li><em>Step 6 – Perform the final comparison/addition/multiplication.</em> With equal exponents, operations reduce to ordinary integer operations.</li>
    </ul>
    <p>This pattern (length check → normalisation → exponent alignment → integer operation) is a common technique for problems involving very large numbers expressed in scientific or “mantissa + trailing zeros” form.</p>
  </algorithm>
</root>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Load Balancing on Servers</title>
      You are given <code>m</code> tasks, each with a processing time <code>t_i</code>. There are <code>k</code> identical servers. In one move you can assign any task to any server. The load of a server is the sum of processing times of tasks assigned to it. The goal is to minimize the maximum load among all servers after all tasks are assigned. Output the minimal possible maximum load.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def min_max_load(tasks, k):
    """
    tasks: list of integers (processing times)
    k: number of servers
    Returns the minimal possible maximum load after assigning all tasks.
    """
    # The total work is fixed.
    total = sum(tasks)
    # The lower bound of the answer is at least ceil(total / k)
    # and also at least the largest single task (cannot split a task).
    lo = max(max(tasks), (total + k - 1) // k)
    # Upper bound can be total (put everything on one server).
    hi = total

    # Binary search on the answer.
    while lo < hi:
        mid = (lo + hi) // 2
        # Check if we can assign tasks so that no server exceeds 'mid'.
        if feasible(tasks, k, mid):
            hi = mid
        else:
            lo = mid + 1
    return lo

def feasible(tasks, k, limit):
    """
    Greedy check: try to fill servers one by one.
    """
    servers_used = 1
    current_load = 0
    for t in tasks:
        if current_load + t <= limit:
            current_load += t
        else:
            servers_used += 1
            current_load = t
            if servers_used > k:
                return False
    return True

# Example usage:
if __name__ == "__main__":
    m, k = map(int, input().split())
    tasks = list(map(int, input().split()))
    print(min_max_load(tasks, k))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read <code>m</code> and <code>k</code>, then the list of task times.
      2. Compute total sum and the maximum single task – they give a lower bound.
      3. Perform binary search between lower and upper bounds.
      4. For each candidate limit, run a greedy feasibility test:
         - Scan tasks in order, keep adding to the current server until the limit would be exceeded.
         - Start a new server and continue.
         - If more than <code>k</code> servers are needed, the limit is infeasible.
      5. The smallest feasible limit is the answer; output it.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Equalizing Array Elements with Increment/Decrement Operations</title>
      You have an array <code>a</code> of length <code>n</code>. In one operation you may choose any index <code>i</code> and either increase <code>a_i</code> by 1 or decrease it by 1. Each operation costs 1 unit. Determine the minimum total cost required to make all elements of the array equal.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def min_cost_to_equalize(arr):
    """
    The optimal target value is any median of the array.
    The minimal total number of +/-1 operations equals the sum of absolute differences
    to the median.
    """
    arr_sorted = sorted(arr)
    n = len(arr)
    median = arr_sorted[n // 2]          # works for both odd and even n
    cost = sum(abs(x - median) for x in arr)
    return cost

# Example usage:
if __name__ == "__main__":
    n = int(input())
    arr = list(map(int, input().split()))
    print(min_cost_to_equalize(arr))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Read <code>n</code> and the array.
      2. Sort a copy of the array to locate the median.
      3. For odd <code>n</code>, the median is the middle element; for even <code>n</code>, any value between the two middle elements yields the same minimal cost, so we can pick the lower middle.
      4. Compute the sum of absolute differences between each element and the chosen median – this is exactly the number of +/-1 operations needed.
      5. Output the computed sum.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Balancing Coins in Piles</title>
      There are <code>n</code> piles of coins, the <code>i</code>-th pile contains <code>c_i</code> coins. In one move you may take a single coin from any pile and place it onto any other pile. Find the minimum possible difference between the largest and smallest pile after any number of moves.
    </description>
    <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

def min_ugliness(piles):
    """
    The total number of coins is fixed.
    After redistribution each pile will contain either floor(avg) or ceil(avg) coins.
    Hence the minimal possible max-min difference is:
        0 if total % n == 0
        1 otherwise
    """
    total = sum(piles)
    n = len(piles)
    return 0 if total % n == 0 else 1

# Example usage:
if __name__ == "__main__":
    t = int(input())
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(input())
        piles = list(map(int, input().split()))
        print(min_ugliness(piles))
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. For each test case read <code>n</code> and the list of pile sizes.
      2. Compute the sum <code>S</code> of all coins.
      3. If <code>S</code> is divisible by <code>n</code>, we can make every pile exactly <code>S/n</code> → ugliness 0.
      4. Otherwise, the best we can do is to have some piles with ⌊S/n⌋ coins and the rest with ⌈S/n⌉ coins, giving a difference of 1.
      5. Output 0 or 1 accordingly.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    # Algorithm for the original problem (minimum possible ugliness after moving blocks)

    The operation allows us to transfer a single unit from any tower to any other tower arbitrarily many times.
    Therefore the only invariant is the total number of blocks **S = Σ a_i**.
    After redistribution each tower can end up with any non‑negative integer height as long as the sum of all heights remains **S**.

    The optimal configuration is to make the heights as equal as possible:
    * Let **q = ⌊S / n⌋** and **r = S mod n**.
    * We can give **r** towers height **q + 1** and the remaining **n‑r** towers height **q**.
    * In this arrangement the tallest tower is **q + 1** (if **r > 0**) and the shortest is **q**.
    * Hence the minimal possible ugliness is  
      **0** when **r = 0** (perfectly equal heights)  
      **1** when **r > 0** (difference between q+1 and q).

    This reasoning leads to a **greedy / constructive** solution that runs in **O(n)** per test case:
    1. Compute the sum of the array.
    2. Check the remainder of the sum when divided by **n**.
    3. Output **0** if the remainder is zero, otherwise output **1**.

    ## Generic Greedy Tutorial for Balancing / Equalization Problems
    Many problems ask for the smallest possible range (max‑min) after unlimited redistribution of a conserved quantity (blocks, coins, workload, etc.). The typical steps are:

    1. **Identify the invariant** – usually the total amount of the resource.
    2. **Compute the ideal average** – `avg = total / n`. Because we work with integers, consider `floor(avg)` and `ceil(avg)`.
    3. **Determine how many items must take the higher value** – the remainder `r = total % n`. Exactly `r` positions will have `ceil(avg)`, the rest `floor(avg)`.
    4. **Derive the minimal possible range** –  
       * If `r == 0` → all values equal → range = 0.  
       * Otherwise → the highest value exceeds the lowest by exactly 1 → range = 1.
    5. **Justify feasibility** – show a constructive way (e.g., repeatedly move from a higher to a lower tower) to reach the configuration described in step 3.

    This greedy reasoning works because the operation permits moving a single unit between any two positions, giving us complete freedom to rearrange the multiset of values while preserving the sum. No more sophisticated techniques (DP, graph algorithms, etc.) are needed.

    **Complexity:** O(n) time to compute the sum, O(1) extra memory.
  </algorithm>

    <problem>
        <description>
            <title>Lexicographically Smallest Permutation Avoiding a Forbidden Subsequence of Length 2</title>
            <p>
                You are given a string <code>S</code> consisting of lowercase English letters and a forbidden
                subsequence <code>T</code> of length 2 (e.g., "ab").  
                You may permute the characters of <code>S</code> arbitrarily.
                Find the lexicographically smallest permutation of <code>S</code> such that <code>T</code> does NOT appear
                as a subsequence.
            </p>
            <p>
                <b>Input</b>:  
                The first line contains an integer <code>q</code> – the number of test cases.  
                Each test case consists of two lines: the string <code>S</code> and the string <code>T</code> (|T| = 2).  
                <b>Output</b>: For each test case output the required permutation.
            </p>
        </description>
        <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

import sys

def smallest_without_two_seq(s, t):
    # Count frequencies of each character
    cnt = [0] * 26
    for ch in s:
        cnt[ord(ch) - ord('a')] += 1

    a, b = t[0], t[1]

    # If a == b, the forbidden subsequence is just two equal letters.
    # In that case any permutation works because a subsequence needs two positions,
    # and we can place all a's together – the subsequence will still exist.
    # The only safe way is to output the normal sorted string.
    if a == b:
        return ''.join(chr(i + ord('a')) * cnt[i] for i in range(26))

    # General case a != b
    # The only dangerous ordering is all a's before any b.
    # To avoid a before b, we can either:
    #   1) place all b's before a's, or
    #   2) interleave them (but interleaving would increase lexicographic value).
    # The lexicographically smallest string is obtained by:
    #   - output all characters < a in normal order,
    #   - then output all b's,
    #   - then all a's,
    #   - then the rest of the characters > b in normal order.
    # However, we must be careful when there are characters between a and b.
    # The standard solution used in Codeforces problem 1731A is:
    #   If a < b and cnt[a] > 0 and cnt[b] > 0 and t == "abc" (special case),
    #   we output a's, then c's, then b's, then the rest.
    # For length‑2 we simply put b before a.

    result = []

    # characters smaller than min(a,b) are safe, output them sorted
    for i in range(26):
        ch = chr(i + ord('a'))
        if ch < min(a, b):
            result.append(ch * cnt[i])

    # now output the larger of the two letters first
    first, second = (b, a) if a < b else (a, b)
    result.append(first * cnt[ord(first) - ord('a')])
    result.append(second * cnt[ord(second) - ord('a')])

    # finally output the remaining characters larger than max(a,b)
    for i in range(26):
        ch = chr(i + ord('a'))
        if ch > max(a, b):
            result.append(ch * cnt[i])

    return ''.join(result)

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    q = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(q):
        s = data[idx]; idx += 1
        t = data[idx]; idx += 1
        out.append(smallest_without_two_seq(s, t))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
        </code>
        <planning>
            1. **Read all test cases** – use fast I/O because the total length can be large.  
            2. **Count frequencies** of each character in <code>S</code> (array of size 26).  
            3. **Handle the trivial case** where the two letters of <code>T</code> are equal – any sorted permutation works.  
            4. **Determine the safe order**: to avoid the subsequence <code>xy</code> we must never have an <code>x</code> before a <code>y</code>.  
               The lexicographically smallest way is to place the larger letter first, then the smaller one, while keeping all other letters in normal sorted order.  
            5. **Construct the answer** by concatenating:
               * all letters smaller than both <code>x</code> and <code>y</code>,
               * the larger forbidden letter repeated its count,
               * the smaller forbidden letter repeated its count,
               * all letters larger than both.
            6. **Print the result** for each test case.  
            The algorithm runs in O(|S| + 26) per test case, i.e. linear time.
        </planning>
    </problem>

    <problem>
        <description>
            <title>Minimum Deletions to Eliminate a Forbidden Subsequence</title>
            <p>
                Given a string <code>S</code> (lowercase letters) and a pattern <code>P</code> (length ≤ 5),
                find the minimum number of characters that must be deleted from <code>S</code> so that <code>P</code>
                is no longer a subsequence of the resulting string.
            </p>
            <p>
                <b>Input</b>:  
                The first line contains an integer <code>T</code> – number of test cases.  
                Each test case contains two lines: <code>S</code> and <code>P</code>.  
                <b>Output</b>: For each test case output a single integer – the minimal deletions required.
            </p>
        </description>
        <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

import sys

def min_deletions_to_break_subseq(s, p):
    # DP[i][j] = minimal deletions after processing first i characters of s
    # such that we have matched first j characters of p as a subsequence.
    # j ranges from 0..len(p). When j == len(p) we have already formed the pattern,
    # which we must avoid, so we treat that state as INF.
    INF = 10**9
    m = len(p)
    dp = [0] + [INF] * m   # before reading any character, we have matched 0 chars with 0 deletions

    for ch in s:
        new_dp = dp[:]  # option: delete current character (cost +1)
        # delete case
        for j in range(m + 1):
            if dp[j] + 1 < new_dp[j]:
                new_dp[j] = dp[j] + 1

        # keep case
        for j in range(m):
            if ch == p[j]:
                # if we keep it, we advance the matched prefix
                if dp[j] < new_dp[j + 1]:
                    new_dp[j + 1] = dp[j]
            else:
                # character does not help the pattern, state stays the same
                if dp[j] < new_dp[j]:
                    new_dp[j] = dp[j]

        # state where pattern already matched (j == m) is forbidden,
        # we keep it as INF (cannot allow)
        new_dp[m] = INF
        dp = new_dp

    # answer is minimal deletions among states that have NOT completed the pattern
    return min(dp[:m])

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(t):
        s = data[idx]; idx += 1
        p = data[idx]; idx += 1
        out.append(str(min_deletions_to_break_subseq(s, p)))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
        </code>
        <planning>
            1. **Dynamic Programming** – we keep track of how many characters of the pattern <code>P</code> have already been matched while scanning <code>S</code>.  
            2. For each character of <code>S</code> we have two choices: delete it (cost + 1) or keep it.  
            3. If we keep it and it equals the next needed character of <code>P</code>, the matched prefix length increases by one.  
            4. The state where the whole pattern is matched is forbidden; we assign it an infinite cost.  
            5. After processing the whole string, the answer is the smallest cost among all states that have not completed the pattern.  
            6. Complexity: O(|S| · |P|) time, O(|P|) memory – easily fast for |P| ≤ 5.
        </planning>
    </problem>

    <problem>
        <description>
            <title>Lexicographically Smallest Anagram Not Containing a Given Substring</title>
            <p>
                You are given a string <code>S</code> (lowercase letters) and a forbidden substring <code>F</code>
                (length ≤ 3). You may permute the characters of <code>S</code> arbitrarily.
                Output the lexicographically smallest permutation of <code>S</code> that does NOT contain <code>F</code>
                as a contiguous substring.
            </p>
            <p>
                <b>Input</b>:  
                First line – integer <code>Q</code>, number of test cases.  
                Each test case – two lines: <code>S</code> and <code>F</code>.  
                <b>Output</b>: For each test case print the required permutation.
            </p>
        </description>
        <code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

import sys
from collections import Counter

def smallest_anagram_without_substring(s, f):
    cnt = Counter(s)
    # If the forbidden string contains a character that does not appear in s,
    # then the normal sorted string is already safe.
    if any(ch not in cnt for ch in f):
        return ''.join(sorted(s))

    # For length 1 the problem is trivial – we just need to avoid that character at the start.
    if len(f) == 1:
        # put all other letters first, then the forbidden one(s)
        first_part = ''.join(ch * cnt[ch] for ch in sorted(cnt) if ch != f)
        return first_part + f * cnt[f]

    # For length 2 or 3 we can try a greedy construction:
    # 1. Sort all characters.
    # 2. If the sorted string already does not contain f, return it.
    # 3. Otherwise, we need to rearrange the first occurrence of f[0].
    #    The classic trick (used in Codeforces 1731B) is:
    #    - If f[0] < f[1] and cnt[f[0]] > 0 and cnt[f[1]] > 0,
    #      we output all f[0]s, then all characters > f[0] except f[1],
    #      then f[1]s, then the rest.
    #    - For length 3 we extend the same idea using the order of the three letters.

    sorted_chars = ''.join(ch * cnt[ch] for ch in sorted(cnt))
    if f not in sorted_chars:
        return sorted_chars

    # Special handling when f has distinct letters and its first two letters are in increasing order.
    a, b = f[0], f[1]
    if a < b and cnt[a] > 0 and cnt[b] > 0:
        # Build answer: a's, then all letters > a except b, then b's, then remaining.
        res = []
        res.append(a * cnt[a])
        for ch in sorted(cnt):
            if ch > a and ch != b:
                res.append(ch * cnt[ch])
        res.append(b * cnt[b])
        # Append any remaining letters (including those equal to a or b already used)
        for ch in sorted(cnt):
            if ch == a or ch == b:
                continue
            # already added above
        return ''.join(res)

    # Fallback: just return the sorted string (should be safe for other cases)
    return sorted_chars

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    q = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out = []
    for _ in range(q):
        s = data[idx]; idx += 1
        f = data[idx]; idx += 1
        out.append(smallest_anagram_without_substring(s, f))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
        </code>
        <planning>
            1. **Count characters** of <code>S</code>.  
            2. **Check trivial safety** – if any character of <code>F</code> is missing, the ordinary sorted string works.  
            3. **Sort the whole string**; if the sorted string does not contain <code>F</code> as a substring, we are done.  
            4. When the sorted string contains the forbidden substring, we need a controlled rearrangement.  
               The classic greedy trick for patterns like “abc” is to output all copies of the first
               character, then all characters that are lexicographically larger but not the second
               character, then the second character, and finally the rest.  
            5. Build the answer according to the rule above; this yields the lexicographically smallest
               permutation that breaks the contiguous occurrence of <code>F</code>.  
            6. Output the constructed string.  
            The algorithm runs in O(|S| + alphabet) time.
        </planning>
    </problem>

    <algorithm>
        # Greedy Algorithm for Lexicographically Smallest Permutation Avoiding a Forbidden Subsequence

        ## When to use a greedy approach
        Greedy algorithms are appropriate when a problem can be solved by making a
        locally optimal choice at each step and this choice leads to a globally optimal
        solution.  Typical characteristics are:
        * The decision at a position does not affect the feasibility of later decisions
          except through a simple, monotone condition.
        * The objective (here: lexicographic minimality) can be expressed as “make the
          earliest possible character as small as possible”.

        ## Problem restatement
        *We have a multiset of characters (the letters of `S`).  
        We must output a permutation of this multiset that is lexicographically smallest
        while guaranteeing that a given three‑letter pattern `T` (a permutation of “abc”)
        does **not** appear as a subsequence.*

        ## Key observation
        For a three‑letter pattern `T = t1 t2 t3` the only way it can become a subsequence
        is if the letters appear in the order `t1` → `t2` → `t3` somewhere in the final
        string.  All other orders are safe.

        The only dangerous situation is when **all** occurrences of `t1` are placed
        before **any** occurrence of `t2`, and those `t2` are placed before **any**
        occurrence of `t3`.  If we break this chain at any point, the subsequence
        disappears.

        Because the alphabet is fixed (`a`‑`z`) we can treat the three letters of `T`
        specially and keep the rest of the characters in normal sorted order.

        ## Greedy construction
        1. **Count frequencies** of every character in `S`.  
        2. If `T` is not exactly `"abc"` (i.e. its letters are not in increasing order),
           the ordinary sorted string already avoids `T`.  The reason: the sorted order
           will place the smallest of the three letters first, then the middle one,
           then the largest – which is the *same* order as `T` only when `T` equals
           `"abc"`.  For any other permutation the sorted string breaks the required
           order, so it is the answer.
        3. When `T == "abc"` we have a special case:
           *If there is at least one `'a'`, one `'b'`, and one `'c'` in `S`*,
           the plain sorted string (`aaa…bbb…ccc…`) would contain the subsequence
           `abc`.  To avoid it while staying lexicographically minimal we:
           - output all `'a'`s,
           - then output all `'c'`s,
           - then output all `'b'`s,
           - finally output the remaining letters (`d` … `z`) in normal order.
           This order is the smallest possible because any string that puts a
           character smaller than `'c'` after the `'a'`s would be larger lexicographically.
        4. If any of the three letters is missing, the sorted string cannot contain
           `abc`, so we simply output the sorted string.

        ## Why the greedy choice is optimal
        *Lexicographic minimality* cares first about the earliest position where two
        candidates differ.  By keeping all characters **smaller than** `'a'` (if they
        exist) at the very beginning we cannot improve the answer – they must appear
        first in any permutation.  
        After those, the next smallest character we are allowed to place is `'a'`.
        We must place all `'a'`s now; postponing any `'a'` would only insert a larger
        character earlier, making the string larger.

        The conflict appears between `'b'` and `'c'`.  In the sorted order `'b'`
        would come before `'c'`, creating the forbidden subsequence.  Swapping the
        whole block of `'c'` before `'b'` is the minimal disturbance:
        * we keep the prefix (`…a…`) unchanged,
        * we replace the next block (`b…c…`) by the lexicographically smallest
          alternative that breaks the order, which is `c…b…`.

        Any other rearrangement would either:
        * keep `b` before `c` → subsequence appears, or
        * move some character `< 'c'` (i.e. `'b'`) after a larger character,
          which makes the string lexicographically larger than the `c…b` swap.

        Hence the described greedy construction yields the globally optimal answer.

        ## General steps of the greedy solution
        1. Count frequencies of each letter.
        2. If `T` is not `"abc"` **or** at least one of `'a','b','c'` is absent → output
           the normal sorted string.
        3. Otherwise (the special case):
           * output `'a'` repeated `cnt['a']` times,
           * output `'c'` repeated `cnt['c']` times,
           * output `'b'` repeated `cnt['b']` times,
           * output the rest of the letters (`d` … `z`) in increasing order.
        4. Print the constructed string.

        ## Complexity analysis
        *Counting* takes `O(|S|)`.  
        *Building* the answer scans the 26 letters a constant number of times,
        therefore `O(26)`.  
        Overall time per test case is `O(|S|)`, memory `O(26)`.

        ## When the greedy idea fails
        Greedy works here because the forbidden pattern length is only three and the
        alphabet size is fixed.  For longer patterns or when the pattern can appear
        in many different interleavings, a greedy local decision may no longer be
        sufficient and more sophisticated techniques (dynamic programming,
        automaton‑based DP, etc.) become necessary.

        ---
        This tutorial explains the reasoning behind the greedy algorithm that solves
        the original problem of finding the lexicographically smallest permutation of
        `S` that avoids the three‑letter subsequence `T`.  The same principle can be
        adapted to similar “avoid a small pattern while keeping the string minimal”
        tasks.
    </algorithm>

<description>
**Problem 1 – Recover a 4‑element array from its non‑empty subsequence sums**  
You are given 15 integers – the sums of all non‑empty subsequences of an unknown array `a` of length 4.  
The subsequence sums are sorted in non‑decreasing order.  
Recover any possible original array `a`.  
(There is always at least one solution.)
</description>
<code>
def recover_four(b):
    # b is a list of 15 sorted sums
    # The smallest element is the smallest single element of a
    x1 = b[0]

    # The second smallest element is either the second single element
    # or the sum of the two smallest elements.
    # The third smallest element must be the second single element,
    # because the sum of the two smallest is >= x1 + x1 and we have
    # only positive numbers.
    x2 = b[1]

    # The largest element is the sum of all four numbers
    total = b[-1]

    # The second largest element is total - smallest element
    # (i.e. sum of the three largest numbers)
    sum_three = b[-2]
    x4 = total - sum_three

    # Now we know three numbers: x1, x2, x4.
    # The remaining number is total - (x1 + x2 + x4)
    x3 = total - (x1 + x2 + x4)

    return [x1, x2, x3, x4]
</code>
<planning>
1. Observe that for a positive array the smallest subsequence sum equals the smallest element.  
2. The second smallest sum is the second smallest element (because any sum of two elements is ≥ the second element).  
3. The largest sum is the sum of all elements.  
4. The second largest sum equals the total minus the smallest element, giving the sum of the three largest numbers.  
5. Subtract this from the total to obtain the largest element.  
6. The remaining element is obtained by subtracting the three known elements from the total.  
7. Return the four recovered numbers.  
Complexity: O(1) per test case.
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 2 – Reconstruct an array from all pairwise sums (Turnpike variant)**  
You are given a multiset `S` containing `n·(n‑1)/2` integers – the sums of every unordered pair of elements from an unknown array `a` of size `n` (all elements are positive).  
The multiset is given in non‑decreasing order.  
Find any array `a` that generates `S`.  
It is guaranteed that a solution exists.
</description>
<code>
def recover_from_pair_sums(pair_sums, n):
    # pair_sums is a sorted list of length n*(n-1)//2
    from collections import Counter

    cnt = Counter(pair_sums)

    # The smallest sum is a1 + a2
    smallest = pair_sums[0]

    # The largest sum is a_{n-1} + a_n
    largest = pair_sums[-1]

    # Guess a1 and a2 using the two smallest sums
    # Let s1 = a1 + a2, s2 = a1 + a3
    s1 = smallest
    s2 = pair_sums[1]

    # a1 = (s1 + s2 - (a2 + a3)) / 2
    # but we don't know a2 + a3 yet.
    # Instead we try all possible values for a1 using the fact that
    # a1 = (s1 + s2 - x) / 2 where x is some element of pair_sums.
    # A simple O(n^2) back‑tracking works for n ≤ 30 (the usual limits).

    def backtrack(current):
        if len(current) == n:
            return current
        # smallest unused sum must be a1 + next unknown
        next_sum = min(k for k, v in cnt.items() if v > 0)
        next_val = next_sum - current[0]
        # try to insert next_val
        needed = [current[i] + next_val for i in range(len(current))]
        # check availability
        for val in needed:
            if cnt[val] == 0:
                return None
        # consume
        for val in needed:
            cnt[val] -= 1
        current.append(next_val)
        res = backtrack(current)
        if res:
            return res
        # backtrack undo
        current.pop()
        for val in needed:
            cnt[val] += 1
        return None

    # Try every possible a1 using the first three sums
    for third in pair_sums[2:]:
        a1 = (s1 + s2 - third) // 2
        a2 = s1 - a1
        a3 = s2 - a1
        # initialise counter again
        cnt = Counter(pair_sums)
        # remove sums formed by a1,a2,a3
        for val in [a1+a2, a1+a3, a2+a3]:
            cnt[val] -= 1
        ans = backtrack([a1, a2, a3])
        if ans:
            return sorted(ans)
    return []
</code>
<planning>
1. Understand that each element of `a` participates in `n‑1` pairwise sums.  
2. The smallest pair sum equals the sum of the two smallest elements; the largest equals the sum of the two largest.  
3. Guess the three smallest elements by trying all possibilities for the third element (the third smallest pair sum).  
4. Once three elements are fixed, remove their pairwise sums from the multiset.  
5. Repeatedly take the smallest remaining sum; it must be `a1 + next_unknown`. Compute the candidate unknown value and verify that all required sums with already known elements exist in the multiset.  
6. If they exist, delete those sums and continue; otherwise backtrack.  
7. The algorithm runs in O(n³) in the worst case, which is fast enough for typical constraints (n ≤ 30).  
Key ideas: multiset handling with a counter, greedy extraction of the smallest remaining sum, backtracking when a guess fails.
</planning>
</problem>

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – Find original numbers from sorted list of all subset sums (including empty set)**  
Given `2^n` integers that are the sums of all subsets of an unknown array `a` of size `n` (the empty subset sum `0` is included).  
The list is sorted in non‑decreasing order.  
Recover any possible array `a`.  
A solution is guaranteed to exist.
</description>
<code>
def recover_from_all_subset_sums(sums):
    # sums is a sorted list of length 2**n, sums[0] == 0
    from collections import Counter

    sums_counter = Counter(sums)
    sums_counter[0] -= 1          # remove empty subset

    result = []

    while sums_counter:
        # the smallest remaining sum is the next element of a
        x = min(k for k, v in sums_counter.items() if v > 0)
        result.append(x)

        # generate new sums that will appear after adding x
        new_sums = []
        for existing in list(sums_counter.elements()):
            new_sums.append(existing + x)

        # remove those new sums from the multiset
        for ns in new_sums:
            sums_counter[ns] -= 1
            if sums_counter[ns] == 0:
                del sums_counter[ns]

    return result
</code>
<planning>
1. The empty subset contributes sum 0, which is always the first element. Remove it.  
2. The smallest remaining sum must be the smallest element of the original array because all numbers are positive.  
3. After we have identified an element `x`, every existing subset sum can be combined with `x` to form a new subset sum. Those new sums must appear in the multiset.  
4. Remove all newly created sums from the multiset; this mimics “peeling off” the contribution of `x`.  
5. Repeat the process: the next smallest remaining sum is the next original element.  
6. Continue until the multiset becomes empty.  
Complexity: O(n·2^n) – each iteration processes the current multiset of subset sums.
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
# Tutorial – Greedy + Multiset Reconstruction for Subset‑Sum Problems  

Many combinatorial reconstruction tasks (e.g., recovering an array from its subsequence or subset sums) share a common structure:

1. **Monotonicity Insight**  
   When all original numbers are positive, the smallest sum that is still present in the multiset always corresponds to the smallest *still‑unknown* element.  
   This is because any sum that involves a larger element is at least as big as the sum of the smallest element alone.

2. **Multiset (Counter) Management**  
   The collection of generated sums is a *multiset*: the same value can appear many times, each occurrence representing a different subset.  
   Python’s `collections.Counter` (or a map from value → frequency) lets us add, subtract, and query frequencies in O(1) average time.

3. **Greedy Extraction Loop**  
   * While the multiset is not empty:  
     * Pick the minimal key with positive frequency – this is the next original number `x`.  
     * For every sum that has already been “explained” (i.e., every sum that belongs to subsets formed by previously extracted numbers), create a new sum `old + x`.  
     * Decrease the frequency of each newly created sum, because those sums are now accounted for by subsets that contain `x`.  

   The loop terminates when all sums have been explained, leaving us with the recovered array.

4. **Why It Works**  
   *Induction on the number of extracted elements*:  
   - Base case: before any extraction, the multiset contains exactly all subset sums.  
   - Inductive step: assume we have correctly identified the first `k` elements and removed all sums that involve only these `k` elements. The remaining multiset consists of sums of subsets that contain at least one of the still‑unknown elements. The smallest among them must be the smallest unknown element (positivity guarantees no smaller combination).  

5. **Complexities**  
   *Time*: Each extraction processes the current set of known sums. After `i` elements are known there are `2^i` known sums, so the total work is `∑_{i=0}^{n-1} 2^i = O(2^n)`.  
   *Space*: We store the multiset of size `2^n` and the list of known sums, also `O(2^n)`.

6. **Variants**  
   - **Fixed length (e.g., 3 or 4 elements)**: The same principle applies, but we can often shortcut the process using the smallest, second smallest, and largest sums, leading to O(1) solutions.  
   - **Pairwise sums only**: The multiset contains `n·(n‑1)/2` elements. Greedy extraction no longer works directly; instead we combine the smallest sums with a back‑tracking verification step (as shown in Problem 2).  

7. **Implementation Tips**  
   - Always remove the empty‑subset sum (`0`) before starting.  
   - When deleting frequencies, delete the key when its count reaches zero to keep the “minimum key” operation cheap.  
   - Use a sorted container (e.g., `sorted(list(counter))` or a heap) if the language does not provide O(1) min‑key retrieval.  

8. **When Greedy Fails**  
   Greedy reconstruction relies on positivity. If negative numbers are allowed, the smallest remaining sum may be a combination of several numbers, and the problem becomes NP‑hard in general. In such cases other techniques (e.g., meet‑in‑the‑middle, integer programming) are required.

**Summary**  
The core algorithm for the original problem (recovering three numbers from seven subsequence sums) is a *greedy multiset reconstruction* that exploits the ordering of sums. By repeatedly taking the smallest remaining sum as the next element and deleting the sums that involve it, we obtain the original array in linear time for fixed‑size inputs, and in `O(2^n)` for the general subset‑sum reconstruction problem.
</algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – Recover the three original numbers from the 7 sorted non‑empty subsequence sums**

---

### 1.  Observations  

For an unknown array `a = [x, y, z]` (all positive integers, order irrelevant) the multiset of its non‑empty subsequence sums consists of  

| Subset | Sum |
|--------|-----|
| {x}    | x   |
| {y}    | y   |
| {z}    | z   |
| {x,y}  | x+y |
| {x,z}  | x+z |
| {y,z}  | y+z |
| {x,y,z}| x+y+z |

When the 7 sums are sorted increasingly (`b[0] … b[6]`):

* `b[0]` is the **smallest element** of `a` (the smallest possible non‑empty sum can only be a single element).
* `b[6]` is the **total sum** `S = x + y + z`.
* `b[5]` is the **second‑largest sum**, i.e. `S – min(a)`.  
  Reason: the only sum that is missing the smallest element is the sum of the other two elements, which equals `S – min(a)`. All other sums contain the smallest element and are therefore ≤ `S – min(a)`.

From these three values we can directly obtain the three original numbers.

---

### 2.  Derivation of the three numbers  

Let  

```
m1 = b[0]                // smallest element
S  = b[6]                // total sum
m2 = b[5]                // second largest sum = S - m1
```

Then  

```
largest element  = S - m2          // because m2 = S - smallest
middle element   = S - m1 - largest // remaining value
```

All three numbers are positive because the input guarantees a valid original array.

Thus the answer for a test case is simply:

```
a1 = m1
a2 = largest
a3 = middle
```

(Any permutation of the three numbers is acceptable.)

---

### 3.  Algorithm (per test case)

```
read the 7 integers into array b[0..6]
m1 = b[0]
S  = b[6]
m2 = b[5]

largest  = S - m2
middle   = S - m1 - largest

output m1, largest, middle   // order does not matter
```

---

### 4.  Correctness Proof Sketch  

*Lemma 1*: In the multiset of non‑empty subsequence sums of positive numbers, the minimum sum equals the minimum element of the original array.  
*Proof*: Any sum containing more than one element is at least as large as each of its components, therefore cannot be smaller than the smallest single element.

*Lemma 2*: The second‑largest sum equals the total sum minus the smallest element.  
*Proof*: The only subset that does **not** contain the smallest element is the subset consisting of the other two elements; its sum is `S – min`. All other subsets contain the smallest element, so their sums are `≥ min + something` and thus ≤ `S – min`. Hence `S – min` is the maximal sum among those that miss the smallest element, i.e. the second‑largest overall.

*Theorem*: The algorithm outputs a triple `{a1,a2,a3}` that generates exactly the given multiset `b`.  
*Proof*:  
- By Lemma 1, `m1 = b[0]` equals the smallest original element.  
- By Lemma 2, `m2 = b[5] = S – m1`. Rearranging gives `largest = S – m2 = m1`. Wait, correction: `largest = S – m2` yields the element that was omitted in `m2`, i.e. the smallest element? Actually `m2 = S – smallest`, so `largest = S – m2 = smallest`. Oops, we need to be careful:  

Let's recompute correctly:  

`m2 = S – smallest` → `largest = S – m2 = smallest`. This shows a mistake in the earlier derivation.  

Correct derivation:  

Let the three numbers sorted be `a ≤ b ≤ c`.  

- `b[0] = a`.  
- `b[6] = a + b + c = S`.  
- `b[5] = b + c = S – a`.  

Thus `b + c = S – a`.  

Now `c = (b + c) – b = (S – a) – b`.  

But we don't know `b` yet. However we can obtain `c` directly:  

`c = S – b[0] – b[1]`? No, we need a different approach.

**Correct simple method** (widely used in editorial):  

- `a = b[0]`.  
- `b = b[1]` **if** `b[1]` is not equal to `a` (it could be the same value). Actually `b[1]` is the second smallest sum; it must be either the second element `b` or `a + a` (impossible because elements are distinct positions, but values may repeat). Since all numbers are positive, the second smallest sum is the second smallest element. Therefore `b = b[1]`.  

- `c = S – a – b`.  

Thus the final algorithm is:

```
a = b[0]
b = b[1]
c = b[6] - a - b
```

This works for all valid inputs (including equal elements) because the two smallest sums correspond to the two smallest single‑element subsets.

*Proof of correctness*:  
- `b[0]` and `b[1]` are the two smallest single‑element sums (they cannot be sums of two elements because any sum of two positives is ≥ each of its addends). Hence they equal the two smallest original numbers (with possible equality).  
- Subtracting them from the total sum yields the remaining element.  

All three numbers are positive and reproduce the given multiset, completing the proof.

---

### 5.  Complexity Analysis  

For each test case we perform a constant amount of arithmetic and array accesses.

- **Time**: `O(1)` per test case, `O(t)` overall (`t ≤ 5000`).  
- **Memory**: `O(1)` besides the input array of size 7.

---

### 6.  Edge Cases & Validation  

| Situation                              | Handling |
|----------------------------------------|----------|
| Some elements are equal (e.g., `[5,5,5]`) | `b[0] = b[1] = 5`, `c = S - 5 - 5 = 5`. Works. |
| Two equal, one larger (e.g., `[1,1,3]`) | `b[0]=1`, `b[1]=1`, `c = S - 1 - 1 = 3`. Works. |
| Input already sorted, but we may output any order. | Output `a, b, c` as derived; order is acceptable. |
| Very large values up to `1e9` → sums up to `3e9` (fits in 64‑bit). Use 64‑bit integer type (`long long` in C++, `int64` in Python). |

---

### 7.  Implementation Checklist  

1. Read integer `t`.  
2. Loop `t` times:  
   * Read list `b` of 7 integers.  
   * `a1 = b[0]`  
   * `a2 = b[1]`  
   * `a3 = b[6] - a1 - a2`  
   * Print `a1 a2 a3` (space‑separated).  
3. Ensure using 64‑bit integers for the subtraction to avoid overflow.  

That completes the concrete plan to solve the problem.

Because all numbers are positive, the two smallest values in the sorted list b must be the two smallest single‑element sums, i.e. the two smallest elements of a (they may be equal). Hence  
 a₁ = b[0] and a₂ = b[1].  

The largest value b[6] is the total sum S. Subtracting the two already identified elements yields the third element:  
 a₃ = S – a₁ – a₂ = b[6] – b[0] – b[1].

This reconstruction works for all valid inputs, including cases with equal elements, and respects the guarantee that a solution exists. The algorithm runs in O(1) per test case and uses only constant extra memory, which satisfies the constraints.
