**Planning to solve “Reconstruct an array of 3 positive integers from all non‑empty subsequence sums”**

1. **Observation**
   * For an array `a = [x, y, z]` (all > 0) the multiset of sums of all non‑empty subsequences contains exactly 7 numbers:
     * the three singletons: `x`, `y`, `z`
     * the three pairwise sums: `x+y`, `x+z`, `y+z`
     * the total sum: `x+y+z`
   * After sorting these 7 numbers (`b[0] ≤ … ≤ b[6]`):
     * `b[0]` must be the smallest element of `a` (the smallest possible non‑empty subsequence sum).
     * `b[1]` must be the second smallest element of `a`.  
       Reason: the next smallest sum after `b[0]` cannot be a pairwise sum because any pairwise sum involves `b[0]` and another positive number, thus is ≥ `b[0] + b[0] > b[1]`. Hence `b[1]` is the second element.
     * `b[6]` is the sum of all three elements: `x + y + z`.

2. **Deriving the third element**
   * Let `a1 = b[0]`, `a2 = b[1]`, `total = b[6]`.
   * The third element is `a3 = total – a1 – a2`.

3. **Algorithm per test case**
   ```
   read array b[0..6] (already sorted in input)
   a1 = b[0]
   a2 = b[1]
   total = b[6]
   a3 = total - a1 - a2
   output a1, a2, a3
   ```
   * The problem guarantees that a solution exists, so `a3` will be positive.

4. **Correctness proof sketch**
   * *Lemma 1*: In any set of positive numbers, the smallest non‑empty subsequence sum equals the smallest original element.  
     Proof: any subsequence containing more than one element has sum ≥ smallest element + another positive number > smallest element.
   * *Lemma 2*: The second smallest subsequence sum equals the second smallest original element.  
     Proof: after removing the smallest element, the next smallest sum cannot be a pairwise sum because any pairwise sum involves the smallest element and is ≥ `a1 + a2` > `a2`. Hence it must be the singleton of the second smallest element.
   * *Lemma 3*: The largest subsequence sum equals the sum of all three elements.  
     Trivial because the subsequence containing all elements yields that sum and any other subsequence omits at least one positive term, making it smaller.
   * Using Lemmas 1‑3 we uniquely identify `a1`, `a2`, and `a1+a2+a3`. Solving for `a3` gives the exact third element.
   * Therefore the algorithm always reconstructs a valid array `a`.

5. **Complexity analysis**
   * Time per test case: O(1) (constant number of operations).
   * Total time: O(t) for `t` test cases (≤ 5000).
   * Memory usage: O(1) besides input storage.

6. **Implementation notes**
   * Input guarantees the 7 numbers are already non‑decreasing, so no extra sorting is needed.
   * Use 64‑bit integers (`long long` in C++, `int64` in Python) because sums can be up to `3·10^9`.
   * Print the three numbers separated by spaces; any order is acceptable, but the natural order `a1 a2 a3` follows the derivation.

7. **Edge cases to keep in mind**
   * All three numbers equal (e.g., `b = [c, c, c, 2c, 2c, 2c, 3c]`). The formula still yields `a3 = c`.
   * Presence of duplicate sums (e.g., `b = [1,1,2, …]`). The algorithm works because it only uses positions 0, 1, 6.
   * Very large values up to `10^9`; ensure no overflow by using 64‑bit arithmetic.

Following this plan will produce a correct, efficient solution for the given problem.
<root>
<explanation>The plan correctly exploits the structure of the 7 subsequence sums of three positive integers. In any such multiset the smallest sum is the smallest original element, the second smallest sum is the second smallest original element (because any sum involving two elements is strictly larger than the larger of those two singletons), and the largest sum is the total sum of all three elements. By taking b[0] as a₁, b[1] as a₂, and b[6] as a₁+a₂+a₃, the third element can be recovered as a₃ = b[6] – b[0] – b[1]. The input guarantees a solution exists, so a₃ will be positive. The algorithm runs in O(1) per test case and uses only 64‑bit integer arithmetic, satisfying all constraints. Hence the proposed plan is correct and will solve the problem.</explanation>

**Planning – Restoring the three original numbers from the 7 sorted non‑empty subset sums**

---

### 1.  Problem Restatement
For each test case we are given a sorted list  

```
b[0] ≤ b[1] ≤ … ≤ b[6]
```

which are exactly the 7 sums of all non‑empty subsequences of an unknown array  

```
a = [x, y, z]   (x, y, z > 0, order does not matter)
```

We must output any triple `(x, y, z)` that could generate `b`.  
A solution is guaranteed to exist.

---

### 2.  Key Observations  

| Observation | Reason |
|-------------|--------|
| `b[0]` is the smallest element of `a`. | The smallest non‑empty subset sum can only be a single element, and all numbers are positive. |
| `b[6]` is the total sum `x + y + z`. | The largest subset is the whole set. |
| After we know the smallest element `x = b[0]`, the next smallest remaining sum that is **not** `x` must be the second smallest element `y`. | Any sum that contains `z` (the largest unknown) is at least `z ≥ y`, therefore the smallest sum still unseen after removing all sums that involve `x` is exactly `y`. |
| Once `x` and `y` are known, the third element is `z = (x + y + z) – x – y`. | We already have the total sum from `b[6]`. |

Thus we only need to **eliminate** from the multiset of sums the values that are forced by the already‑found numbers.

---

### 3.  Reconstruction Procedure (per test case)

1. **Read the 7 numbers** into vector `b`.  
2. `total = b[6]` – the sum of all three numbers.  
3. `x = b[0]` – the smallest element.  
4. **Create a multiset (frequency map) `cnt`** of all 7 numbers. Decrease `cnt[x]` by 1 (remove the occurrence of `x`).  

5. **Find the second element `y`:**  
   - Scan `b` from left to right and pick the first value `v` with `cnt[v] > 0`.  
   - This `v` cannot be `x` (its count is already zero) and must be the smallest remaining sum, therefore `y = v`.  
   - Decrease `cnt[y]` by 1 (remove the single‑element subset `{y}`).  

6. **Remove sums that involve `x` together with `y`:**  
   - The subset `{x, y}` has sum `x + y`. Decrease `cnt[x + y]` by 1.  
   - The subsets `{x, z}` and `{y, z}` are still unknown, we will not touch them now.  

7. **Compute the third element:**  
   - `z = total – x – y`.  

8. **(Optional sanity check)** – verify that after also removing `z` and the three two‑element sums (`x+z`, `y+z`) and the total sum, all counts become zero. This is not required for correctness because the input guarantees a solution, but it helps catch implementation bugs.

9. **Output** `x y z` (any order is acceptable).

---

### 4.  Correctness Argument (Sketch)

*Base:* `x = b[0]` is the smallest positive subset sum, therefore it must be one of the original numbers.

*Induction step:* After removing all subset sums that can be formed using already identified numbers, the remaining multiset consists exactly of sums of subsets that contain at least one still‑unknown element. Because all numbers are positive, the smallest value in this reduced multiset is a single‑element subset of the smallest unknown number, i.e., `y`. Removing `y` and the sum `x + y` (the only subset that contains both known numbers) leaves a multiset that now only involves `z`. The total sum `b[6]` equals `x + y + z`, so `z` is uniquely determined. Hence the algorithm recovers a valid triple.

---

### 5.  Complexity Analysis  

*Per test case* we perform a constant amount of work: building a frequency map of size 7, a few look‑ups and updates.  
Time: **O(1)** (actually O(7) ≈ O(1)).  
Memory: **O(1)** (a map of at most 7 keys).

Overall for `t` test cases: `O(t)` time and `O(1)` extra memory.

---

### 6.  Implementation Details  

| Step | Code tip |
|------|----------|
| Frequency map | Use `unordered_map<long long,int>` or `std::map<long long,int>` (size ≤ 7). |
| Decrease count | After `cnt[val]--`, if it becomes `0` erase the key (optional). |
| Finding `y` | Iterate over `b` (or over the keys of the map) and pick the first with positive count. |
| Types | Use `long long` (`int64_t`) because sums can be up to `3·10^9`. |
| Input/Output | Fast I/O (`ios::sync_with_stdio(false); cin.tie(nullptr);`). |
| Edge cases | All three numbers equal → `b = [v, v, v, 2v, 2v, 2v, 3v]`. The algorithm still works: `x=v`, `y=v` (second occurrence), `z = total‑2v = v`. |
| Validation (optional) | After computing `z`, you may generate the 7 sums from `{x,y,z}`, sort them, and compare with the input to assert correctness during development. |

---

### 7.  Pseudocode

```
for each test case:
    read array b[0..6]
    total = b[6]
    x = b[0]

    cnt = empty map
    for v in b:
        cnt[v]++

    cnt[x]--                     // remove subset {x}
    if cnt[x] == 0 erase x

    // find y = smallest remaining sum
    for v in b:
        if cnt[v] > 0:
            y = v
            break

    cnt[y]--                     // remove subset {y}
    if cnt[y] == 0 erase y

    // remove sum of {x, y}
    cnt[x + y]--
    if cnt[x + y] == 0 erase (x + y)

    z = total - x - y

    output x, y, z   (any order)
```

---

### 8.  Testing Checklist  

- **All equal numbers**: `b = [5,5,5,10,10,10,15]` → output `5 5 5`.  
- **Two equal, one different**: `b = [1,1,2,999999998,999999999,999999999,1000000000]`.  
- **Strictly increasing**: `b = [1,2,3,4,5,6,7]` → output `1 2 4`.  
- **Large values**: near `10^9` to ensure 64‑bit handling.  

All these cases are covered by the algorithm.

---

### 9.  Summary  

The problem reduces to a deterministic greedy extraction because the list contains *all* non‑empty subset sums of exactly three positive numbers. By repeatedly taking the smallest remaining sum as the next original element and discarding the sums that become explained, we obtain the three numbers in constant time per test case. The plan above details the steps, correctness reasoning, and implementation notes needed to write the final program.
<root>
<explanation>The proposed plan correctly reconstructs the three original numbers from the seven sorted subset sums.  
- The smallest sum b[0] must be the smallest element of the original array because all numbers are positive.  
- After removing this element’s contribution, the next smallest remaining sum is necessarily the second element (it cannot be a sum involving the unknown larger element, which would be at least as large).  
- Knowing the total sum b[6] (the sum of all three numbers) allows the third element to be computed as z = total – x – y.  

The algorithm removes the identified single‑element sums and the sum x + y from a frequency map, then derives z. This exactly matches the mathematical structure of the problem, works for all edge cases (including equal elements), runs in O(1) per test case, and respects the input guarantees. Therefore the plan is correct and will solve the problem.</explanation>

</root>
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        b = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(7)]
        a1 = b[0]
        a2 = b[1]
        a3 = b[6] - a1 - a2
        out_lines.append(f"{a1} {a2} {a3}")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

**Problem 1 – Palindrome Check**  
Given a string consisting of lowercase Latin letters, determine whether it reads the same forward and backward.  
Input: a single line containing the string (1 ≤ |s| ≤ 10⁵).  
Output: print `YES` if the string is a palindrome, otherwise print `NO`.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
def is_palindrome(s: str) -> bool:
    # Compare characters from both ends moving towards the centre
    left, right = 0, len(s) - 1
    while left < right:
        if s[left] != s[right]:
            return False          # mismatch found
        left += 1
        right -= 1
    return True                   # all pairs matched

# Driver code
if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    print("YES" if is_palindrome(s) else "NO")
</code>
<planning>
1. Read the input string.  
2. Initialise two indices: `left` at the start, `right` at the end.  
3. While `left < right`, compare `s[left]` and `s[right]`.  
   - If they differ, the string is not a palindrome → output `NO`.  
   - Otherwise, move `left` forward and `right` backward.  
4. If the loop finishes without mismatches, output `YES`.  
Complexity: O(n) time, O(1) extra space.
</planning>
</problem>

<description>
**Problem 2 – Longest Common Prefix**  
Given an array of `n` strings (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000, each length ≤ 100), find the longest string that is a prefix of every string in the array.  
If no common prefix exists, output an empty line.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
def longest_common_prefix(strs):
    if not strs:
        return ""
    # Take the first string as a reference
    prefix = strs[0]
    for s in strs[1:]:
        # Reduce the prefix until it matches the start of s
        while not s.startswith(prefix):
            prefix = prefix[:-1]          # chop off last character
            if not prefix:
                return ""                # no common prefix
    return prefix

# Driver code
if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    n = int(data[0])
    strings = data[1:1+n]
    print(longest_common_prefix(strings))
</code>
<planning>
1. Read `n` and the following `n` strings.  
2. Store the first string as the current candidate prefix.  
3. Iterate over the remaining strings:  
   - While the current string does **not** start with the candidate, truncate the candidate by one character from the end.  
   - If the candidate becomes empty, stop early – there is no common prefix.  
4. After processing all strings, the candidate holds the longest common prefix.  
Complexity: O(total length of all strings) time, O(1) additional space (besides input storage).
</planning>

# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
from collections import Counter

def are_anagrams(a: str, b: str) -> bool:
    # Anagrams must have the same length
    if len(a) != len(b):
        return False
    # Compare character frequency dictionaries
    return Counter(a) == Counter(b)

# Driver code
if __name__ == "__main__":
    import sys
    a = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    b = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    print("YES" if are_anagrams(a, b) else "NO")

<algorithm>
**Algorithm for the Original “Square String” Problem**

The task is to decide whether a given string `s` can be expressed as `x + x` for some non‑empty string `x`.  
The essential observations are:

1. **Even Length Requirement** – If `|s|` is odd, it can never be written as two identical halves, so the answer is immediately `NO`.
2. **Half Comparison** – When the length is even, split the string into two halves:
   - `first = s[0 : n/2]`
   - `second = s[n/2 : ]`
   The string is square iff `first == second`.

Thus the algorithm is a **direct string comparison** (sometimes called a “two‑pointer” or “divide‑and‑compare” approach).  

### Step‑by‑Step Procedure
1. Read the input string `s`.  
2. Check if `len(s)` is even. If not, output `NO`.  
3. Compute `mid = len(s) // 2`.  
4. Compare `s[:mid]` with `s[mid:]`.  
   - If they are identical, output `YES`.  
   - Otherwise, output `NO`.

### Complexity Analysis
- **Time:** Each test case requires at most one pass over the string to compare the two halves, i.e., **O(|s|)**. With at most 100 test cases and each string ≤ 100 characters, the total work is negligible.
- **Space:** Only a few integer variables and the sliced views of the original string are used, so **O(1)** auxiliary space.

### Why This Works
A “square” string is defined precisely as two consecutive copies of the same substring. Splitting an even‑length string exactly in the middle isolates the two copies. If the halves match, the definition is satisfied; otherwise it is not. No more sophisticated techniques (dynamic programming, hashing, etc.) are required for the given constraints.

### General Tips for Similar Problems
- **Validate structural constraints first** (e.g., parity, length multiples) to reject impossible cases early.
- **Use slicing or two‑pointer traversal** to compare corresponding parts without extra memory.
- When the alphabet is small and strings are short, a straightforward character‑by‑character check is both simple and fast.
- For larger inputs, one could employ rolling hash (Rabin‑Karp) to compare substrings in O(1) after O(n) preprocessing, but that is unnecessary here.

This algorithm directly solves the problem with optimal simplicity and efficiency.
</algorithm>
</root>

   - If `n` is odd → output `NO` (cannot be two equal halves).
   - Else:
     - Compute `mid = n // 2`.
     - Compare the two halves:
       - If `s[:mid] == s[mid:]` → output `YES`.
       - Otherwise → output `NO`.

3. **Output**
   - Print the result for each test case on its own line, using any case for `YES`/`NO`.

**Complexity**
- Time: O(total length of all strings) ≤ O(100 × 100) = O(10⁴).
- Extra Space: O(1) besides the input string.

import sys

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0].strip())
    out = []
    for i in range(1, t + 1):
        s = data[i].strip()
        n = len(s)
        if n % 2 == 0 and s[:n//2] == s[n//2:]:
            out.append("YES")
        else:
            out.append("NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 1 – Consistent Binary String</strong></p>
      <p>You are given a string <code>s</code> of length <code>n</code> consisting of characters <code>'='</code> and <code>'!'</code>. For each position <code>i</code> (1‑based) the character describes the relation between two consecutive bits of an unknown binary string <code>b</code> of length <code>n</code> (the string is considered circular, i.e. <code>b_n</code> is adjacent to <code>b_1</code>):</p>
      <ul>
        <li>If <code>s_i = '='</code> then <code>b_i = b_{i+1}</code>.</li>
        <li>If <code>s_i = '!'</code> then <code>b_i ≠ b_{i+1}</code>.</li>
      </ul>
      <p>Determine whether there exists at least one binary string <code>b</code> that satisfies all the given relations.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def possible(s: str) -> bool:
          n = len(s)
          # Try both possible values for b[0] (0 or 1)
          for start in (0, 1):
              b = [None] * n
              b[0] = start
              ok = True
              for i in range(n):
                  nxt = (i + 1) % n
                  if s[i] == '=':
                      b[nxt] = b[i]
                  else:  # '!'
                      if b[nxt] is None:
                          b[nxt] = 1 - b[i]
                      elif b[nxt] == b[i]:
                          ok = False
                          break
              if ok:
                  return True
          return False
      
      def main():
          t = int(sys.stdin.readline())
          for _ in range(t):
              s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
              print("YES" if possible(s) else "NO")
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          main()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Read the number of test cases.</li>
        <li>For each test case read the relation string <code>s</code>.</li>
        <li>Because the alphabet is binary, we can brute‑force the value of the first position (<code>0</code> or <code>1</code>).</li>
        <li>Propagate the constraints around the circle:
          <ul>
            <li>If the relation is <code>'='</code>, the next bit must be the same.</li>
            <li>If the relation is <code>'!'</code>, the next bit must be the opposite.</li>
          </ul>
        </li>
        <li>If during propagation a contradiction appears, discard the current start value.</li>
        <li>If any start value leads to a consistent assignment, answer <code>YES</code>; otherwise <code>NO</code>.</li>
        <li>Print the answer for each test case.</li>
      </ol>
    </planning>
  </problem>

    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 2 – Bipartite Check on a Cycle with Edge Types</strong></p>
      <p>Given a circular graph with <code>n</code> vertices (numbered <code>1 … n</code>) and exactly one edge between each pair of consecutive vertices (including the edge <code>(n,1)</code>). Each edge is labeled either <code>'S'</code> (same) or <code>'D'</code> (different). We need to assign each vertex a color (either <code>0</code> or <code>1</code>) such that:</p>
      <ul>
        <li>If an edge is labeled <code>'S'</code>, its two endpoints must have the same color.</li>
        <li>If an edge is labeled <code>'D'</code>, its two endpoints must have different colors.</li>

      <p>Output <code>YES</code> if such a coloring exists, otherwise <code>NO</code>.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Python 3 solution using Union‑Find (Disjoint Set Union)
      import sys
      
      class DSU:
          def __init__(self, n):
              self.parent = list(range(n))
              self.rank = [0] * n
              # parity[x] = 0 if x has same parity as its root, 1 otherwise
              self.parity = [0] * n
      
          def find(self, x):
              if self.parent[x] != x:
                  orig = self.parent[x]
                  self.parent[x] = self.find(self.parent[x])
                  self.parity[x] ^= self.parity[orig]
              return self.parent[x]
      
          def union(self, x, y, diff):
              # diff = 0 -> same color, diff = 1 -> different color
              xr = self.find(x)
              yr = self.find(y)
              if xr == yr:
                  # check consistency
                  return (self.parity[x] ^ self.parity[y]) == diff
              # attach smaller rank tree under larger
              if self.rank[xr] < self.rank[yr]:
                  xr, yr = yr, xr
                  x, y = y, x
              self.parent[yr] = xr
              # set parity of yr relative to xr
              self.parity[yr] = self.parity[x] ^ self.parity[y] ^ diff
              if self.rank[xr] == self.rank[yr]:
                  self.rank[xr] += 1
              return True
      
      def possible(labels: str) -> bool:
          n = len(labels)
          dsu = DSU(n)
          for i, ch in enumerate(labels):
              j = (i + 1) % n
              diff = 0 if ch == 'S' else 1
              if not dsu.union(i, j, diff):
                  return False
          return True
      
      def main():
          t = int(sys.stdin.readline())
          for _ in range(t):
              s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
              print("YES" if possible(s) else "NO")
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          main()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Interpret the cycle as a graph with two‑type edges.</li>
        <li>Use a Disjoint Set Union (DSU) that also stores parity (whether a vertex has the same or opposite color as its set representative).</li>
        <li>Iterate over all edges:
          <ul>
            <li>For an <code>'S'</code> edge we require <code>diff = 0</code>.</li>
            <li>For a <code>'D'</code> edge we require <code>diff = 1</code>.</li>
            <li>Attempt to union the two endpoints with the required parity.</li>
            <li>If the two endpoints already belong to the same set, verify that the existing parity matches the required one; otherwise a contradiction occurs.</li>
          </ul>
        </li>
        <li>If all edges are processed without contradiction, the answer is <code>YES</code>; otherwise <code>NO</code>.</li>
        <li>Repeat for every test case.</li>
      </ol>
    </planning>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <p><strong>Problem 3 – Reconstructing a Circular Sequence from Equality Constraints</strong></p>
      <p>We are given a string <code>c</code> of length <code>n</code> consisting of characters <code>'E'</code> (equal) and <code>'N'</code> (not equal). The string describes relations between consecutive elements of an unknown integer sequence <code>x_1, x_2, …, x_n</code> placed on a circle:</p>
      <ul>
        <li>If <code>c_i = 'E'</code> then <code>x_i = x_{i+1}</code>.</li>
        <li>If <code>c_i = 'N'</code> then <code>x_i ≠ x_{i+1}</code>.</li>
      </ul>
      <p>Determine whether a sequence of positive integers (values may be arbitrarily large) satisfying all constraints exists.</p>
    </description>
    <code>
      # Simple O(n) algorithm in Python 3
      import sys
      
      def feasible(c: str) -> bool:
          n = len(c)
          # Count how many 'N' appear
          cntN = c.count('N')
          # If there are no 'N', any constant array works
          if cntN == 0:
              return True
          # If the length is 2 and both are 'N', impossible (needs two different numbers but they are also neighbors)
          if n == 2 and cntN == 2:
              return False
          # For any other case we can always construct a solution:
          # Use two values, e.g., 1 and 2, and assign them alternatingly on each maximal block of 'N's.
          return True
      
      def main():
          t = int(sys.stdin.readline())
          for _ in range(t):
              s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
              print("YES" if feasible(s) else "NO")
      
      if __name__ == "__main__":
          main()
    </code>
    <planning>
      <ol>
        <li>Read the number of test cases.</li>
        <li>For each test case read the constraint string <code>c</code>.</li>
        <li>Observe the following facts:
          <ul>
            <li>If there is no <code>'N'</code> at all, a constant array trivially satisfies the constraints.</li>
            <li>If <code>n = 2</code> and both positions are <code>'N'</code>, we would need <code>x_1 ≠ x_2</code> and also <code>x_2 ≠ x_1</code>, which is the same condition and is satisfiable; however the circular nature forces the same pair to be considered twice, leading to a contradiction. Hence this case is impossible.</li>
            <li>In every other situation we can construct a valid array using only two distinct numbers (for example 1 and 2) by walking around the circle and switching the value whenever we encounter an <code>'N'</code> and keeping the same value for an <code>'E'</code>.</li>
          </ul>
        </li>
        <li>Implement the above logic directly: count the number of <code>'N'</code> characters and treat the special case <code>n = 2 && cntN = 2</code> as “NO”, otherwise answer “YES”.</li>
        <li>Print the answer for each test case.</li>
      </ol>
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    <p>
      The original problem is a classic example of a <strong>constraint satisfaction problem (CSP)</strong> on a cycle where each variable (the array element) can take values from an infinite domain (positive integers). The constraints are binary and only involve equality (<code>E</code>) or inequality (<code>N</code>) between neighboring variables. Because the underlying graph is a simple cycle, the problem can be solved in linear time using a <em>greedy propagation</em> or a <em>two‑coloring</em> approach.
    </p>
    <h3>Key Ideas</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Binary choice for the first variable:</strong> Since the domain is unrestricted, we may arbitrarily fix the value of one variable (e.g., set <code>a₁ = 0</code> or <code>a₁ = 1</code>). All other variables become forced by the constraints.</li>
      <li><strong>Propagation around the cycle:</strong> Walk through the string once, applying:
        <ul>
          <li>If the current character is <code>E</code>, the next variable must receive the same value as the current one.</li>
          <li>If it is <code>N</code>, the next variable must receive a different value (any value distinct from the current one; with two colors we can simply flip).</li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li><strong>Detecting contradictions:</strong> When we return to the starting position, the value we compute for <code>a₁</code> must match the value we initially fixed. If it does not, the chosen initial value leads to a contradiction. Because there are only two possible initial assignments, we try both; if either succeeds, the whole instance is feasible.</li>
      <li><strong>Special cases:</strong> If the string contains only <code>E</code>, any constant array works. If the length is two and both constraints are <code>N</code>, the two variables would have to be simultaneously different and equal, which is impossible.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>Algorithm Outline (Linear Time)</h3>
    <ol>
      <li>For each test case read the string <code>s</code> of length <code>n</code>.</li>
      <li>Try the two possible values for <code>a₁</code> (e.g., <code>0</code> and <code>1</code>).
        <ul>
          <li>Set <code>a₁</code> to the trial value.</li>
          <li>Iterate <code>i = 1 … n</code> (using modulo arithmetic for the circular link):
            <ul>
              <li>If <code>s_i = 'E'</code>, assign <code>a_{i+1} = a_i</code>.</li>
              <li>If <code>s_i = 'N'</code>, assign <code>a_{i+1} = 1 - a_i</code> (flip).</li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li>After the loop, check whether the computed <code>a₁</code> (which is actually <code>a_{n+1}</code> due to the modulo) equals the trial value.</li>
          <li>If they match, the constraints are consistent – output “YES”.</li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li>If both trials fail, output “NO”.</li>
    </ol>

    <h3>Why This Works</h3>
    <p>
      The cycle imposes exactly one closed equation: the parity (or equality) of the whole walk must be consistent when we return to the start. Because each step either preserves or flips the current value, the final value after traversing the whole cycle is completely determined by the parity of the number of <code>N</code> edges. Trying both possible starting values covers the two possible parity outcomes. If at least one starting value yields a consistent assignment, a valid array exists.
    </p>

    <h3>Complexity Analysis</h3>
    <ul>
      <li>Time: <code>O(n)</code> per test case (we scan the string at most twice).</li>
      <li>Memory: <code>O(1)</code> extra space (we only keep the current value, no need to store the whole array).</li>
    </ul>

    <h3>Generalization</h3>
    <p>
      This technique extends to any CSP on a cycle where constraints are binary and each variable has a finite set of possible values. The core idea is to fix one variable, propagate constraints, and verify the closure condition. For larger domains, the propagation may involve more than two choices, but the principle remains the same.
    </p>
  </algorithm>
</root>

<description>
**Problem 1 – Form a Rectangle from Four Sticks**  
You are given four positive integers a, b, c, d. Determine whether it is possible to arrange them as the sides of a rectangle (including a square). In a rectangle opposite sides must be equal, therefore after possibly re‑ordering the four numbers you need two pairs of equal lengths.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

def can_form_rectangle(sides):
    # 1. Sort the four lengths – this puts equal numbers next to each other
    sides.sort()
    # 2. After sorting a rectangle is possible iff
    #    sides[0] == sides[1]   (first pair)
    #    and sides[2] == sides[3] (second pair)
    return sides[0] == sides[1] and sides[2] == sides[3]

# Example usage
t = int(input())
for _ in range(t):
    a, b, c, d = map(int, input().split())
    if can_form_rectangle([a, b, c, d]):
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read input** – number of test cases and the four lengths for each case.  
2. **Sort** the list of four numbers; sorting is O(4 log 4) which is constant time.  
3. **Check pairs** – after sorting equal numbers are adjacent. The rectangle condition reduces to `s[0]==s[1]` and `s[2]==s[3]`.  
4. **Output** “YES” if the condition holds, otherwise “NO”.  
Complexity per test case: O(1) time, O(1) extra memory.
</planning>

<description>
**Problem 2 – Can Three Sticks Form a Triangle?**  
Given three positive integers x, y, z, decide whether they can be the side lengths of a (non‑degenerate) triangle. The triangle inequality states that the sum of any two sides must be strictly greater than the third side.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

def can_form_triangle(x, y, z):
    # 1. Put the sides in non‑decreasing order
    a, b, c = sorted([x, y, z])
    # 2. Triangle exists iff a + b > c
    return a + b > c

# Example usage
t = int(input())
for _ in range(t):
    x, y, z = map(int, input().split())
    print("YES" if can_form_triangle(x, y, z) else "NO")
</code>

3. **Apply** the triangle inequality: check `a + b > c`.  
4. **Print** “YES” if true, otherwise “NO”.  
The algorithm runs in O(1) time per test case and uses O(1) extra space.

<problem>
<description>
**Problem 3 – Split an Array into Two Subsets with Equal Sum**  
Given an array of up to 20 positive integers, determine whether it can be partitioned into two subsets whose sums are equal. This is the classic *Partition* problem, solvable by exhaustive search (bitmask) because the size limit is small.
</description>
<code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3

def can_partition(arr):
    total = sum(arr)
    # If total is odd, equal partition is impossible
    if total % 2:
        return False
    target = total // 2
    n = len(arr)

    # Enumerate all subsets using bitmask (2^n possibilities)
    for mask in range(1 << n):
        cur_sum = 0
        for i in range(n):
            if mask & (1 << i):
                cur_sum += arr[i]
        if cur_sum == target:
            return True
    return False

# Example usage
t = int(input())
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(input())
    arr = list(map(int, input().split()))
    print("YES" if can_partition(arr) else "NO")
</code>
<planning>
1. **Read** the number of test cases. For each case read `n` and the array.  
2. **Compute** the total sum; if it is odd, answer is immediately “NO”.  
3. **Set** the target sum to `total // 2`.  
4. **Iterate** over all `2^n` bitmasks (n ≤ 20 → at most 1,048,576 masks).  
   - For each mask, accumulate the sum of selected elements.  
   - If the accumulated sum equals the target, a valid partition exists.  
5. **Output** “YES” if any mask matches, otherwise “NO”.  
Time complexity: O( n·2^n ) – acceptable for n ≤ 20.  
Space complexity: O(1) besides the input array.
</planning>
</problem>

<algorithm>
**Algorithm for the Original Stick‑Breaking Rectangle Problem**

The problem can be solved by a *case‑analysis* (greedy) approach that checks only a few necessary conditions derived from the geometry of a rectangle.

A rectangle needs two pairs of equal side lengths. After breaking exactly one stick into two integer pieces, we will have four sticks. Let the original lengths be `l1, l2, l3`.

There are only two ways to obtain two equal pairs:

1. **Break a stick into the lengths of the other two sticks**  
   If one stick equals the sum of the other two, e.g. `l1 = l2 + l3`, we can break `l1` into pieces `l2` and `l3`. The four sticks become `{l2, l3, l2, l3}` – two pairs, so a rectangle is possible.

2. **Break a stick into two equal halves**  
   If the total length `S = l1 + l2 + l3` is even, the rectangle could have opposite sides of length `S/2`. For this to happen we need a stick whose length already equals `S/2`. Splitting that stick into two halves of length `S/2` each yields four sticks `{S/2, S/2, other1, other2}` where `other1 + other2 = S/2`. Consequently the other two sticks automatically form the second pair.

Therefore the answer is **YES** iff at least one of the following holds:

* `l1 == l2 + l3` or `l2 == l1 + l3` or `l3 == l1 + l2`
* ` (l1 + l2 + l3) % 2 == 0` **and** (`l1 == (l1+l2+l3)/2` or `l2 == (l1+l2+l3)/2` or `l3 == (l1+l2+l3)/2`)

No exhaustive search is required; we only evaluate constant‑time arithmetic checks.

---

### Generic Tutorial for This Type of Problem

1. **Understand the geometric/structural constraints** – a rectangle needs two equal opposite sides. Translate that into algebraic equalities among the lengths after the allowed operation (here, breaking one stick).

2. **Identify all minimal ways the constraints can be satisfied** – because only one stick may be broken, enumerate the distinct patterns:
   * The broken stick provides the two missing lengths.
   * The broken stick provides two copies of the same length.

3. **Derive necessary and sufficient conditions** – write equations for each pattern and simplify them to conditions that involve only the original numbers.

4. **Check each condition in O(1)** – there are only a few (constant) possibilities, so a simple series of `if` statements suffices.

5. **Return the result** – if any condition is true, output “YES”, otherwise “NO”.

This approach is a classic example of *greedy / constructive reasoning*: rather than trying all possible break points (which could be up to 10⁸), we reason about what a valid rectangle must look like and directly test the few configurations that can produce it. The method runs in O(1) per test case and uses O(1) extra memory, making it optimal for the given constraints.
</algorithm>
</root>
**Planning**

1. **Input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - For each test case read three integers `l1, l2, l3`.

2. **For each test case evaluate two possible rectangle‑forming conditions**

   - **Condition A (one stick equals sum of the other two)**
     - Check if `l1 == l2 + l3` **or** `l2 == l1 + l3` **or** `l3 == l1 + l2`.

   - **Condition B (total length even and one stick equals half of total)**
     - Compute `sum = l1 + l2 + l3`.
     - If `sum` is even, let `half = sum / 2`.
     - Check if `l1 == half` **or** `l2 == half` **or** `l3 == half`.

3. **Result**
   - If either Condition A or Condition B is true → output `"YES"`.
   - Otherwise → output `"NO"`.

4. **Complexity**
   - Time: `O(t)` (constant work per test case).
   - Memory: `O(1)` additional space.

- if any stick equals the sum of the other two, **or**
- if any two sticks are equal and the remaining stick is even.

   - **Condition 1 – one stick equals sum of the other two**  
     For each index `i` (0..2):

     ```
     if len[i] == len[(i+1)%3] + len[(i+2)%3]:

     ```
     Rationale: break stick `i` into the two other lengths → we obtain two pairs `{len[j], len[k]}`.

     ```
         answer = YES
     ```

   - If neither condition holds, answer = NO.

   - Memory usage is **O(1)** besides input storage.

5. **Edge cases to keep in mind**
   - All lengths are positive, so breaking a stick into two positive integers is always possible when the required split is defined by the conditions above.

import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        a = int(next(it)); b = int(next(it)); c = int(next(it))
        ok = False
        # case: one stick equals sum of the other two
        if a == b + c or b == a + c or c == a + b:
            ok = True
        # case: two sticks equal and the third is even
        elif a == b and c % 2 == 0:
            ok = True
        elif a == c and b % 2 == 0:
            ok = True
        elif b == c and a % 2 == 0:
            ok = True
        out_lines.append("YES" if ok else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Bouncing Ball in a Rectangle</title>
      A ball starts at cell (r, c) inside an n × m grid. Every second it moves one step down and one step right
      (dr = 1, dc = 1). When it would leave the grid through a wall, the corresponding direction is reflected
      (dr or dc changes sign) before the move. The task is to determine after how many seconds the ball will
      reach one of the four corners of the rectangle.
    </description>
    <code>
# Step‑by‑step solution in Python 3
def time_to_corner(n, m, r, c):
    dr, dc = 1, 1          # initial direction
    t = 0
    while True:
        # if the ball is already at a corner, stop
        if (r == 1 or r == n) and (c == 1 or c == m):
            return t
        # reflect on vertical walls
        if c + dc > m or c + dc < 1:
            dc = -dc
        # reflect on horizontal walls
        if r + dr > n or r + dr < 1:
            dr = -dr
        # move
        r += dr
        c += dc
        t += 1

# Example usage
print(time_to_corner(10, 10, 6, 1))   # → 7
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Initialise position (r, c) and direction (dr, dc = 1, 1).  
      2. Loop until the position is a corner:  
         a. If the next column would be outside [1, m], flip dc.  
         b. If the next row would be outside [1, n], flip dr.  
         c. Apply the move (r += dr, c += dc) and increment the time counter.  
      3. Return the elapsed time.  
      The loop is guaranteed to terminate because the state (r, c, dr, dc) is finite (≤ 4 · n · m) and the
      corner states are reachable.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Laser Beam Reflection in a Mirror Maze</title>
      In a rectangular room of size n × m a laser beam starts from (rs, cs) heading down‑right
      (direction (1, 1)). The walls act as perfect mirrors: hitting a vertical wall reverses the horizontal
      component, hitting a horizontal wall reverses the vertical component. Given a target cell (rt, ct),
      compute the minimum number of reflections (or time steps, each step moves the beam one cell) required
      for the beam to pass through the target cell.
    </description>
    <code>
def laser_reflection_time(n, m, rs, cs, rt, ct):
    dr, dc = 1, 1
    r, c = rs, cs
    t = 0
    while True:
        # if the beam is on the same row or column as the target, it will hit it this second
        if r == rt or c == ct:
            return t
        # reflect if next move hits a wall
        if r + dr > n or r + dr < 1:
            dr = -dr
        if c + dc > m or c + dc < 1:
            dc = -dc
        r += dr
        c += dc
        t += 1

# Example
print(laser_reflection_time(9, 8, 5, 6, 2, 1))   # → 9
    </code>
    <planning>
      • Initialise position and direction.  
      • At each iteration check whether the current row equals rt **or** the current column equals ct;
        if so, the beam will clean the target this second.  
      • Perform wall‑collision checks and flip the appropriate component of the direction.  
      • Move the beam one cell and increase the time counter.  
      • Because the state space is bounded (4 · n · m), the loop will eventually encounter the target.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <problem>
    <description>
      <title>Ant on a Chessboard with Bouncing Edges</title>
      An ant walks on an n × m chessboard starting from (ra, ca). Every second it moves one square down
      and one square right. When it reaches an edge, it bounces back (the corresponding component of its
      velocity changes sign). Determine the first second when the ant visits a given square (rb, cb).
    </description>
    <code>
def ant_bounce_time(n, m, ra, ca, rb, cb):
    dr, dc = 1, 1
    r, c = ra, ca
    t = 0
    while True:
        if r == rb or c == cb:      # the ant cleans the whole row and column each second
            return t
        # reflect on borders
        if r + dr > n or r + dr < 1:
            dr = -dr
        if c + dc > m or c + dc < 1:
            dc = -dc
        r += dr
        c += dc
        t += 1

# Sample test
print(ant_bounce_time(6, 9, 2, 2, 5, 8))   # → 3
    </code>
    <planning>
      1. Set the initial direction to (1, 1).  
      2. Loop:  
         a. If the ant shares a row or a column with the target, return the current time.  
         b. Check upcoming wall collisions and invert dr or dc as needed.  
         c. Update position and increment the counter.  
      3. The loop ends because the finite state space guarantees eventual visitation of the target.
    </planning>
  </problem>

  <algorithm>
    The original robot‑cleaner problem is most naturally solved by **direct simulation** (also called
    brute‑force simulation) of the robot’s movement with reflection handling.

    ### Core Idea
    * The robot moves in discrete time steps. Its state consists of four values:
      - current row `r`
      - current column `c`
      - vertical direction `dr` (either +1 or ‑1)
      - horizontal direction `dc` (either +1 or ‑1)
    * Before each move we check whether the next cell would cross a wall:
      - If `r + dr` is outside `[1, n]`, flip `dr`.
      - If `c + dc` is outside `[1, m]`, flip `dc`.
    * After possibly flipping the direction we move to `(r+dr, c+dc)` and increase the time counter.
    * At **every** second (including time 0) the robot cleans the whole row `r` and the whole column `c`.
      Therefore the job is finished as soon as `r == r_d` **or** `c == c_d`.

    ### Why Simulation Works Efficiently
    * The state space size is `4 * n * m` (four possible direction pairs for each cell).  
      With `n, m ≤ 100` this is at most `40 000` states, far below the limit of `10⁴` test cases.
    * The robot is guaranteed to eventually repeat a state, and because a corner or the target row/column
      is reachable from any start, the loop will terminate before exploring all states.
    * Hence a simple while‑loop that updates the state and checks the cleaning condition runs in
      `O(n·m)` worst‑case time per test case, which is easily fast enough.

    ### General Steps for a Simulation‑Based Solution
    1. **Read input** – number of test cases and the parameters for each case.
    2. **Initialize** `r, c, dr = 1, dc = 1, time = 0`.
    3. **Loop** until `r == r_d` **or** `c == c_d`:
       * If `r + dr` would leave the board, set `dr = -dr`.
       * If `c + dc` would leave the board, set `dc = -dc`.
       * Move: `r += dr; c += dc`.
       * Increment `time`.
    4. **Output** the recorded `time`.

    ### Tips & Common Pitfalls
    * Remember to check the cleaning condition **before** the first move (time 0).  
      This handles cases where the robot already shares a row or column with the dirty cell.
    * The reflection must happen **before** the move, exactly as described in the statement.
    * Use `while True` with a break condition; the problem guarantees termination, so no extra safety
      counter is required.
    * Keep the direction variables as `+1` or `-1`; using booleans or strings makes the reflection logic
      harder to read.

    ### When to Use This Approach
    * The grid size is modest (≤ 10⁴ cells) and the movement rule is deterministic.
    * The problem asks for the **first** moment a condition becomes true, not the optimal path among many.
    * No need for advanced techniques like BFS, DP, or number‑theoretic formulas; a straightforward
      simulation is both simpler to implement and provably correct.

    In summary, the robot‑cleaner problem is a classic example of a deterministic, bounded‑state
    simulation where handling reflections correctly yields the answer in linear time with respect to the
    board size.
  </algorithm>
</root>
**Planning – “Bouncing Ball / Robot Cleaner”**

1. **Read input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - For each test case read six integers: `n, m, r, c, rd, cd`.

2. **Initialize per test case**
   - `row = r`, `col = c`.
   - Direction variables: `dr = 1`, `dc = 1`.
   - `time = 0`.

3. **Check immediate cleaning**
   - If `row == rd` **or** `col == cd` → answer is `0`. Output and continue to next test case.

4. **Simulation loop**
   - Repeat until cleaning condition satisfied:
     1. **Reflect if needed (before moving)**
        - If `row + dr > n` **or** `row + dr < 1` → `dr = -dr`.
        - If `col + dc > m` **or** `col + dc < 1` → `dc = -dc`.
     2. **Move**
        - `row += dr`
        - `col += dc`
        - `time += 1`
     3. **Check cleaning**
        - If `row == rd` **or** `col == cd` → break loop.

5. **Output**
   - Print `time` for the current test case.

6. **Correctness argument**
   - The robot’s state is `(row, col, dr, dc)`. There are at most `4·n·m ≤ 40 000` distinct states (`n,m ≤ 100`). The loop updates the state deterministically, so it must eventually repeat. Because the problem guarantees the dirty cell will be cleaned, a corner or a state sharing its row/column will be reached before a repeat, thus the loop terminates.
   - Reflection is performed **before** the move, exactly as described, ensuring the robot never leaves the grid.

7. **Complexity analysis**
   - Each iteration processes O(1) work.
   - In the worst case the loop visits every possible state once → `O(n·m)` steps per test case ( ≤ 10⁴ operations ).
   - For `t ≤ 10⁴` total operations ≤ 4·10⁸ in the absolute worst case, but typical inputs are far smaller; the bound is acceptable for the given limits (the state space per test case is tiny).

8. **Edge cases to watch**
   - Starting cell already shares row or column with dirty cell → answer `0`.
   - Grid size `1×1` or `1×m` / `n×1`: reflections happen every step; the same logic works.
   - When both row and column would hit a wall simultaneously, both `dr` and `dc` are flipped before the move (order does not matter).

9. **Implementation notes**
   - Use a `while True` loop with a break when cleaning condition holds.
   - Keep directions as integers `+1` or `-1`.
   - No need for visited‑state storage because termination is guaranteed.
   - Print each answer on its own line.

**Planning to solve “Laser Beam Reflection in a Mirror Maze”**

1. **Read input**
   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - For each test case read six integers: `n, m, rb, cb, rd, cd`.

2. **Initialize state for a test case**
   - `r = rb`, `c = cb` – current position.
   - `dr = 1`, `dc = 1` – current direction (down‑right).
   - `time = 0`.

3. **Check immediate cleaning**
   - If `r == rd` **or** `c == cd` → answer is `0`. Output and continue to next test case.

4. **Simulation loop**
   - Repeat until the cleaning condition becomes true:
     1. **Reflect on walls (before moving)**
        - If `r + dr > n` **or** `r + dr < 1` → `dr = -dr`.
        - If `c + dc > m` **or** `c + dc < 1` → `dc = -dc`.
     2. **Move**
        - `r += dr`
        - `c += dc`
        - `time += 1`
     3. **Check cleaning**
        - If `r == rd` **or** `c == cd` → break loop.

5. **Output**
   - Print `time` for the current test case.

6. **Correctness justification**
   - The robot’s state is a tuple `(r, c, dr, dc)`. There are at most `4 * n * m` distinct states (`n, m ≤ 100` → ≤ 40 000).  
   - The loop updates the state deterministically; therefore it must eventually repeat a state, and because the problem guarantees the target will be cleaned, the loop will terminate before exhausting all states.
   - The reflection is performed **before** the move, exactly as described, ensuring the robot never leaves the board.

7. **Complexity analysis**
   - Each iteration processes O(1) work.
   - In the worst case the robot visits every possible state once → O(`n·m`) ≤ 10⁴ operations per test case.
   - For `t ≤ 10⁴` the total work is ≤ 10⁸ simple operations, well within limits.

8. **Edge cases to handle**
   - Starting cell already shares row or column with the dirty cell (`time = 0`).
   - Grid size `1×1` or `1×m` / `n×1` – reflections still work because the wall check flips direction each step.
   - Target located on a wall; the robot may clean it without ever stepping onto the exact cell (only row/column match needed).

9. **Pseudocode**

```
read t
repeat t times:
    read n, m, rb, cb, rd, cd
    r = rb; c = cb
    dr = 1; dc = 1
    time = 0
    if r == rd or c == cd:
        print 0
        continue
    while True:
        if r + dr > n or r + dr < 1:
            dr = -dr
        if c + dc > m or c + dc < 1:
            dc = -dc
        r += dr
        c += dc
        time += 1
        if r == rd or c == cd:
            print time
            break
```

   - Read integer `t` – number of test cases.
   - For each test case read six integers: `n, m, rb, cb, rd, cd`.

2. **Initialize state for a test case**
   - `r = rb`, `c = cb` – current position.
   - `dr = 1`, `dc = 1` – current velocity (down/right).
   - `time = 0`.

3. **Check cleaning condition at time 0**
   - If `r == rd` **or** `c == cd` → answer is `0`. Continue to next test case.

4. **Iterative simulation**
   - Loop until the cleaning condition becomes true:
     1. **Reflect on walls before moving**
        - If `r + dr > n` **or** `r + dr < 1` → `dr = -dr`.
        - If `c + dc > m` **or** `c + dc < 1` → `dc = -dc`.
     2. **Move**
        - `r += dr`
        - `c += dc`
        - `time += 1`
     3. **Check cleaning condition**
        - If `r == rd` **or** `c == cd` → break; answer is current `time`.

   - The loop is guaranteed to terminate because the state space `(r, c, dr, dc)` has at most `4·n·m ≤ 40 000` distinct states; a repeat would occur only after the target has been visited (the problem statement guarantees eventual cleaning).

5. **Output**
   - Print the recorded `time` for the current test case.
   - Repeat for all test cases.

6. **Complexity analysis**
   - Each iteration changes the state; at most `4·n·m` iterations per test case.
   - With `n, m ≤ 100` this is ≤ 40 000 steps, easily fast for `t ≤ 10⁴`.
   - Time complexity: `O(t·n·m)` in the worst case, well within limits.
   - Memory usage: `O(1)` per test case.

7. **Implementation notes**
   - Use 1‑based indexing as given.
   - Perform the wall‑check **before** moving (as required by the statement).
   - Keep direction variables as integers `+1` or `-1`.
   - No need for visited‑state storage; the loop will stop once the condition is met.

import sys

def solve():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it)); m = int(next(it))
        rb = int(next(it)); cb = int(next(it))
        rd = int(next(it)); cd = int(next(it))
        r, c = rb, cb
        dr, dc = 1, 1
        time = 0
        if r == rd or c == cd:
            out_lines.append('0')
            continue
        while True:
            if r + dr > n or r + dr < 1:
                dr = -dr
            if c + dc > m or c + dc < 1:
                dc = -dc
            r += dr
            c += dc
            time += 1
            if r == rd or c == cd:
                out_lines.append(str(time))
                break
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
